# Involuntary Architecture

# Chapter 1

The filing charm collapsed on a Tuesday evening, and three years of sorted correspondence slid from its shelves and pooled on the floor of Hermione's office in a disordered heap.

She stared at it. The charm was hers — a standard administrative enchantment she'd anchored to the oak cabinet during her first week in the Department of Mysteries, the kind of magic that sat in wood and worked without attention. She'd refreshed it once, maybe twice, in three years. It was not the kind of spell that failed.

She knelt and picked up the nearest scroll. An internal memo from August, the wax seal still intact. She turned it over in her hands and tried to re-engage the filing charm, feeding magic into the cabinet's grain. The spell caught, wavered, and held — but thin, the way a candle looks in a draft. She could feel the difference. The same amount of magic leaving her wand, the same intention, the same precisely shaped enchantment, but the spell sat in the wood like something balanced on a ledge rather than rooted in place.

She filed the memo by hand. Then the next one. Then the next. By the time she'd finished, it was past eight and her knees ached from the stone floor and the office was quiet in the particular way the Department of Mysteries got quiet after hours — not just empty but sealed, the soundproofing charms thick enough to make her own breathing the loudest thing in the room.

The corridor lights thinned as she left. Not a flicker — a slow dimming, as if the illumination charms were being drawn across more space than they could sustain. She'd been watching this for weeks. The preservation enchantments on the east archive had developed a stutter in September. Three diagnostic arrays in Section G had returned anomalous readings that she was no longer confident were anomalous — they might simply be accurate measurements of a field that had shifted outside expected parameters. The ambient-temperature charms in her office had been running two degrees cold since mid-October, which was not noticeable if you were wearing a jumper but was absolutely noticeable if you were Hermione Granger and you measured things.

She'd written the report in September — detailed, thorough, specific to the point of tedium, because she knew Croaker and she knew that anything she submitted would be read once, evaluated for political risk, and filed. She'd been right. Croaker had read it, asked her to continue monitoring, and not mentioned it again. The Department of Mysteries did not acknowledge crises. It acknowledged data.

The data said the ambient magical field across Britain was declining. Not in the dramatic, headline-making way — no buildings collapsing, no mass spell-failures, no Daily Prophet special editions. The decline was the kind you noticed in accumulation: a charm that should have lasted three years failing after two and a half. A Floo connection dropping mid-sentence. A Healer at St. Mungo's needing to cast twice where once would have been enough. Each failure small. Each one individually dismissible. The pattern unmistakable, if you were the kind of person who kept a separate log — date, location, spell type, ambient-field reading at the time of failure. By mid-October, her log was thirty-seven pages and she'd stopped showing it to anyone because the graph she'd plotted from the data was the kind of thing that made people either argumentative or quiet, depending on whether they understood what they were looking at.

Hermione had chased the cause through every archive available to her. The Department's restricted collection, which was considerable. The Hogwarts library on special dispensation from McGonagall, who had asked no questions and imposed no deadline, which was the kindest thing anyone had done for her that year. Two private collections — one from a retired Unspeakable in Devon, one from a Ravenclaw academic who owed her a favor and had been surprisingly generous about it. And everything the Ministry had ever published on ambient magical theory, which was less than she'd expected and more contradictory than she'd hoped.

None of it was enough. The decay was old — older than the Ministry, older than Hogwarts, rooted in infrastructure that predated modern magical governance by centuries. And the records of that infrastructure, the foundational texts that documented how Britain's ambient magical field had been constructed and anchored, were held almost exclusively in private family collections. Most of those families had cooperated with post-war scholarship. Most of those collections had been catalogued.

Most.

She'd spent three weeks in October cross-referencing the Devon collection's notes on pre-Ministry anchoring sites with the Hogwarts library's theoretical frameworks, and the only conclusion she'd reached was that someone, centuries ago, had built something into the deep structure of British magic that no surviving public text adequately described. She could see the shape of it in the margins — references to *anchoring lines*, to *foundational nodes*, to a network of ley-line connections that sustained the ambient field the way a root system sustained a tree. But the primary sources were absent. Every secondary reference pointed to knowledge held privately by the founding families, and those families had either died out, cooperated with post-war scholars and turned up nothing she didn't already have, or were named Malfoy.

She took the lift to the Atrium, which was empty except for a maintenance wizard refilling his cleaning bucket from the fountain for the third time — the charms on the water kept evaporating the supply before the mop could reach the floor. He swore at the bucket and tried again. She watched him for a moment longer than was useful, then turned and walked out through the visitor entrance into the October night.

London was cold and damp and entirely itself. The Muggle streets operated on their own logic, their own infrastructure, their own forms of decline — plumbing and pavement and budgets, not magic. The streetlights worked. The buses ran. She walked past the Tube station and kept walking, because forty minutes of cold air and concrete felt more reliable tonight than any transportation involving enchantment. She'd started walking home in January, after the Knight Bus had lurched so badly one evening that she'd been sick on the pavement outside a Tesco, standing there with her shoes splashed and her wand useless in her coat pocket and the thought arriving fully formed: *I could just use my legs.* The walking had stuck. Forty minutes through Holborn and Camden, past Muggle lives unfolding on their own terms — a chip shop with its windows steamed, a man arguing into his mobile on the corner, a woman carrying a sleeping child out of a minicab. None of it needed magic. She liked the walk the way she liked the gas hob — for its reliability, which was not a quality she had ever expected to prize above convenience.

---

Her flat was in Kentish Town. One bedroom above a shuttered dry cleaner's, the lease cheap enough that she'd taken it after the divorce without negotiating and stayed because momentum and inertia looked the same if you didn't examine them too closely.

The walls were cream. She'd hung nothing on them. The books filled the shelves and overflowed onto the table, the kitchen counter, and the armchair by the window, which she'd stopped sitting in around February when the warming charm on the cushion began bleeding out by midnight. She hadn't recast it. She'd bought a blanket instead, which was still folded on the arm of the chair, unused, because by the time she'd bought it she'd already migrated to the bed for evening reading and the chair had become a book surface. The rest of the flat followed the same logic of accretion. A stack of case files by the door that she'd been meaning to return to the office for two weeks. A Muggle umbrella in the bathroom, left there after a rainstorm in September and never moved, because the drying charm she'd cast on it had held long enough to become invisible and then failed, and now the umbrella was just sitting in the bathtub, dry and purposeless and somehow permanent. She hadn't invited anyone over since she'd moved in. The flat was a place to sleep and read and eat, not a place to be with people, and she'd stopped noticing the difference between choosing to be alone and simply being alone sometime around spring.

She dropped her coat on the hook by the door. The hook was a sticking charm she'd cast in September, and it had begun to peel from the wall — the adhesion thinning the same way everything was thinning, slowly and undramatically. She pressed it back into place with her thumb and heard the charm catch. Barely.

She heated water on the hob. The kettle charm had been unreliable for two weeks — some mornings it worked fine, some mornings the water got to tepid and stopped, and she'd given up the fight with the graceless efficiency of someone triaging her entire relationship with magic. The hob was gas. The gas was Muggle. She stood in her kitchen in her work clothes and watched the flame do what fire did without enchantment, and she did not let herself follow the thought about what it meant that she'd begun to find the Muggle way more trustworthy.

Dinner was toast with the last of the cheddar. Not because she couldn't cook — she was a competent cook when the effort seemed proportionate to the outcome — but because the expansion charm on her refrigerator had failed the previous week, contracting everything back to actual size, and a jar of chutney had cracked against the back wall. She'd cleaned the glass, restocked the basics, and filed the incident under the same word she used for everything now. Managed. Her flat was managed. Her routine was managed. Her days were managed. The divorce had been managed — amicable enough to leave no good stories, clean enough that when people asked, the only honest answer was *it just ended*, which satisfied no one, including her.

Ron had called two months ago to tell her he'd started seeing someone. A witch from his department. He'd been careful about it, decent in the particular way he was decent when he was trying not to be presumptuous, and she'd said the right things and meant them. When she'd put the phone down and looked around the flat, she hadn't felt hurt, or jealous, or anything with enough shape to hold. Just the flatness. The residual zero of a marriage that had subtracted itself so gradually that by the time they'd signed the paperwork, there was nothing left to divide.

She ate the toast standing at the counter and read a journal article on her tablet — ambient-field theory, a reexamination of a conjecture she'd already dismissed twice. The author proposed that magical fluctuation was cyclical, self-correcting, and ultimately benign. The author's data stopped in 2001. She read it to the end because she could not selectively disable thoroughness, and then she set the tablet down and washed her plate and put it on the rack and stood in the kitchen and felt the specific tiredness of a person who had exhausted every available avenue and could not locate the next one.

Her left forearm itched. She tugged at the sleeve — unconscious, habitual, so automatic she was sometimes surprised to catch herself doing it. The glamour was holding. It was always holding. She checked it every morning the way she checked the wards on the flat: touch the skin, feel the smooth surface of the charm, confirm the concealment was intact. She'd been doing this for seven years and the muscle memory was so deep it had become part of getting dressed — wake up, shower, charm the forearm, choose a shirt, leave.

The Floo coughed.

Green flame surged to knee-height in the fireplace and collapsed. She waited. The flame tried again — a wheeze, a flare, three seconds of unsteady connection before the network dropped. She heard a voice in the flare, indistinct, cut off.

She knelt and cast a diagnostic on the Floo. The network was there — she could feel the relay, the low hum of the north London node that served four thousand households — but the link refused to sustain. Harry's fireplace, Grimmauld Place. He'd been trying to reach her.

She could Apparate over. She could send an owl. She wrote a note instead — *Floo dropped again. I'm fine. Talk tomorrow?* — and sent it through the flat's mail charm, which still worked, mostly, though last month it had delivered a letter to the wrong address and she'd had to retrieve it from a Muggle postbox three streets away.

She knew what Harry would ask. He'd ask if she was sleeping. She'd say yes. He'd make that face — the mouth neutral, the eyes saying *I have known you for fifteen years and you are lying to me right now* — and she'd change the subject because she didn't want the conversation where she told him the truth, which was not that she wasn't sleeping but that the sleeping was fine, the eating was fine, everything was managed, and the problem was that managed was all she was and she didn't know what she was for.

She sat on the sofa between two stacks of journals and listened to the Floo cough once more. Green flame flickered. She heard — "Hermione?" — before the connection died again.

She didn't try to reconnect.

---

The memo arrived Thursday morning on pale lavender parchment, interdepartmental seal, the carefully imprecise language that Croaker used when he wanted something to look routine. His handwriting in the margin, small and tight: *Granger — relevant to your research. Last uncatalogued major collection. Authorize at your discretion.*

The substance was simple. The Malfoy family library, held at the family estate in Wiltshire, constituted the last uncatalogued private magical collection of significant size in Britain. The collection was known to contain pre-Ministry records of ambient-field infrastructure, foundational magical theory, and archival material not held in any public institution. A qualified researcher was requested to assess and catalogue the collection for materials relevant to ongoing departmental concerns.

Hermione read it three times. Once for information, once because the words *Malfoy family library* had landed in her chest like something dropped from height, and once to note what the memo did not contain: no mention of the decay, no reference to the stabilization indices she'd been logging for three months, no acknowledgement that the texts she was being pointed toward might be the only surviving documentation of the infrastructure that was currently failing across Britain.

Croaker was being careful. The memo could be filed as routine heritage work. But the margin note — *relevant to your research* — was a directive dressed as a suggestion, and she'd worked for Croaker long enough to know the difference.

She set the memo flat on her desk and pressed her palms against the wood. Oak, Ministry-standard, older than she was. She needed something that was not going to move under her hands.

The Malfoy library. Malfoy Manor.

She had not been back. In seven years she had not set foot on the property, had not spoken to Draco Malfoy since his trial — the spring of 1999, when she'd been nineteen and still carrying the particular fury of someone who had survived something enormous and was not yet willing to let the survival become a story that other people shaped. The Wizengamot had been prepared to send him to Azkaban. Sixteen when he took the Mark, seventeen during the battle, and the prosecution had framed the entire arc as choice rather than coercion, as if any sixteen-year-old in that house had been choosing freely, as if the word *choice* meant anything useful when the Dark Lord was living in your dining room.

She'd testified because it was right. She'd been precise and thorough and she had not talked about what else had happened in that room because the Wizengamot had the record and because saying it aloud would have required her to name the word carved into her arm, and she was not going to do that in a room full of people holding quills. She'd sat in the witness box and said, under oath, that Draco Malfoy had been a child in a war and that when it mattered — when the Snatchers had dragged them into the drawing room and demanded identification — he had looked at Harry's swollen face and lied. She'd said: he was frightened, and he saved us, and I don't think those two things cancel each other out.

The verdict was acquittal with conditions. A two-year wand restriction. He'd stood when it was read, and he'd looked at the bench and then at his mother and then at the door, and he'd walked out. She had been sitting in the gallery with her sleeves pulled down and her glamour freshly cast, waiting for something she couldn't have named, and he had not looked at her. Had not nodded, or glanced, or given her anything at all.

She hadn't needed his thanks. She hadn't done it for thanks — she'd done it because the principle mattered, because the war's moral accounting could not be reduced to sides without destroying something essential about justice. But the silence had caught in her like a hook, and in six years she had not found a way to unhook it that didn't require examining what she'd actually wanted from him, which she was not prepared to do.

She looked at the memo. *Authorize at your discretion.*

The library was the right lead. She'd known for weeks that her research gap was historical — the ambient-field infrastructure was older than the Ministry, built by founding families during the original construction of Britain's magical framework, and the records of that construction had been passed down through the families who'd done the work. The Malfoys were one of those families. Their library was the last uncatalogued collection of that era because the family had refused outside access for generations and because after the war, the politics around the Malfoy name had made any formal request radioactive. Nobody wanted to be the department head who officially dispatched a researcher to a convicted Death Eater's estate. Nobody wanted the Prophet headline.

What she knew about the collection itself was partial and mostly secondhand. Bathilda Bagshot had mentioned it in a footnote in *A History of Magic* — *the Malfoy archive, unmatched in its holdings on pre-Ministry magical infrastructure, remains closed to outside scholarship.* A colleague in the Heritage Division had referenced it over lunch once, half-envious: apparently the catalogue, such as it existed, was handwritten in three languages and organized according to a system that only the family understood. Lucius Malfoy had shown selected texts to visiting scholars during his years of political influence, but always supervised, always on his terms, and never anything touching the foundational infrastructure records. Which was, of course, exactly what she needed.

Nobody except Hermione, who had run out of every other option and who was watching the numbers in her log decline week by week while the Ministry issued memos in lavender parchment and the maintenance wizard in the Atrium fought with a mop that wouldn't wet.

She picked up her quill. She drafted the authorization in her own hand, signed it, attached it to the memo, and set it aside. Then she drafted the access request — a formal letter to the current head of the Malfoy household, outlining the scope of the work, the Department's interest in texts relating to ambient magical theory, and the proposed terms of access. The language was professional, precise. She did not mention the trial. She did not mention the room with the chandelier and the marble floor and the specific cold that she could still feel against her back if she let herself think about it for longer than a second.

She wrote the letter in her best professional hand, the one she used for external correspondence — neat, impersonal, the handwriting of a colleague rather than a person with memories. She'd learned this during the war, the discipline of putting words on paper that did exactly what they needed to do and carried nothing underneath. The letter read like a request between strangers, which was what it was. She hadn't spoken to Draco Malfoy in six years. Whatever he was now, she didn't know him. Whatever she was now — the flat, the toast, the log — he didn't know her either. The letter was accurate in its distance.

She signed it, set the quill down, and pushed her chair back from the desk.

Her hands were shaking. She watched them for a moment — both of them, resting on the arms of her chair, trembling slightly, as if the decision she'd already made hadn't reached her body yet. She put them in her lap, which was absurd — the office was empty, no one was watching — but the shaking was not about being observed. It was her hands catching up to what her handwriting had already committed to. They knew where she was going.

She sat at her desk for a long time. The corridor lights outside her office thinned and steadied and thinned again, the illumination charms struggling with whatever they were struggling with. She sat there and breathed until her hands were still.

---

Harry's note arrived Friday evening by Ministry owl, carried by a bird that looked harrowed. The Floo had been down for most of the day — the entire north London relay offline for the second time that month — and the owl network was straining to compensate. The Ministry's public statement blamed scheduled maintenance. Hermione had read it in the morning Prophet and felt the specific irritation of watching an institution she belonged to lie plainly about something she had measured.

His handwriting was tight and slightly crooked, the penmanship of someone who'd learned to write in a cupboard under the stairs and had never entirely corrected for the cramped conditions.

*Floo's out again. Are you all right? Ginny says come for dinner Sunday. Don't bring wine, we've still got the six bottles from your birthday that no one drank because you left early.*

*Are you sleeping?*

She sat on the sofa between the journal stacks and read the note and smiled, and the smile was genuine in a way that made the quiet of the flat afterwards feel thinner. Harry. The constancy of him, who had known her since she was eleven and bossy and certain about everything and who had watched her become someone less certain and less bossy and had stayed. He didn't ask *how are you* because he'd figured out years ago that the question gave her room to construct an answer. *Are you sleeping* required specifics.

She wrote back:

*I'm sleeping. I slept eight hours last night. I've taken up watercolours. I have a robust social calendar and many leisure interests. Tell Ginny yes to dinner. And I left early because your friend Robards cornered me with a thirty-minute story about ward calibration and I could feel parts of my brain shutting down in self-defence.*

She paused, quill hovering. She could tell him about the Manor. He'd find out — he was senior enough in the Auror office that access requests to notable properties crossed someone's desk, and that someone would mention it, and it would reach Harry inside a week. Better from her. Better framed as what it was: a professional decision, a research lead, the only viable path left.

She didn't write it. She'd tell him Sunday, face to face, where she could manage the conversation and deploy the particular tone she used when she needed Harry to hear *this is handled* without him asking the follow-up questions she didn't have answers for.

She folded the note and sent it and sat in the quiet.

The access request was on its way to Wiltshire. She'd dispatched it that afternoon — a Ministry owl, formal correspondence, her name and her title and her signature arriving at Malfoy Manor by nightfall. The thought of it — her handwriting inside that house, her name on a table in a room she couldn't picture, being read by a person she hadn't spoken to in six years — settled in her forearm with the dull, familiar wrongness that she'd learned to distinguish from ordinary sensation. It was not pain, not quite a phantom — just the place where the scar lived, making itself known the way it did when she thought about things she kept below the surface.

She pulled her sleeve down. The glamour was fine. The Healers had explained it years ago — cursed wounds produced phantom sensation, the nerve damage from Bellatrix's knife meant the forearm would never feel entirely normal, the itch was not the scar opening or the curse reactivating but simply the body remembering what had been done to it. She'd written down the explanation, filed it, and lived with the itch. The intellectual understanding and the physical fact occupied the same arm and had never quite introduced themselves to each other.

She made tea on the hob. Not with magic — the kettle charm had actually worked that morning, for the first time in days, but she no longer trusted it, which was a thought she didn't follow to its conclusion. She sat with the mug on the edge of her bed because the sofa had journals on it and the chair was cold and the bedroom was where she spent her time now, which was another thing she did not examine.

She'd been sleeping badly since the summer, before the decay had become undeniable — since the first diagnostic had come back wrong and she'd sat in her office past ten at night and felt the first real edge of the thing she'd been pushing down for months. Not anxiety, exactly. Something closer to professional vertigo. She was supposed to be able to solve things. That was what she did — she solved them by being more thorough and more prepared and more rigorous than the problem required, and the surplus effort was the margin that let her sleep. This problem was bigger than surplus effort. The margin had eroded, and the sleep had gone with it, and what was left was the toast and the managed flat and the walking home through Camden because at least the walk was something she could finish.

She thought about Malfoy Manor. She thought about it the way she thought about everything: in layers, each one rational, each one defensible, stacked neatly on top of the thing she did not want to look at.

The library was the best remaining lead. The collection was likely to contain the only surviving documentation of the ambient-field infrastructure that was currently failing. She was the most qualified person for this work, and declining would be professionally indefensible when the decay was measurable and accelerating and the Ministry was pretending it wasn't happening. All of this was true.

Underneath the true things was the other true thing, the one she kept placing at the bottom as if layering rational arguments on top of it would make it weigh less: the Manor was the place where Bellatrix Lestrange had held her down on a marble floor and carved a word into her arm with a cursed knife while she screamed until something in her throat tore, and Draco Malfoy had stood against the far wall with his hands at his sides, and the chandelier had thrown cold light across the ceiling, and at some point during it — she didn't know when, the memory was fractured and merciless in unpredictable places — she had turned her head and seen his face and it had been white and fixed and he had not moved.

Not what he'd done — what he hadn't.

She drank the tea. It was too hot and she drank it anyway, and the scald was at least a definite sensation, a thing she could locate.

She couldn't refuse the assignment. She'd known this since Thursday morning, maybe since before Thursday, since the morning she'd watched the corridor lights dim in the Department and thought: *I've reached the end of what the existing archives can give me. The answer is older than what we have.* She had never actually decided not to go back to the Manor — she'd never said it, never declared it, never made a promise. She'd simply not gone, the way you simply don't return to the place where you were hurt worst, and the not-going had hardened over seven years into something that felt like a position even though it had only ever been avoidance.

Now avoidance and professional necessity had collided, and professional necessity was going to win, because it always won with her, because competence was the architecture she'd built her life inside and if she removed that she was a woman in a one-bedroom flat eating toast and checking her glamour and watching the magic fail.

She set the mug on the nightstand. She turned off the lamp — Muggle, plug-in, purchased in January for reasons she'd told herself were aesthetic. The room went dark. The silence was London silence: pipes settling, a distant siren, someone's television through the wall, the low urban hum of a city that ran on electricity and plumbing and did not depend on an ambient magical field that was quietly running out.

Nothing shifted. Nothing watched. The walls stayed exactly where they were.

She lay in the dark and thought about the letter arriving at the Manor. She pictured the owl crossing the wards, landing on a sill, waiting. She tried to picture Draco Malfoy reading her name on Ministry parchment and couldn't get past the image of him at seventeen — the courtroom, the grey suit, the way he'd looked at the door and then through it and then was gone.

He'd be twenty-five now. Her age. She didn't know what he looked like at twenty-five. She didn't know if the sharp angles of his boyhood had settled or sharpened, if he looked tired the way everyone from the war looked tired — that particular exhaustion of people who'd survived something and hadn't figured out what surviving it was going to cost them for the rest of their lives.

She turned onto her side. The pillow was flat. She punched it into a better shape and it didn't help. She closed her eyes and her mind offered the stabilization index, the decay curve, the shape of a system slowly running out of breath.

Then it offered the drawing room. The chandelier. The cold marble against her back. The sound she'd made that she still couldn't think about without her jaw clenching shut.

She opened her eyes.

She thought about the maintenance wizard in the Atrium, swearing at his bucket with the weary, private fury of a man whose tools had stopped working. She thought about the reports she'd read last week — accidental magic spiking in Muggle-born children across the country, the ambient field too unstable to regulate their magical development the way it was supposed to, children setting fires and shattering windows and frightening their parents, who had no framework to understand what was happening. She'd filed the reports with everything else she couldn't fix from her office. She thought about the fact that she was the person most likely to understand what was happening to British magic, and that she was lying awake in a cold flat in Kentish Town because she was afraid of a house.

The fear was not irrational. She could evaluate her own responses — she was a researcher, that was the one thing she could still do. The fear was proportionate to the stimulus. She was going back to the place where she had been tortured, to work in proximity to the person who had watched it happen, and the professional necessity of the trip did not cancel what her body knew about that house. The body kept its own records. Her forearm kept its own records. The glamour was a correction applied to the surface, and underneath it the letters were still raised against the skin, and those letters had been carved in that house, and the house was where she was going.

She stared at the dark ceiling of her managed flat and did not sleep until sometime past three, when she dropped into something that was closer to collapse than rest, and when the alarm went off at six the bedroom was cold — the warming charm had bled out in the night, silently, while she slept — and she got up and showered and stood in front of the bathroom mirror and cast the glamour on her left forearm with the same precision she brought to everything, and the charm settled over the scar tissue and smoothed the skin and hid what was written there. She rolled her sleeve down and buttoned the cuff and got dressed and left for work.


# Chapter 2

The Mark woke him at four again.

Not the old way — not the burn that had pulled like a fishhook behind his navel, the Dark Lord's summons dragging every branded body toward whatever room he'd chosen for that night's audience. That signal had died with the man who cast it. What remained was different: a slow, arrhythmic ache that started in the bones of his forearm and radiated nowhere. It was pain without direction — magic trying to move through a channel that no longer existed, pressing against dead ends in his nervous system until it gave up and pooled, hot and restless, beneath the ink.

Draco lay in the dark and waited for it to pass. His bedroom was too large for one person — his parents' old suite, which he'd moved into three years ago not because he wanted it but because the alternative was the room he'd slept in at sixteen, and that room still smelled faintly of the boy who'd knelt on its floor and dry-heaved after taking the Mark. He'd tried airing it out. He'd tried redecorating. Eventually he'd simply shut the door and left it shut and taken the master suite with its absurd four-poster bed and its windows that looked out onto the east lawns where no one walked.

The ache subsided around five. He got up because there was no reason not to.

The kitchen was at the far end of the ground floor — the servants' kitchen, not the formal one. He'd stopped using the formal kitchen two years ago because it was built for a household staff that no longer existed, and cooking a single egg in it felt like performing a monologue in an empty theatre. The servants' kitchen was smaller, stone-floored, with a window above the basin that let in grey light from the courtyard. He made tea. He made toast and didn't bother with a plate. He ate standing at the counter because sitting at the table meant choosing one of four chairs and the other three being empty, and that was somehow worse than no chairs at all.

His route from the kitchen to the library took him through the east corridor, down the back stair, through the gallery with the portraits — several of whom still tried to speak to him; he'd turned most of them to face the wall — and along a narrow passage that connected to the library's south entrance. It was not the most direct route. The most direct route went through the ground-floor reception rooms, and he didn't take it. He hadn't taken it in years. He didn't think about why, the same way you didn't think about which eye you led with or which hand opened a door — the avoidance was structural, built into his body's map of the house so completely it no longer registered as a choice.

There were other rooms he didn't enter. The east drawing room where his father had held meetings. The conservatory where Aunt Bellatrix had sometimes sat in the evenings, humming to herself. The second-floor study where — but Draco did not catalogue these absences, because cataloguing them would mean acknowledging that his home was as much a map of avoidance as of habitation, and he couldn't afford that thought before his second cup of tea.

The library was the one room that justified the house. Two stories of shelved walls, a mezzanine gallery with rolling ladders, tall windows on the west side that flooded the reading tables with afternoon light. The Malfoy collection was extraordinary — centuries of acquisition, some legitimate, some not, texts in Latin and French and English and languages he couldn't identify, magical treatises and mundane histories and things that were neither, exactly, but sat in the uncomfortable space between. Most of it was uncatalogued. His grandfather had maintained a system; his father had not. Draco had made several attempts, abandoned each one when the scale of it overwhelmed him. The books were patient. They had outlasted better Malfoys than him, and they would outlast him too.

He settled into the chair by the south window — his chair, shaped to him by years of the same posture — and opened the text he'd been working through for the past week. A seventeenth-century treatise on sympathetic magic, dry and dense and entirely unhelpful, which was standard. He'd been looking for anything related to the Mark's new behaviour for eight months. The Healers at St. Mungo's had refused to examine it — Dark Lord magic, liability concerns, the very specific discomfort that crossed their faces when he showed them the brand and they understood what it was and what it meant about the arm it was on. Two private practitioners had attempted diagnostic spells. The first had recoiled physically. The second had completed the examination and told him, with genuine regret, that she couldn't help: the magic was layered in ways that exceeded her training, the curse structure unlike anything in the standard literature. She'd suggested he try the Unspeakables. He'd laughed, not unkindly. Unspeakables didn't take walk-ins from former Death Eaters.

So he read. He searched his own family's library for something — a reference, a precedent, a theory — that would explain why a dead man's brand was waking up seven years after the man had died. He found nothing. The Dark Lord's innovations had been his own; the Death Eaters had not been given schematics.

The Mark pulsed, and he pressed his thumb hard against it through his sleeve until the pressure matched the ache and the two cancelled each other out, mostly. It was an old trick. It didn't work as well anymore.

The house was quieter in the cold months. He'd grown up in this building and he knew its seasons — the way it expanded in summer, rooms breathing, curtains lifting in air that came from nowhere; the way it contracted in winter, pulling inward, corridors narrower, certain wings going dark as if the Manor itself were conserving heat. In November the contraction was well underway. The east wing had gone cold weeks ago, the upper corridors drafty, the rooms above the library settling into a silence that felt more chosen than accidental. He didn't anthropomorphize the house. He didn't call it moody or sulking or alive, because those words would have required him to reconsider the basic terms of his cohabitation with the place, and he was not prepared for that renegotiation. But there were mornings — this morning, for instance, the Mark pulsing and the November dark pressing against the windows — when the Manor felt less like a house and more like something he was living inside of, the distinction between container and companion less stable than he'd have liked.

His mother hadn't visited in two weeks. This was deliberate, and he knew it, because Narcissa Malfoy did not do anything accidentally. She'd moved out of the Manor three years ago — a townhouse in Bath, tasteful and proportionate, rooms that stayed where you left them. She'd been diplomatic about it. *The Manor is yours, darling. It needs someone in residence.* She'd never said *I can't live here anymore*, but the way she'd touched the front doorframe on her way out — two fingers pressed flat against the stone, held for a beat too long — had said it plainly enough. The house had done something to her over twenty-five years of marriage, something she didn't discuss, and Draco hadn't asked. Asking would mean acknowledging that the house did things to people, and he still preferred to think of the Manor as architecture. Stone and timber and old magic. Not something with preferences. Not something that listened.

He read until noon. The treatise yielded nothing. He ate lunch alone — soup, bread, an apple he forgot about and found later on the library side table, browning against the wood. He walked the east lawn in the thin November light because his body needed to move and the grounds were the only space large enough to accommodate motion without routing him past rooms he'd rather not see. The Mark ached steadily, a low-grade persistence that he'd learned to fold into the background noise of his body the way you folded a ringing in your ears — always there, beneath everything, no longer remarkable except in its occasional absence.

He was twenty-five years old and he lived alone in a house built for a family and a staff of twelve, and his days had the precise, unvarying quality of someone who had methodically eliminated every variable that might produce surprise, because surprise, in his experience, preceded damage.

This was the shape of his life. He didn't feel sorry for himself. That was a habit he'd broken in the first year after the trial, when self-pity had threatened to become his organizing principle and he'd recognized, with the cold clarity that was either his best quality or his worst, that if he went down that road he wasn't coming back. So he'd built routine instead. Tea, library, grounds, tea, library, dinner, the upper corridor at dusk where the windows caught the last light, then his room, where he slept badly and woke to the Mark and started again. It worked. It was enough. It had to be enough, because the alternative was admitting that it wasn't, and then what.

The owl arrived at half three.

Ministry seal. Departmental stationery — Department of Mysteries, which meant someone with clearance and a specific kind of authority. He opened it at the library desk, because that was where he opened mail, because routine was the architecture of a manageable life.

The letter was from Granger.

He read it twice. The first time for content: she was requesting access to the Malfoy library for research purposes related to the ongoing investigation into systemic magical decay; the Ministry would provide a standard stipend for room and board during the research period; she anticipated a minimum stay of several weeks; she would require unrestricted access to all library holdings including restricted sections. It was formal, precisely worded, on departmental letterhead.

He read it a second time for what it didn't say. That she hadn't set foot in this house since the night she'd been dragged across the floor of a room he no longer entered. That the last time they'd spoken was his trial, seven years ago, where she'd testified on his behalf — measured, factual, unsparing of both of them — and he'd sat in the accused's chair and not said a single word to her, not before and not after, because there was no word in any language that could do the work required of it, and an inadequate word would have been worse than silence. That they hadn't spoken since. That she knew, presumably, what she was asking — to be inside this house, under this roof, working in a room that existed because his family had spent centuries accumulating things, some beautiful, some stolen, some dark, and the distinctions between those categories were not always clear.

And underneath all of it — carefully not written, but legible in the bureaucratic framing, the departmental authority, the professional distance of the request — the fact that Hermione Granger would not have come to him unless every other option was exhausted. He was the last door she'd tried, because this door required entering the building where she'd been tortured.

He set the letter on the desk. The Mark pulsed, sharper than its usual rhythm, and he pressed his thumb against it and thought about the shape of what she was asking.

She needed the library. And he needed — the thought formed before he could redirect it, specific and humiliating in its directness — he needed someone who could look at the thing on his arm and not flinch.

He wrote back that evening. He agreed to her terms. He proposed additional ones.

---

The morning she was due, Draco cleaned.

Not the whole Manor — that project belonged to someone with staff or delusions — but the library, the guest wing, the kitchen, and the corridors between them. He spelled dust from shelves and swept stone floors and replaced the candles in the library's wall sconces because half of them had burned down to nothing months ago and he hadn't bothered, because who was going to notice. He observed, as he worked, that this was the most sustained effort he'd put into the house's appearance in over a year. He observed himself observing this. He didn't like either observation.

The guest wing was in the east arm of the Manor, up a flight and across a landing. He hadn't entered these rooms in — he tried to remember and couldn't place a specific date, which was itself an answer. The beds were held by preservation charms, unchanged since whoever had last stayed. The air had the stale quality of space that had been sealed too long, the particular staleness of rooms where time had not so much stopped as lost interest. He opened a window and the November cold came in like a correction.

He was in the entrance hall by eleven because that was where you received guests, and she was a guest, and he was going to do this correctly if he did nothing else correctly for the rest of the arrangement. The entrance hall was the one formal space he'd maintained throughout his years alone: high ceiling, the floor black-and-white marble in the Malfoy diamond pattern, a staircase wide enough for four people abreast. His family had always understood entrances. It was the rest of the interaction they'd consistently botched.

He heard the Apparition crack from the drive. She hadn't used the Floo, which meant either the network was acting up again or she'd chosen the method that left her outside the wards, where she'd have to walk the drive and ring the bell rather than appearing directly in his fireplace. He thought that was probably deliberate. She was giving both of them the distance of a door between them.

He opened it before she could knock.

She looked the same and entirely different, which was the kind of contradiction that only applied to people you hadn't seen in years. Hermione Granger stood on his front step in a dark coat, a leather bag over one shoulder, her hair pulled back and already escaping in the way it always had. She was smaller than he remembered, or he'd made her larger in the years of not seeing her, built her up into the version that existed in courtrooms and Ministry corridors and the stories the Prophet ran about the war's brightest veteran. The actual woman on his doorstep was just a woman — specific, present, and wearing an expression he recognized: she'd prepared for this, organized her approach, decided how the first thirty seconds would go. He'd seen it at the trial. He'd seen it across a courtroom when she was twenty years old and calmly describing to a panel of wizards the specific ways in which his family's actions had been monstrous and the ways in which his, specifically, had been coerced, and she'd been right about all of it, and he had sat there and let her be right because his own words would have been insufficient.

"Malfoy," she said.

"Granger."

She didn't offer her hand. Neither did he. The absence of the gesture was its own negotiation — two people acknowledging that they weren't going to pretend this was a normal professional meeting between people with a normal history.

"Come in."

She stepped past him and he caught the precise moment her body registered the Manor — a slight change in her breathing, a tension across her shoulders that appeared and was controlled in the same motion. She was in. She was inside the house where it had happened, and her composure was a wall she'd built before she arrived and was maintaining through the kind of effort that didn't show unless you knew what controlled effort looked like. He knew. He'd been building similar walls for seven years.

The entrance hall held them in its formal hush. Granger looked at the marble floor, the staircase, the arched ceiling with the specific gaze of someone who was cataloguing — comparing what she saw now against what she remembered, and finding the differences insufficient.

"The library is through the east corridor," he said. "I'll show you after we've discussed terms."

"Your letter mentioned additional conditions."

"It did."

He led her to the morning room — small, south-facing, used for household business by three generations of Malfoys and therefore as close to neutral as any room in this house could claim. He'd set tea there, which was either hospitality or habit. She sat in the chair across from the desk without waiting to be offered it, and he sat behind the desk because the desk was useful, because it put a surface between them, because he'd been raised in rooms where furniture did political work and the habit hadn't left him.

"You'll have full access to the library," he said. "All holdings, including restricted sections. I've nothing to hide in there that you couldn't get a Ministry warrant for."

"I appreciate that."

"The guest wing is functional. It's in the east arm — I'll walk you through. You'll have your own rooms, private entrance. The kitchen's shared — I eat there, you're welcome to use it. The rest of the house —" He paused. The sentence wanted to go somewhere specific and he redirected it. "The rest of the house is yours to move through as needed. I'd ask you not to enter private rooms without notice, but I don't expect that'll be a problem."

"It won't."

This part was easy. This was logistics, and logistics were a language they could both speak without anything underneath it getting loose.

"My additional condition," he said.

She waited. Her hands were in her lap, still, and he noticed that her left sleeve was pulled down to her wrist — long, covering, the kind of habitual coverage that could pass for style if you didn't know what it was covering. He noticed it the way he noticed everything about her position in this room: with the involuntary hyperawareness of someone who had spent the worst night of his life watching her on the floor of a room not far from this one, and who'd been reading her body language against that memory since the moment she'd walked through his front door, checking for — what. He didn't want to finish the thought. He checked anyway.

"The Mark," he said.

He pushed up his left sleeve. He did it without preamble because there was no version of this reveal that benefited from buildup, and because if she was going to flinch he wanted to know now, before they were deeper into the arrangement. The Mark was there on his forearm, the skull and serpent, darker than it had been in years — the ink, or whatever the brand was made of, had deepened as the pain increased, as though the magic in it were condensing, pulling tighter into itself.

Granger looked at it. She did not flinch. Her eyes moved across the Mark with the precise, lateral scanning that he recognized from the trial — the way she processed information, absorbing the full shape of it before she let herself react. He could see her noting the colour, the slightly raised quality of the skin around the edges, the faint shimmer where the residual magic was active enough to be visible in the morning-room light.

"It's been waking up," he said. "Not the summoning — that's gone. This is different. Pain without a signal. The magic in it's restless. It's trying to do something, but whatever it was connected to is dead, and it's just — " He stopped himself before the sentence turned into something that sounded like asking for sympathy. "I've had two private Healers look at it. Neither could help. St. Mungo's won't touch it."

"When did it start?"

"Eight months ago, give or take. Occasional at first — nighttime, mostly. Now it's more or less constant."

She leaned forward slightly. Not toward the Mark but toward the problem. He could see the shift in her — from the guarded woman who'd stood on his doorstep to something else, something closer to the version of her he remembered from school, the one who'd vibrated at a frequency he could almost hear in the presence of a question she didn't yet have the answer to.

"Can I?" she said.

He understood. He extended his arm across the desk.

Her fingers were warm, which he hadn't expected — or hadn't allowed himself to think about in advance, because thinking about the temperature of Hermione Granger's hands while she touched the brand that defined what he was would have required a level of emotional preparation he hadn't managed. She held his wrist — clinical, professional, her thumb bracing against his pulse point in a way that was entirely diagnostic, and if his pulse responded to the contact she gave no sign of noticing.

She cast a preliminary diagnostic spell. He felt it: a cool wash of her magic against the Mark, careful and layered, nothing like the blunt probes the Healers had used. It moved across the surface of the curse like a hand testing water temperature, and the Mark — the Mark did something it had never done before under examination. It quieted. Not fully, not like the pain was gone, but the restless thrashing that had been his constant companion for eight months eased, pulled back, settled into something almost bearable. And then it shifted, oriented itself toward her spell the way a compass needle finds north. The ache gathered at the point where her thumb pressed against his wrist, concentrated there, as though the dark magic in the brand were reaching for her diagnostic contact.

He nearly pulled his arm back. He didn't. Her fingers tightened fractionally on his wrist, and he could feel her pulse in her fingertips against his skin, or he was imagining it, and either possibility was worse than the other.

"That's not standard curse behaviour," she said.

"No."

"The magic is layered. There's the original brand — the Dark Lord's casting — and then something underneath it. Older, or different, at least. I'd need sustained diagnostic contact to map the structure properly." She released his wrist and he pulled his arm back and rolled down his sleeve. "What are you proposing?"

"You get the library. Full access, for as long as your research requires. I get your expertise on the Mark. Sustained examination, diagnostic work, whatever you need to do to understand what it's doing and whether it can be stopped or managed."

"You want me to treat a curse that two Healers couldn't."

"I want you to study it. You're —" He caught the word *better* before it landed, because it would have sounded like flattery and this wasn't a room where flattery would serve either of them. "You're the most qualified person to examine magic this complex who'd also be willing to examine it on me."

The second clause sat between them. *Who'd also be willing.* Who would look at a Death Eater's mark and not refuse on principle, on disgust, on the reasonable grounds that the man who bore it had earned it and might deserve whatever it was doing to him now. He hadn't meant to say it so plainly. He saw her register the honesty of it — something shifting in her expression, a recalculation he could watch happen, the moment when she heard what he'd actually said underneath the words he'd chosen.

"The terms are professional," she said.

"Entirely."

"Last names. Clear boundaries around the examination work. I'll keep records and share them with you. If I find something treatable, I'll recommend a course of action. If I find something dangerous — to you or to anyone else — I'll tell you directly."

"Agreed."

"And the research comes first. The library is my reason for being here. The Mark work is a secondary engagement."

"Understood."

She looked at him for a long moment. He let her. This was the part where she decided whether he was tolerable — not whether he was trustworthy, because that question had been litigated at the trial and they both knew the verdict, but whether she could exist in proximity to him for weeks without the proximity becoming something neither of them could manage. He didn't know what she saw. He hoped it was close enough to the truth: a man who was tired and in pain and asking for help in the only way he could manage, which was by offering something she needed in return, so that neither of them had to call it what it was.

"All right," she said.

He nodded. She nodded. The quiet that followed was not comfortable, but it was functional — the silence of two people who had agreed on the shape of something and were now sitting with the weight of what the shape contained. He poured tea because the pot was there and because the act of pouring gave his hands something to do that wasn't pressing against the Mark or gripping the edge of the desk. She took the cup and held it without drinking, and the steam rose between them in the morning-room light, and neither of them said the thing they were both thinking, which was that seven years of silence had produced this: a desk and a teapot and an arrangement that was professional and civil and not even close to sufficient.

"I'll show you the library first," he said. "Then the guest wing."

---

The library earned the silence he saw cross her face when they walked in.

He took his usual route — the east corridor, the back passage, avoiding the ground-floor reception rooms — and if she noticed the circuitous path she didn't comment on it. Then through the south entrance, and her reaction was visible in spite of herself: a caught breath, a half-step that slowed, her eyes pulling upward to the mezzanine gallery and the shelved walls that reached the ceiling, two stories of accumulated Malfoy intellect and acquisition and, in some cases, theft.

"Your catalogue," she said, after a moment.

"Nonexistent, mostly. My grandfather kept a ledger. It covers roughly a third of the holdings and hasn't been updated since 1971."

"The restricted section?"

"North wall, behind the screen. Warded, but I'll key you in. Most of it's Dark Arts adjacent rather than strictly Dark — theory, history, magical taxonomy. Some of it's genuinely dangerous. You'll know which."

She was already moving through the space, her hand trailing along a shelf spine, tilting her head to read titles. He watched the transformation happen: the guarded woman from the morning room was still present, still holding, but layered over now by something else — the researcher, the person who'd probably been the real one underneath the composure all along. She pulled a text from a shelf, opened it, frowned at the language, replaced it, moved to the next section. He could see her building a system already — not physically, not yet, but in her head, the preliminary architecture of how she was going to approach several centuries of accumulated disorder.

He let her look. He leaned against the doorframe with his arms crossed and watched Hermione Granger fall in love with his library, which was the only kind of falling any version of this arrangement was supposed to involve.

She turned back to him after a few minutes. "This will take longer than several weeks."

"I'm not going anywhere."

"No," she said, and something in the word acknowledged more than the library. "I don't suppose you are."

He showed her the rest of the ground floor that he was willing to show. The morning room she'd already seen. The kitchen — the servants' kitchen, the real one — where the stone floor was clean but uneven and the window above the basin let in the flat light of a November afternoon. She looked at the kitchen the way she'd looked at everything: cataloguing, measuring, filing. He watched her note the single mug on the drying rack, the single plate, the economies of a solitary life visible in the domestic evidence, and he watched her choose not to remark on it.

The back corridor to the gardens. The passage that connected the kitchen wing to the library. He did not show her the west wing. He did not comment on the doors they passed without opening, or the corridors that branched off the main route and led to parts of the house he hadn't entered in years. She didn't ask.

Then the guest wing. Up the main stair, across the landing, into the east arm. The corridor here was panelled in dark wood, lit by sconces that had been enchanted centuries ago to respond to presence — they brightened as he and Granger passed, which was the standard old charm, keyed to body heat and proximity. The guest rooms were at the corridor's end: a bedroom, a sitting room, a private bath. The windows looked out onto the east lawns and, past them, the tree line at the estate's edge. The November light through the glass was thin and grey, the kind of light that revealed rather than flattered — it made the room feel honest in a way the warmer rooms downstairs, with their charmed sconces and heavy curtains, did not.

"It's adequate," she said, which he understood was her version of generous.

"There are preservation charms on the bedding. The bath plumbing is temperamental — the hot tap sometimes has ideas of its own. I'd call it charming, but I think the accurate word is malfunctioning."

Something crossed her face — not a smile, not quite, but the place where a smile would have gone if the circumstances had been different or if either of them had been someone else. He filed it away. He would take what was available.

"If you need anything adjusted, let me know. I'll be —" He gestured vaguely at the house beyond the doorway, which was absurd, because the gesture encompassed thirty-six rooms and he occupied maybe four of them, and the gap between the sweep of his hand and the reality of his life was visible enough that he saw her see it. "Around."

"Thank you, Malfoy."

"Granger."

He left her to settle in. The walk back to the library was the usual route: east corridor to the landing, down the main stair, through the back passage toward the portrait gallery.

Except that at the base of the stairs, where the passage should have turned left toward the gallery, there was a corridor he didn't recognize.

It wasn't new. The stonework was old, matching the rest of the ground floor — the same grey limestone the whole house was built from. But it was a corridor he hadn't walked in years, a shortcut that connected the base of the east staircase directly to the library's north entrance. He'd used it as a child, he was fairly sure — there was a memory, vague and uncertain, of running through it, his mother's voice behind him telling him to slow down — but it had been closed for as long as he could easily remember. Doors in the Manor didn't always stay where they were. Corridors silted up, filled themselves with whatever the house used to fill spaces it was done with. He'd stopped questioning that years ago. The house had its habits, and he had his, and they coexisted.

But this corridor was open now. The sconces along its walls were lit, warm and steady, the air inside moving faintly as though the passage had drawn a breath. It had not been open this morning. He'd walked past this exact spot on his way to the kitchen and there had been wall here — solid stone, unremarkable, the same wall that had been here every day of his adult life.

He stood at the corridor's mouth and looked down its length to the library door at the far end, which he could see from here, standing slightly ajar, the warm light of the library spilling through the gap. The corridor was perhaps forty feet long. Clean stone. No dust, which was wrong — a passage that had been sealed for years should have been thick with it. The air should have been stale. Instead the corridor smelled faintly of the library itself, of old paper and preservation charms, as if the house had prepared the space and cleaned it before offering it up.

The house had opened this corridor. The house had opened it now — not this morning, not last week, not in the months since his mother had stopped visiting regularly, not in the years since his father had been taken to Azkaban. Now. This afternoon. Within an hour of Hermione Granger walking through the front door.

He felt something then — not the Mark, for once, but something else entirely. A shift in the house's attention, the way you might feel a large animal turn its head in the dark. The walls around him were the same walls he'd known his whole life, and they were not the same, because whatever animated them had stirred — woken up or turned over or simply noticed, for the first time in a long time, that something in the house had changed.

Draco stepped into the corridor. The sconces brightened around him. His footsteps echoed on the stone — a different echo than his usual routes produced, fuller, the sound of a passage that had been quiet too long now carrying noise again.

He walked through to the library. The corridor delivered him to the north entrance, which put him three steps from the reading tables, four from his chair. A shortcut. A gift, if you were inclined to call it that. He was not yet inclined to call it anything.

He sat down and opened his book and did not mention the corridor to anyone.

The house had noticed her. He would sit with that knowledge quietly, turning it over, not yet willing to name what it might mean. The Manor had noticed Hermione Granger and it had changed for her, opened something for her, in a way it hadn't changed for him in all the years he'd lived here alone. He added it to the list of things he registered and did not speak about. The list was long. It was the only filing system in the Manor that was reliably maintained.

Upstairs, he could hear her footsteps moving through the guest wing. The Manor's old stones carried sound unpredictably — some rooms were silent as crypts and others transmitted every shift and creak — and her footsteps reached him through whatever combination of acoustic chance and architectural intention delivered them. She was unpacking, or pacing, or standing in a room that was not hers in a house that was not hers, getting her bearings inside the place where the worst thing that had ever happened to her had happened.

He turned a page he hadn't read. He stared at words he didn't absorb. He thought about the corridor and the sconces and the seven years of silence between them that had ended today with last names and a desk between them and terms that were professional and clear and nowhere near sufficient for what they were actually doing, which was agreeing to live inside the same walls — the two of them, in the house that remembered everything they'd both spent seven years trying to forget.


# Chapter 3

She'd prepared for this the way she prepared for everything — overprepared, really, three parchments of diagnostic protocols she'd adapted from published literature on curse-mark analysis, two standard Dark object assessment frameworks, and a sequence of probing charms she'd modified herself to account for the specific layering described in Baruffio's monograph on voluntary curse-binding. The protocols assumed a compliant subject. They assumed adequate workspace and controlled light. They assumed the examiner would not need to hold the subject's arm for sustained periods.

They did not assume Draco Malfoy, sitting across from her at a table in the study adjoining the library, his left sleeve rolled to the elbow, his forearm laid flat on the oak surface between them with the careful precision of someone placing evidence into the record.

The room had good morning light. That was what she'd told herself when she chose it — the east-facing windows, the sturdy table, the neutral territory between his private rooms and the library she'd claimed as her workspace. Good light for detailed diagnostic work. Practical. She'd set out her notes, her wand, a quill for annotations. Everything arranged.

"I'll need to touch your arm," she said. "For sustained contact during the diagnostic sequence. Some of the probing charms require a physical anchor point."

"I assumed."

"I'm telling you so you're not startled."

"I won't be startled." He said it evenly, looking at the far wall.

She pulled her chair closer. The legs scraped against stone and the sound was too loud in the quiet room. She could see the Mark from here — it was not what she'd expected from wartime photographs or Ministry documentation. The Dark Mark in those images had been vivid, almost aggressive: the skull, the serpent, the ink-black lines that moved like something alive. What she saw on his forearm was faded. Not gone — nothing about a Dark Lord's curse-brand would simply fade — but diminished, the black softened to a dark grey, the lines less sharp, the serpent's movement sluggish rather than sinuous. It looked like a bruise that had been healing for years and then stopped healing.

She took his wrist.

His skin was warm. That was the first thing she registered, and she registered it with the clinical detachment she'd been practicing since the night she agreed to this — warm, dry, the tendons of his wrist taut under her fingers. The last time she'd touched Draco Malfoy — she tried to remember. She couldn't. The trial, perhaps, a handshake that didn't happen, a distance maintained across a courtroom. Before that, nothing she could call contact. They'd existed in the same rooms, the same corridors, the same war, without touching. Seven years of shared history and she was counting the instances of physical contact on no fingers.

She turned his arm in the light, positioning it for the first diagnostic pass. He was rigid. Not just his arm — his whole body had locked, shoulders drawn back, jaw set, breathing controlled in the way that meant it was being controlled. She could feel the effort of his stillness through the point of contact, the muscles in his forearm refusing to relax under her hands. His wrist was narrow in her grip. She could feel the radius and ulna beneath the skin, the architecture of him, and the Mark sat on top of it all like a stain on a map — the skull and serpent rendered on the specific topography of his forearm, following the line of muscle, curving slightly where the arm narrowed toward the wrist.

"First pass is a general curse-structure analysis," she said, because talking through the procedure was what the protocols called for and because the silence in the room was becoming its own kind of pressure. "It shouldn't be painful. You might feel warmth or a mild buzzing."

He didn't respond. She hadn't expected him to.

She cast the first diagnostic charm. The familiar amber light settled over his forearm, and she watched through the diagnostic overlay as the curse's outer layer resolved into legible structure — standard Dark magic signatures, consistent with what she'd read. Voldemort's work had a recognizable magical fingerprint: cold, layered, designed to bind loyalty through pain. The surface reading was unremarkable. She could feel the curse-magic through the contact point, a low wrongness against her fingertips, like holding something that vibrated at a frequency just below perception.

She cast the second charm, probing deeper. The Mark's middle layers were more complex — not just a brand but a communication architecture, the remnants of a network that had once connected every Death Eater to its maker. The network was dead. Voldemort was dead. But the infrastructure remained in the curse-mark itself, channels that had carried signal and now carried nothing, like veins in a body after the heart has stopped. This was consistent with published research. She annotated her notes without looking up.

The third charm was one she'd modified herself, designed to interact with the curse's deepest layer — the binding that connected the Mark to its bearer's magical core. She cast it, and the amber light shifted, probing down into the structure where the curse met Draco's own magic.

The Mark moved.

Not the sluggish serpentine drift she'd been observing on the surface. The dark magic in the Mark's deep layer shifted toward her probing charm like a thing orienting. The lines on his forearm darkened by a shade. The serpent, which had been barely twitching, raised its head.

"Granger."

"I see it." Her voice came out steady, which was a triumph of training over instinct. "Hold still."

She adjusted her grip on his arm, fingers tightening fractionally around his wrist, and pushed the diagnostic charm deeper. The Mark's response intensified. The dark magic wasn't resisting her probe — Dark curse-marks resisted, that was their most basic defensive characteristic, they deflected or lashed out or went inert under examination. This one was reaching. The magic in the Mark flowed toward her charm the way iron filings orient to a magnet, and under her fingers his arm — which had been rigid since she first touched him — went slack.

His breathing changed. She could hear it, the controlled rhythm faltering into something less managed. The Mark's lines were sharper now, the faded grey deepening toward black under her sustained contact, and the restless ache he'd described during their negotiation — the pain without signal, the magic trying to move through channels that connected to nothing — had stopped. She couldn't see pain stopping, but she could feel the tension drain from his forearm, the muscles releasing under her hands in a way that was involuntary and obvious.

The Mark was quiet. Quiet in a way it apparently hadn't been in months, and the quieting had begun when she touched him and intensified when her diagnostic magic reached its deeper layers. She held the charm steady and watched the dark magic settle into something that resembled, if she was going to use a word she wasn't ready to use, rest.

She catalogued this. She set it aside. She ran three more diagnostic passes, each one confirming what the first had shown: the Mark's curse structure was more layered than any published analysis suggested, the deep binding between the curse and Draco's magical core was active in ways that should have been impossible seven years after the caster's death, and the Mark responded to her magic specifically — not just to diagnostic probing in general but to her probing, her magical signature, with a differential response she could measure and could not yet explain.

She released his arm. Drew back. Picked up her quill.

Her hand was steady. She had decided it would be steady and it was. She wrote three lines of annotation and then stopped because the quill nib had caught on the parchment and she needed to look at something that wasn't his forearm, wasn't the Mark, wasn't the place where her thumb had been resting against the inside of his wrist for the past fifteen minutes.

"The curse structure is more layered than the literature accounts for," she said. "I'll need multiple sessions. The response pattern is — " She paused. Chose a word. "Unusual."

"Unusual how?"

"The deep layer reacted to the diagnostic probe in a way I haven't seen documented. It's not hostile. It's more — responsive. I need to review my readings before I can say more than that."

He rolled his sleeve down. The fabric covered the Mark and something in the room loosened, some held breath that wasn't hers or his but belonged to the space between them. He stood. She noticed that he moved his arm carefully, the way someone moves a limb that has stopped hurting and doesn't trust the absence of pain.

"Same time tomorrow?" she asked, because the protocols called for daily diagnostic sessions during the initial assessment phase, and because filling the silence with logistics was the closest thing she had to a plan.

"If you need it."

"I need it."

He left the study. His footsteps echoed in the corridor outside and then didn't — the stone swallowing the sound at a distance she couldn't judge, or Malfoy walking in the particular way he had that she was noticing without wanting to, deliberate, each step placed as though the floor might not be trusted.

She sat with her notes and her quill and the east-facing light moving across the table. The oak surface still held the faint impression of warmth where his arm had rested. She hadn't anticipated how physical it would be — not the touching itself, which she'd prepared for, but the accumulating awareness of another person's body at close range for sustained minutes. The fine blond hair on his forearm. The scar tissue at the edge of the Mark where the curse-brand met unbranded skin, a faintly raised border she could feel under her fingertips. The way his pulse had been running fast when she first took his wrist and had slowed, gradually, as the Mark's pain eased — as if his whole body were recalibrating to the absence of something it had been carrying for months.

She did not think about the fact that the Mark had reached for her. She thought about it anyway. The dark magic had oriented to her with something that, in any other context and any other subject, she would have called recognition. She wrote this word in the margin of her notes and then crossed it out and replaced it with *anomalous differential response* and then stared at the crossed-out word underneath because the clinical language was a lie and she knew it.

She'd examined Dark objects before. Cursed jewelry, hexed artifacts, a boggart-infested wardrobe that had required a week of sustained diagnostic work. None of them had reached for her. Dark magic was hostile or inert; it did not welcome examination. What the Mark had done was something she had no precedent for, and the absence of precedent in her carefully accumulated knowledge was its own kind of disorientation, quieter than the corridors would be later but no less real.

---

The library saved her.

Not from anything dramatic — from the residue of the examination, the sensory memory of his wrist under her fingers, the anomalous thing the Mark had done that she didn't have a framework for yet. The library gave her a problem large enough to occupy every available cognitive resource, and she fell into it the way she'd been falling into problems since she was eleven years old.

It was extraordinary. She'd known the Malfoy collection was significant — every serious researcher in Britain knew, the way you know about a country you've never visited, by reputation and rumor and the occasional smuggled artifact that surfaced in other collections with a Malfoy provenance stamp. But knowing it was significant and standing inside it were different orders of experience.

The room was vast. Two stories of shelved walls with a mezzanine gallery running around the upper level, rolling ladders fixed to brass tracks, reading tables positioned under windows that admitted long shafts of autumn light. The air smelled of old paper and dust and something underneath both — the faint mineral tang of preservation charms that had been working for so long they'd become part of the room's atmosphere. The books went back centuries. She could see it in the shelf nearest the door without touching anything: a run of household accounts from the early 1700s bound in cracked calfskin, next to a grimoire with a spine that hummed faintly when she passed — not threatening, just present, the way a sleeping cat might purr if you brushed against its chair.

She started at the southeast corner, which was arbitrary but she had to start somewhere and arbitrary was better than standing in the middle of the room trying to absorb the whole thing at once. She pulled her cataloguing framework from her Department of Mysteries work: classification by subject, subclassification by language, notation for preservation state, age, and provenance where identifiable.

Within an hour she'd added two new categories. *Resistance to diagnostic magic* — for the texts that wouldn't be sorted, whose pages deflected her identification charms with the patient stubbornness of objects that had opinions about being organized.
