And *unknown script* — for the alcove she found on the mezzanine where three full shelves of texts were written in a magical notation she'd never encountered, angular and precise, the characters laid down in ink that still looked wet though the parchment underneath was brittle with age. She photographed the symbols with a copying charm and set the images aside for later analysis.

The collection had no organizing logic she could detect. Someone — generations of someones — had simply acquired. A shelf of astronomical observation journals sat next to a crate of loose letters in French. A section of alchemical texts shared space with what appeared to be a complete run of a seventeenth-century magical periodical she'd thought was lost entirely. One shelf held nothing but bestiaries, illustrated with moving drawings of creatures that pressed themselves against the pages as she passed — a Graphorn raised its head, a Demiguise flickered visible and invisible, something she didn't recognize clawed at the margin with tiny painted talons. She found a first-generation copy of the Flamel notation on anchor-point theory wedged between two cookbooks, and she had to sit down on the floor with it because her hands were shaking — not from the morning's examination, from the specific vertigo of holding a text she'd been told no longer existed.

The Flamel text was water-stained in one corner and someone had annotated the margins in a cramped hand she couldn't read, but the primary notation was intact — the anchor-point diagrams, the mathematical proofs for ley-line stabilization, the material Flamel had considered too theoretical to publish in his lifetime. She sat on the cold stone floor and turned pages and forgot, briefly and completely, where she was and why she was there and what had happened that morning with the Mark and the man it was branded on. This was the thing about her that nobody seemed to find charming and she could not help: a sufficiently interesting problem could erase everything else.

She worked through the morning and into the afternoon without stopping. Somewhere around midday she became aware that the library's upper gallery had changed — a section of shelving she hadn't approached yet had shifted forward, angling itself toward the light from the nearest window as if presenting its contents for easier reading. She noted this. She set it aside the way she was setting aside everything about this house that she couldn't process while maintaining the pace of cataloguing.

The floor around her accumulated sorted piles — texts she could read, texts she couldn't, texts that resisted classification, texts that needed immediate preservation work before they could be handled further. Her knees ached from sitting cross-legged on the stone floor. Her fingers were grey with dust. She'd filled eleven pages of notes and hadn't covered a quarter of the southeast corner.

The scope settled over her like weather. She'd estimated weeks. This was months, possibly longer. The collection was too large, too disordered, too multilingual for one person to process in any reasonable timeframe, and the texts that resisted her cataloguing spells would need individual attention — each one a small puzzle, a negotiation between her magic and whatever protective charms the original owners had laid down centuries ago.

She didn't mind. She adjusted her mental timeline without drama, the way she adjusted everything — practically, efficiently, with the faint irritation she directed at herself for having underestimated the problem in the first place. She kept working.

The light changed. The library's west-facing windows caught the late afternoon sun, which came in low and gold through old glass and made the dust visible in the air — not dirt, just the particulate evidence of a room full of very old paper, each mote a tiny fragment of something that had once been a page or a binding or a margin note. The room became warm. Not the Manor's ambient warmth, which she was learning had its own logic, but simple sunlight on stone and wood and leather, and for a stretch of minutes that she did not count, the library felt like nothing more complicated than a very good place to work.

She was sitting on the floor between two shoulder-high stacks of sorted texts when she realized she hadn't eaten since the tea and toast she'd managed that morning before the examination. Her stomach hadn't told her. Her stomach was an unreliable reporter when she was deep in a problem, a fact Ron had observed approximately two hundred times during their marriage and which she had never once conceded was a character flaw rather than a feature.

She stood. Her back informed her it had been holding this opinion for several hours. She stretched, shelved the current stack with placement notes, pocketed her wand, and went to find tea.

She passed Malfoy in the corridor outside the library. He was carrying a cup of something and a book and he looked like he'd been somewhere else in the house for hours — his hair was slightly disordered, which she suspected was as disordered as it got, and there was dust on his sleeve that he hadn't noticed or hadn't bothered with.

"The Flamel notation," she said, because she was still holding it and the excitement was stronger than the awkwardness. "You have a first-generation copy. Do you know what that is?"

He looked at the book in her hands. "It's been there since before I was born. Possibly before my father was."

"It's one of maybe three copies in existence. The Ministry's been looking for one for decades."

"Then I suppose it's fortunate you checked between the cookbooks."

She couldn't tell if he was joking. His face gave her nothing. She held the book against her chest and went to find the kitchen, or whatever passed for a kitchen in a house that produced food trays from nowhere, and behind her she heard him go into the library and the door close and the house was quiet again.

---

The house was quiet at half past nine. She'd eaten — a tray that appeared on the small table in the guest wing sitting room, warm and correctly proportioned, which raised questions she was not yet addressing about who or what was doing the cooking. She'd worked in the library until the light went and then by wand-light for another hour, sorting the unknown-script texts into subgroups by character set and ink composition. Now she wanted her room, her bed, her notes for tomorrow's examination protocol revisions.

The route was simple. She'd walked it that morning: left out of the library, down the long corridor with the tall windows and the portrait of a witch in green robes — a Malfoy ancestor, she assumed, from the pale coloring and the expression of amused disapproval — then right at the staircase, up one flight, and the guest wing opened before her. Fifteen minutes of walking at most, in a straight line with two turns.

She turned left out of the library. The corridor stretched ahead of her, properly, the windows letting in thin moonlight that laid silver rectangles across the stone floor. The portrait was there. But it wasn't the witch in green. It was a man — tall, bearded, dark-haired, in robes of a cut she associated with the late sixteenth century. He watched her pass with the specific stillness of a painted figure that could move and was choosing not to.

She'd noted the wrong portrait. She must have, that morning — tired from a night of poor sleep, distracted by the examination ahead of her. She was not someone who misremembered visual details, but she allowed the possibility because the alternative required engaging with the house and she was walking to her room, not conducting an investigation.

The staircase was where she expected it. She climbed, counting — one flight, as she'd done that morning. Except the landing she arrived at had a window facing south. Her room's window faced east. She was on the wrong side of the house, which should not have been possible from a single flight of stairs that she had climbed in a straight line.

She stood on the landing and looked at the south-facing window. Outside, the Manor's grounds were silver-grey in the moonlight — a formal garden she hadn't seen from this angle, hedges trimmed to geometric precision, a stone bench, the dark line of trees beyond. The air up here was cooler than the corridor below, and the stone under her feet had a slightly different texture, rougher, as though this section of the house was older or less maintained.

She went back down the staircase. The corridor at the bottom was not the corridor she'd left. It was narrower, with a vaulted ceiling that belonged to a different architectural period than the rest of the floor, and it ran in a direction she couldn't reconcile with her mental map of the house. The portrait was gone. The windows were gone. The corridor was lit by wall sconces that burned with a bluish flame she hadn't seen elsewhere in the Manor, and her footsteps didn't echo the way they had been echoing — the sound was absorbed rather than returned, as though the stone were softer here, or listening.

She stopped walking.

The house was not hostile. She could feel this the way she could feel weather — not a conscious assessment but an ambient read, something in the quality of the air and the light and the silence that communicated *not danger.* But not-danger was not the same thing as safe, and the corridor ahead of her went on further than her wand-light reached, and she did not know where she was.

She cast Point Me. The spell worked cleanly — her wand swung north and held, and when she specified her room, the wand pointed left and slightly up. Twenty meters, by her estimation. She walked twenty meters left, found a door, opened it. A broom cupboard. Mops, buckets, a bottle of something that smelled of lemon and old magic. Her room was not here.

She closed the cupboard. The lemon smell followed her out, sharp and incongruous, and she stood in the corridor with it fading around her and felt the first real pulse of something that wasn't annoyance — something colder and less useful.

She tried another direction. Found a corridor that looked like the one from that morning — the windows, the moonlight — but the staircase at its end led down instead of up, and the floor below was a hall she'd never entered, vast and dim and smelling of cold stone and disuse. A tapestry on the far wall showed a hunt scene, the horses and hounds frozen mid-stride, and she could have sworn that as she turned away the nearest hound moved its head to watch her go. She retraced her steps. The corridor behind her was longer now than it had been thirty seconds ago, and the door she'd come through opened onto a different hallway entirely — wider, warmer, with a carpet she hadn't walked on before.

She checked her watch. It had been eleven minutes since she left the library.

She kept walking. Not frantically — she was methodical about it, trying each corridor, each staircase, marking her path with small illumination charms that she could track, except the markers kept appearing behind her when she hadn't doubled back, as though the house were folding the space she'd already crossed into the space ahead. She passed through a gallery of windows overlooking a courtyard that should have been the central courtyard but was smaller, planted with something dark and thorned that she couldn't identify by moonlight. She climbed a staircase that deposited her on a landing with three doors, none of which led anywhere she recognized. She found a corridor that she was almost certain she'd walked down twice already, except the sconces were different and the ceiling was higher and there was a smell she couldn't place — not unpleasant, just wrong, something vegetal and slightly sweet, like a greenhouse at night.

Twenty minutes. She checked her watch again when the guest wing materialized around her — between one step and the next, without transition, without the sense of having turned a corner or opened a door. She was simply there. The corridor was exactly as she'd left it that morning: the rug with the faded blue pattern, the sconce with the slightly crooked shade on the left wall, the window at the far end showing the grounds under a sky that had clouded over while she was lost. Her door was the second on the left. She could see the light she'd left burning inside, the warm yellow line under the door.

She stood in the hallway for a while. Her breathing was even. Her hands were not shaking. She was not frightened — or she was, but not in a way that felt immediate. The fear was analytical, which was the worst kind, because analytical fear meant she understood the thing she was afraid of well enough to be afraid precisely and she couldn't yet find its edges.

She went into her room and sat on the bed and didn't undress. The room was the same as she'd left it — her notes stacked on the small desk, her overnight bag on the chair by the window, the bed made with the austere precision of house-elf work. The walls were solid. The window faced east. Everything was where it should be.

She thought about Point Me. The spell had worked. It had pointed accurately to her room, given her a correct distance — twenty meters — and the twenty meters had led to a broom cupboard. The spell hadn't failed. The house had simply declined to let the space between her and her destination remain the space the spell described. The navigation was correct. The territory was unreliable.

She thought about the portrait that wasn't the portrait she'd passed that morning. She thought about the staircase that went up one flight and arrived on the wrong side of the house. She thought about the corridor with the blue-flame sconces and the sound-absorbing stone and the twenty meters that ended in mops.

She thought about the Mark. The way the dark magic had oriented to her diagnostic charm with what she'd crossed out and replaced with *anomalous differential response.* The way Draco's arm had gone slack under her hands. The way the room had felt, afterward, like something in it had been settled by her presence. The way he'd moved his arm when he left, careful, as if the painlessness were fragile. As if she might have left something behind in the curse that could be broken by rough handling.

She thought about the library shelves that had angled themselves toward the light while she worked, presenting their contents like a host opening a cabinet for a guest. She thought about the food tray that appeared without being summoned, warm and correct, and about the guest wing room with its too-good mattress and its east-facing window that showed her exactly what the grounds looked like in the morning light, as if the house wanted her to find the view pleasing.

She pulled her legs up onto the bed and leaned against the headboard and looked at the room that was behaving, for now, like a room. The Manor was not a house with old magic and eccentric architecture. She'd been telling herself that version — the rational version, the version where centuries of accumulated charms produced occasional spatial anomalies, the version where a house could be strange without being aware. She'd been telling herself that version because it was the version that allowed her to stay here as a researcher studying a problem, not as a person living inside one.

The house was aware. She didn't have proof in any framework that would satisfy a Department of Mysteries review, but she had twenty minutes of corridors that rearranged themselves around her, a portrait that changed faces, a navigation spell that worked perfectly in a space that didn't hold still, and the specific quality of the silence in this room right now — the deep quiet of a large building at night, which could be emptiness but which she could no longer make herself believe was emptiness, because emptiness didn't have this weight, this attention, this patient held quality of something waiting to see what she would do.

She was living inside something that watched her. She'd agreed to a research arrangement. She'd negotiated terms and access and brought her cataloguing framework and her diagnostic protocols and her professional composure, and she was inside a sentient structure that had rearranged itself around her for twenty minutes and then let her go, and she didn't know why it had done either thing.

She turned off the light with her wand and lay in the dark. The sheets were cool. The mattress was better than the one in her London flat, which was a detail she resented noticing. Through the window the sky was fully clouded now, the last moonlight gone, and the room was dark in the way that only countryside dark achieves — total, soft, the kind of dark that has texture.

The house settled around her. She could hear it, or feel it — a sound below hearing, a vibration in the stone, the particular quality of a very large building shifting into its nighttime configuration. She'd heard houses settle before. Old buildings moved. Timber and stone expanded and contracted with temperature, and magical construction added its own layer of ambient adjustment.

This wasn't that. This was the house settling the way a living thing settles — deliberately, attentively, with the weight of awareness distributed through the walls and the floors and the corridors that had rearranged themselves around her and then, at the precise moment she'd been about to stop being methodical and start being frightened, had delivered her to her door.

She lay in the dark and didn't sleep and tried to rebuild her assessment of the situation from the ground up. She was here to research the magical decay. She was here to examine the Dark Mark. She had negotiated access to the library and residential accommodation in exchange for diagnostic work. These were the terms. These were the professional boundaries.

The boundaries did not account for a house that moved. They did not account for a curse-mark that reached for her magic. They did not account for the fifteen minutes she'd spent holding Draco Malfoy's wrist while the dark magic in his arm settled under her touch like something that had been waiting for her.

She'd have to account for all of it. She'd have to add the Manor to her variables, alongside the Mark and the library and the decaying magical field and the man whose arm she would hold again tomorrow morning because the diagnostic sequence required it and because the Mark's anomalous response demanded further investigation and because — this was the part she was not examining — under her hands, for the first time in what was clearly months, it had stopped hurting him.

She turned onto her side. The pillow was better than her London pillow. She added this to the list of things she resented about the Manor and closed her eyes and did not sleep for a long time, and the house around her was quiet in the specific way of something that is choosing not to make noise.


# Chapter 4

The east corridor had a draught problem. It had always had a draught problem — a thin persistent breath of cold air that seeped between the stones near the third window, the one with the cracked leading that had been cracked since before Draco's father was born. The draught was a known quantity. Draco dressed for it in winter, carried warming charms through the passage in October, and had stopped noticing it the way people stop noticing the sound of their own house settling at night.

Granger had been in the Manor for four days, and the draught was gone.

He discovered this on a Tuesday morning, walking from his rooms to the main staircase with his hands in his pockets and his collar turned up out of habit. He was halfway down the corridor before he registered what was missing — the cold, the slight pulling sensation along the left side of his neck. He stopped. The air was warm. Not heated-warm, not spelled — the particular warmth of stone that has absorbed heat from the inside, the way the Manor felt in late summer when the walls gave back the day's light.

It was November.

He pressed his palm to the wall. The stone was warm. Not radiating but holding, the way a living body held heat, and under his hand there was something that was not quite a pulse but was not quite the absence of one either. He stood there for a long moment with his hand flat against the corridor wall, feeling whatever it was he was feeling, and then he took his hand away and walked the rest of the passage without touching anything.

She was already in the library when he came down. He could tell before he reached the doors because the doors were open — both of them, the heavy oak panels that he had to push with his shoulder and that had required a specific knocking pattern since he was twelve. Open. Not ajar, not cracked. Open wide, the way they had opened for his grandfather, who the house had loved.

Granger was at the long table with her sleeves pushed up and three texts spread in a semicircle around her, the organizational system she'd built in a day and a half doing more to make the library navigable than anything Draco had attempted in years. She had a quill in her left hand and a wand in her right, cross-referencing something between a catalogue entry and a passage that was giving her trouble. She didn't look up when he came in.

"The Alderic folio was on the third shelf yesterday," she said, not looking up. "The one with the red binding, the preservation charms on the spine. It's moved."

"Moved where?"

"Here." She pointed. The folio was on the table next to her, propped against a stack of Draco's great-aunt's astronomical journals. "I came in this morning and it was here. I didn't move it."

He looked at the folio. He looked at the shelf where it had been — he could see the gap, the dark rectangle on the wood where the binding had sat for years, the dust line broken. The shelf was across the room and up a rolling ladder. The book was now eighteen inches from her right hand.

"The house does that," he said. "It rearranges."

"It rearranges for me." She said it precisely, the way she said everything — not an accusation, just a correction. The distinction between the house moves things and the house moves things to where I can reach them. She looked up then, and her expression was the one she'd been wearing since the corridors had shifted on her two nights ago: carefully interested, carefully not frightened. "Three other texts I'd been planning to pull today are on this table. I had them on a list. I hadn't told anyone the list."

He didn't say anything to that. There was nothing to say that wouldn't sound like he was defending the house or agreeing that its behavior was alarming, and both of those felt wrong. The house moved things. It had always moved things. His mother's reading glasses had traveled from room to room throughout his childhood, appearing wherever she sat down, which she had never once commented on aloud.

But the glasses had been his mother's, and the house had known her for twenty-five years.

"I'll make tea," he said, which was what he said when he didn't know what to say, and he went to the small service alcove off the library that the house had decided to keep in the same place two days running, which was new.

While the kettle heated he stood in the alcove and listened. The library had particular sounds — the creak of the upper gallery, the faint ticking of preservation charms cycling through their work, the occasional papery sigh of a text settling on its shelf. He had grown up with these sounds. They were the house breathing, his father had told him once, which at five had been comforting and at fifteen had been horrifying and at twenty-five was just the fact of the place he lived.

The sounds were different now. There was a hum he didn't recognize — low, almost subsonic, the kind of sound you feel in your sternum before you hear it in your ears. It had started the day Granger arrived or possibly the day after; he wasn't sure because it was the sort of thing you only noticed when you tried to locate when you first noticed it. The hum was present when she was in the library and absent when she wasn't. He had checked.

He made the tea. Two cups, because she was there, and he'd been making two cups for three days now without having decided to.

When he brought the tea back she was standing at the far wall where the genealogical section was shelved — the family histories, the marriage records, the blood-status documentation that made his skin crawl every time he walked past it. She was reaching for a volume on the top shelf, stretching on her toes, and the shelf lowered.

Not dramatically. Not a visible movement — more as though the proportions of the wall had quietly adjusted so that the top shelf was reachable where it hadn't been before. She got the volume, pulled it down, and didn't appear to notice that the architecture had rearranged itself to accommodate her height.

Draco set her tea on the table and sat down with his and did not comment. He had spent twenty-five years in this house. He had never seen a shelf lower for anyone.

---

He spent the morning in a different part of the Manor, in the study his father had used and that Draco used now for correspondence and for the particular kind of sitting that was not quite brooding and not quite rest. The Dark Mark ached — a low, restless pulse that had nothing to do with the old signal and everything to do with whatever the magic in it was becoming. It had been worse in the night, worse enough to wake him, and his forearm had that hot-wire feeling along the lines of the brand that meant the skin was inflamed again even though the surface looked the same as always.

He rolled his sleeve up and looked at it. The skull and serpent, grey-black against his pale skin, the lines still sharp after all these years. Curse marks didn't blur. They didn't fade or soften at the edges the way a tattoo would. The Dark Mark looked the same as it had the night Voldemort pressed his wand to Draco's forearm in the study at Riddle House while Draco's father held his shoulder and Draco bit through the inside of his cheek hard enough to taste blood and didn't scream because his father was watching and his father did not raise sons who screamed.

He rolled his sleeve back down. The fabric was cool against the inflammation and it helped slightly and he sat in his father's chair in his father's study and didn't think about anything useful.

The house was different. He had been aware of it before Granger arrived — a restlessness in the corridors, rooms he hadn't visited in months opening unexprompted, the library reorganizing with more frequency than usual. He had attributed it to the Mark's reactivation, or to the general magical decay that was making everything in Britain slightly wrong, or to the fact that houses, even sentient ones, had moods.

But the changes since her arrival were specific. The warming of the east corridor. The library doors. The shelf that lowered. The hum he could feel in his chest when she was in the room. These were not the Manor's general restlessness; they were directed, oriented toward a particular person who had been in the house for less than a week and who the house had apparently decided to accommodate with a thoroughness it had never shown its own heir.

He resented this. The resentment was familiar — it had the same flavor as his resentment of the name, the legacy, the assumption that the house was his inheritance when the house had always felt more like a custodial arrangement in which he was the least important party. He had grown up being told the Manor was his, would be his, was built for him and his children and their children. But the Manor had never once opened both library doors for him at the same time.

There was a knock at the study door. He recognized the knock — careful, rhythmic, the way Granger knocked on everything, three evenly spaced raps like a diagnostic. She was thorough even in the way she announced herself.

"Come in."

She pushed the door open — she had to push it; it didn't open for her here, in his father's study, which was something — and stood in the doorway holding a text she'd found.

"I think this is a diagnostic manual," she said. "For curse marks. It's old, but the methodology section is detailed. It references examination techniques I haven't seen in the standard literature." She paused. "Your great-great-grandmother wrote it, apparently."

"Cassiopeia."

"There's an inscription. She was a Healer?"

"Of a kind. She was — " He stopped, because the answer involved too much family history and because Granger was looking at him with an expression of genuine curiosity that wasn't about the text. She was looking at the study. At him in the study. He realized she hadn't been in this room before and she was registering his father's desk, his father's shelves, the weight of a room arranged by someone who was in prison and maintained by someone who had changed nothing.

"She didn't practice publicly," he said. "She was a woman in the 1870s. She researched privately."

"Of course she did." Granger said it without bitterness, which surprised him. She looked at the text she was holding with more tenderness than the observation warranted. "She was good. The methodology is rigorous. It's going to be useful for the Mark work."

"Good."

She nodded and left, pulling the door closed behind her. It latched with the ordinary click of an ordinary latch. No special treatment, no warm stone, no humming. His father's study, at least, was still his.

He sat with that small, pathetic consolation and then got up because sitting with it felt worse than moving.

The afternoon was a series of small observations that accumulated into something he couldn't ignore. He went to the west gallery and the lights came up a beat late, the way they always did — the gallery had been temperamental since his mother left, responsive but grudging, as though it couldn't be bothered. He walked back through the central hall and the temperature was neutral, neither warm nor cold, the Manor's baseline indifference to his passage.

Then Granger crossed the central hall on her way from the library to the kitchen — she'd started using the kitchen, making sandwiches from the stores the house-elves kept stocked, eating at the long table because the dining room was too formal for one person eating a sandwich and because she was Hermione Granger and she would eat where it was practical or not at all — and the hall warmed. He felt it. A slow bloom of heat in the stone under his feet, radiating upward through his shoes, and the light shifted, the November grey in the tall windows softening into something almost golden. The change lasted the thirty seconds it took her to cross the hall and then it faded, not back to cold but to that flat neutral that now felt, by contrast, like being ignored.

She hadn't noticed. She was reading as she walked — a habit he was beginning to catalogue, along with the way she pushed her hair behind her left ear when she was concentrating and the fact that she took her tea with milk and no sugar and that she sometimes mouthed words to herself when she was working through a difficult translation. He was cataloguing her and he knew he was cataloguing her and the knowing didn't make him stop.

The house was cataloguing her too. That was the difference. Whatever the Manor was doing — warming for her, rearranging for her, opening and adjusting and humming in its stones when she walked through — it was the behavior of a house that had found something it wanted. Draco had lived in this house his entire life and the house had never wanted him. Tolerated him, maintained him, provided the specific tepid reliability of an institution that would not let its heir freeze or starve but would not go out of its way to make him feel welcome either.

He thought about this while he pretended to read in the smaller sitting room, which was the room he actually used because the formal sitting rooms felt like display cases. He thought about how the house had always been warmer in his mother's wing, how it had closed off his father's wing entirely after the arrest, how the corridors to the drawing room —

He did not think about the drawing room.

The smaller sitting room was cool and the light was grey and outside the window the grounds were going brown with autumn's last work. A good day for self-pity, if he'd been the type, which he was not. He was the type who sat in reasonable rooms and noticed things and felt faintly resentful of a house and did nothing about any of it.

---

His mother arrived at half three, which was her habit on Tuesdays. She came by Floo — the connection was unreliable lately but she treated its intermittent failures as a personal affront rather than a systemic crisis, which was the most Narcissa Malfoy response to institutional collapse that Draco could imagine. She stepped out of the fireplace in the small parlour, brushed a non-existent speck of soot from her sleeve, and looked at the room the way she always looked at the Manor: the specific survey of someone who had lived in this house for a quarter century and still checked on it the way you'd check on a large animal you'd once been responsible for.

"The parlour fire is higher than usual," she said. It was her version of hello.

"I hadn't noticed."

"You wouldn't." She touched the mantelpiece, her fingertips trailing along the stone. It was something she'd always done — touching the house, making contact with its surfaces the way another person might touch a wall for balance. But it wasn't for balance. Draco had understood since he was old enough to understand anything about his parents' marriage that his mother's relationship with the Manor was its own separate thing, older and more complicated than whatever she and his father had built together. She had lived in this house from the age of twenty and the house knew her hands.

"Tea?" he said.

"Please."

He went to make it and she walked. This was also habit — she didn't sit down immediately when she arrived, she circled. Through the parlour, into the corridor, her heels precise on the stone. He could hear her moving while he waited for the kettle, that particular rhythm of a woman who knew every room and was checking on all of them.

When he brought the tea back she was standing in the doorway of the corridor that led to the central hall, her hand resting on the doorframe. She was very still.

"You have a guest," she said.

"I told you about the arrangement."

"You told me about the arrangement." She turned from the doorway and took the tea from him and her face was composed in the specific way that meant she was choosing what to show. "I didn't expect the house to know about it quite so thoroughly."

"What do you mean?"

She sipped her tea instead of answering, which was her preferred method of not answering, and walked into the parlour and sat in the chair she'd sat in for twenty-five years and that the house had kept in the same position despite rearranging everything around it. The chair was a fixed point. Draco had asked his father about it once and his father had said something about the Manor respecting tenured occupants, which had been both true and insufficient.

"The corridor is different," Narcissa said, after a pause long enough to establish that she would answer on her terms. "The one from here to the central hall. It used to turn left at the portrait of your great-uncle Septimus. Now it goes straight."

"It does that. It rearranges."

"It rearranges," she repeated, and the repetition was not agreement. She looked at him over her teacup. "It has rearranged to make the path from the guest wing to the library shorter. The path from this parlour to the library is also shorter. The path from your study to the library is shorter." She paused. "You see the pattern."

He did. The house was reorganizing around the library, pulling its rooms closer, tightening its geography around the space where Granger worked. The corridors that connected to the library were straightening, shortening, losing the deliberate indirection that the Manor usually maintained between its spaces. The house was making it easier for everyone to get to where she was.

"The house likes company," Narcissa said. She said it lightly, the way she said things she didn't mean lightly. "It has been a long time since anyone used the library with real purpose."

"I use the library."

"You sit in the library." She said this without cruelty, or at least without what she would consider cruelty. It was an observation. He sat in the library; Granger worked in the library. The distinction was real and he felt it like a thumb on a bruise.

He drank his tea. The parlour was warmer than it had been when he'd walked in twenty minutes ago, which he attributed to the fire and not to his mother's presence, because attributing it to his mother's presence meant thinking about the house responding to people and he was already thinking about that too much.

"She has the Mark work well in hand," he said, because it was the kind of thing you said. "The first examination was — thorough."

Narcissa's face did something brief and controlled that he couldn't fully read. His mother had opinions about the Mark that she had never stated directly — that it was an obscenity, that it was his father's fault, that she had watched them brand her sixteen-year-old son and done nothing because doing nothing was how she survived and she would carry that weight forever without discussing it. He knew all of this without her having said it, which was how most of what he knew about his mother worked.

"I'm glad," she said, and then she stood, which meant the tea conversation was ending and the walking-through-the-house portion of the visit was beginning. This was also habit. She came, they sat, she walked, she left. The middle portion — the walking — was the actual purpose. She was checking on the Manor the way a retired keeper checks on a facility. Making sure the walls were where they should be. Making sure the doors opened correctly. Making sure the thing she'd lived with for so long was behaving itself.

He followed her, because she expected him to and because he wanted to see what she saw.

She moved through the central hall without comment — he watched her note the temperature, the quality of the light, the stone's warmth, in a series of quick proprioceptive checks that looked like walking but were actually a full diagnostic. She paused at the bottom of the main staircase and put her hand on the newel post. Her thumb moved against the carved stone. She tilted her head slightly, the way she did when she was listening for something he couldn't hear.

"Hmm," she said, and continued up the stairs.

The first floor corridor. The gallery. She touched the wall at the junction where the gallery met the east wing and her hand stilled.

"The draught," she said.

"It's gone."

"Yes." She pressed her palm flat against the stone. She was quiet for several seconds. "Since when?"

"A few days."

She didn't ask what had changed a few days ago. She didn't need to.

They turned the corner toward the library wing and Narcissa stopped. There was a door in the corridor that should not have been there — a narrow dark-wood door, set into the stone between two sconces, in a stretch of wall that had been blank stone for as long as Draco could remember. The door had a brass handle, slightly tarnished, and the wood was old — older than the surrounding stonework, as though the door had been here first and the wall had grown around it.

Narcissa looked at the door. And Draco, watching his mother's face, saw it: not surprise. Recognition. The brief, controlled settling of features that meant she was seeing something she had expected to see eventually, something she had perhaps hoped not to see, something she understood.

"Mother."

"Leave it," she said. Her voice was steady but her hand was on the wall and her fingertips were white where they pressed the stone.

"That door wasn't here this morning."

"No." She removed her hand from the wall. She turned away from the door. Her composure reassembled itself in the time it took her to blink, so completely that if he hadn't been watching her face at the precise moment she'd seen the door he would have thought she was simply uninterested. "It was not."

"You've seen it before."

"The house has rooms that open when conditions change," she said, and this was an answer to a different question than the one he'd asked. "When I first came to live here, after the wedding, rooms appeared that had been closed for years. Your grandmother told me it was the house welcoming me. I found that explanation charming at the time."

"And now?"

"And now I find it insufficient." She walked past the door without touching it, which was notable because she had touched every other surface in the house. "The house has preferences. I would not call them opinions."

She said this with finality — the single declarative that ended a line of inquiry, the sentence that was a closed door of its own. He knew this mode. Pushing against it produced nothing except his mother's silence and the particular quality of her displeasure that felt worse than being shouted at.

They continued past the library — the doors were closed now, Granger presumably still inside — and Narcissa's route took them through the guest wing corridor, which she walked slowly, trailing her hand along the wall. She stopped again, this time at a window alcove where the stone had a different quality, warmer and smoother, almost polished.

"She is comfortable here?" Narcissa asked.

"She's working."

"That isn't what I asked."

He thought about it. He thought about Granger in the library that morning, unsurprised by a book that had moved to her table overnight, correcting him with the easy precision of someone who felt secure enough in a space to argue about it. He thought about the sandwich she'd made at lunch, moving through the kitchen with an ease that had taken him years to develop and that she'd managed in days. He thought about the way she'd looked at his father's study — registering but not judging, taking in the room as data rather than spectacle.

"I think so," he said. "As comfortable as anyone could be, given — " He stopped. Given what. Given that she'd been tortured in this house. Given that the person who'd tortured her was his aunt and that he'd stood against the wall and watched. Given that she was sleeping in a guest wing down the corridor from a room whose existence he could not think about without his lungs tightening.

"Yes," Narcissa said, and the way she said it suggested she had heard the rest of the sentence in his silence. "Given."

They had circled back to the small parlour. His mother collected her cloak from the stand where the house had placed it — Draco hadn't hung it there, the stand had been empty when she arrived and then it was there with the cloak draped neatly and who knew if the house had done that or if she had and he simply hadn't seen.

"The house is not malicious," Narcissa said, fastening the cloak at her throat with the silver clasp that had been her mother's. She said it the way she said important things: once, quietly, without emphasis.

"I know."

"But it is not neutral." She looked at him. Her eyes, which were his eyes — the grey, the specific grey that ran through the Black line and the Malfoy line and met in his face — were steady and serious and giving him nothing he could use. "Those are not the same comfort."

She kissed his forehead, which she had done since he was small and which he endured because refusing it would have been worse than the brief, embarrassing tenderness. Then she stepped into the Floo, said her destination with the crisp certainty of someone who expected the network to function through sheer force of will, and disappeared in green flame.

He stood in the parlour for a while after she left. The fire subsided to its normal level — lower, now, without her presence to justify the display. The room cooled. The house was settling around him with the familiar ambient indifference that was not exactly neglect and not exactly comfort — the tolerance of a large old structure for the small creature it was obligated to shelter.

He went back to the corridor where the door had appeared. It was still there. Dark wood, brass handle, the faint smell of old varnish and something underneath it that might have been cedar or might have been nothing. He put his hand on the handle and turned it. The handle gave slightly and then stopped, not locked but resistant, the way a door resists when the room behind it isn't ready or isn't sure.

He let go. The handle settled back to its resting position. The door remained, neither opening nor closing, neither welcoming nor refusing. It simply existed in a space where it hadn't existed that morning, and the fact of its existence was a question to which his mother had the answer and would not give it.

He went downstairs. The library doors were open again. From the corridor he could see Granger at the long table, lit by the amber glow of the reading lamps that the house had quietly adjusted to the warm end of the spectrum — the temperature of light she worked best in, apparently, which the Manor had determined independently and implemented without consultation. She was writing notes in the margin of a scroll with a self-inking quill she'd brought from home, her handwriting small and dense and certain.

He didn't go in. He stood in the corridor and watched the doors that had opened for her and the light that had warmed for her and the house that had reorganized its corridors and lowered its shelves and surfaced its texts and spent the warmth of its old stones to make itself ready for her, and he thought about what it meant that the thing he'd inherited — the house, the name, the weight of all of it — had recognized something in her before he had been willing to look closely enough to see it himself.

Not that he'd recognized her. That was the wrong word. She was Granger: precise, relentless, too thorough, impossible to argue with about matters of accuracy. She was the woman who'd been tortured on his drawing room floor and who had come back to his house anyway because there was work to do and she would always choose work over self-preservation and that was either admirable or pathological and he wasn't sure it mattered which.

He didn't go in. He went back upstairs, to his rooms, where the corridors were their normal length and the stone was its normal temperature and the house was its normal self — present, indifferent, his. The Mark ached on his forearm, the restless pulse that had no signal and no source, and he lay on his bed in the grey November light and felt the house settle around him like something waiting.

Below him, two floors down, the library hummed.


# Chapter 5

She'd been at the Manor for eleven days, and the mornings had developed a rhythm she hadn't asked for and didn't resist.

Up at six, because the light in the guest room entered through an east-facing window the house had angled, she suspected, to catch the first grey of dawn. Tea from the small pot that appeared on the side table — she hadn't worked out whether a house-elf was delivering it silently or whether the Manor was doing it, and she'd decided not to investigate because the answer to either question was uncomfortable in different ways. Dress, check the glamour on her forearm — a quick pass of her wand, the skin smoothing over the scar tissue until it looked like any other patch of arm — and then the walk to the library, which the house had been keeping consistent at about four minutes, though the route was never identical twice.

She was settling in. That was the word for it, and she didn't like it. Settling in implied duration, implied a provisional domesticity that was not part of the arrangement. She was here to research the library and examine the Mark. She had a room in London with a functional kitchen and a bed that didn't rearrange its own headboard, and she would go back to it when the work was done.

But the mornings had a rhythm, and the library was extraordinary, and the work was the kind of work that made her feel like herself in a way she hadn't felt since — she didn't finish the thought. She went to the library.

Malfoy was already there. He was often there before her, which she'd found irritating for the first few days because she was used to being the earliest person in any room and it felt competitive, which was childish, which she recognized and could not entirely suppress. By the second week the irritation had faded into something else — not quite comfort but the reliable presence of someone who took the work as seriously as she did. He looked up when she came in, nodded once, and returned to a text he'd been reading. His tea was half-finished. The lamp nearest his end of the table was lit against the grey morning.

The argument started over a word.

*Deperdere*, in a text dated 1643, written in the cramped hand of Abraxas Malfoy's great-great-grandfather. The standard Latin translation was *to lose, to destroy*, but Malfoy was insisting the family usage was narrower — that in the Malfoy notation, the word had acquired a specific technical meaning closer to *to disperse irretrievably*, and that the distinction mattered for their working model of magical decay.

"It's not a synonym for *amittere*," he said, turning the text toward her. His finger traced the line where the word appeared in a longer passage about anchoring infrastructure. "My grandfather's journals use the same construction. It's about dissipation, not loss. The magic isn't being taken or destroyed. It's bleeding out."

She'd been annotating a parallel text — a theoretical treatise from the 1790s that used standard Latin without the family modifications — and she could see what he meant but didn't agree with his conclusion. "The distinction only holds if you assume the original builders conceived of ambient magic as a contained system. Which these texts don't support." She pulled her own volume closer and found the passage she wanted. "Look — here. *Fundamentum magiae est fluvialis, non lacustris.* 'The foundation of magic is riverine, not lacustrine.' They're describing flow, not containment. So *deperdere* in context means dissipation from a system that was already in motion, not leakage from a sealed—"

"I'm not arguing it was sealed. I'm arguing there's a difference between natural flow loss and structural hemorrhage, and this word" — he tapped the page — "is the one they used for the second."

Hermione sat back. The library was quiet around them, the late afternoon light slanting through the high windows and catching dust motes in long diagonal lines. The desk they'd claimed — the largest of the reading tables, close to the east wall where the catalogued texts were shelved — was covered in books, scrolls, her notes, his annotations, two cold cups of tea.

She reread the passage. He was right. Not entirely — the theoretical framework she'd identified was also correct — but the family-specific usage added a layer she'd missed. The Malfoys who'd built the infrastructure had distinguished between natural attrition and catastrophic failure, and they'd built separate vocabulary for each.

"Fine," she said. "You're right about the word. But the riverine model still holds for the broader system."

"Obviously."

"So we need both frameworks. The macro model is flow-based, but the failure mode the texts describe is structural. Which means the decay we're seeing now isn't the system running down — it's the system breaking."

Malfoy was already writing. His handwriting was small and even, nothing like the erratic scrawl of his ancestor. He noted the distinction in the margin of his working copy, cross-referenced the passage numbers, and added a line she couldn't read from his angle.

She found herself watching him work. He had a way of going still when he was thinking — not the rigid stillness of the Mark examinations, but a quieter thing, his pen paused over the page, his eyes unfocused on the middle distance. Then the pen would move again, and whatever he'd been working through was already resolved in his head before the first word hit the parchment.

It had been years since she'd worked with someone who could do this. Harry was brilliant in his own way — instinctive, brave, capable of leaps she couldn't make — but the research had always been hers alone. Ron had been better at strategy than he'd ever got credit for but had never cared about the puzzle for its own sake. Her colleagues at the Ministry were competent. Some of them were good.

Malfoy was good. He was also fast, precise, and willing to be wrong, which was rarer than being smart. He'd conceded three points to her this week without making a production of it, and he'd been right about two things she'd missed, and neither of them had pretended the score didn't matter because it did, in the way that scores matter between people who are serious about the work.

"There's a third instance," she said, pulling another text from the stack at her elbow. "Seventeen-twelve. Different author — looks like the usage survived across at least three generations."

He reached for it without looking up from his annotation. Their fingers didn't touch — the book was large enough that the handoff was unremarkable — but she was aware of the distance in a way she hadn't been a week ago. An inch of air between his hand and hers over a leather binding.

She turned back to her own work.

They stayed in the library until the light failed. The house did something to the lamps — they brightened gradually as the windows darkened, the transition smooth enough that she'd lost track of sunset entirely the first time it happened and had looked up to find the sky black and the room golden and Malfoy still reading across from her as though neither of them had anywhere else to be.

Which, she supposed, they didn't.

"The Floo in the east parlor has been unreliable," he said, not looking up. "If you need to contact the Ministry, the study fireplace is more stable."

"Thanks."

"Mm."

She made a note about the seventeen-twelve text and added it to her reference chain. The translation work was slow, the kind of slow that felt productive rather than frustrating — each text opened connections to three more, and the network of cross-references she was building had started to take on its own shape, pointing toward something she couldn't quite see yet but could feel the outline of. Like excavating a fossil. Brush away enough sediment and the shape declares itself.

"Are you hungry?" he asked.

She checked. She was, though she hadn't noticed. "Yeah, probably."

"The kitchen puts out supper at seven. It's" — he looked at the clock on the far wall — "twenty past."

"I can eat late."

"You could also eat on time."

There was nothing in his tone. It was a flat observation, the kind of thing anyone might say. She thought about arguing and then didn't, because there wasn't anything to argue with. She was hungry and it was past seven and the text would be there in the morning.

"Fine," she said, and closed the book, and if she noticed that he'd waited until she moved before he stood, she didn't examine why.

---

The meal was simple — roasted chicken, potatoes, greens, bread that was still warm. The house-elves at the Manor operated at a level of quiet competence that she found both impressive and politically uncomfortable, a familiar tension she'd long since learned to hold without resolving. Malfoy ate neatly, without conversation at first. The dining room they used — not the formal one, a smaller room near the kitchen that felt like it had been a breakfast room in some earlier configuration of the house — had windows that looked out over the west grounds, though at this hour there was nothing to see but their own reflections in the glass and the dark beyond.

"The seventeen-twelve text references something called the Tethys Working," she said, because the silence had settled and she'd rather fill it with something useful than let it become an object. "Have you come across that?"

"Once. In my grandfather's library, not here. He had a manuscript — handwritten, no attribution. I think it was a copy of something older."

"Can you get it?"

"I can look. He kept his personal collection at the townhouse. My mother might know where it ended up."

She nodded and ate a piece of potato and didn't say anything else for a while. The bread was very good. She tore a piece and ate it with butter, and the butter dish had migrated closer to her side of the table at some point during the meal, which she registered without comment because the house did things like that now and she'd stopped being startled by small courtesies from the architecture.

"How's the Mark?" she asked.

"The same."

"The same as when?"

"The same as yesterday. Active. Not worsening."

"Is that true, or is that what you think I want to hear?"

He looked at her for the first time since they'd sat down. His eyes were grey and steady and slightly amused in a way that didn't quite reach his mouth. "It's true. I'd tell you if it changed. You'd need to adjust your diagnostic approach."

"Right," she said. "Good."

"Granger."

"Mm."

"Eat the greens."

She ate the greens. They were fine.

They cleared the table together — not by arrangement, but by a kind of mutual practicality that had developed without discussion. She stacked the plates and he vanished them to the kitchen with a flick of his wand, a bit of household magic so casual it was nearly Muggle in its domesticity. The bread basket lingered on the table, and she took the last piece and ate it standing up, which felt like something a person who lived here would do and she pushed that thought down before it could finish forming.

"I'm going to write up the model tonight," she said. "The two-framework distinction. I want to get the terminology right before we go further into the seventeen-twelve text."

"I'll check the notation in the morning. If I'm wrong about the family usage, better to catch it before you've built a reference chain on it."

"You're not wrong."

He paused at the door. Almost smiled — or the muscles near his mouth moved in a direction that, in someone less guarded, would have become a smile. "I might be wrong."

"You're not wrong about this."

"Well." He looked at the door frame rather than at her. "Goodnight, Granger."

"Goodnight."

He left. She heard his footsteps on the stone of the corridor — sharp at first, then softening as the house absorbed the sound, as it always did, folding each person's movement back into its own quiet.

She wiped a crumb off the table and stood there for a moment longer than necessary, in the small warm room with its dark windows and its cleared table and the faint smell of rosemary from the chicken that was already being taken apart by efficient hands somewhere in the depths of the house.

---

It happened on the walk back to the guest wing.

The route the Manor gave her that evening was straightforward — a single long corridor from the kitchen end of the house to the guest wing, lined with portraits that had mostly given up trying to talk to her. She was thinking about the Tethys Working, about the structural-hemorrhage distinction, about whether the seventeen-twelve text's description of magical infrastructure could be reconciled with the theoretical models the Ministry currently used, and she was not thinking about Malfoy at all when she became aware that he was on the third floor.

She stopped walking.

It was not a thought. It was not a guess. She could feel his position in the house the way she could feel the weight of the wand in her pocket — present, specific, directionally certain. Third floor, east side. His rooms. He'd gone upstairs after dinner while she'd stayed to finish her tea, and she hadn't heard his footsteps or marked his direction, and she knew where he was.

Under that awareness, lower and steadier: a dull ache in her left forearm, not the scar. The scar's pain, when it came, had a hot scratchy quality, like a healing burn that had never quite finished healing. This was different. Deeper. A slow pulse of something that felt borrowed, as though her arm had picked up a frequency that belonged to a different body.

She pressed her palm flat against her forearm through her sleeve and felt the ache shift but not subside. It wasn't in her skin or her muscle. It was somewhere else — in the diagnostic trace, maybe, the residual magical connection she'd built over three examinations of the Mark, hours of sustained contact with the curse-magic in his arm. She'd read about this. Sustained diagnostic exposure to heavily cursed objects could create temporary trace bonds — sensory artifacts, usually minor, usually transient. The standard recommendation was reduced contact frequency and time.

She pressed harder and the ache sharpened, briefly, into something that felt like his — the specific restless quality of the Mark's new pain, the magic trying to move through a channel that connected to nothing.

Then it faded to its earlier level. She was standing in a corridor lined with sleeping portraits, her hand clamped around her own forearm, her breathing faster than it should have been. The Manor was quiet around her. A lamp at the far end of the corridor flickered, steadied, and the shadows rearranged themselves along the stone.

She let go of her arm.

Diagnostic residue. That's what it was. She'd been maintaining sustained spell-contact with an unusually active Dark artifact, and the trace connection was an expected side effect. It would degrade with reduced exposure, and if it didn't, she could modulate her diagnostic approach to minimize the resonance.

She walked the rest of the corridor to her room, and when she closed the door behind her she stood with her back against it and made herself think clearly.

The trace-bond literature she'd read at the Ministry — Belby's monograph, the St. Mungo's protocol for cursed-object examiners — described residual sensory artifacts as minor, transient, and non-directional. A faint echo of the curse's quality, usually lasting hours, occasionally days. What she'd just experienced was directional. It had located him in the house with a specificity that residual traces didn't explain. She could either revise her model to account for the Mark's unusual properties — an active, reactivating curse rather than a stable dark artifact — or she could acknowledge that this was something the literature hadn't documented because it wasn't supposed to happen.

She chose the first option, because the first option was the one that let her keep working. The Mark was unusual. Everything about this case was unusual. That's why she was here, and that's why the standard literature didn't apply cleanly.

She unpacked her notes and worked for another two hours at the small desk the house had placed under the window, and she did not catalogue the exact moment the ache returned — faintly, like a radio signal at the edge of reception — or the way it told her, without any mechanism she could name, that he was still awake.

At eleven she put out the lamp. At midnight she was still lying in the dark with her left arm resting on the covers, her fingertips against the inside of her forearm, feeling something that was not hers pulse faintly against the scar that was.

She would tell him if it persisted. There was no reason not to. It was a clinical finding, relevant to their diagnostic protocol, and withholding it would compromise the quality of the data they were collecting.

She rolled over. The mattress was good — better than her bed in London, which had been adequate for years and would have to go on being adequate. The sheets smelled faintly of lavender and something older, a cedar preservation charm that the house had been laying down for centuries.

She'd tell him tomorrow, if it was still there.

She would not tell him tomorrow. She knew this in the same way she knew where he was in the house — not as a decision she'd made but as a fact her body had already settled. The information felt private in a way that had nothing to do with clinical relevance and everything to do with the fact that she could feel him in his rooms, the ache thinning as the Mark's activity ebbed toward whatever passed for rest in cursed tissue, and the intimacy of knowing this — of being inside the peripheral weather of his body without his knowledge or consent — was something she was not prepared to hand over.

It was diagnostic residue. It would fade.

She closed her eyes and let the ache keep her company, which was not the same thing as wanting it.

---

Three days later she met Harry for lunch at a pub in Bethnal Green, a Muggle place he'd found when he and Ginny first moved to East London. It served mediocre food and excellent chips and had the specific virtue of being nowhere anyone from the Ministry would think to look for either of them.

She Apparated to the alley behind the pub and walked around to the front, and the city hit her like a wall — the noise, the diesel, the sheer density of people moving in every direction with their own problems and their own schedules and absolutely no interest in her. After the Manor's watchful quiet, London felt like being dropped into a river.

Harry was already there, sitting at a table near the back with a pint and the look of someone who'd arrived early on purpose to get fifteen minutes of not being Harry Potter. He stood when he saw her and hugged her, and she let herself be hugged, and the ordinariness of it — his familiar shape, the smell of his jacket, his hand on her shoulder — settled something in her that she hadn't realized was unsettled.

"You look all right," he said, sitting back down. "Bit tired."

"Thanks."

"I'm saying you look all right. I could say you look terrible."

"I look fine."

"Yeah, you do." He picked up his pint and drank. "How's the library?"

She told him. Not everything — not the resonance, not the way the house moved around her, not the translation arguments that felt like the most engaging work she'd done in years — but the shape of it: the scale of the collection, the texts on magical infrastructure, the emerging picture of war-damage to the foundational anchoring system. It was easy to talk about the research. The research was clean, professional, the thing she was there for.

"So it's real," Harry said. "The decay has a cause."

"It has a cause. Whether we can reverse the damage — that's what I don't know yet."

"But the texts are there. The information exists."

"Some of it. The Malfoys weren't the only family involved in building the original infrastructure, but they kept the best records. Or the only surviving records, which amounts to the same thing."

Harry nodded. He was good at this — listening to the part she was saying, hearing the part she wasn't. He didn't push. He ate a chip and looked at her with the steady, patient attention that had been exactly the same since they were eleven.

"Are you sleeping?"

"Yeah."

"Hermione."

"I'm sleeping. The guest room's comfortable, the house is quiet at night, I'm getting six hours minimum."

Five of those hours involved lying awake feeling an ache that didn't belong to her, but he hadn't asked about that and she wasn't offering.

"You look tired," he said again, without accusation.

"I'm working hard. It's good work. I'm fine." She reached for her water glass and as her arm extended across the table her sleeve rode toward her elbow. She caught it before the motion completed and pulled it back down with her other hand, wrapping her fingers around the glass. The adjustment was small, habitual — she'd been doing it for seven years. It didn't register as an action. It was simply the way she moved through the world, with one arm perpetually managed.

"Ginny says hi," Harry said. "She also says you should come for dinner when you're back in London for more than an afternoon. She's got opinions about the Floo situation."

"The Floo's been bad?"

"Three failures on our line last week. One of them dropped me in Birmingham."

"Birmingham."

"I'm not joking. The Midlands hub is degrading. The Ministry's pretending it's maintenance, but Ginny talked to someone at the Prophet and they're sitting on two more stories about infrastructure failures. It's getting worse."

The information landed in the space the research had opened. If the Floo network was degrading at the hub level, that was consistent with the anchoring-damage model — the same model she and Malfoy were building from the texts. The hubs were connected to the same deep infrastructure as the ley-line network. If the war had fractured the anchoring points, the hubs would fail in exactly this pattern — peripheral first, then cascading inward.

"I need to see those Prophet reports," she said.

"I'll ask Ginny. She'll have copies." He ate another chip. "Ginny thinks the Ministry's going to have to say something public within the month. She's angry about it, actually — says they should've acknowledged the pattern six months ago instead of letting people think their individual Floo connections were the problem. She had a woman in her department crying because she thought she'd broken her own fireplace."

"That's — yeah, that's consistent with what I'm seeing in the texts. Individual users would notice degradation and blame local causes. But it's systemic."

"You sound like you've been waiting for someone to confirm this."

She had. The texts described the failure pattern in theory. Harry's Floo data was the first field confirmation she'd had that the theory mapped to current conditions, and the alignment between the centuries-old model and real-time infrastructure collapse was, professionally, the most important thing she'd heard in weeks. She also knew that if she explained this at full speed Harry's eyes would glaze at around the forty-second mark, so she compressed. "The research matters more than I thought it did. It's not just historical — it's diagnostic."

"Right," Harry said. He looked at her the way he looked at her when she was running fast and he was trying to keep up without admitting he'd lost the thread. "Good."

"So the Manor. It's all right? The house is treating you all right?"

"The house is a house, Harry."

"That's not what you told me last time."

She had told him, briefly, in a fire-call the previous week, that the Manor's layout was variable. She'd kept it matter-of-fact, framing it as architectural curiosity rather than the unsettling reality of living inside something aware. She hadn't mentioned that the house surfaced books she was looking for, or that the butter dish migrated across the table toward her at meals, or that the corridors between the library and her room shortened on mornings when she woke early and was eager to get to work.

"It's strange," she allowed. "The corridors shift. Some of the rooms rearrange. It's not dangerous, just — variable."

"Variable."

"Like a building with preferences."

Harry considered this. "And Malfoy? How is he with all of it?"

The question was careful. Harry hadn't asked about Malfoy directly until now, and the way he did it — folded into a question about the house, as if the two were obviously connected — was as close to a leading question as Harry got.

"He's fine. He's been helpful with the translations. His family used a specific notation in their texts that I can't read without context, and he has the context." She was talking faster than she needed to, which was a thing she did when she was being precise as a form of evasion. She made herself slow down. "He's a good researcher."

"Okay."

"I mean it. He's rigorous. He catches things I miss."

"I believe you."

"You're doing the face."

"I'm not doing a face."

"The face where you're thinking something and deciding not to say it."

Harry put down his chip. "I'm not thinking anything. I'm glad the research is going well. I'm glad the library's useful. I'm asking if you're all right because you look tired and you've been living in a house that moves its own walls, and I know you, and I know what that house is."

She adjusted her sleeve again. A different motion this time — a pull at the cuff, tugging it down over her wrist while she reached for a chip of her own. The two gestures were mechanically similar and functionally different. The first had protected the scar. This was something else — a tic, a tell, the thing her hands did when she was sitting across from someone who loved her and who she was not telling the truth.

"I'm all right," she said. "Promise."

Harry looked at her for a long moment. He had very green eyes and they were very honest and she could see him deciding to let it go. Not because he believed her, but because he knew the difference between not-ready-to-talk and lying, and he was giving her the benefit of the doubt that this was the first and not the second.

It was the second.

"Okay," he said. "Come for dinner. Ginny'll do that chicken thing you like."

"The one with the lemon."

"The one with the lemon."

"I'll try."

"Don't try. Come." He smiled and it was Harry's smile, the crooked honest one that hadn't changed since he was twelve, and she wanted to tell him everything — the resonance, the ache, the way the house warmed when she and Malfoy worked well together, the way the arguments about Latin felt like the closest thing to play either of them had allowed themselves in years, the fact that she'd started knowing where a man was in his own house by the borrowed pulse in her own arm — but the words were too strange and too new and she didn't have a framework for them yet, and Hermione Granger did not talk about things she couldn't frame.

"I'll come for dinner," she said.

"Good."

They finished the meal. Harry told her about a case — a string of minor enchantment failures on Muggle objects that were supposed to have been decommissioned after the war. "Someone charmed a postbox in Croydon to eat letters and never undid it. Took us three weeks to figure out why the Royal Mail was blaming ghosts." She laughed, and it felt strange — the sound of it, the way it came out easily in this pub with this person when the Manor's quiet had been making her careful with every noise she made, as though the house might be listening and drawing conclusions.

Harry noticed the laugh. She could see him noticing, the way his expression softened into something that was both glad and worried, the specific face of someone watching a friend remember how to do something they'd forgotten.

He paid because he always paid and she let him because the argument was fifteen years old and neither of them was going to win it.

Outside the pub, the afternoon was grey and cold. November had settled into London the way it always did — not dramatically, just a slow withdrawal of warmth. She pulled her coat tighter.

"Come back soon," Harry said. "Not in three weeks."

"It hasn't been three weeks."

"It's been twelve days."

She hadn't counted. She'd been at the Manor for just under two weeks and it felt like longer — not tedious but dense, packed with work and the particular texture of a place that demanded her full attention.

"I'll come back for dinner," she said. "Tell Ginny I'll bring wine."

"She'll say you don't have to."

"I know. Tell her anyway."

He hugged her again, and she held on for a second longer than usual, and if he noticed he didn't say anything. Then he was walking toward the Apparition point at the end of the street and she was standing on a pavement in Bethnal Green with her coat pulled tight, feeling the gap in her arm where the resonance wasn't — because here, in London, miles from the Manor and its corridors and the man who lived in them, her forearm was just her forearm, carrying only its own scar and its own familiar weight.

The absence was information. She filed it and Apparated home.

---

She came back to the Manor at half three. The grounds were cold, the formal garden brown and withdrawn for the season, the house rising behind the hedgerow with its pale stone and its black windows and the look of having been watching the road.

The front door opened before she reached it. The hallway beyond was warm and lit and smelled of wood polish and the faintly sweet undertone of very old stone. She hung her coat on the hook that had appeared for her by the door — a hook that hadn't existed when she'd first arrived, or at least hadn't been visible — and stood for a moment in the entrance, letting the Manor's warmth replace the November she'd carried in on her skin.

The resonance came back between the front door and the staircase.

A low pulse in her forearm, like a second awareness starting up. She stopped on the third stair and pressed her palm against her sleeve. He was in the library. She could feel that much — the directional sense, less sharp than it had been three nights ago but there, insistent, a quiet certainty. The Mark was active. Not badly — a moderate ache, the restless magic cycling through its patterns.

Diagnostic residue. Proximity-dependent, which was consistent with residual trace bonds. In London she couldn't feel him. In the Manor she could. The simplest explanation was that the Manor's ambient magic was conducting the trace — boosting a signal that was too weak to transmit through non-magical space.

She had four possible explanations and all of them were adequate and none of them were quite right, and the mechanism mattered less to her in this moment than the fact that she'd been away from the house for four hours and the first thing it gave back was him.

She climbed the stairs and went to the library and found him at the reading table with a stack of texts she didn't recognize and a pot of tea that was still hot. He'd been working while she was gone — his notes had grown by several pages, and the texts were flagged with scraps of parchment at intervals that suggested he'd been cross-referencing. The sight of it — evidence of his afternoon, the work continuing in her absence — produced a feeling she didn't examine.

He looked up when she came in.

"London?" he said.

"Lunch with Harry. The Floo network's degrading at the hub level — three failures on the Potter line last week. It's consistent with the anchoring-damage model."

"I know. I tried the east parlor Floo this morning. Took four attempts to get a stable connection."

"You didn't mention that."

"You weren't here." He said it without emphasis. A fact, not a reproach. She sat down across from him and pulled the nearest text toward her and opened it, and the library settled around them, and the ache in her arm was steady and his and close.

She didn't tell him. About the Floo, she'd told him. About the resonance — the fact that she'd walked into the house and the first thing she'd felt was his location, his Mark, the borrowed ache that tracked him through stone and staircase — she said nothing.

She opened her notebook and began working, and the room was warm, and the light was good, and the man across the table turned a page and the motion was small enough that she should not have felt it in her own arm, but she did — a brief brightening of the signal, as though his moving hand had passed through the frequency she was receiving, and the resonance had noted it, and passed the notation along to her.

She made herself look at the text. The words were Latin and old and they described a world built on magic that was now dying, and she was the person who could read them, and that was why she was here. The only reason.
