The ache in her arm was an artifact and the man across the table was a research partner and the house around them was architecture, however animate, and none of this was complicated by the quiet, uninvited fact of her awareness — that she could feel the shape of his presence in the room the way you feel the heat from a fire you aren't looking at.

She worked until the light failed again. They didn't speak much. He refilled her tea without asking and she thanked him and the cup was warm in her hands and outside the autumn dark came early and total and the Manor's windows reflected the two of them back at themselves — heads bent over their separate texts, close enough that the table between them was more bridge than barrier, more shared space than distance.

At nine he said he was going upstairs and she said goodnight and he left, and the resonance tracked him — up the main staircase, through the corridor, up again to the third floor, east side. The signal thinned as he moved further from her, fading like sound through walls, but it didn't disappear. She could feel him settle. The Mark's ache eased slightly, the way it did in the hours before sleep when the restless magic slowed its cycling.

She sat in the library with her books and her tea and her borrowed awareness and told herself it was residue, artifact, the expected consequence of sustained diagnostic contact with cursed tissue.

She almost believed it.


# Chapter 6

She was trying something new.

Draco had learned her routines by now — the way she arranged her instruments before each session, the angle she preferred for his arm, the habitual push of her sleeves to her elbows that she always corrected before he could register what she was correcting. He'd learned to sit still through the initial passes of diagnostic magic, which ran under the skin like cold water, and to breathe evenly through the deeper probes, which pressed on some bruise he could never locate. He'd learned that her face went remote during examinations, all the restless intelligence narrowing to a single point, and that the silence between them in these sessions was not the same silence they shared in the library. That silence was becoming comfortable. This one was clinical. He preferred the clinical. The clinical had rules.

But today was different, and she had warned him. Sustained contact. Her hand on the Mark itself for the full duration, rather than the hovering wand-guided passes of the previous sessions. She'd explained — precisely, without looking at him — that wand-mediated diagnostics produced an interference pattern that filtered the Mark's raw magical signature. Direct palm contact would bypass the filter.

He'd understood the theory. He understood the implication too: her hand pressed to his forearm, to the stain that had been there since he was sixteen, for however long the session required. Neither of them had mentioned that the last time her hand had lingered on the Mark — during the third session, a brief accidental contact — the magic in it had surged toward her like something recognizing a voice.

They were in the small sitting room off the library's east wall. Not a room anyone would design for medical examinations — a writing desk pushed against one wall, two chairs, a side table — but it had natural light, which Granger had mentioned once, in passing, mattered for the diagnostics. The next morning the Manor's heavy drapes had been gone and the windows clean, winter light coming through glass that hadn't seen a cloth in years. He hadn't asked the house to do that. She hadn't asked the house to do that. The house had done it anyway.

December light. Thin, grey, the sun already failing at half past two. Frost had built along the edges of the glass overnight and the room was cold in the way the Manor's rooms got cold when the house was distracted or indifferent — not aggressive cold, just the absence of effort a building this size defaulted to.

"Ready?" she said.

He rolled up his left sleeve. The Mark was vivid today, the skull and serpent sharp against his skin, the lines almost raised. It fluctuated like this — some mornings nearly invisible, others so dark it looked freshly done. He couldn't predict it. He'd stopped trying.

"How's the pain been?"

"Same. Usual."

"Your usual or regular-person usual?"

He almost smiled. "Manageable."

She didn't believe him. He could tell by the way she didn't argue, which was Granger's particular method of recording disagreement for later use. She set her wand on the table.

"Standard wand-pass first for baseline, then I'll transition to direct contact. The transition might feel strange. I don't know exactly how. Tell me if you need to stop."

He nodded. She picked up her wand and began the initial pass — the familiar cold sweep, the Mark shimmering under the diagnostic light, the sensation of being read by magic that was smarter than it had any right to be. He leaned back in the chair and watched the frost on the window. A thin crack ran through the ice along the lower pane, splitting and branching. He followed it while the diagnostic spells did their work.

The Mark tolerated her. This had been remarkable from the first session and had not stopped being remarkable. The few Healers he'd approached in the years after the war had refused to examine Dark Lord magic — the professional risk, the legal liability, the simple atavistic fear of what the skull on a young man's forearm meant about the young man. The one who'd agreed had lasted ninety seconds before the Mark's defensive response had shattered his diagnostic framework. The man's hands had shaken for an hour afterward. Draco had not tried again.

Granger's spells didn't shatter. They settled into the Mark like water into sand, and the magic in it let them.

"Baseline stable," she murmured, scanning the readings. "Reactivity up slightly from Tuesday. Within your range." She put the wand down. Paused. "Switching now."

She reached across the table and put her hand on his forearm.

He had been expecting it. He had prepared for it. It didn't matter. The contact — warm palm, firm grip, her fingers wrapping around his forearm so that the Mark sat under the center of her hand — hit him with the specificity that only unexpected touch could produce. Not magical. Not painful. Just the fact of another person's skin on his, which should not have been noteworthy and was. Her thumb settled against the inside of his wrist. His pulse was there and she knew it.

"Starting the deeper reading," she said. "Stay still."

He stayed still.

She closed her eyes. No wand now — she was channeling the diagnostic directly through her palm, a technique that required sustained magical focus of a kind most practitioners couldn't maintain for more than a few seconds. She could maintain it. Her brow drew in and her breathing slowed and the magic flowed from her hand into the Mark, and it was nothing like the wand-passes. The wand-passes were cold and analytical, an instrument reading an object. This was warmer. The magic entered the Mark's structure not as an examination but as a presence — something alive meeting something alive — and the dark magic in the Mark, the restless agitated thing that had been his constant companion for months, the ache that woke him at three in the morning and made his hand clench and his teeth set and his sleep a nightly negotiation with pain —

It stopped.

Not gradually. Not the way a headache fades or a cramp releases. The pain was there and then it was gone, like a sound cut off mid-note, and in its place was a silence so complete that Draco couldn't process it.

The absence was louder than the pain had been. He'd been carrying it for so long — not just the recent months of the Mark's reawakening but the years of low-grade ache that he'd dismissed as phantom, as memory, as the body's insistence on remembering what had been done to it — that the relief was disorienting. He had organized his life around this pain. He slept on his right side because the left arm's weight against the mattress made the ache worse. He carried his teacup in his right hand. He dressed with the left arm last because the sleeve dragging over the Mark was a small daily unpleasantness he'd learned to back-load. All of that infrastructure — the minor accommodations, the unconscious adjustments, the constant low-frequency attention the pain demanded — was suddenly obsolete. His arm felt weightless. The Mark under her hand was still and flat and quiet, and the absence of pain had opened a space he hadn't known he was filling.

He must have moved, or his breathing changed, because her eyes opened.

"What happened?"

"It stopped." His voice was rough. He hadn't expected that. "The pain. It's — all of it."

She stared at him. Her hand was still on his arm. In the previous sessions, any disruption had broken her concentration and they'd started over. This time she held on.

"Stopped," she repeated. "The baseline ache, the persistent —"

"All of it. Since the direct contact."

She looked down at where her hand covered the Mark. He looked too. Her grip was clinical, fingers arranged for contact rather than comfort, and beneath her palm the Mark was the stillest he had ever seen it. The dark shimmer was gone. The restless movement of magic through dead channels — gone. The skull and serpent lay flat and inert under her hand, just lines on skin. Just a scar.

She moved her thumb in a slow arc across the Mark's edge, testing something. The magic under the skin didn't react. It should have — even her gentlest diagnostic passes in previous sessions had drawn a shimmer, the Mark's residual defenses flaring before settling. Now there was nothing. Whatever her touch was doing to the dark magic, it wasn't just suppressing the pain. It was quieting the magic itself, the whole restless architecture of it, as if her presence was the answer to a question the Mark had been asking since the Dark Lord died and the signal cut out.

"I'm going to lift my hand," she said. "Tell me when the pain returns."

She pulled her hand away. His forearm was cold where her warmth had been.

He counted. At seven seconds the Mark twitched, a familiar low ache like a muscle remembering an injury. At ten the restless magic was back, not as severe as before the session but unmistakably present, the pain a dull broadcast he couldn't turn off.

"Back," he said.

She put her hand on the Mark again. The pain stopped. Again. Immediate and total, like a switch thrown.

They sat with this. The frost cracked quietly on the window. A tree branch scraped glass somewhere above them, and the thin December light moved a degree across the floor.

"That's not the diagnostic," she said, and he could hear her thinking — the words running behind something that was already ahead of them. "The technique isn't therapeutic. It's observational. The magic shouldn't be interacting, it should be reading. What I'm doing shouldn't have any effect on the pain, let alone —" She shook her head. "It's not responding to the method. It's responding to —"

"To you."

She didn't answer for a moment. Her thumb shifted on his wrist — a tiny adjustment that she might not have been conscious of and that he was extremely conscious of.

"I don't have an explanation yet," she said.

"Yet."

"I want to try something. Maintain contact longer, see if the effect persists after I break contact or if there's a cumulative —" She was talking faster, the cadence he recognized: Granger converting something she didn't understand into something she could measure. "I'll need precise onset and offset data. And a comparison against the wand-mediated baselines from the last three sessions to rule out —"

"Granger."

She stopped.

"You're holding my arm. You don't have a quill."

She looked at the notepad and quill she'd arranged on the opposite side of the table, well out of reach. She looked at her hand on his arm. The problem occurred to her — that logging data required a hand she was currently using to keep his Mark quiet — and something crossed her face that wasn't embarrassment. Something that acknowledged, without naming, that she'd been holding his arm longer than any protocol required and that neither of them had said anything about it.

"Right," she said. "Could you —"

He reached across with his free hand and slid the notepad toward her. She picked up the quill left-handed — she was right-handed, her right hand on his arm — and began writing in a script that looked nothing like her usual careful hand. Cramped, tilted, the letters struggling.

"Onset of pain cessation: immediate on direct palmar contact," she said as she wrote. "Pre-contact pain level —"

"Four. I've been generous about calling it a four."

She wrote that without comment, which told him more than an argument would have. She'd been estimating his pain lower.

"Current level?"

"Zero."

She wrote. The quill scratched against the notepad. Her hand stayed on his arm, warm and steady, and the Mark stayed silent, and the frost built its slow patterns on the glass, and he looked at the ceiling and then at the wall and then at her hand and then at the ceiling again, because if he looked at her face while she was concentrating — lip caught between her teeth, the crease between her brows, her hair escaping the tie she'd put it in that morning — he would have to contend with the fact that her hand on his arm was the most peaceful his body had felt in seven years, and he was not going to contend with that. Not now. Not while she was holding him and writing left-handed and neither of them was naming what this was.

"Gradual release test," she said. "I'm going to reduce contact incrementally. Tell me the moment the pain returns."

She lifted her palm, leaving only her fingers on the Mark. No pain. She curled her fingers back one at a time. Ring finger, middle finger. No pain. Just her index finger and thumb on the edge of the Mark.

"Still zero?"

"Still zero."

She lifted her thumb. One finger. A single point of contact on the serpent's tail.

There. Not the full ache — a whisper of it. A suggestion.

"One," he said. "Maybe less."

She pressed down slightly, increasing pressure without increasing surface area, and the suggestion retreated.

"Pressure component," she murmured, making a note. "Not just contact area. There's a —" She stopped. Shook her head. "I need to think about this."

She hadn't taken her finger off the Mark.

He waited.

She seemed to realize, all at once, the posture they'd arrived at — him leaned back, arm extended across the table, her leaned forward with one finger pressed to his forearm like she was marking a passage in a text — and something shifted in her expression. Not embarrassment. Something adjacent to it, something that registered the shape of what they were doing without having a name for it. She pulled her hand back.

The pain returned fully within ten seconds. He breathed through it. Baseline. The thing he carried. Fine.

"Thank you," she said, standing, gathering her notes. Her movements were precise and slightly too quick, the way they got when she was processing something she wasn't going to discuss yet. "I need to cross-reference this with the ambient resonance literature. There's a text in the east stacks that —"

"Granger."

She paused at the door.

He didn't know what he'd been going to say. Something about the pain stopping. The quality of its absence. That her hand on his arm had achieved what no spell or potion or Healer's incantation had managed in seven years. That the darkest magic anyone had ever put in him went quiet when she touched it, and neither of them knew why, and not knowing should have frightened him more than it did.

He said: "Your notes. They're upside down."

She looked at the notepad in her hand, flipped it, and left without another word.

He sat in the chair for a long time. The frost had built a white border around the window and the light had shifted to the darker grey that meant mid-afternoon in December, the day nearly done at barely three o'clock. His arm ached. The Mark was restless again, the magic probing its dead channels, a phantom trying to complete a circuit that no longer existed.

He pressed his own hand over the Mark. Held it there. Nothing happened. The pain didn't change. His hand was just his hand, and the Mark didn't care about it.

He sat with this. Her hand, and the pain went. His hand, and nothing. There was something in that specificity that he wasn't going to name but that he couldn't quite ignore, either. The Mark — the thing the Dark Lord had branded into him at sixteen, the magic that had been meant to bind him to a dead man's will — recognized her. Not her magic, or not only her magic. Her. The distinction mattered and he didn't know why yet.

The writing desk against the wall held a stack of parchment and an inkwell that had dried years ago. A crack ran through the desk's surface from one end to the other, the wood splitting along its grain. He'd been meaning to fix it or transfigure it or simply move it out, and he'd done none of these things because the room hadn't mattered until she needed it for the examinations, and now it mattered because she was in it three times a week with her hands on his arm, and he was thinking about this too much.

He rolled his sleeve down and went to find her.

***

The library was where she was. Always the library. In the weeks since her arrival, the vast room had become hers more thoroughly than the guest wing the Manor had assigned her. Her organizational system — colour-coded flags, charmed index cards, a fleet of expanding notebooks that seemed to breed when unobserved — had claimed three of the six main reading tables and was encroaching on the fourth. He'd stopped objecting. His table was the one nearest the south window, where the light was best and where she'd left him a cup of cold tea that morning with a note charmed to the handle: *Preservation charm on the Avery folio is failing. Handle with clean cotton.*

He pocketed the note. The previous one had been about an inconsistency in a ley-line construction timeline. Before that, a correction to his translation of sixteenth-century Malfoy marginalia. He was keeping them because they were useful research artifacts and for no other reason, and the fact that he'd started checking the cup each morning the way you'd check for post was an observation he declined to make.

She was cross-legged on the floor between two shelf units, three books around her, the upside-down notepad replaced by a fresh one. She didn't look up when he came in. He sat at his table and opened the text he'd been working through — early Malfoy estate-binding rituals, the archaic notation dense and fussy — and they worked.

The corridor from the sitting room to the library had been short today. Some days it stretched — the Manor adding alcoves and side passages that hadn't been there the morning before, the house's architecture in slow conversation with itself. Today the house had contracted the route, four turns reduced to two, as if it wanted him in the library quickly. He hadn't fought it. He'd stopped fighting most of the Manor's architectural suggestions, partly because resistance was futile and partly because the house's suggestions were usually right, and partly because admitting that the house was usually right was easier than admitting what it was usually right about.

Forty minutes. The productive silence that had become their default: pages turning, quills scratching, the occasional murmured half-question. "The Flemish derivation here — is the root 'vastmaken' or are they using it metaphorically?" "Metaphorical. Look at the compound two lines down." "Mm." "Wait, no. Look at the next page. They're using it both ways." "Hm. Yeah. That's — that's interesting, actually." Back to the text. The silence closing over the exchange.

He'd read for another ten minutes before he realized he'd been following the same paragraph three times, his attention snagging on the same sentence and losing its grip. The passage described how sympathetic resonance between a warded structure and its inhabitants could create — he glanced at the archaic notation, trying to render it in modern terms — a feedback loop. The building responds to the inhabitants, the inhabitants respond to the building, and the cycle reinforces itself until the structure and its people are, in some magical sense, calibrated to each other.

He put the text down and picked up a genealogical supplement instead. Safer material. Names and dates and inheritance records didn't remind him of anything.

It was good, though. The work. Her. The minutes when they shared the room and the silence between them was companionable rather than wary. He could admit that to himself without examining what the admission cost.

He was working through the genealogical supplement, a dry accounting of inheritance transfers, when she said his name. Not his name. The name she used.

"Malfoy."

Her voice was different. He looked up. She was standing at the far end of the room, near the east wall where the Victorian shelving ended and the stone was bare. She was staring at the wall.

He crossed the library, which took thirty seconds, and in those seconds the air changed — a draft that shouldn't have existed, the smell of stone dust and something older. Sealed parchment. Centuries of unbreathed air.

She was standing in front of a door.

There had never been a door there. He'd grown up in this library. He'd spent years of his childhood reading in the window seats along that wall, his back against stone that was solid and unbroken. The east wall was shelving from floor to ceiling on the north half and bare stone on the south, and there had never, in his memory or in any architectural plan he'd seen, been a door.

There was one now. Dark wood, older than the library's Victorian fittings, with hand-forged iron hardware gone black with age. The stone around it was fitted seamlessly — no cracks, no fresh mortar, no evidence of construction. The house hadn't added this door. The house had uncovered it.

"That's new," he said.

"I noticed."

They looked at each other. She was doing the thing she did when discovery and wariness competed for control of her hands — her fingers reaching toward the iron handle while her stance said she was braced for the door to be warded, hexed, or merely a wall pretending to be interesting.

"You want to open it."

"Don't you?"

He did. He reached past her — close enough that his arm nearly brushed hers, a proximity that was becoming habitual in ways he chose not to examine — and pulled the door open.

The room behind it was small. Four metres by five, maybe less. Low stone ceiling, no windows, the air cool and still and carrying the smell of a space that had been sealed for a very long time — not decay but stasis, the absence of time rather than its evidence. The walls were carved with deep stone alcoves rather than lined with shelves, and in them were scrolls, bound codices, loose folios that looked older than anything in the main collection. Older than the library. Possibly older than the current Manor.

Granger made a sound that was not quite a word. She was inside before he'd finished taking in the room, pulling a scroll from the nearest alcove with the particular care she used on texts that might fall apart under a rough hand. She unrolled it partway and her whole posture changed — the body orienting toward the text with a focus that excluded everything else in the room, including him.

"This is anchoring theory," she said. "Original documentation. Pre-Statute, at least — maybe fourteenth century. Look at the notation." She unrolled further. "This is source material. The main collection references these texts but doesn't contain them. This is — Malfoy, this is the primary literature on ley-line construction and maintenance. This is what we've been looking for."

He stepped into the room and felt the house. Not its thoughts — the Manor didn't think in any way he could translate — but its attention. The stone under his feet was warm. The air in the archive was easier to breathe than the library's permanent staleness, as if the preservation was of a different order — not the slow losing battle against rot that the main collection fought but something more deliberate, the air itself held in place.

He pulled a folio from the nearest alcove on his side. The parchment was thick and stiff, hand-made, the ink faded to a brown that was still perfectly legible. Malfoy family notation — he could read parts of it, the letter-forms ancestral versions of the system his father had used in his own records. Estate-binding protocols. The procedures his ancestors had used to tie the Manor's magic to the family bloodline, written in a hand that was careful and professional and six hundred years dead.

"There's more," she said. She had a codex down now, heavy leather cracked with age, and she was turning pages with the kind of reverence that would have looked performative from anyone else. "Ambient field measurements. Fourteenth or fifteenth century. They were monitoring the magical substrate. They knew it could degrade." She looked up at him, and the expression on her face was pure unguarded want — not for him, for the knowledge, for the answer to the question that had brought her to this house — and it was close enough to another kind of want that something caught in his chest, which he ignored. "This room is everything."

"I need the mapping kit," he said. "For the cataloguing protocol. It's in the main stacks."

He stepped out of the archive into the library.

Three paces. That was how far he got before the sound started — stone against stone, low and enormous, the grind of architecture in motion. He turned.

The doorway was narrowing. The door hung open but the stone frame was contracting, the walls of the archive pressing inward. Through the shrinking gap he could see Granger, frozen, the codex in her hands, watching the walls move toward her.

"Malfoy —"

He stepped back in. The contraction stopped. The walls steadied. The doorway held at its original width as if nothing had happened.

Silence.

"Step out again," she said.

"What?"

"I need to see if it's you specifically or either of us leaving."

He stepped into the library. The grinding started immediately — the house learning its own rule, or enforcing it faster. He stepped back in. Stopped.

"My turn," she said. She put the codex down with a care that cost her visible effort and walked past him through the doorway. Three seconds into the library, the ceiling began to drop, the stone alcoves compressing, the scrolls shifting on their shelves. She came back and the room held.

"Both of us," she said. "The room requires both."

"One more test." He was already moving. "Both leave. See if it comes back."

They stepped into the library together. Behind them, the grinding — louder this time, the room contracting fast and sure. The doorway narrowed to a slit and closed, and the wall was bare stone again. Seamless. No door. No crack. No evidence the room had existed ten seconds ago.

They stood in front of blank wall.

"Touch the stone," he said.

She put her palm flat against the wall. Nothing. He put his beside hers. Nothing. They stood with their hands on cold stone and the room was gone.

Then — slowly, the way the house did things when it wanted the process to be felt — warmth under their palms. A vibration in the stone, faint enough to be blood in their own fingers. The outline of the door emerging like something drawn in fog, the iron hardware materializing, the dark wood forming grain by grain until the door was there again and they were both touching it and the smell of sealed air crept through the seams.

He opened it. The archive was intact. The scrolls on their shelves, the codex where she'd set it down, the air unchanged. The room had waited.

"Both present to open," she said. "Both present to hold it. The house is gating access on proximity."

They went back in. The room held.

He leaned against the stone wall. Cool against his back, the surface slightly rough, the texture of hand-cut stone. The archive was quiet — deeper than the library's quiet, the silence of a room that had not heard voices in a very long time and was adjusting to the intrusion.

He thought about his father, briefly and without wanting to. Lucius had used the library every day for years — Draco had childhood memories of his father at the long table, surrounded by correspondence and financial ledgers, the family's business conducted in the room that now belonged to Granger's research. Lucius had never mentioned a hidden archive. Had never mentioned a room behind the east wall, or texts older than the main collection. Either his father hadn't known — which would mean the house had kept this from him too — or he had known and hadn't shared it, which would be entirely in character but raised its own questions about what the house had shown Lucius and under what conditions.

"It knows," he said.

She looked up from where she'd already reclaimed the codex. "What?"

"What we need. The anchoring texts, the decay documentation — the house had all of it, behind a door it never showed me. I've been in this library my whole life." He heard his own voice and didn't like what was in it. "It shows this room now. Today. After —" He didn't finish. After you touched me and the pain stopped. After whatever happened between your hand and my Mark. He wasn't going to say that.

She waited. She was good at waiting when the waiting served her.

"The house opened a corridor for you the first week," he said instead. "Rearranged the shelves. That was hospitality. Preference, maybe. This is different. Both of us, or the room closes. That's not a preference. That's a condition."

"I know."

"It's a requirement. Proximity as a condition of access. The house is making rules about us being in the same room. As a price for what's in here."

"Yes."

"Doesn't that —" He stopped, recalibrated. "Does that not concern you?"

She set the codex down. "Of course it concerns me. A sentient building just showed us that it gates irreplaceable research based on whether we're both physically present. That's not a quirk. That's a policy. That's the house making a structural claim about our togetherness."

The word sat in the room. Neither of them touched it.

"I don't want the house deciding what I can reach in my own library," he said, and the edge in his voice was harder than he'd intended. "I grew up here. I've spent twenty-five years in this building. It never showed me this room. It shows it now, to both of us, with a lock that only opens when we're standing next to each other. That's not hospitality. That's —"

He stopped himself. The sentence he'd been constructing had something honest in it about what the proximity requirement felt like — intimate and imposed, the house treating their closeness as a kind of currency — and he wasn't going to let it land.

"It's a manipulation."

"Maybe," she said. "Or it's a lock, and we're the key. Not the house choosing to manipulate — the house built this way. Like a ward that requires two signatures."

"A ward doesn't have preferences. A ward doesn't open corridors for one person and rearrange shelves for another."

She acknowledged this with a slight movement of her head. Not agreement. Acknowledgement that he'd said something she couldn't dismiss.

"We need what's in this room," she said.

"I know."

"These anchoring texts — if the decay is caused by war damage to the original infrastructure, this is the only record of how that infrastructure was built. This is the only documentation anywhere. And it's behind a proximity gate that requires us both." She paused. He could see her working through the tangle and choosing which strand to pull. "We can object to the house's conditions and still work within them. Those aren't mutually exclusive."

He looked at her. Cross-legged on the stone floor of a hidden archive, surrounded by texts that could explain why British magic was dying, making the pragmatic case for accepting the terms of a sentient house that had decided their togetherness was a resource it could gate and spend. She was right. She was usually right. The fact that she was right didn't change what the house was doing, or what the proximity requirement meant, or the fact that his Mark had gone quiet under her hand an hour ago and the house had opened this room immediately after, and the connection between those two facts was legible to anyone willing to look at it.

Her hand on his Mark. The pain stopping. The house opening its oldest room.

He didn't say any of this.

"Fine," he said. "We work in here."

"Together."

"Obviously together. The room won't hold otherwise."

She picked up the codex. He pulled a scroll from the nearest alcove — the genealogical record he'd returned to, the estate-binding protocols in notation he could mostly read. He sat down across from her. The room was small enough that his knee was near hers, and if either of them shifted the distance between them would close to nothing, and the distance was the same distance her hand had crossed that morning to reach his arm.

She kicked off her shoes and tucked her socked feet beneath her. She was already into the text, murmuring about anchor-point degradation patterns and ambient field tolerances, making notes in her fresh notebook. He watched her mouth a word in what sounded like medieval Latin, try it again, shake her head, and write it down with a question mark. He unrolled his own scroll and tried to read.

The estate-binding protocols were dense and procedural — instructions for rituals that hadn't been performed in centuries, requiring components and conditions that no longer existed. But threaded through the procedural language were observations about the house itself. The ancestor who'd written this — a Malfoy whose name he didn't recognize, someone from before the family started keeping formal records — had described the Manor as *attendant*. The house attends. It watches. It maintains awareness of who is within its walls and adjusts itself accordingly. The specific word the ancestor had used, in the archaic notation, was closer to *listens* than to *watches*, but neither translation was exact. The house perceived its inhabitants through some sense that didn't map onto human categories, and it responded.

Six hundred years ago, a Malfoy had sat in this room and written about the house doing exactly what the house was doing now. The knowledge should have been reassuring — this was precedented, understood, documented. It wasn't reassuring. It meant the house had been doing this for centuries, shaping the conditions of its inhabitants' lives according to its own assessment of what was needed, and the inhabitants had always been inside the shaping whether they chose it or not.

He read. She read. The archive room was cool but the stone where they sat was warming — the house seeping heat through the floor, through the walls, the building doing the thing it did when it was satisfied with the arrangement of its inhabitants. Her foot nudged his ankle as she shifted position and she didn't seem to notice, and he noticed, and the Mark on his arm ached in the old familiar way, and her hand was three feet from his arm and not touching it, and the house held the room open around them and waited.

The winter dark came down outside windows they couldn't see. The archive had no daylight to lose and no darkness to gain, just its own steady sourceless glow, and in that glow they read, and the house held the room around them — stable, warm, watchful — and the distance between them did not change, and neither of them tried to change it, and the house did not explain what it was doing, because it had never explained anything to him, and he had stopped expecting it to.


# Chapter 7

The room had not been there yesterday.

Hermione was fairly certain of this. She'd walked this corridor twice the previous evening — once to the library and once back — and the door on the left had been a linen closet. She'd opened it by mistake, looking for the bathroom, and found shelves of folded towels and a smell of cedar. Now the door was taller by a foot, dark-paneled, and it opened onto a sitting room she had never seen.

South-facing windows. Two chairs angled toward a low fire. A tea service on a table between them. The winter light was grey and thin, the frost on the garden visible through glass that carried no condensation, which meant heating charms, which meant the room had been prepared. For whom was answered by the woman already occupying one of the chairs.

"Ms. Granger." Narcissa Malfoy did not rise. "Do sit down."

Hermione stood in the doorway a moment longer than was natural, cataloguing: the tea already poured, two cups, milk in neither. The fire steady and low, the kind that burned without consuming its fuel. Narcissa in a grey winter cloak she had not removed, though the room was warm enough. And the room itself — the proportions, the height of the windows, the slight asymmetry of the fireplace surround — looking as if it had occupied this corridor for centuries, not assembled overnight from the house's private reserves.

She sat. She picked up the cup Narcissa had poured for her. The tea was strong, black, unfamiliar in a way that spoke of a blend someone kept specifically for themselves.

"I didn't know you were coming today," Hermione said.

"I did not announce myself." Narcissa's cup rested in her lap, held with both hands. Her fingers were long and bare of jewelry except for a ring on her left hand — gold, plain, the kind of ring that becomes invisible through decades of wear. Her hands were the only part of her that looked her age: veined at the backs, the knuckles slightly swollen, a woman's hands that had done more work than the rest of her composure suggested. "Draco is addressing the ventilation charms in the east wing. He will be occupied for some time."

The precision of this — Narcissa knowing where her son was, knowing how long the task would take, having arrived specifically when he would be elsewhere — was not subtle. Hermione could appreciate the craftsmanship of it even as she recognized she was being managed.

"You want to talk to me alone," she said.

"I thought that would be more productive."

Hermione drank the tea. It was better than she'd expected — the bitterness clean, not stale, the kind of black tea that improved by being left too strong. Outside, the garden lay under frost, the hedges dark, the gravel paths white-grey, the bare trees holding their shapes against a sky that would darken by half past three. Early November had tipped into December while she wasn't paying attention. The days had shortened around her and the Manor's warmth had absorbed the change, so that the outside cold felt increasingly theoretical, a thing that happened to other buildings.

"I want to ask you about the house," she said.

"I assumed you would."

Narcissa's gaze moved to the window, then returned. "The house has been busy since my last visit. The corridor outside your guest room has shortened by a third — it used to run the length of the east facade. And there is a door near the main staircase that was not there when I came for tea in October."

"I've been keeping a log of the changes," Hermione said.

"Of course you have." The tone was not condescending — it was the brief acknowledgement of one careful woman toward another. "I kept one as well, when I first moved in. A full inventory of every shift, every new door, every corridor that changed direction between morning and evening. It lasted eight months before the record became longer than was useful and I admitted that the log was for my sense of control, not for accuracy."

She smoothed her cloak over her lap — not fussiness but the need to have her hands occupied while she said difficult things. Outside, a bird landed on the frozen hedge and left again immediately, finding nothing.

"Last week we found a room in the library that opens only when both of us are present. It destabilizes if one of us leaves. The house is gating access to research material based on who's in the room." She paused, making sure the next part was precise. "Based on both of us being in the room together."

"Yes. Draco mentioned something of this."

"He told you?"

"He told me very little. I asked questions he could not avoid answering." Narcissa's mouth moved — not a smile, but the acknowledgement that a smile might exist in the vicinity. "He has not yet learned how transparent he becomes when he is trying to be opaque."

This was said with the warmth of a mother describing her child's faults and finding them, on balance, endurable. Hermione noted it and set it aside. Narcissa's love for Draco was useful information, not because it could be leveraged but because it explained the architecture of what she would and wouldn't say.

"Has the house done this before?" Hermione asked. "Gated rooms based on who's present?"

Narcissa drank from her cup. She set it down on the table between them — the motion exact, the cup placed so the handle pointed toward Hermione — and folded her hands in her lap.

"The Manor was built by Aurelius Malfoy in 1247," she said. "Though built is generous. Initiated, perhaps. The original construction has been revised, expanded, damaged, and rebuilt so many times that I doubt a single stone from Aurelius's work remains. But the foundational enchantments are his, or derive from his. The house sits on them the way a cathedral sits on its crypt."

This was Narcissa being precise, not digressing. Hermione recognized the approach — her own, when she was building toward a conclusion that required scaffolding.

"Aurelius intended the house to sustain his line," Narcissa continued. "Not shelter it. Sustain it. The distinction matters. A shelter protects what is already present. A sustaining structure..." She paused, and the pause was selection, not hesitation. "Cultivates."

"Cultivates what?"

"Whatever it determines the line requires. Magical strength. Useful alliances. Productive associations." The word associations was chosen over something else. Hermione could hear the gap where a more specific word had been considered and set aside. "The house has a mandate, Ms. Granger. It reads the people inside it. It responds to what it finds between them. It creates conditions."

"And gating the archive room — that's part of the conditions."

"The house rewards what serves its mandate. It withdraws access from what does not."

Hermione set her cup down. The china was thin enough that she could feel the temperature changing through the ceramic, the tea cooling in real time. She was aware that Narcissa was watching her hands and not looking away.

"You lived in this house for twenty-five years," she said.

"I did."

"And the house — cultivated things. Between you and Lucius."

Something recalibrated in Narcissa's posture. Not a flinch — Narcissa Malfoy did not flinch — but a shift of weight, a tightening of the hands in her lap. She touched her cuff. Adjusted it. The motion was small and automatic, the gesture of someone who had been controlling her responses for so long that the control itself had become a tell.

"When I first arrived — nineteen years old, married three weeks — the Manor was very attentive." Her voice had dropped register, losing the lecture quality, settling into something that sounded more like memory than instruction. "Rooms I had never been told about appeared in corridors I walked every day. The library opened sections that had not been accessible to Lucius. The house would arrange the architecture so that we — met. Unexpectedly. In corners and corridors and rooms that hadn't been there an hour before. The Manor was invested in what we might become to each other."

"Invested how?"

"Invested in the way a gardener is invested in two plants sharing the same bed. Whether the plants wish to be there is a secondary concern."

"That's a metaphor."

"Yes. The reality is less tidy." Narcissa picked up her cup, found it empty, set it down. The empty cup remained on the saucer with the precision of someone who never rattled anything by accident. "The house created conditions. Warmth. Proximity. Access. For a time, the Manor was the most generous version of itself — every room golden, every corridor the right length, the library opening further than it had in decades. And eventually, it stopped."

"Stopped."

"Stopped investing. The rooms returned to their fixed positions. The library sealed the sections it had opened. The corridors stopped rearranging themselves. The Manor — settled." She turned the ring on her finger, one slow deliberate rotation. "Lucius preferred the house that way. Predictable. He said the shifting made him irritable. I do not think he noticed when it ended, or if he did, what he felt was relief."

She did not say loss. The word was present in the space where she had not placed it.

"Mrs. Malfoy—"

"I am telling you this," Narcissa said, and the composure was not a mask now but a frame, the thing that held her together rather than the thing that concealed her, "because the house has not stopped for you. In twenty-five years, the Manor never gated a room behind both my presence and Lucius's together. Not once. Whatever this house is doing with you and my son, it is doing something it did not do for us."

The conversation shifted. Hermione felt it — the dynamic moving, the careful economy of information that Narcissa had been managing suddenly inadequate, because this was not strategic disclosure. This was a woman in her fifties telling a woman in her twenties that the house had tried with her marriage and given up. That whatever it was doing now, with Hermione and Draco, it was trying harder than it had ever tried with her.

"What does that mean?" The question came out stripped of Hermione's usual qualifiers, bare.

"I do not know." For the first time, Narcissa sounded as though she was telling the complete truth. "I know the house's mandate is to sustain the line. I know it reads the people inside it and responds to what it finds. I know it creates conditions and gates access. But I do not know the limits of what it will do, or the mechanism by which it chooses." She stopped. Chose her next sentence with the care of someone setting a stone in mortar. "And I do not know whether what it cultivates would exist without the cultivation."

This was the question Hermione had been circling since the gated room — since before the gated room, since the corridors started shifting for her and the library started rearranging its shelves. Hearing Narcissa articulate it, the older woman's voice careful and almost gentle, made the thing solid in a way it hadn't been before. A named doubt was heavier than an unnamed one.

"The house is not malicious," Narcissa said. "I lived here for twenty-five years and I want to be clear. It did not harm me. It did not harm Draco. But it is not neutral." She straightened in her chair — not standing, not yet, but preparing to. "It has preferences. It acts on them. And you should understand that what you feel inside these walls may not be entirely separable from the walls themselves. That is not an accusation, Ms. Granger. It is a fact about where you are living."

"What would you advise?"

Narcissa stood. The motion was unhurried, the kind of standing-up that someone learned to do elegantly before they learned to read. Her gloves lay on the arm of the chair — grey leather, lined — and she picked them up with a deliberation that closed the conversation.

"I do not advise. I do not have the standing to advise you. I am neither pardoned nor convicted — I live in a rented house in Knightsbridge because the Ministry cannot decide whether my war service was treason or not, and until they decide, I am provisional." She pulled on one glove, then the other, smoothing the leather over her fingers with the attention of someone dressing for an audience with no one. "But my son has been alone in this house for three years, and whatever the Manor is doing, he is less alone than he was. I prefer that to the alternative. I prefer it considerably."

This was the warmth — not diffuse, not general, but targeted and precise, the love of a mother who had calculated the risks and decided that Hermione Granger in her son's house was a risk worth tolerating, even welcoming, provided Hermione understood what she was standing inside.

"One more thing," Hermione said.

Narcissa waited. One hand on the doorframe. Her nails were short and unpainted, a practical detail that caught Hermione off guard for no reason she could name.

"When the house stopped — with you and Lucius. What was that like? Afterward?"

The question was too intimate. Hermione knew it as she asked it. But Narcissa had come here to be honest within her chosen boundaries, and this question fell inside them. Just.

"The house became ordinary architecture," Narcissa said. "The rooms stayed where they were. The walls did not listen. It was quieter." She adjusted her cuff again — the same small motion, the tell she could not fully suppress. "Lucius preferred it."

"Did you?"

Narcissa's face was composed, held. Underneath, the expression of someone who had answered this question for herself many times and never once aloud.

"The house tells you what it thinks of your marriage," she said. "Whether you wish to hear it or not."

She left. Hermione heard her footsteps on the stone corridor — unhurried, even, the stride of someone who knew these halls in the dark. The sitting room remained. The fire continued. The two cups sat on the table between them, one empty and one cold.

---

Hermione stayed in the room for ten minutes after Narcissa left, drinking cold tea and watching the frost thicken on the garden hedges.

She wanted to catalogue what she'd heard. Break it into categories, apply the analytical framework that had carried her through every previous crisis of comprehension. Facts: the Manor's foundational enchantments were designed to sustain the Malfoy line. The house read the people inside it. It responded, conditioned, arranged. It had gated rooms for Hermione and Draco that it had never gated for Narcissa and Lucius in twenty-five years of marriage. The house was more invested in whatever was happening here than it had been in a marriage that produced the heir it was supposedly designed to protect.

But the facts had gaps, and the gaps were Narcissa's, deliberately shaped. She had described what the house did — conditions, access, investment — without explaining the mechanism. She had said the house cultivated without saying what cultivation produced. She had described her own marriage's trajectory with the house as a parable — attentive, then withdrawn — but had not said why the house gave up. Whether it was because the marriage lacked something the house wanted, or because Narcissa and Lucius had refused something the house offered, or because the connection between them had simply not been what the house was looking for.

The distinction mattered. If the house withdrew because the raw material wasn't there — if it tried and found nothing between them worth sustaining — that was one kind of story. If Narcissa and Lucius had actively resisted the house's conditions, and the house had eventually stopped trying, that was a different story entirely. Narcissa had not told her which one it was, and the omission was too precise to be accidental.

She wanted this to be data. It wasn't. It was a woman in grey gloves telling her that the house had stopped trying with her own marriage and then watching it begin again with someone else.

The cup was cold in her hands. She set it down, stood, and went to find the library.

Draco was already at their shared worktable, the ventilation charms apparently handled. He'd spread out a stack of texts he'd carried from the gated archive before it sealed — the room closed without both of them, but its contents, once physically removed, stayed in the main library. He had his cotton gloves on and was annotating in pencil, his handwriting cramped and precise.

"Your mother was here," Hermione said.

He didn't look up. "I know."

"She wanted to talk to me alone."

"I know that too." He made a note in the margin of whatever he was reading. "She does that. You don't have to report."

The exchange was brief and did not pretend to be more. He didn't ask what Narcissa had said. She didn't volunteer it. This was the register they'd settled into over the past weeks — functional, workmanlike, with a dry undercurrent that could on good days pass for warmth. She'd noticed, over the weeks of shared work, that he was most at ease when the conversation was about the research — his attention sharpening rather than his guard, his opinions about runic notation offered with a directness he never managed about anything personal. The difference was legible, and she found herself calibrating to it, steering toward the texts when the alternative was silence or the kind of careful circling that neither of them was good at.

She watched him annotate for a moment. He was left-handed — the pencil held in the deliberate grip of someone taught to write with his right who had eventually refused. His left sleeve was pushed to the elbow, the Mark visible on his forearm, dark and motionless, the snake and skull ink-sharp against skin that rarely saw sunlight. She looked at the Mark and then at the cramped, precise handwriting and then at the text he was studying, and did not let herself think about Narcissa in the sitting room, about the house reading what was between the people inside it.

"The archive?" she asked.

"Whenever you're ready."

They walked to the gated room together. The library was vast enough that the walk took two minutes, past shelves that climbed to the ceiling and rolled ladders Hermione had taken to using with a recklessness that she would have found alarming in a student. The door to the archive appeared as they approached — she'd stopped being startled by this — and the room opened with its particular quality of having been waiting for them. The air inside was warm and carried the smell of old vellum and the faint mineral scent of preservation charms working on material that was centuries older than the spells designed to preserve it.

The ledger she'd been translating was where she'd left it, open on the broad oak table to the section recording Tiberius Malfoy's field work from 1364. Draco took his usual seat. She took hers, across from him, close enough to pass pages between them. He kept his cotton gloves on. She wore one and left the other off — the left hand bare so she could grip a pencil, the right gloved against the vellum. This had become habitual without discussion, the way routines do when two people share a workspace long enough.

The notation system that Tiberius had used was dense — Latin prose with runic modifiers functioning like mathematical operators, each glyph changing the meaning of the base text in ways that were systematic but not immediately intuitive. Draco read the Latin. Hermione decoded the runic syntax. They passed pages, annotated on separate sheets, argued about root forms in a dead scholarly language that neither of them could have read alone.

At one point he passed her a page with a runic compound she'd never encountered — the base form looked like a standard preservation glyph, but an additional stroke changed the root meaning.

"I don't know this one," she said.

"Family variant." He reached to a shelf behind him and produced a separate sheet of vellum she hadn't noticed, covered in annotations in a different hand, centuries newer than Tiberius's. "The Malfoys modified the standard runic system for their own records. My grandfather kept a key." He set the sheet between them. "This one means sustained duration — not preserved as in static, but sustained as in actively fed. The magic required regular renewal."

She studied Abraxas Malfoy's handwriting — ornate but legible, the hand of a man taught penmanship as moral discipline. The key decoded fifteen family-specific variants, each a departure from the standard system that would have been invisible to any outside researcher.

"You didn't mention this before," she said.

"I didn't know where he'd left it." A pause. "The shelf it was on wasn't there yesterday."

She could have pressed this. The house surfacing a reference key at exactly the moment they needed it was either convenient coincidence or the Manor doing what Narcissa had described — investing, cultivating, rewarding the work that served its mandate. She let it go. The work mattered more than the question of who was directing them toward it.

They worked in the focused quiet of collaboration that had found its rhythm — when to speak and when to let the other think. She passed him a page. He returned it with three annotations. She disagreed with two and was right about one, and they spent five minutes arguing about a verb form before she conceded that his reading was more consistent with the earlier entries. This was the part she hadn't expected: not the research itself but the pleasure of the mind across the table. He was precise where she was connective, careful where she was willing to leap. They were better together than apart, which was a fact about their complementary training or another thing the house was arranging, and in the warm archive with Narcissa's words still turning in her head, she could not separate the two.

"This isn't possessive," Hermione said, pointing to a glyph clustered in the margin of a construction entry. "Look at the stem. It's got the temporal marker underneath. He's saying the anchoring was maintained, not that it was owned."

"Maintained by whom?"

"That's what the next entry should tell us."

It did. The next page documented a specific site — described in Latin with enough topographical precision that Hermione could match it to modern geography — where Tiberius Malfoy had performed a series of sustaining rituals on what he called a radix. A root-point. A place where magic was anchored to the physical landscape, driven into the earth and bound there by enchantments that were meant to hold for centuries.

"There's another one here," Draco said, three pages further in. "Severn valley. And here — this one's near what would be the Devon coast."

"How many?"

"At least twelve so far. He's documenting a circuit."

They mapped it on a blank sheet, marking each location as the ledger yielded it. The anchor points formed a rough network spanning southern and central England — places where the foundational magic that sustained the ambient field was pinned to geography. The Salisbury plain. Glastonbury. The Welsh marches. A point in London that corresponded to the approximate location of what was now the Ministry of Magic. And Godric's Hollow — the name leaping from the page in Tiberius's careful Latin, and Hermione's pencil stopping on the paper.

"Godric's Hollow," she said.

"I see it."

"The battle sites. Both wars. The amount of magical combat that happened at or near these locations—"

"Would have damaged the anchor points." Draco's voice had gone careful, precise — the register he used when he was following a line of reasoning faster than he wanted to show. "If they're real. If they're still functional."

"They were functional when Tiberius documented them six hundred years ago. The question is whether anyone maintained them between then and now."

"Given that no one appears to know they exist, I'd say not."

They looked at the map they'd drawn. Twelve points, roughly evenly distributed, connected by what Tiberius described as filum — threads of sustaining magic running between the anchor points and maintaining the ambient field across the territory between them. The infrastructure underlying all of British wizarding magic, built or at minimum documented by a Malfoy ancestor six centuries ago. Unmaintained for most of that time. Sitting directly beneath locations where some of the heaviest magical combat in modern history had taken place.

"This is what's failing," Hermione said. "The ambient decay isn't random degradation. It's structural. The anchor points were damaged — probably during the first war and again during the second — and without maintenance, the damage has been propagating through the network. The filum are degrading. The whole system is coming apart."

"And the only documentation of the system—"

"Is in this library. In this room."

The archive hummed around them. The warm air. The faint vibration in the walls that Hermione had felt on each visit and still could not classify as mechanical or magical. The house was holding them in the one space that contained what the wizarding world needed, and gating that access behind their togetherness.

Draco leaned back. His left hand went to his right forearm — the Mark, bothering him, doing the restless low-grade ache it did between examinations. She could feel it. The pressure behind her own sternum that mapped to the Mark's activity, not pain exactly but the shadow of pain, the way you could feel a sound too low to hear. She filed this under things she was choosing not to examine yet, which was a file that was getting thicker by the day.

"The research isn't optional," she said. "This isn't historical curiosity. If these anchor points can be identified, assessed, potentially repaired — this could be the answer to the decay. The whole decay."

"And you need these texts to do it."

"We need these texts. You can read the Latin. I can read the runes. Neither of us can do both."

He held her gaze across the table, the ledger open between them. Something crossed his face that was not the careful formality, not the dry deflection — something less rehearsed, an acknowledgement of what she was saying and what she was not. That she was here. That she would continue to be here. That whatever the house was doing and whatever his mother had come to say about it, the work demanded their proximity, and the work was not a thing either of them could afford to walk away from.

"Right," he said. "Well. Back to Tiberius, then."

They bent over the ledger. The archive held steady around them, warm and humming, and the work continued.

---

She lay in bed that night and listened to the house.

The Manor made sounds after midnight — not the creaks and groans of old construction settling under temperature changes, though it made those too. Underneath was something else, a rhythm that rose and fell with the slow regularity of breath, if breath could come from stone and plaster and the spaces between walls. Hermione had been sleeping in this house for weeks and she still hadn't decided whether the sound was unsettling or, worse, comforting. Whether she was getting used to it or whether the house was getting used to her.

Her room was steady. The house had been consistent about this — her room in the guest wing, the path to the library, the bathroom down the corridor. These things stayed where they were. Small courtesies, she'd started thinking. A word that implied an intelligence behind the giving.

Two floors above and to the east, Draco was awake.

She knew this the way she'd known it for the past week and a half — a low hum of awareness at the edge of her attention, something she could tune into or ignore but could not turn off. The resonance. She'd been documenting it in her research notes under the heading Diagnostic Residue: Mark-Adjacent Sensory Artifacts, which sounded clinical enough to be reassuring and was becoming less accurate by the day. It wasn't residue. Residue dissipated. This was building.

She could feel the Mark's restlessness translated into her own body — not pain but the awareness of pain, a shadow sensation pressing behind her sternum like a hand held flat against a door. She could distinguish, now, between the Mark's active spikes — sharp and sudden, a match struck in the dark — and its baseline unease, the cursed magic moving restlessly through channels that no longer connected to anything alive. She could tell when he got up and paced, because the signal shifted, became kinetic and irregular. She could tell when he settled, standing or sitting, because the resonance went dense and still.

She knew his patterns of insomnia. She hadn't asked for this knowledge and she could not give it back.

Narcissa's words sat with her in the dark. The house reads the people inside it. It responds to what it finds between them. What you feel inside these walls may not be entirely separable from the walls themselves.

The resonance had been faint when it started, those first days after the initial Mark examination — easy to rationalize, easy to file under known artifacts of sustained spell-contact with cursed objects. Three separate reference texts described the phenomenon. She'd reread those passages multiple times, looking for the clinical framework that would make this manageable. But the texts described residual effects that faded with time and distance. What she had was not fading. Each examination deepened it. Each day in the house made the signal more specific, more detailed, more intimate.

She was carrying information about his body that he hadn't consented to share. His sleeplessness, his pain, the rhythm of his nights. She knew things about his private hours that he didn't know she knew, and she kept this knowledge the way she kept the scar under her sleeve — hidden, habitual, a concealment she maintained because the alternative was a conversation she didn't know how to begin.

She turned on her side. The pillow was too soft — a detail she kept meaning to address in daylight and kept forgetting, because in daylight the house's attentions seemed less personal, more architectural. The sheets were warm. The room was quiet, except for the house's slow breathing underneath the silence.

Above her, two floors east, the resonance shifted. He'd gotten up. She could picture it — though she'd never seen his room, had no reason to know its layout — the way he moved at night, restless and deliberate, the pacing that was not aimless but not purposeful either, the walking of someone who was trying to tire himself into sleep and not succeeding. She could feel the Mark flaring faintly with his movement, the dark magic in it stirred by the act of getting up, of being vertical and awake and alone in a house that never quite let him forget it.

She pressed her face into the too-soft pillow and tried to think about the anchor points, about Tiberius Malfoy's six-hundred-year-old field records, about the research that was important and real and not tangled up with the ache behind her sternum. The decay was structural. The infrastructure was damaged. The work was necessary. These were facts, clean and verifiable, the kind of facts she had built her professional life around.

But she was lying in a house that breathed, in a bed the house had warmed for her, feeling the insomnia of a man two floors away in her own chest, and the clinical vocabulary she'd built to contain this was not holding.

The anchor points. The filum. Tiberius Malfoy's six-hundred-year-old maintenance records and the damaged infrastructure that was killing ambient magic across Britain. She'd spent the afternoon and evening building the argument — the research case, the one she would present to the Department of Mysteries when she could get the data out of this library into a form the Ministry would accept. It was solid work. Important work. The kind of work she was built for, that justified the hours and the proximity and the fact that she was sleeping in a house that a woman in grey gloves had warned her not to trust.

But the resonance didn't care about the research. It cared about the body two floors above her, the Mark's dark magic restless and aching, the particular texture of his wakefulness at — she checked the clock on the nightstand — half past one in the morning. She'd started timing it. She knew that his worst nights ran past three. She knew that the Mark usually settled around four, as if the cursed magic exhausted itself and went dormant with the approach of dawn. She knew these things and she had told no one, and the knowing was becoming its own kind of intimacy, unwanted and ineradicable.

The house settled. The resonance hummed. Above her, Draco stopped pacing and was still.

She did not sleep for a long time.


# Chapter 8

She wanted to start early.

Something about a new calibration — she'd been refining it for three days, cross-referencing the diagnostic array against the archive texts, and that morning over tea she'd explained what she thought the deeper probe would show. The Mark's surface readings mapped the same instabilities the ambient field exhibited: degradation patterns, cyclical spikes, the magic trying to move through channels that no longer connected to anything. But beneath that surface, she believed, the Mark's original architecture was intact — the spellwork Voldemort had laid down, layered deep enough that years of dormancy and reactivation hadn't degraded it. If she could reach those deep layers, she might understand how the Mark's magic interfaced with the broader decay.

He'd said fine. He'd been saying fine to the examinations for weeks, the word worn smooth enough that neither of them questioned it anymore.

The room they used was a small study off the library's south corridor. Not the gated archive — that demanded both their presences just to remain accessible — but a quieter space: a writing desk, two straight-backed chairs, windows facing the east lawn that caught the morning light on the rare days there was light to catch. By half three on a November afternoon, the glass was already dark. The frost that had formed overnight had not thawed. She'd lit the sconces when they came in, a flick of her wand so automatic she probably didn't register it, and the candlelight pulled the room's dimensions inward, made it feel closer than it was.

He sat across from her and rolled his left sleeve to the elbow.

The gesture had lost its edges. The first time — weeks ago, in October, when the arrangement was new and the formality between them still had teeth — his jaw had locked so hard the ache persisted for hours. Not from the examination itself but from the exposure, the animal wrongness of laying bare the thing he spent every waking hour keeping covered. The Mark on the inside of his left forearm. The skull and serpent, faded to something between grey and black, the design's edges softened by years but the lines still legible, still unmistakably what they were. The ink-that-wasn't-ink shifting faintly under candlelight as if the magic underneath were breathing.

Now it was just a motion. Button, fold, forearm on the desk. The routine had overwritten the dread, mostly.

She reached for his arm.

Her hands were cool. They always were at the start — she'd mentioned once that diagnostic spellwork drew heat from the caster's extremities, redirected it, and the clinical explanation had made something easier for both of them, giving the cold of her fingers a reason that had nothing to do with nervousness, or care, or the particular temperature of a person's skin when they were concentrating very hard on being professional. She held his wrist with her left hand — her grip light, calibrated through weeks of repetition — and laid her right palm flat over the Mark.

"I'm going past the surface today," she said. "First two layers will feel the same as usual. After that it'll be different."

"Different how?"

"I don't know yet. That's the point."

He made a sound that wasn't quite agreement. She glanced up — the quick, assessing look she gave before each session, checking for something she never named. He kept his face neutral. He was good at neutral.

"Tell me when the pressure changes," she said. "And I mean actually tell me. Not the thing where you absorb it and I find out twenty minutes later because your pulse does something odd."

"My pulse is fine."

"Your pulse is a liar." She said it mildly, without looking up, already settling into the focus that preceded her spellwork. Her thumb was resting on the inside of his wrist. She could feel exactly what his pulse was doing, and they both knew it, and neither of them mentioned this.

The first diagnostic layer was familiar. A cool wash of her magic over the Mark's surface, methodical and thorough — reading the configuration, the density, the way the dark magic distributed itself across his forearm. He'd come to think of it as being scanned. His role was to sit still and let his mind go somewhere neutral, which was harder than it sounded when her thumb rested against the thin skin over his radial artery. He used to think about the library — the shelves, the spines, the organizing system she'd built. Lately the library made him think about her in the library, which was counterproductive.

He thought about the frost instead. The patterns on the windows, fractal and branching, each crystal following its own architecture. The house kept the interior warm enough, most days, but the study windows faced east and caught the worst of the cold, and the frost had been growing thicker as November deepened. He'd watched it form last week during another calibration, the ice spreading across the glass in patterns that looked designed, as if the cold were writing something the warm air would erase by morning.

The second layer was less comfortable. Her magic pressed deeper into the Mark's structure, the pressure changing from the impersonal wash of a surface scan to something more specific — a sustained probing, like a hand pressing into bruised muscle. The serpent shifted under his skin. He felt it the way you feel a twitch you can't consciously control, the dark magic rearranging itself in response to her probe, and the ache that lived perpetually in the Mark sharpened from its background hum to something closer to actual pain.

He didn't mention it. She'd pause and recalibrate if he mentioned it, and the recalibration would add twenty minutes and the extra time would be worse than the ache.

"Holding steady," she murmured. Talking to herself more than to him — the running commentary she maintained during spellwork, half-conscious, her attention on the magic rather than the words. "Layer two consistent with last week's readings. Decay-adjacent patterning, cyclical, same frequency as the ambient spikes in the Ministry's field reports..." Her brow pulled together. "Starting the deeper probe now. You'll feel it."

He felt it.

Not in the Mark — or not only in the Mark. The sensation began at the point of contact between her palm and his skin and then pushed past it, through the curse's architecture, into something underneath. Something older. As if her diagnostic magic had found a floor beneath the floor and was pressing through the boards. The ache in the Mark changed quality, sharpening from its usual restless throb into a point of pressure that felt less like pain and more like a lock being forced — something giving way that had not given way before.

His fingers curled against the desktop.

"That's—"

"I feel it." Her voice had changed. The clinical murmur gone, replaced with something tighter, more alert. "There's a substrate. Below the curse matrix — compressed magical residue, layered. I think it's from the original casting. The signature's older than the reactivation, it's—"

The Mark moved.

Not the slow undulation he was used to during sustained diagnostic contact. This was a lurch — sudden, violent, the dark magic inside the Mark rolling over like something that had been sleeping at the bottom of a well and had felt light on its face for the first time in nine years. He felt it in his forearm and his shoulder and his chest, a wave of wrongness that started at the Mark and radiated outward, and her hand on his wrist tightened involuntarily, her fingers pressing into his skin—

And the room went away.

---

He was sixteen.

He knew this with the body's certainty, not the mind's — not because he could see his own face but because his bones knew what sixteen felt like, the particular way the body sits inside itself at that age, too tall and too thin, the joints not yet settled into their adult configuration. He was standing. The floor under his feet was stone, and the stone was cold through his shoes, and the room was his father's study.

Except it wasn't his father's anymore.

The heavy curtains were drawn against a July evening. The desk — carved oak, enormous, the desk he'd hidden under as a child, curled between the pedestal legs during dinner parties, listening to boots and laughter and the clink of glasses above — was bare except for a single candle and a length of black cloth. The bookshelves had been emptied. The portraits that had hung on the far wall were gone — the frames remained, dark rectangles against the paneling, but the canvases inside them were blank. The subjects had fled or been dismissed. Even paint-and-charm renderings of long-dead Malfoys knew when to leave a room.

The air smelled wrong. Old leather and cold stone — those were the room's own smells, they belonged — but underneath them was something else: sweet, chemical, cloying. The olfactory signature of magic under too much pressure, the air saturated with it, and underneath that, threaded through everything, the animal smell of sustained fear in an enclosed space. His sweat. His father's. The smell had soaked into the wood and the stone and the curtains and it would still be there in the morning when someone opened the drapes and pretended none of this had happened.

His father's hand was on his shoulder.

Lucius's grip. The thumb pressing into the muscle above the collarbone, too hard for comfort, too deliberate for accident. His father stood behind him and to the left, and Draco could feel, inside the memory's pitiless reconstruction, the fine tremor in his father's hand — the fingers rigid, the tremor coming from somewhere deeper, the forearm or the shoulder, the kind of shaking that would not stop because its source was not muscular but existential. His father was afraid. Draco had not understood this at sixteen. He had understood only the grip and the message it carried: stand still. Don't move. Don't flinch.

His left arm was extended across the desk.

The sleeve of his shirt — a white shirt, a school shirt, because he'd arrived at the Manor that morning from King's Cross and hadn't changed, because no one had told him to change, because perhaps no one had known what this evening would hold or perhaps everyone had known and no one had thought to mention that he might not want to be wearing his Hogwarts uniform when it happened — was rolled to the elbow. His forearm lay on the dark wood and his hand was open, fingers spread, and the skin of the inner arm was pale and unmarked and looked, in the single candle's light, impossibly young. The wrist bones visible. The veins visible, thin and blue. A boy's arm, with a boy's lack of scarring, a boy's ignorance of what that skin was about to become.

The Dark Lord stood on the other side of the desk.

Draco's memory of the face was mercifully degraded — the specifics had eroded over nine years, or perhaps they'd never fully formed, the mind refusing to hold what the eyes were seeing. What remained was impression: white, hairless, the features not quite arranged for a human skull. The eyes were clear. Red irises, vertical pupils, and they did not blink. The mouth was a thin line that moved like a wound opening when it spoke.

"Your father assures me you are ready."
