Draco's mouth opened. His throat produced nothing. The room was so quiet he could hear the candle flame breathing. Behind him, his father's hand tightened. He felt the tremor transmitted through the grip — the vibration of a man holding himself together through the point of contact with his son's body.

"He is, my Lord." Lucius's voice. Steady. The voice was steady even though the hand was not, because Lucius Malfoy had been trained from birth to sound controlled when everything behind the control was coming apart. Draco felt the words through his father's grip, the way sound travels through the speaker's body when you're close enough — the chest vibrating against his back, the resonance in the fingers.

The wand was pale. Bone-colored. Longer than any wand Draco had seen, and it moved through the air with a quality the sixteen-year-old couldn't articulate and the twenty-five-year-old, trapped inside the memory, could: the wand did not move as if held. It moved as if it were deciding where to go and the hand around it was following.

The tip came to rest against the inside of his forearm.

Cold. Not the cold of wood or the cold of the room but a temperature that belonged to the object's nature — a cold that had never been warm and could not become warm, as if warmth were a condition it existed outside of. The wand pressed into the soft skin of his inner arm and he could feel the magic before the incantation, a current running through the point of contact into his flesh the way water finds a crack in stone, dark and purposeful and alive in a way that made his own magic recoil, the healthy tissue of him flinching from what was about to be done to it.

He did not hear the incantation. He knew words were spoken — he could see the mouth moving, the thin lips forming syllables in a language he didn't know — but the sound was gone from the memory. Erased, or never stored, the boy's brain shutting down inputs one by one as the pain arrived.

The burning.

It started at the point where the wand met skin and it was not like fire. Fire happened to surfaces. This was inside — inside the arm, inside the muscle and the bone and the channels where his own magic ran, a searing that spread in both directions from the wand's tip, down toward his fingers and up toward his shoulder, and it was not only burning but carving, the dark magic cutting channels into him the way a chisel cuts stone, making room for itself, hollowing out pathways that would carry the Dark Lord's mark for the rest of his life. His own magic tried to fight it and was overwhelmed in the first second, the way a stream is overwhelmed by the river it feeds into. His body tried to jerk away and his father's hands — both of them now, no longer on the shoulder but on his upper arm and his back — held him against the desk while the wand burned its claim into his child's forearm.

He heard himself make a sound. Not a scream. Screaming required air and coordination and intent, and he had none of those. The sound was what a body makes when it has been surpassed — involuntary, animal, the noise of an organism whose pain has exceeded its capacity to process pain as information. He heard it and could not connect it to himself. It was coming from somewhere far away. It was coming from the boy in the school shirt who had walked into his father's study twenty minutes ago and who would not be that boy when he walked out.

His vision went white at the edges. Then grey. Then the floor was tilting, or he was tilting, his knees folding under him, his father's grip the only structure between him and the stone — and the last thing the sixteen-year-old registered before consciousness abandoned him was the wand tip still pressed to his arm, still burning, still writing its permanent claim into the skin of a boy whose forearm had been unmarked that morning and whose school trunk was still packed in the entrance hall.

---

The study came back in pieces.

The desk under his forearm. The chair under his weight. The candles in the sconces — different candles, these ones steady, their light warm and clean and nothing like the guttering flame that had lit his father's study nine years ago. The frost on the windows. November, not July. Twenty-five, not sixteen. The sleeve of his shirt rolled to the elbow in the same gesture, across a different desk, for a different pair of hands.

Her hands were gone.

She'd pulled back — both palms lifted, her body pressed against the back of her chair — and her face was the colour of the frost on the glass. Her mouth was open. No sound. Her eyes were fixed on something that wasn't in the room anymore, something that had passed through her and left the shape of itself behind.

He could feel her.

Not her hands. The resonance — the thing between them that had started weeks ago as a low-grade awareness at the edge of his perception, a sense of her proximity that he'd never named because naming it would have meant admitting it existed. It had blown open. The quiet hum had become a roar. The wall between them had not thinned — it had come down, suddenly and completely, as if the Mark's eruption had torn through whatever membrane had been keeping their separate experiences separate. He could feel her the way you feel weather: not her thoughts, not language, but the texture of what was happening inside her. And what was happening inside her was his memory. The fear. The pain. The specific quality of being sixteen and held in place while something permanent was done to you.

She hadn't watched it. She'd been inside it. In her body, in her chest, in whatever the resonance used to carry sensation between them. The terror of the boy. The age of him.

He pulled his arm off the desk.

The Mark looked the same — skull, serpent, the grey-black stain of it — but the skin around it was flushed, the veins raised and visible, as if the magic underneath had surged toward the surface and brought the blood with it. The Mark ached in a way it hadn't ached in years — not the restless throb of the reactivation but a deep tenderness, like a wound that had been closed for a decade and then opened by a hand that didn't know the wound was there.

He rolled his sleeve down. His fingers were not steady. The cuff button took three tries, the buttonhole refusing to align, and he concentrated on this — the button, the fabric, the small mechanical task — because it was something to do with his hands that was not pressing them over his face.

"Malfoy—"

"Don't."

The word came out harder than he intended. Not loud. He didn't do loud — that reflex had been trained out years ago, the instinct to raise walls by raising volume replaced by something quieter and more effective. But sharp enough to close a door. He watched her close her mouth and hated himself for it in the same motion, because she had just been inside the worst thing that had ever happened to him and his response was to turn the lock.

The room was very quiet. The particular quiet of two people who have both stopped breathing at a normal rate.

He could hear the candles — the small, intimate sound of flame in a drafty room, the way it pulls and stutters against moving air. He could hear his own breathing, too fast, too shallow. He could hear hers.

And underneath the sound, through the resonance that had not closed — that was still wide open between them like a channel blown past its capacity — he could feel her shock, her grief, her overwhelming need to do something with what she'd just experienced, and, worse than any of those, the thing he identified with the precision of long practice as pity and then rejected the identification because it wasn't exactly pity, though it lived in the same territory, and the distinction mattered to him more than he could have explained.

She pressed the heels of her hands against her eyes. Her shoulders were rigid under the wool of her jumper. She held the position for long enough that he understood it wasn't for his benefit — she needed the dark behind her own hands, needed to not see anything while her body processed what the resonance had forced into it.

When she lowered her hands, her eyes were red but dry.

"How old were you?" Her voice was rough, stripped of the clinical precision she usually wore. "I knew — the Wizengamot records said sixth year, so I knew it was sixteen, but I didn't—"

"Sixteen." His voice was flat. He was making it flat. The flatness was a choice, the way all his vocal registers were choices, and underneath it was something he was not going to let into the room. "I'd just turned. My birthday's in June. It was July."

She nodded. The nod was mechanical — her body performing comprehension while the rest of her was still somewhere in the aftershock.

"Your father was there."

Not a question. She had felt Lucius's hands. Through the resonance she'd felt the grip, the pressure, the tremor.

"Yes."

"He held you."

He looked at the desk. The surface was old, marked by generations — ink stains, the pale rings of cups set down without coasters, a gouge near the edge that might have been a letter opener or a penknife centuries ago. The grain was visible in the candlelight, whorls and lines of it, each one distinct.

"He held me still," he said. "There's a difference."

She made a sound he didn't look up to identify.

"You don't know that," she said. "You were sixteen. You don't know if you would have—"

"I know I didn't."

"Because he held you."

"Because I didn't." He heard the edge in his own voice and pulled it back. The precision tightened — his stress response, the careful clipped register that sounded polite and was actually the sound of every door closing at once. "I walked into that room. I stood there. I held out my arm. My father's grip was — insurance. Not the deciding factor."

"Malfoy—"

"Don't." Quieter this time. "Don't decide what I was. You felt it, fine. You felt the fear. But fear doesn't mean I didn't choose it."

"I'm not deciding anything."

"You're deciding I was a victim. I can hear it."

"I'm not — you were in pain. You were terrified. Your father was holding you down against a desk—"

"Holding me still."

"And the difference?"

He didn't have an answer to that. Or he had several, and none of them would survive the air between his mouth and hers, because she would take them apart with the same thoroughness she applied to everything, and he could not afford to have this particular piece of himself disassembled in this room, on this afternoon, with the resonance still carrying the raw data of his worst night.

He stood. The chair scraped stone and the sound was too loud. He moved to the window and stood with his back to her, looking at the frost and the dark lawn beyond it and the nothing that was visible in a November evening at half four.

The Manor was still around them. The particular stillness of a house that had felt what had just happened — or he was projecting, he was always possibly projecting when it came to the house, his mother had warned him about that without ever quite saying what she was warning him about. The temperature in the study had dropped by a degree that might have been the evening or might have been the house.

She didn't follow him. She didn't speak. He could hear her breathing — controlled now, the deliberate rhythm of someone managing their own system — and beneath the sound, through the resonance, he could feel her reaching for the analytical frame. Trying to classify what had just happened in terms that would make it holdable. Experiential imprint. Resonance transmission. Compressed casting residue. The clinical vocabulary she used when feeling exceeded what she could tolerate, deployed like scaffolding around a structure that was threatening to come down. He could feel her reaching for it and he could feel it not fitting, the words falling short of what she'd experienced, which was not a phenomenon but a boy.

"The Mark's never done that," he said. To the window. To the frost. "I didn't know it stored anything."

"Neither did I." Her voice was steadier now, or working harder at steadiness. "The deeper layer — the compressed residue — I think it's the experiential imprint of the original casting. Not a representation of the memory. The actual sensory record, preserved in the curse architecture."

She was rebuilding the clinical frame around herself. Brick by brick, word by careful word. He recognized the impulse because it was his too, the reach for language that could hold something at a manageable distance. But the frame was cracked. He could hear it — the terminology sounding wrong against what she'd felt, like someone describing a drowning in terms of fluid dynamics.

"The resonance carried it," she said. Quieter now. "I didn't see it. I was inside it. In the room. I could feel his hand on—"

She stopped.

The silence that followed had weight.

"I know," he said. "I could feel that you were there."

She exhaled. A long, controlled breath — the sound of someone setting something down carefully because they were afraid of what would happen if they dropped it.

"We should stop for today," she said.

"Yes."

Neither of them moved.

---

He went to his rooms.

He stood in the corridor outside his bedroom with his palm flat against the wall and the stone was cold against his skin and the Manor's bones were solid and offered nothing. He stayed there longer than made sense — minutes, maybe more — his shirtsleeve buttoned, his forehead against the stone, and the thought circling with the dumb persistence of something that had gotten stuck: she felt it, she felt it, she felt it.

Not the pain. He could have survived her knowing the pain. Pain was impersonal enough — everyone understood pain in the abstract, and the Dark Mark was a known quantity, documented and discussed and filed away in the public record as one of many horrors committed by a dead man. She could have known about the pain and it would have changed nothing between them.

But she'd felt the age of him. The sixteen. The specific texture of a boy's terror — too old for the fear to be innocent, too young for it to be brave, the terror of someone who understood exactly what was happening and could not, at sixteen, fully comprehend what it would cost him for the rest of his life. She'd felt his father's hand. She'd felt the moment his knees gave and Lucius caught him. She'd felt the sound he made, the one that wasn't a scream.

She knew him now in a way no one had ever known him, and the knowledge could not be returned.

He washed his face. The water from the taps ran cold at this hour — the Manor's plumbing followed its own logic about when warmth was warranted, and apparently the aftermath of having your worst memory torn open and handed to a woman you were not going to think about in those terms did not qualify. He dried his face on a towel that smelled of lavender because the house-elves laundered everything with lavender, a habit inherited from his mother's decades in the house, and the domesticity of the scent — clean linen, a maintained life — was so far from the room in the memory that the gap between them felt vertiginous.

He went downstairs.

The library was warm. The fire had been built up — someone had added a log, Granger or the house-elves or the Manor itself, it didn't matter — and the flames were steady, the light reaching the far shelves where the older texts lived in their glass-fronted cases. The room smelled the way it always smelled: old paper, dust, the faint astringent bite of preservation charms working on bindings older than some institutions.

She was in her chair. The one near the fireplace — she'd claimed it in the first week without discussion, and the leather had shaped itself to her over the weeks so that it held an impression of her even when she wasn't in it. She had a book open. The Marchetti translation, the one they'd been cross-referencing against the archaic notation from the gated archive. Her shoes were off — she'd started leaving them by the hearth in the second week, a concession to domestic ease that she probably hadn't registered.

She was not reading.

He could tell because her eyes weren't moving. The page she was staring at was one they'd already annotated that morning, her small precise handwriting filling the margins with cross-references and questions. She was looking at it the way you look at a surface when you need something to rest your eyes on.

He sat in his chair. Across from hers, separated by the small table that accumulated books and cups and whatever they'd been working on. He picked up the nearest volume — a genealogical record, centuries old, the leather cover cracked along the spine — and opened it.

The fire shifted. A log settled, sending a cascade of sparks up the flue. The sound was soft and ordinary.

She turned a page she hadn't read.

He looked at the genealogy and saw nothing. The names swam — Abraxas, Brutus, Septimus — generations of Malfoys inscribed in the same iron-gall ink, the same angular hand, the family recording itself as if writing the name down were enough to make it last. His own name was in one of these volumes somewhere. His birth. His Hogwarts placement. He didn't know what else his father had recorded. Lucius had maintained the family ledger with the seriousness of a man who believed lineage was architecture — something load-bearing, something that held the house up.

His father, whose hands had shaken while they held him still.

He turned a page.

The silence in the library was different from any silence that had come before.

He'd learned, in the weeks since she arrived, to read the Manor's quiets the way you learn the moods of a house you've lived in long enough. The professional silence of their early days — courteous, careful, two people agreeing to share space without crowding it. The working silence of collaborative research — comfortable, absorptive, the quiet of two minds on the same problem. The charged silence after the gated archive had held them together. The loaded quiet after his mother's visit, Narcissa's careful non-answers hanging in the room like smoke.

This was none of those.

This was the quiet that comes after something irrevocable. Not the silence of restraint — not two people choosing not to speak — but the silence of inadequacy, where the thing that had happened existed so far outside the register of their relationship that no available sentence could acknowledge it without making it smaller or making it worse. She had been inside his worst memory. She had felt it in her body. Any words he could assemble would either minimize the violation or ask her to carry something that wasn't hers to carry, and both options were intolerable.

She turned another page. He heard it — the soft scrape of old paper on old paper, the spine's faint protest.

The fire popped. An ember split and a small flare of light rose and died.

He thought about the boy. He thought about him with the distance of years and the impossible clarity of a memory that had just been replayed in full sensory fidelity — not in the degraded version he carried day to day, the version that had been worn smooth by repetition and repackaged into something he could hold, but in the original, the undamaged recording that the Mark had apparently been storing at the bottom of its architecture like a letter it had never sent. The boy in the white school shirt with the wrist bones showing and the unmarked skin. How thin he'd been. How the fear had made his hands shake and he'd pressed them flat against the desk to stop the shaking because the desk was cold and the cold was sensation and sensation was something to hold on to when everything else was being taken.

She'd felt all of it. The shaking. The cold desk under his palms. The way sensation became the only anchor when the body's other systems failed.

He set the genealogy on the arm of his chair. The cracked spine protested.

She looked up. Their eyes met across the small table, across the books and the lamp and the cooling tea neither of them had touched, and the resonance between them — still open, still far wider than it had been that morning or any morning before — carried something he could only call recognition. Not the recognition of seeing someone familiar. The recognition of being known. The uncanny, unwilled intimacy of having another person feel what you felt at your worst and sit across from you afterward without commentary, without analysis, without the clinical frame that had been the agreed-upon fiction of their arrangement.

Her eyes were dry. She had not cried. He was grateful for this with a ferocity that embarrassed him — if she'd cried he would have had to do something about the crying, and doing something about someone else's reaction to his own pain would have been the one thing too grotesque for this evening to contain.

"Tea?" she said.

The word was ordinary. It was the most ordinary thing she could have offered — the small domestic reflex they'd built over weeks of shared evenings in this room, the question that was also an offer and was also, he understood, a way of saying *I'm still here and this room is still real and we can still do this one small normal thing.* The ordinariness of it arrived somewhere in his chest, and what he felt was the specific relief of a long-held tension releasing — like the ache in your hand when you finally set down something you've been gripping too hard, and the ache is not new but old, the accumulated cost of carrying arriving at the point where it can be felt.

"Yeah," he said. "All right."

She stood. The Marchetti stayed open in her chair, the unread page keeping her place. She moved to the side table where the tea things lived — the ceramic pot, the cups that didn't match because they'd been collected across centuries of Malfoy habitation, the tin of loose leaves the house-elves refilled without being asked. He watched her set the kettle with a tap of her wand. Watched her measure the leaves — the same exactitude she brought to her diagnostic work, the precise amount, the water heated to the precise temperature. The small perfectionism that was so fundamental to how she moved through the world that she probably didn't know she was doing it.

The glamour on her left forearm was holding. He noticed this because he always noticed it — the faint shimmer where the charm met the air, the slight distortion in the light that was visible only if you knew to look. He didn't know what was underneath. He had an idea that he would not examine because the idea, if confirmed, would make his current position — a man whose arm had just revealed his worst memory to a woman who was concealing something on her own arm — symmetrical in a way he was not prepared to think about.

She brought the tea back. His cup first, then hers. She knew how he took it — she'd learned through observation in the first week, because observation was how she learned everything, cataloguing the world's details with a thoroughness that was either admirable or unnerving depending on how exposed you felt. The tea was right.

He held the cup and the ceramic was warm against his palms and the warmth was specific to this room, this evening, this version of his life in which someone made him tea after the worst afternoon he'd had in years.

She sat. She picked up the Marchetti.

He drank his tea. The taste registered at a distance — dark, faintly smoky, the blend his mother had selected years ago that the house-elves still kept stocked. He watched the fire and didn't see it. The flames moved and the shadows on the shelves moved with them and the library breathed around them both, the Manor's largest room settling into evening the way a body settles after exertion.

The Mark ached. A deep, bruised tenderness, the magic easing back into channels that had been jolted from their resting state. Through the resonance he could feel that she felt it — the faint sympathetic register of his pain arriving in whatever her body used to carry the awareness between them. She didn't react. She turned a page. But her thumb paused on the paper's edge for a moment longer than the page required, and he understood that she had felt the ache and chosen to say nothing, and the choice was a kindness he could accept because it didn't ask him to acknowledge it.

"Granger," he said.

She looked up.

He didn't know what he'd been going to say. Her name had come out by itself — the way speech escapes when you've been quiet long enough that the body produces a sound whether or not you've prepared one. He looked at her across the table, across the books and the fire and the weeks of carefully maintained distance that had been rendered meaningless in the space of ten minutes, and he searched for a sentence that was honest without being more than either of them could carry.

"I'm sorry you felt that," he said.

It was wrong. He heard it as he said it — too close to apologizing for her discomfort rather than his exposure, as if the resonance were an inconvenience she'd suffered rather than a violation that ran both ways. But it was what he had, and he let it stand.

She closed the Marchetti. She held it in her lap, both hands flat on the cover, her fingers spread — the way you hold something solid when you need something to hold.

"I'm not," she said. "Sorry. That I felt it."

He looked at her.

"I am sorry — for you," she said. "That it was taken from you. That you didn't choose whether—" She stopped. The sentence restructured itself. "I'm not sorry I know. I think I needed to. I think someone should have known a long time ago."

"Nobody knew."

"I know." She held his gaze. "That's what I mean."

The resonance carried the rest of it — what the words had reached for and missed. That she understood the boy in the memory was not the man in the chair. That the man in the chair was still, in a way he would never fully outgrow, the boy. That the Mark had taken something from him at sixteen that could not be returned at twenty-five or at fifty, and she had felt this, and she was sitting across from him in a warm room with tea in her hands, and she was not looking at him differently. Or she was, but the difference was not what he'd feared. She was not looking at him with less. She was looking at him with more, and the more was harder, and he did not know what to do with it.

He picked up his tea. It had cooled. He drank it anyway.

She opened the Marchetti.

The fire burned low. The library darkened around them, the shelves receding into shadow, the world contracting to the circle of firelight and the two chairs and the table between them. He should add a log. He didn't want to move. Moving would change whatever this was — the quiet, the proximity, the experience of sitting in a room with someone who had been inside the worst of him and was still here, unhurried, turning pages.

The Mark settled. The ache subsided from sharp to dull to the low persistent hum that was its resting state, the disturbed magic finding its channels again. Through the resonance he felt her awareness of it — a faint warmth at the edge of his perception, steady, present. Not touching. Just there. Close enough to feel.

She turned a page. He thought, this time, she might have actually read it.

He sat in his chair with his empty cup and watched the fire die. He did not think about the next examination — when it would come, what it would mean, whether the sleeve he rolled and the arm he offered would ever again be a clinical gesture between researcher and subject. The answer was already in the room. The clinical frame had been a fiction they'd maintained by mutual agreement, and the fiction was over, and what had replaced it was this: the library, the fire, the quiet. The woman across from him who had been inside his worst moment and said *I'm not sorry I know,* and meant it, and stayed.

The fire went to embers. She conjured a small light above her book — wandlight, warm and steady, the kind she used for late reading — and it touched her face and her hands and the open pages and nothing else, and the rest of the library fell into dark around them.

He sat in the dark and the silence between them held.


# Chapter 9

The lamb was overcooked.

Hermione noticed this the way she noticed most things about Malfoy — sideways, while thinking about something else. He'd been in the kitchen for forty minutes, which was longer than usual, and when he set the plates down in the library she saw the edges of the chops gone grey and tight. He didn't mention it. She didn't mention it. They ate at the long table between stacks of Abelard Malfoy's correspondence and a genealogical chart she'd been cross-referencing against anchoring point locations, and the lamb was overcooked and fine and she ate all of it.

Three weeks. She'd been in the Manor for three weeks and she was eating overcooked lamb in a library that rearranged its own shelves and not finding this remarkable.

He was reading the Abelard letters while he ate, one hand holding a chop and the other turning pages. He'd switched to the originals after she'd found a transcription error in the copies — a single diacritical mark that changed "to anchor" to "to sever," which was the kind of mistake that could have sent them down the wrong research path for days. He'd been quietly furious about it. Not at her. At the transcription, at whoever had made the copies three hundred years ago, at the accumulated carelessness of his own family's record-keeping. She'd watched him go through the first twenty letters checking every mark against the originals, his handwriting in the margins getting smaller and more precise as he went, and she'd felt something that was not professional satisfaction.

Now he was eating with one hand and annotating with the other, his fingers stained with ink at the tips, and she was looking at his hands.

She was looking at his hands.

The realization arrived late, the way these things did — she'd been doing it for several minutes before the watching became conscious. His fingers were long and thin and there was a smudge of blue-black ink on the side of his right index finger where he'd been pressing too hard on the nib. He turned a page and his thumb caught the edge and he licked it, absently, to get a better grip. She followed the motion of his thumb to his mouth and back to the page and her stomach did something that had nothing to do with lamb.

She looked down at her plate. There was a bone on it and a scrape of mint jelly and her knife was at an angle she didn't remember leaving it at.

The library was warm. It had been warm for days — since the examination that went wrong, or right, since the memory that had poured through the Mark and left them both wrecked and silent in this room. The Manor had responded to whatever had shifted between them by becoming almost generous. The corridors from the guest wing to the library had stabilized into a consistent route. The shelves surfaced texts she needed before she asked for them. The fires lit themselves when she entered a room and banked when she left. She was being tended to by a building, and the building was getting better at it.

She should have found this more disturbing than she did.

"The third letter references a node at Glastonbury," Malfoy said, without looking up. "But it's using a locational cipher I haven't seen before. The first glyph might be directional."

"Or temporal. Abelard's notation system uses the same base glyph for spatial and temporal orientation — the modifier's in the subscript."

He turned the letter toward her and she leaned across the table to look, and for a moment their heads were close enough that she could smell the ink on his fingers and something underneath it, soap or skin, something warm and plain. She pointed to the subscript. Her sleeve brushed his wrist. Neither of them moved.

"Temporal," he said, after a pause that was slightly too long.

"Temporal," she agreed, and sat back, and picked up her water, and drank it, and the water was room temperature and tasted like nothing and her hand was steady.

Outside, it was already dark. Early December in Wiltshire meant the light went by half three, and they'd been working since morning. The frost on the library windows had melted from the inside, leaving tracks of condensation that caught the firelight. She could feel him — the low, constant awareness the Mark resonance had settled into, his presence two feet away registering as a kind of warmth in her peripheral attention that was distinct from the fire, distinct from the library. She'd stopped trying to rationalize it as diagnostic residue. She hadn't started calling it what it was.

She was comfortable. That was the word she'd been avoiding. Not safe, exactly — the Manor didn't permit safe, not with its corridors shifting and its rooms appearing according to logic she couldn't map. But comfortable. The work was absorbing. The library was extraordinary. The company was—

She put the thought down before she finished it. Picked up the genealogical chart instead.

"I think the Glastonbury node was one of the original twelve," she said. "The distribution pattern makes more sense as a dodecahedral lattice than a grid. Your family didn't build in straight lines."

"My family didn't build anything in straight lines." He set down the chop bone and wiped his fingers on a cloth, methodically, one finger at a time. "Including their record-keeping, their politics, and most of their marriages."

She almost laughed. She caught it behind the water glass but he saw it anyway, and something in his expression shifted — not a smile, he didn't smile easily, but a loosening around the jaw, a settling. He looked less careful for a moment. He looked like someone who had just made another person almost laugh and was pleased about it in a way he wouldn't admit.

She bent back to the chart and the evening continued. They worked for another two hours. At some point he got up and made tea without asking, and he brought it back with milk in hers and nothing in his and the milk was the right amount — not because he'd asked, not because she'd told him. He'd learned it the way the house learned corridors: through repetition, through attention, through the specific dailiness of being in the same place as someone for three weeks.

She wrapped her hands around the cup and the warmth of it went through her palms. He sat back down and pulled the next letter toward him and they worked in the quiet of the library while the fire popped and the frost thickened on the windows, and outside the Manor the English winter was doing its ordinary work of making everything dark and cold, and inside the Manor the light was warm and steady and the silence between them was not empty. It was full of turning pages and the scratch of his pen and the occasional murmur when one of them found something worth sharing.

At one point she stretched and her back popped, three vertebrae in quick succession, and he looked up from the letters and said "That sounded structural."

"It's fine."

"You've been hunched over that chart for four hours."

"I hunch. It's a professional hazard."

"It's going to be a medical hazard if you don't move your neck occasionally."

She rolled her neck, deliberately, and something else popped, and he winced on her behalf and she said "See? Fine," and went back to the chart, and the exchange was nothing — was the kind of thing that happened between people who spent time together, the flat unremarkable texture of shared hours — and it was so ordinary and so easy that she didn't register it as significant until later, when it was too late for the recognition to be anything but painful.

The fire burned and the library held them in its generous warmth, and Hermione Granger was comfortable in Malfoy Manor.

---

She left the library at half eleven.

He'd already gone up twenty minutes earlier, stacking the Abelard letters in the order he'd reach for them in the morning — a particular kind of optimism, organizing for a tomorrow he assumed they'd both be here for. She'd said goodnight and he'd said goodnight and neither of them had paused on it, and that was new too. For the first week, the goodnights had been stiff little negotiations. Now they were just the end of the day.

The corridor from the library to the guest wing was the route she'd walked most often, and the Manor had learned her habits or decided to accommodate them — the same sequence of turns, the same portraits in the same order, the same stretch of stone floor where the third flagstone from the left had a crack she used as a landmark. She walked it without thinking. Her mind was still in the Abelard letters, turning over the Glastonbury cipher, and underneath that, not quite submerged, the shape of his hands on the page. The way he'd winced when her neck popped. The milk in her tea.

She was thinking about the milk in her tea when she turned the corner where the corridor jogged left toward the guest wing staircase.

The door was open.

It was a room she had never seen in three weeks of living in this house, and she recognized it instantly.

The chandelier. Massive, ornate, hanging from a ceiling so high the chain disappeared into shadow. Crystal drops catching light from somewhere — there were no candles lit, no fire, but the chandelier held a pale luminescence that might have been magical residue or might have been the moonlight coming through the tall windows at the room's far end. The windows were uncurtained. The glass was dark.

The marble floor. White, veined with grey, polished to a depth that reflected the chandelier above it like still water. Cold — she could feel the cold coming off it through the open doorway, a different cold than the Manor's usual winter chill. This was preserved cold. The temperature of a room that had not been warm in seven years.

She knew this floor. She had lain on this floor.

Her feet stopped moving. Not a decision — the information bypassed the part of her brain that made decisions and arrived directly at her legs, her spine, the soles of her feet on the flagstone. She was standing in the corridor looking into the drawing room of Malfoy Manor and her body had remembered it before she had.

The room was exactly as it had been. That was the worst thing — not a detail changed, not a piece of furniture moved. The same high-backed chairs arranged along the wall. The same dark fireplace, massive, unlit, the mantel carved with serpents. The same expanse of marble floor between the door and the far wall, and she knew how long that expanse was because she had been dragged across it.

Bellatrix's hand in her hair. That was the first sensory memory to arrive — the grip, the pull, the specific angle of her neck as she was hauled from the entrance hall into this room. The others were behind it, pressing forward in a sequence her body had filed with merciless precision while her conscious mind had been doing its best to leave.

The knife.

The sound it made on her skin was not the sound she'd expected. She'd expected a cutting sound, something sharp and clean. What it had sounded like was tearing — the cursed blade didn't cut so much as separate the skin, and the sound was wet and wrong and Bellatrix had been talking the whole time. Not shouting. Talking. Conversational. Almost cozy. "Tell me," she'd said, and the knife had pressed into the inside of Hermione's left forearm, and Hermione had screamed and Bellatrix had said "oh, don't do that yet, we've barely started," as if Hermione were being rude.

The M first. The blade starting at the inside of the wrist and carving upward. Two humps. Bellatrix's hand was steady. That was a detail Hermione had never told anyone — how steady Bellatrix's hand had been, how precise, how she'd carved the letters like someone writing a note. No haste. No trembling. She had all the time she wanted and she knew it and the knowledge was part of the cruelty, the way she'd paused between letters to examine her work, tilting Hermione's arm in the light.

Hermione's left forearm ached under the glamour. The scar tissue was responding to the room or to the memory or to some residual curse-magic in the marble and it didn't matter which because the effect was the same — a hot, deep throb along each letter, as if the wound were opening in sympathy with its own origin.

U-D-B-L-O-O-D.

She had counted the letters as they were being carved because counting was the only thing she could do. Seven letters. Seven separate cuts. She'd gotten through the first four before her vision had gone white and she'd screamed in a way she'd never screamed before — not pain exactly, or not only pain, but the particular horror of being written on, of having someone else's word put into her skin by force. The M and the U and the D and the B and then the white-out and then she was back and Bellatrix was on the L and Hermione had looked to her right—

There had been people in the room. The Snatchers. Greyback, massive and breathing too loud. Peter Pettigrew somewhere behind her. Ron and Harry shouting from below, from the cellar, their voices muffled by stone. And against the far wall, standing very still, a boy with white-blond hair and grey eyes who was looking at her and then was not looking at her.

She had seen him look away.

She was still standing in the corridor. She hadn't moved. The door was open and the room was there and her body would not cross the threshold and would not retreat and she was stuck in the doorway of the worst room in the world and the chandelier above her was the same chandelier she had pulled down with a blasting spell to create the chaos that let them escape. She could see the repair — a hairline seam in the plaster where the ceiling had been mended, the chain reattached. Someone had fixed the damage she'd done on her way out. Of course someone had. This was the Malfoy house. Damage was repaired and preserved and maintained. Everything was kept.

She could hear her own breathing. It was too fast. Her hands were at her sides and they were fists and her fingernails were cutting into her palms and she knew this was a trauma response, she had read about trauma responses, she had discussed trauma responses with a Mind Healer in the year after the war. She knew what was happening to her body and the knowledge was useless, absolutely useless, because knowing the name for freezing did not unfreeze you.

The marble floor stretched out before her, pale and depthless in the chandelier's cold light.

She had bled on that floor. She remembered that too. The blood had been bright on the white marble, brighter than blood should have been — shock made it look vivid, oversaturated, like a colour she'd never seen before. It had pooled in the veining of the stone. She'd watched it from the floor, her cheek pressed against the cold marble, while Bellatrix leaned over her and said something she couldn't hear because the pain had become a sound of its own, a high ringing that filled her skull and replaced all other input.

The Manor had opened this room for her.

She understood that with the same certainty she understood the anchoring lattice or the Mark's resonance — not through proof but through the sudden, total clarity of something she'd been refusing to see. The house had opened this room because she had been getting comfortable. Because she had been eating lamb in the library and looking at his hands and feeling something that approached safety, and the house had decided — or the house's architecture had reached a condition where — or the house did not decide anything and the room had simply manifested because the conditions aligned, the way weather aligned, the way magic aligned—

It didn't matter. The room was here. She was here. The house had put them together.

She could not feel Malfoy through the resonance. She tried and there was nothing — the low background awareness she'd grown accustomed to was gone, replaced by something dense and opaque, as if the Manor's attention were focused so narrowly on this corridor and this room that it had no bandwidth left for anything else. She was alone with the drawing room and whatever the drawing room was.

The cold crept from the marble across the threshold and found her feet. She was wearing the wool socks she'd brought from her flat, thick and practical, and the cold went through them as if they were nothing. As if the room had been saving its temperature for her. As if seven years of sealed doors had preserved this cold the way a vault preserves its contents — not because the cold served a purpose but because the room did not know how to be anything other than what it had been on the night that defined it.

An hour ago she'd been drinking tea with the right amount of milk. An hour ago the library had been warm and the fire had been steady and she'd been thinking about a cipher and a pair of ink-stained hands. That woman was still somewhere inside her, behind the freeze response, and she could not reach her.

---

She didn't know how long she'd been standing there when she heard him.

Footsteps on stone, approaching from the direction of the library. His particular footsteps — she knew them now, the way she knew the third flagstone's crack — measured, even, the sound of someone who'd been walking these corridors since childhood. They slowed as they reached the jog in the corridor. They stopped.

She didn't turn around. She couldn't. She was looking at the chandelier and the floor and the windows with their dark glass and the chairs along the wall and the fireplace with its serpents and she could not turn around.

He was quiet for a long time. Long enough that she thought he might leave. Long enough that she started to want him to leave and then to fear that he would and then to hate herself for both.

"Granger."

His voice was very careful. She recognized the register from the first day she'd arrived, from the negotiation in his study — controlled, precise, every word checked before it was released. He was being careful because he was standing behind a woman who was frozen in the doorway of the room where she'd been tortured and he didn't know what to do and careful was his default when he didn't know what to do.

"I didn't know it was here," he said. "This corridor — the room hasn't been accessible. I thought—" He stopped. Started again, and the restart was audible, effortful. "I thought I'd sealed it."

She laughed. It came out wrong — too high, too short, not really a laugh. More like the sound a laugh would make if you stripped all the humour out of it and left only the breath.

"You thought you'd sealed it." Her voice was flat and strange in her own ears. "You sealed the room."

"Years ago. After the war. I had the house seal it. The corridor was supposed to — the Manor was supposed to keep it inaccessible."

"Well," she said. "It didn't."

She heard him step closer. Not close — he stopped several feet behind her, still in the corridor, not crossing the line where the flagstone met the marble threshold. She could feel him now, faintly, the resonance stuttering back to life as if his proximity had broken through whatever the Manor had been doing. He was afraid. She could feel that through the bond — his fear, distinct from hers, a different temperature and texture, sharper and more controlled.

"Come away from the door," he said. Very quiet. "Please."

"I can't."

"You can. It's just a room. The house is—"

"Don't." The word came out hard and fast and she felt him go still behind her. "Don't tell me it's just a room."

Silence. The chandelier above them held its pale, residual light. Somewhere deep in the Manor a clock struck midnight, or something that sounded like a clock — the house produced sounds sometimes that had no visible source.

"No," he said. "You're right. I'm sorry."

She closed her eyes. The marble floor was still there behind her eyelids, white and veined, and Bellatrix's voice was there too, that conversational tone, that patience. She opened her eyes because open was better than closed, because at least with her eyes open the room was a room and not a memory.

"I'm going in," she said.

"Granger—"

"I'm going in." She said it to the room, not to him. Her voice had levelled out into something she recognized — the register she used when giving testimony, when presenting findings to the Wizengamot, when she needed to sound like a person who made decisions. "If the house opened it, then I'm going in. I won't stand in a doorway."

She made her feet move. The first step was the worst — the transition from flagstone to marble, from corridor temperature to the drawing room's preserved cold. Her shoes on the marble made a sound she remembered. Flat, hard, echoless. Marble ate sound. She had screamed in this room and the marble had eaten the screams.

She walked to the centre of the room and stopped. She was standing on the floor she had bled on. The blood was gone — cleaned, of course, long ago — but she looked down and she could see exactly where it had been, could map the stain on the stone like a chart she'd memorized. Here was where her head had been. Here was where her arm had been stretched out for the knife. Here was where she'd curled into herself after Bellatrix finished and stepped back to admire.

Behind her, she heard him enter.

His footsteps were wrong — uneven, halting, the careful stride broken. He got three steps onto the marble and stopped. She turned around and looked at him.

He was grey. Not pale the way he usually was — grey, the colour of someone who has been hit. His eyes were on the floor, not on her, and they were tracking across the marble the same way hers had, reading the room's geography, and she realized he could see it too. He knew where the blood had been. He knew the layout of this room from the other side — not from the floor looking up but from the wall looking down, and the knowledge was on his face.

"You stood over there," she said. She pointed to the far wall, between the second and third chairs. "Against the wall. Your mother was next to you."

His jaw worked. She watched the muscle in the side of his face move and it was not enough. It was a reaction and it was not enough.

"You were wearing your school robes," she said. "Easter holidays. You'd been home a week. I remember because I spent a month afterward thinking about how you'd been home a week and this was what your home looked like."

"Granger, I—"

"You looked at me." She hadn't planned to say this. The words were coming from the place where the memory lived, not from the part of her that decided what to say. "When she started on the M. You looked at me and I looked at you and you looked away. Do you remember that?"

He was standing very still. His hands were at his sides. She noticed they were shaking — a fine tremor, barely visible, the kind of shaking you could hide in pockets if you had pockets. He didn't have pockets. He was in shirtsleeves.

"Yes."

"I don't know what I wanted from you." She heard her own voice, analytical, almost conversational, and recognized the register as dangerous — this was her dissociation voice, the one that sounded calm because it had disconnected from everything underneath. "I was seventeen and I was on your floor and your aunt was carving a slur into my arm and I looked at you and I think I wanted — I don't know. Something. Some sign that you—"

She couldn't finish the sentence. Not because of emotion but because there was no honest way to end it. What had she wanted? For him to save her? He was sixteen. For him to protest? He'd have been killed. For him to look and keep looking? He'd looked. He'd seen it. And then he'd turned his face to the wall.

"I remember everything about that night," Malfoy said. His voice had gone flat, stripped of its usual careful modulation. This was a new register — not controlled, not careful, just levelled by the weight of what it was carrying. "I remember the sound she made when she laughed. I remember Pettigrew's breathing. I remember the — the sound the knife made. I didn't know a knife could make that sound."

"It was a cursed blade," she said, because precision was the only thing she had left. "It wasn't cutting. It was separating the skin. Bellatrix's knife was designed—"

"Stop." He closed his eyes. "Please."

"Why? You were there. You heard it."

"I know I heard it."

"Then why does it bother you to hear me describe it?"

His eyes opened. They were bright and she understood he was close to something — tears or rage, she couldn't tell, and she didn't think he could either. "Because you're describing it like a research finding. Like it's data. And it's not — Granger, it happened to you. It happened to you on my floor."

"I know whose floor it was." The dissociation cracked. She heard it in her own voice — the flat register splitting, something raw underneath pressing through. "I know whose house this is. I know whose family she was. I know you were sixteen and scared and there was nothing you could have done that wouldn't have gotten you killed. I know all of that. I've known it for seven years. I testified at your trial and I meant every word. I believe you were not in a position to intervene. I believe it. Do you understand? I believe it and it doesn't help."

The last word echoed off the marble. Flat, hard, swallowed.

He stood there. He looked at her across the distance of the room — the same distance that had been between them that night, wall to floor, except now they were both standing and neither of them was screaming and the aunt who had done it was dead and none of that made the distance smaller.

"What do you want me to say?"

She almost said something cruel. She could feel it in her mouth — the shape of a sentence that would have been true and vicious and entirely unfair, something about how he'd had seven years to figure out what to say and if he hadn't managed it by now then maybe there wasn't anything. She didn't say it. She held it behind her teeth and it tasted like metal and shame.

"I don't know," she said instead. "I don't know what there is to say."

He took a breath. She watched him take it — watched his chest expand, watched the effort of pulling air into a body that clearly didn't want to be in this room. He walked forward. Not toward her — toward the wall. His wall. The spot between the second and third chairs where he had stood at sixteen and watched Bellatrix Lestrange torture Hermione Granger on the marble floor.

He put his back against the wall and stood there.

"I didn't look away because I didn't care," he said. He was speaking to the room, to the floor, to the spot where she'd been. "I looked away because I was — I couldn't—" He stopped. The sentence restructured itself behind his expression. "I was afraid that if I watched I'd do something. Try to stop her. And she'd have killed me and then she'd have killed you and it would have been a gesture, not a rescue, and I couldn't—" His hand moved, a sharp abortive motion. "I did the math. In my head. While it was happening. I stood there and I did the math on whether I could reach my wand before she cursed me and I couldn't. The numbers didn't work. And so I stood here. And I turned my face to the wall. And I listened to it."

The room held the words the way it had held the blood — absorbed them, offered no resonance, no echo, no comfort. Marble ate sound.

"That's supposed to make me feel better?" She said it before she could stop herself, and she heard how it sounded — ugly, unkind, the voice of someone who was being given an honest account of someone else's worst moment and responding with bitterness. She heard it and she couldn't take it back and part of her didn't want to.

"No." He didn't flinch. His back was against the wall and his hands were still shaking and his voice was the flat, levelled thing it had become. "Nothing about this is supposed to make you feel better. I'm telling you what happened because you asked what I remember and I'm not going to lie to you in this room."

"You did the math." She said it back to him like a stone she was turning over, looking at from different angles. "You stood against that wall and you calculated whether you could intervene and the numbers didn't work and so you watched."

"I listened. I turned my face away and I listened. It was worse."

"It was worse for you. Listening."

Something crossed his face. "That's not what I—"

"Because I want to be very clear about what it was like for me." The dissociation was gone now. What was in its place was older and simpler and it ran under her ribs like current. "I was on this floor. I was seventeen. A woman I'd never met held me down and carved a word into my arm and I screamed so hard I tore something in my throat and I looked at you — the only person in this room close to my age, the boy I'd gone to school with for six years — and you turned away. You did the math and you turned away and I was alone on this floor."

He pressed his shoulders harder against the wall. His eyes were fixed on her and they were wet and he wasn't blinking. "Yes."

"And I have to live with that. I have to walk through this house — your house, the house where that happened — and I have to work with you and eat with you and sit across from you in the library and I have to carry that. Every day. The knowledge that you were in this room and you turned away."

"I know."

"You don't know." It came out harder than she meant. "You don't — you know your version. You know the wall and the math and the decision. You don't know what the floor felt like. You don't know what the marble sounded like from down there, or what her breath smelled like, or what it's like to be held still while someone writes on you. You know you were in this room and I know I was in this room and those are not the same room."

The silence that followed was not the silence they'd shared in the library after the Mark memory. That silence had been connective — the quiet of two people inside the same pain. This silence was the opposite. This was the silence of two people discovering that their versions of the same event were not reconcilable, that the room they'd shared at seventeen had been two rooms all along, and the distance between wall and floor was not a distance that could be closed by standing in the same space seven years later.

Malfoy slid down the wall. Not dramatically — his legs went out from under him in a slow controlled folding, knees bending, back sliding against the stone, until he was sitting on the marble with his arms on his knees. He was sitting on the floor. The same floor. He put his head in his hands and she watched his shoulders move and she felt nothing, or she felt everything and couldn't sort it, and the resonance between them was doing something new and unbearable — transmitting his grief into her awareness while she was trying to sustain her anger, mixing the channels, making it impossible to feel only one thing at a time.

"I know it's not the same," he said from behind his hands. His voice was muffled. "I've never thought it was the same."

She looked at him on the floor. The boy from the wall, seven years later, on the floor. It was not enough that he was sitting there. It was not an adequate gesture. But she recognized what it was — not performance, not strategy, not the Malfoy precision she'd learned to expect. His legs had given out and he was on the floor because the floor was where he'd ended up and he hadn't tried to make it look like anything.

She didn't go to him. She couldn't. The anger was still there, and the grief, and the ugly unkind thing she'd said about it being worse for him was still in the room and she wasn't sure she was wrong.

"I testified for you because it was right," she said. Her voice was rough. She'd been talking too much or too loudly in a room that ate sound and her throat was raw with the effort. "I testified because you were a child and the system was going to destroy you for choices you didn't fully make. I believed that. I still believe it. But I need you to understand that believing it doesn't mean I've forgiven it, and forgiving it — if I ever do — won't mean it didn't happen, and the fact that it happened here, in this house, in this room, in the house I'm now — in the house where I—"

She ran out. The sentence was too big and too honest and she couldn't finish it. The part she couldn't say was the part about the lamb and the library and his hands on the page and the way the Manor felt like something she could come home to, and how all of that existed simultaneously with this floor and this chandelier and this cold, and how she did not know how to hold both, and how she was furious that she had to try.

He raised his head. His face was wet. He didn't wipe it. He looked at her from the floor and his expression was the most undone she had ever seen him — not the careful mask, not the brittle formality, not even the rawness of the Mark memory. This was underneath all of that. This was the version of Draco Malfoy who had stood against this wall at sixteen and listened to a girl scream and done the math and turned away and had never, in seven years, found a position from which to look at it straight.

"I should have done something," he said. "The math didn't matter. I should have tried."

"You'd have died."

"Maybe."

"You'd have died and it wouldn't have stopped her. She'd have killed you and finished the word and it would have been the same except you'd be dead."

"Then I should be dead." He said it flat. Not theatrical, not self-pitying. The voice of someone who had done the long division on his own survival and come up short. "If the choices were watch or die trying, then I chose wrong."

"That's not—" She stopped. Breathed. The marble cold was in her feet, climbing her shins. "That's not what I'm saying. I'm not saying you should have died. I would never — Malfoy, I testified—"

"I know you testified. You keep saying that like it settles something."

The words landed and she felt them land — not as an attack but as a truth she didn't want. She did keep saying it. She kept reaching for the testimony the way she reached for data, for evidence, for the solid ground of something she could prove. I testified for you. I believed in your defense. As if saying so were a shield against the uglier, less defensible feeling underneath, which was: I testified for you and you were in this room and I was on this floor and believing you were a victim too did not change the fact that you were standing and I was not.

"I don't know how to do this," she said. She was not talking about the conversation. She was talking about all of it — the Manor, the research, the Mark, the lamb, the library, the growing thing between them that she could not name and could not verify and could not, apparently, stop. "I don't know how to be in this house and know this room exists. I don't know how to look at you and not see the wall."

He didn't answer. He sat on the floor with his back against the wall and his arms on his knees and his face wet and he didn't answer because there was no answer, because the room was the room and the floor was the floor and they had been in it together seven years ago and they were in it together now and both times the distance between them was the same unbridgeable measure.

The chandelier above them held its pale light. The marble floor was cold and clean. The room kept everything.

She waited for something else. For him to stand, to argue, to offer the thing — apology, justification, the word that would make any of this navigable. He stayed on the floor. She stayed standing. The chandelier watched them both with its cold, repaired light, and the distance between standing and sitting was a different measure than the distance between standing and lying down, but it was still a distance, and she didn't know how to cross it and didn't know if she wanted to.

She walked out.

She didn't look back. She crossed the threshold from marble to flagstone and the corridor's ordinary temperature hit her like bathwater, warm after the drawing room's preserved cold, and she kept walking. Down the corridor, past the portraits, past the flagstone with the crack. The crack she'd used as a landmark for three weeks, the small domestic familiarity of knowing a floor, and the floor she'd just been standing on was also a floor she knew and the knowledge was different and the difference was the whole problem.

She heard the door close behind her — the Manor closing the room or Malfoy closing it or the room closing itself, and she didn't turn to see which.

The route back to the guest wing was the same route it had been two hours ago, when she'd left the library thinking about the milk in her tea. The portraits were the same. The turns were the same. The Manor had not rearranged anything. It had not needed to. It had already done the only rearrangement that mattered.

In the guest wing, in her room, she sat on the bed and held her left forearm in her right hand. The scar was throbbing under the glamour. She could feel each letter, a hot pulse that followed the sequence — M-U-D-B-L-O-O-D — as if the wound were reciting itself. She pressed her thumb against the inside of her wrist, hard, over the start of the M, and held it there until the throbbing compressed into a single point of pressure, sharp and specific, and then she let go and the throbbing spread back along the letters like water filling a channel.

The teacup from earlier was on her bedside table. She'd carried it up from the library two nights ago and hadn't brought it back down. The milk residue had dried to a skin on the inside. He'd made her that cup of tea. He'd known the milk. He'd sat across from her in the warm library and winced when her neck popped and made a dry joke about straight lines and cooked her overcooked lamb and none of it, none of it, cancelled the drawing room. She wanted it to. She wanted the ordinary hours to weigh more than the worst one. She wanted the arithmetic to come out differently and it didn't, and wanting it to was not the same as it being so, and she was so tired of knowing the difference.

The Manor was quiet around her. No shifting corridors, no adjusting temperature, no surfaces tending to her needs. The house was still, and whether the stillness was guilt or indifference or the simple mechanical absence of the conditions that made it move, she couldn't tell.

Through the resonance, faint and bruised, she felt Malfoy two floors below. Still in the drawing room. Still on the floor.

She pulled the blanket over her shoulders and sat in the dark and did not sleep.


# Chapter 10

Draco made tea at six in the morning because the alternative was his ceiling and the running inventory of things he'd said wrong in the drawing room.

The kitchen was the oldest part of the Manor that still worked like a kitchen — stone floor, scarred counters, a kettle that had sat on the same stretch of counter since before his grandfather's time. He used the Muggle one now. The enchanted kettle needed a heating charm, and his heating charms had been unreliable for weeks, stuttering mid-boil or producing tepid water that sat in the cup like a reproach. He'd rather manage a manual flame than explain to Granger that his household magic was deteriorating along with everyone else's.

He didn't think about the drawing room.

He poured water over the tea bag. Counted. Removed the bag. Added milk — less than he wanted, because the cold-box enchantment was struggling and they were running low.

He thought about the drawing room.

What he'd said. What she'd said. The specific quality of her voice when she'd told him what she remembered — the detail, the precision, as if the trauma had been preserved under glass and she was describing items in an exhibit, except her hands had been shaking. He'd stood in the doorway and had the chance to say any number of things and what he'd actually managed was a defence so thin it had embarrassed them both.

The stairs creaked — third step, seventh, landing — and she came into the kitchen wearing a jumper that didn't fit and her hair in the particular state it achieved when she'd slept too hard and given up.

She should not have been awake. She worked until one or two most nights and slept through dawn with a commitment that suggested dawn had personally wronged her. He had never seen her in the kitchen before seven.

"Morning," she said.

"Morning."

She looked at the kettle. He looked at her looking at the kettle. The space between these two events was nowhere near large enough to contain everything that had happened in the drawing room, so neither of them tried.

"There's water," he said.

"Thanks."

She made her own tea. He already knew how she took it — milk, no sugar, steeped too long because she forgot about it while thinking about something else — and the fact that he knew this was one of several pieces of knowledge he'd been carefully not examining. She sat at the kitchen table, which was scarred oak and older than most of the doors in the building, and wrapped her hands around the mug, and the silence between them was unlike any of the silences that had preceded it.

Not the careful silence of two people maintaining professional distance. Not the raw silence of the drawing room's aftermath, the night before, when they'd gone to separate floors and the house had felt like it was holding its breath. This was something else. Looser. As if the confrontation had stripped a layer of insulation and what was underneath was simply two people in a kitchen, early, tired, drinking tea.

"I didn't sleep," she said. Not a confession. Closer to a weather report.

"No."

"You?"

"Not particularly."

She nodded. Drank. Looked out the window, where the December dark was just starting to thin along the treeline, frost on the grounds catching what little light there was.

"Your heating charm," she said, glancing at the Muggle kettle.

"Unreliable."

"I can look at it. If you want."

This was the first thing either of them had offered the other since the drawing room that wasn't about research, and it was about a kettle. He could have said no. He could have said it was fine, the Muggle method worked, he was managing. All of which was true and none of which mattered.

"All right," he said.

She pulled it toward her across the table and turned it over in her hands, ran a diagnostic — quick, efficient, her wand hand moving with the kind of fluency that came from years of not thinking about the motion. The easy competence was so far from the woman who'd stood frozen in the drawing room doorway that he had to look at the window instead.

"It's not degrading on its own," she said. "The underlying enchantment's solid, it just isn't getting enough ambient throughput. The general decay." She tapped the kettle twice, murmured something, set it back. "That'll hold for a month. Maybe six weeks, then I'll need to redo it."

"Thank you."

"Sure."

He made more tea with the fixed kettle and brought her a cup without asking. She took it without comment. They sat with the new tea and the window showing the frost and the kitchen settling into the sounds it made when the house was at rest — old wood, a faint whistle from the gap above the window that had been there as long as Draco could remember.

"This is better," she said, and held up the mug. The tea, or the morning. She didn't specify.

---

The days after were like that.

She stopped eating in the library. Or rather, she started appearing in the kitchen when he was already there, which meant they ate together, which meant meals became something that happened between them rather than in adjacent solitude. He didn't comment on this. She didn't frame it as a decision. The old choreography of calibrated separation — one eating at the kitchen table, the other in the library, each pretending the arrangement was convenience — had simply stopped, and what replaced it was breakfast with the frost in the windows and neither of them performing anything.

They talked about the research. The anchoring theory she was developing, the decayed infrastructure she'd traced through three centuries of Malfoy texts, the specific notation system his ancestor Septimus had invented that refused to map onto any published schema. But the conversations had a different grain now. She interrupted herself less. He let sentences finish without deflecting into something drier. The professional frame was still there — the work was urgent, the magical decay was not waiting for them to sort out anything personal — but the frame had thinned from a wall to something more like a screen, and what came through it was closer to actual speech.

He brought her tea in the library. Not every time, and not on a schedule, but when he noticed her pace change — the focused stillness loosening into restless movement that meant she needed to stand up and was too stubborn — he made tea and brought it and set it at the edge of her desk, far enough from the texts that a spill wouldn't touch them. She didn't look up. Ten minutes later the mug was in her hand.

She left the library door open. This was a choice — the door was heavy enchanted oak, and leaving it open meant the corridor sounds reached her, which meant she could hear him moving through the house. He chose not to think about why she'd want that.

He started eating dinner at seven because she ate at seven and had mentioned, offhand, that eating alone made food boring. He wasn't sure she'd meant it as an invitation. He showed up at seven. She didn't comment.

On the third morning she brought a passage from the fourteenth-century archive to breakfast and said, "Your ancestor Septimus was a terrible writer but a brilliant engineer. Look at this."

"He was also, by all accounts, a terrible person." He took the page. "Though that's most of them."

She looked at him. Not with pity, not with the careful absence of judgment he'd learned to read in people who were trying very hard not to judge him. Just looked, as if he'd said something she was filing for later reference.

"The binding schema here," she said, pointing. "I've never seen it outside Egyptian work. Centuries ahead of the published European record."

"You can admire the architecture and despise the architect. We're standing in the proof."

A week ago any reference to the Manor's darker qualities would have tightened the air between them. Now it passed through like weather.

"I suppose we are," she said, and reached for the jam. She spread it on toast with the same focused precision she brought to fourteenth-century notation, and he watched her do it and thought that this — the kitchen, the toast, the morning argument about dead Malfoys — was as close to a normal breakfast as the Manor had produced in seven years.

That afternoon he brought her tea and she said, without looking up from her translation, "You always get the timing right. How do you know?"

"Know what?"

"When I need it. You bring it right before I'm about to get up and make it myself."

He didn't have an answer for this he was willing to say out loud, so he said, "The house tells me," which was not entirely false — the Manor did something subtle to the air when she was restless, a faint shift he'd learned to read — and she accepted this with a skeptical noise and went back to work.

One evening over soup she said, "I keep expecting to turn a corner and find a room I haven't seen. But the house has been settled this week. Steady."

He'd noticed. The corridors held their shapes. The library stayed where it was. The route from the guest wing to the kitchen hadn't rearranged overnight. He didn't know if the house was responding to whatever had shifted between them or if the shift was something the house had arranged. The question applied to everything happening under this roof, and he had decided, provisionally and probably unwisely, to stop asking it.

"Maybe it's the season," he said. "It tends to settle in winter."

"Does it?"

"I don't actually know. It might just be quiet."

She looked at him and said, "Quiet's all right," and he couldn't read her expression but he didn't think he needed to.

The Mark ached that night — the dull restless pulse that had become background since the examinations began, the old curse magic shifting under his skin without purpose or destination. During the examinations, when her hands were on his arm and her diagnostic spells were running their slow careful work, the ache went still. Not painless — settled, which was different. The dark magic calmed under her attention in a way no Healer's touch had achieved, and the contrast between her presence and her absence was something he'd been tracking without deciding to. He sat in his room and pressed his thumb against the Mark's centre and the ache sharpened and he stopped pressing. The absence of her hands was not something he was going to think about. He was a person who did not think about people's hands.

* * *

By the end of the first week after the drawing room, the library had become her territory in a way that should have bothered him and didn't.

Not the books — those reorganized themselves at night according to whatever logic the Manor was running that week, and he'd given up predicting where anything would be from one morning to the next. But around and among the books, Granger had built a working habitat. Three translations in progress, each weighted open with whatever she'd grabbed nearest — a teacup, a saucer, once his reading glasses, which she'd appropriated from the side table without asking and returned without apology. Notes in her handwriting, which was atrocious and precise, covering sheets of parchment she arranged in patterns that looked chaotic and weren't. A stack of reference texts on the floor by the south window, because the window seat got the best light before noon and she liked to work there with her feet tucked beneath her.

He knew about the feet because he had been looking.

She'd left a book on his chair — his chair, by the fireplace, the one he'd been sitting in since he was twelve — open to a passage about resonance patterns in enchanted structures. A note in the margin, in her worst handwriting, which meant late at night: *Cf. your notes on the east wing fluctuation — I think this explains the periodicity.*

He sat in the chair with the book pressed against his leg and read the page, then her note, then found his own notes. She was right. The resonance pattern explained the fluctuation he'd been tracking for weeks, and the connection had the quality her best work always had — so clear once articulated that the gap felt almost insulting. He should tell her. He would tell her.

The afternoon passed. The winter light — what there was of it, the sun giving up by half three — came through the library windows at the low amber angle that only happened in early December. In that light the library was the closest thing to a living room the Manor had ever produced. The preservation charms gave the air its permanent base of old paper and dust and the faintly metallic trace of stabilised ink, and layered on top was the tea she'd made an hour ago and the wool of whatever jumper she was wearing and the particular smell of someone else in a space that had been only his for years.

She was on the floor by the south wall, cross-legged, too near the shelf she was referencing to bother with the desk, surrounded by open texts like someone building a nest out of fourteenth-century parchment. Her lips moved when she hit a difficult passage — the Latin, working through syntax — and her right hand was in her hair, twisting a strand without knowing she was doing it.

He was cataloguing her.
