He'd been doing it for days. Not deliberately — not the way she catalogued the archive texts, with purpose and a system. This was more the way you catalogued weather: involuntarily, because you were inside it and it was happening and your body registered it whether or not you'd decided to pay attention.

He knew the expressions. Concentration drew her eyebrows together and made her look angry; people who didn't know her would have read annoyance. Frustration made her tap the page with her index finger, a steady rhythm, as if the text were being obstinate on purpose and she meant to let it know. When she found something significant she went very still — the lip-movement stopped, the hair-twisting stopped, everything paused for a held breath — and then she wrote with an urgency that suggested the idea might escape if she didn't pin it down fast enough.

He knew the rhythm of her work. Long focused stretches — two hours, sometimes three, when the library might as well have been empty. Then a burst of movement, sudden and total, crossing the room with the specific walk of someone whose body existed mainly to transport her brain to the next relevant text. Then the slow re-settling. Feet tucked. Hair-twist resumed. Gone again.

He knew she took her tea too strong and forgot it until it was cold. That she cracked her knuckles after an hour of sustained writing, left hand first. That she talked to the texts under her breath when they contradicted each other, arbitrating centuries-old disputes with a patience she rarely extended to the living.

He knew the laugh.

She'd found something in Septimus Malfoy's personal annotations — the man's opinion of French magical theorists, which was unprintable even by the standards of someone who'd been dead for six hundred years — and the laugh had come out of her sudden and unreserved. Head back, the sound of it hitting the high ceiling and ringing in a room that hadn't heard much laughter in years. It had changed the shape of her face entirely, and she'd looked over to see if he'd heard, and he'd been sitting perfectly still with his cold tea and an expression he was not going to examine.

"Sorry," she'd said. "Your ancestor really hated the French."

"Family tradition. We've narrowed it down to the Normans."

She'd laughed again — shorter, more controlled — but the first one was the one he kept. He did not think about why he was keeping it. He was aware that not thinking about it was itself a kind of thinking, and that the recursion was the problem.

One afternoon she read aloud from a passage that was defeating her — the Latin so archaic that the syntax doubled back on itself — and he corrected a verb declension without looking up from his own work.

"Thank you. How'd you know that was wrong?"

"I didn't know it was wrong. I knew what it should say."

"That's the same thing."

"It's not. Knowing the error requires knowing the system. Knowing the answer just requires having read the text before."

She was quiet for a moment. "You've read this before?"

"Parts. When I was younger. My father thought the family texts were important."

"Were they?"

"They were to him." He turned a page. "I don't think he understood what they described. But he understood they mattered."

She didn't push — didn't ask the follow-up question about what his father had been like, what the family legacy meant from the inside. She just nodded and went back to the Latin, and the restraint of it, the discipline of her curiosity, was a kindness he noted without knowing how to return.

On the Tuesday night she fell asleep over a translation past eleven.

He didn't notice at first — the last hour of her work was always quiet, pace slowing as exhaustion overtook precision. But the quill dropped from her hand and she didn't reach for it, and he looked up and her head was on her arm on the desk and her breathing had changed and she was asleep.

The fire was low. The library was cold at the edges after midnight — the preservation charms kept the books at stable temperature but did nothing for the people. He should have rebuilt the fire. Should have woken her, told her the desk would destroy her neck by morning. Should have said goodnight and gone upstairs and maintained the boundary between colleague and whatever word he was avoiding.

He picked up the dropped quill and set it in the ink rest so it wouldn't stain the translation she'd been working on for three days. He took the throw from the back of his chair — the old wool one, there since his mother had put it there when he was fourteen and reading past curfew — and laid it over her shoulders. She shifted under the weight of it. Didn't wake.

He sat back down.

Twenty minutes. He didn't read. He listened to her breathe and watched the fire settle into embers and did not look at what he was doing, because looking at it would require naming it, and the name was not something he could afford. Wanting things was the most dangerous habit he'd ever broken. He'd spent seven years learning not to want — not company, not forgiveness, not for the world to look at him and see something other than the Mark on his arm. He'd gotten good at it. He'd built a life around the absence of want, and the life was small and quiet and precisely controlled and it had been survivable.

He was sitting in a library at midnight listening to a woman breathe and the habit had not broken at all. He had just run out of things to want that the world was positioned to take from him, and now there was something new, and the shape of it was so precise he could feel it like an architectural drawing held up to light, and he would not look at it directly.

He left. The corridor to his rooms was shorter than usual — the house being kind, or efficient, or simply noting what he refused to note himself. Behind him, the fire kept burning. The Manor could manage that much.

* * *

Theo arrived on a Thursday, because Theo arrived on whichever day suited Theo.

He came through the Floo — it held, barely, stuttering for half a second before depositing him in the entrance hall with a film of ash on his coat that he brushed off with more attention than the gesture warranted. The coat was new. Dark, well-cut, worn with the specific ease Theo could make look effortless when it was anything but. He'd always had the talent of presenting well, which was, he'd once told Draco, the entire operating principle of post-war Slytherin life: look like you belong in the rooms that don't want you until the rooms forget to check.

"You have a guest," Theo said. Not a question. He'd seen the second pair of boots by the door, or the notes stacked on the hall table, or something else Draco hadn't thought to conceal. Theo noticed things the way other people breathed — constantly, without apparent effort, with an accuracy that could be unsettling if you weren't used to it.

"Granger's doing research in the library. The magical decay — the archive has texts no one else can access."

"Granger." Theo brushed a last fleck of ash from his lapel. "As in Hermione Granger. The Hermione Granger who's been the subject of three gossip columns this month about why she's spending all her time in Wiltshire."

"She's researching the decay. The library —"

"Has texts. You said." Theo hung his coat on the hall stand. "Are you going to feed me, or should I have eaten first?"

"Are you staying for lunch?"

"I'm staying for lunch, and I'm staying for an hour after lunch to make sure you've actually been eating, and then I'll go." He followed Draco toward the kitchen. "I'd have come sooner but the Floo's been — well, you know. I tried Tuesday and ended up in a pub in Berkshire. The landlord was very confused."

"Were you confused?"

"I adapted. There was a reasonable bitter on tap." Theo settled against the kitchen counter with the ease of someone who'd been in this room a hundred times — after school, during the war, in the awful first year when Draco had been under house arrest and Theo had been the only person who visited. "The network's getting worse. My contact at Floo Regulation says they're running half capacity and still losing ground."

"Granger thinks it's structural. War damage to the foundational anchoring."

"Granger thinks." He said this flatly, no particular emphasis, which was its own emphasis. "You talk about what she thinks."

"She's in my library. We discuss the research."

"Sure."

Draco put the kettle on — the repaired one, which held. The silence between them was companionable in a way he'd missed, the specific quality of being with someone who knew him well enough that silence wasn't a failure of conversation but a rest inside it.

"How's the business?" he said.

"Slowly." Theo took the tea Draco offered. "I've got a few consulting contracts — potions supplier, everything aboveboard, everything documented six ways because when your surname is Nott they check the paperwork twice. And I've got a list of people who won't return my owls. So. Progress, loosely defined."

"And the storefront?"

"Third application for Diagon Alley. Third polite rejection. The landlord sent a letter explaining the space had been let." Theo drank. "It hasn't been let. I walked past it yesterday. The windows are still papered over."

"Theo."

"It's fine. I've got a line on a place in Horizont Alley. Worse location, cheaper rent, and the landlord's Muggle-born, so he doesn't give a damn about my father's sentencing record." He said this evenly — the bitterness present but managed, folded into pragmatism like a crease in good fabric. "I don't need Diagon Alley. I need a door and a counter."

Draco had known Theo since they were eleven. He knew the register shifts — the performative ease that got smoother when the thing underneath was worse. Theo's father had been a Death Eater of minimal conviction and maximal cowardice, which in practice meant he'd done slightly fewer terrible things than Lucius and received a nearly identical sentence. Theo carried the name the way Draco carried his own: carefully, with a constant low awareness of what it cost to put it on a lease or a handshake or a business card.

But where Draco had retreated — the Manor, the isolation, the careful small life of someone who'd decided that wanting less meant losing less — Theo had gone forward. Into the world with the name on him, daring the world to reject him and adjusting the plan each time it did. Draco had never worked out whether this was bravery or a different shape of the same stubbornness.

"You should talk to Granger," he said. "About the lease. She'd know people at —"

"I'm not asking Hermione Granger for help with my commercial tenancy."

"She'd do it. She's —"

"I'm sure she's fair and competent and would handle it with exactly the right amount of principled outrage. That's not the point." Theo set down his cup. "I don't want to be someone's project. I want to rent a shop."

The kitchen door opened.

Granger paused in the doorway. She'd heard voices, Draco realised, and hadn't expected anyone else. She was in the work jumper — the oversized one, the one she wore when she expected to be alone with the texts all day — sleeves pushed up, ink on her fingers. Her hair had abandoned whatever arrangement she'd attempted that morning and was doing what it always did by midday, which was everything at once.

"Oh," she said. "I didn't realise —"

"Granger, Theodore Nott. Theo, Granger."

"We were in the same year," she said, recovering quickly. She crossed the kitchen and offered her hand. "Though I don't think we ever properly spoke."

"We didn't." Theo shook it. "Different social circles. Mine being the ones that were socially untenable." His delivery was flat enough that it took a beat to register as a joke.

Granger's mouth twitched. "I remember your Potions marks. They were better than mine in fifth year."

"Sixth, actually. Fifth year you beat me by two points on the final and I've been bitter about it for a decade."

"Have you been keeping score for ten years?"

"Only on that one. The rest I've made peace with."

She laughed — brief, surprised — and Draco watched this exchange and did not enjoy the discovery that Theo could make her laugh in under a minute, when Draco had managed it exactly once and was still carrying the sound of it around.

"I hear you're solving the magical decay from Draco's library," Theo said.

"Trying to understand it. Solving it's ambitious. I'm not there yet."

"She's being modest," Draco said, and then both of them looked at him, and he wished he hadn't spoken because the expression on Granger's face — surprised, a little pleased, trying not to be either — was precisely the kind of thing he'd been cataloguing, and Theo would have seen it.

Theo had seen it.

"I was going to make lunch," Draco said, which was not true until he said it. "Stay. Both of you."

They stayed.

Lunch was sandwiches, because that was what the kitchen could produce with the cold-box failing and the preservation charms at half-strength. They ate at the scarred oak table and the conversation moved from the Floo failures to the anchoring theory to Theo's storefront search to the specific fourteenth-century text Granger was translating, and it was ordinary. Three people in a kitchen, eating sandwiches, talking. Granger was sharper with Theo than she was with Draco — not hostile, but more willing to push back, to test his precision with hers. Theo matched her, and Draco sat between them and felt something he hadn't felt in this kitchen in years: the particular warmth of people filling a room that had been empty too long.

Theo talked about the post-war commercial licensing climate — the way applications from Slytherin-sounding surnames drew documentation requests that a Bones or a Longbottom would never see — and Granger set down her sandwich and her face did something serious and focused that he recognised as her enforcement-statute expression.

"That's systemic," she said. "Not anecdotal. There's a pattern in the licensing data."

"There's absolutely a pattern," Theo said. "There's also no enforcement mechanism and no political appetite for sympathising with children of convicted Death Eaters while the war's victims are still rebuilding."

Draco looked at the table.

"You're right that it's systemic," Granger said, more quietly. "That doesn't mean it's tolerable."

"I didn't say tolerable. I said real. Those are different problems."

"I know three people at the Enforcement Office."

Theo looked at her. Draco looked at Theo looking at her. The kitchen was warm.

"I'll think about it," Theo said, and Draco could tell he meant it. Theo did not accept help from people he'd just met. The fact that he was considering it from Granger said something about the quality of the last hour that Draco wasn't going to examine.

Granger went back to the library after lunch carrying her tea and a stack of pages she'd been marking during the meal. She paused in the doorway.

"It was good to meet you," she said to Theo. "Properly."

"Likewise."

"Come back whenever you want. The Floo's more reliable from this end — I think the Manor stabilises the local connection."

She left. The kitchen was quieter. Theo let ten seconds pass.

"Don't," Draco said.

"I haven't said anything."

"You're composing something. I can tell."

"I'm absolutely not composing anything." Theo picked up his tea, found it empty, set it back down. "I was going to ask about the Mark. How's it been."

This was not what he'd been going to ask and they both knew it. Draco took the offered deflection because the alternative was whatever Theo had actually been composing.

"Worse in some ways. Better in others."

"Helpful. Very specific. Thank you."

"It aches more. Consistently. But during the examinations, when she's doing the diagnostic work — it's quieter. Calmer. I don't know why." He paused. "She doesn't know why either, which bothers her more than it bothers me."

Theo was quiet. Not Draco's guarded silence, not Granger's thinking silence. Theo's specific quiet — someone deciding how much honesty the moment could bear.

"I'm going to say one thing," Theo said, "and then I'm leaving, and you're not going to argue with me about it because I'll already be in the fireplace."

"That's generous advance warning."

"You look like someone who lives here."

Draco waited for the rest. Theo pushed his chair back and stood.

"That's it?"

"That's it." He found his coat in the hall. Draco followed him. "You've looked like a guest in your own house for seven years. Walking through the place like you were waiting for someone to tell you it wasn't yours anymore." He buttoned the coat with the careful precision he gave everything. "Today you look like you live here. I won't say what's different. You're not stupid."

"Theo —"

"The Floo should hold if I go now. Probably. If I end up in Berkshire again I'll owl you from the pub." He stepped into the fireplace, said the address, and the green flames took him.

The ash settled. The entrance hall was quiet.

Draco stood in the house he'd grown up in and had not felt at home in since the war and thought about what Theo had said. He thought about the library and the kitchen and the woman who was in both of them. The throw on her shoulders. The laugh hitting the ceiling. The fixed kettle. The way she'd said *come back whenever you want* to his closest friend, casually, as if the Manor were partly hers to open.

He went back to the kitchen and washed the mugs. Three of them. He dried them and put them away and wiped the table where the crumbs from their sandwiches were still scattered. The jam she liked was still out. He put that away too, and then stood at the window and looked at the grounds, which were silver with frost under a sky that was almost, not quite, clear enough for stars. The December dark pressed against the glass, and inside the house was warm — not the charged warmth the Manor sometimes produced, just the ordinary warmth of a building that had people in it and fires lit and a kettle that worked.

Somewhere above him she was working and her door was open and the library fire she'd started that morning was still burning. He stood at the window for a long time and didn't name anything, and the house settled around him, and the dark came down, and he let it.


# Chapter 11

They had developed a routine for the examinations, and the routine itself was a kind of intimacy neither of them acknowledged.

She would set out her diagnostic instruments on the library's long table — the resonance crystals, the notation ledger, the stabilization framework she'd designed specifically for the Mark's magical signature. He would roll up his left sleeve and lay his forearm on the padded surface they'd improvised from a folded cloak, and then he would pick up whatever he was reading with his free hand, as if the examination were no more notable than having his pulse taken at a routine physical.

It hadn't always been like this. The first examinations had been rigid, formal — his jaw set, her instructions precise, both of them maintaining the clinical frame with an effort that was itself a kind of confession. Now he read during examinations. She hummed sometimes, without meaning to, when the diagnostic results were interesting. He'd told her once that the humming was how he knew she'd found something, and she'd been self-conscious about it for two days before deciding that self-consciousness about humming was its own pointless category of intimacy she didn't need.

This morning the library was doing its usual thing — fire lit, lamps adjusted to the grey December light, the room's configuration subtly rearranged since yesterday so that the working table sat closer to the hearth and the windows showed a slightly different angle of the grounds. Frost on the lawns. The last leaves gone from the elms. She'd stopped trying to catalogue the Manor's daily spatial adjustments. The changes were always minor, always oriented toward comfort, and paying too much attention to them meant thinking about why the house was so invested in their comfort, which was a question she'd been skirting since Narcissa's visit.

"Ready?" She placed her hands on either side of his forearm, fingers bracketing the Mark.

"Mm." He didn't look up from his book.

She cast the diagnostic. The spell settled over the Mark like a second skin, translucent, mapping the magical layers underneath. The top layer — Voldemort's binding spell, degraded, its network anchor dissolved — registered as a fading lattice, dark and intricate, losing coherence at the edges. Below that, the self-perpetuating Dark magic that fed on Draco's own core, parasitic and adaptive. And below everything, the layer she still couldn't classify — older than the casting, deeper than the curse, strengthening where everything above it was decaying.

"Third layer's shifted again," she said, adjusting her grip. "Hold still."

"I am still."

"You just —" She stopped. He hadn't moved. What she'd felt was his pulse, which had always been there under her thumb, and the diagnostic's sensitivity had increased to the point where his circulatory system registered as movement in her spell field. She was calibrated too finely. "Sorry. Never mind."

He glanced at her over the top of his book. A brief, assessing look — deciding whether to ask. He decided not to. Looked back down.

She pushed the diagnostic deeper. The Mark's magic resisted at the second-layer boundary — it always resisted, the Dark magic protective of its own infrastructure — and then the resistance gave way in the specific reluctant unfurling she'd come to recognize. Like a fist releasing one finger at a time. The deeper layers exposed themselves to her diagnostic, and she mapped them with the precision that was the one thing about these examinations she still fully trusted.

The base layer was stronger this week. She noted it in the ledger without commentary, recording the measurement alongside the previous week's readings. A clear upward trend. The unidentified magic at the bottom of the Mark was growing, and its growth correlated with — she checked the dates — every sustained examination session. Every hour of her diagnostic contact with his arm.

She didn't write that correlation in the ledger.

"I'm going to probe the base layer directly," she said. "You might feel pressure."

"I always feel pressure. That's what the Mark does."

"Different pressure. This is the diagnostic interacting with the deeper —"

"Just do it, Granger."

He said it without irritation. It was the register they'd found after the drawing room — shorter with each other, less padded, but not unkind. She'd stopped noticing when "Granger" had shifted from a wall to something else. Something he used the way you'd use a name you'd gotten comfortable saying.

She pushed the diagnostic into the base layer.

The Mark opened.

And the room doubled.

She was in the library. She could see the table, her own hands, his forearm, the ledger, the fire. And simultaneously, overlaid like a transparency laid over a photograph, she was somewhere else — the same library, the same table, but from the other side. She was seeing the room from his angle. She could feel the book's edge where his right hand held it — the leather binding, slightly cracked, a ridge pressing into his middle finger. She could feel the padded surface under his forearm, warm from the hours of his body heat. She could feel the place where her thumb rested against his wrist and it was — the sensation of being touched, registered from his side, specific and present in a way that told her his attention had not been on the book for some time.

He was aware of her. Not as a diagnostician or a researcher or a colleague. Aware of her the way a body is aware of another body in close proximity — the weight of her, the heat of her, the ink on her fingers and the wool of the jumper she was wearing. From inside his experience, she understood that he'd been letting these details accumulate — not studying them, nothing so deliberate, but the way a room gathers the scent of whatever's been burning in its hearth. Slowly. Without having decided to.

And underneath that — she almost lost it, almost couldn't hold the doubled awareness, but for one bright terrible second she felt his awareness of the Mark under her hand. Not the pain of it, though the pain was there, a constant low-grade burn he'd stopped bothering to register. The shame. The old sick shame of the thing branded into his arm, and laid against it, pressing down on it like a hand on a wound, the precise relief of her touch. How the Mark quieted when she touched it. How he'd come to associate the absence of pain with the specific pressure of her fingers. How the two had become, in his body, the same thing — her presence and the absence of hurt — tangled together below the level of language, where the body kept its own accounts.

Three seconds. Maybe five.

She pulled her hand off his arm as if the Mark had burned her.

The library snapped back to a single perspective. Her own. The fire, the table, the frost outside. Her hands in her lap, the tips of her fingers buzzing with residual sensation — not pain, but the disorientation of having been in two places at once and now being in only one.

Draco put the book down. The studied casualness was gone. He was looking at her and his face had the expression she'd come to associate with his most careful management — controlled, attentive, a slight tension at the corners of his mouth that meant he was working to keep something off his face.

"What just happened."

Not a question. Or a question that had already arrived at its own answer and was only asking for confirmation.

"Resonance feedback," she said. Her voice was even. She was good at even. "The diagnostic created a sympathetic channel — it happens with sustained —"

"You flinched."

"I didn't —"

"You pulled your hand away like I'd burned you. That wasn't resonance feedback. What happened?"

She looked at him. His grey eyes, lighter near the fire. The sharp angles of his face, the jaw she'd watched soften over weeks of meals he no longer ate alone. His forearm on the table, the Mark exposed, black against pale skin, and the memory of what it had felt like to be inside his experience — the accumulated attention, the specific quiet want that he kept pressed down so far inside himself that she wasn't sure he fully recognized it as want.

She could not tell him what she'd felt. Not because it was clinical data she needed to process, but because what she'd experienced from inside his body was something private he had not chosen to show her, and she had no right to it. The shame and the relief — those were his. The way her touch had become synonymous with the absence of pain in whatever subconscious ledger his body was keeping — that was his, and he didn't know she'd read it, and telling him would be a violation she couldn't dress up as science.

"The resonance is bidirectional now," she said. "That's new. I could feel sensory input from your — from your perspective. Temperature. Pressure. It lasted a few seconds."

"What did you feel?"

The question was quiet. He asked it the way he asked things that mattered to him — directly, without the usual deflection, the effort of it visible in the stillness of his hands on the table.

"Peripheral sensation," she said. "Your hand on the book. The table surface. It was disorienting, that's all. I wasn't expecting it."

He looked at her for a long moment. She could feel him deciding whether to push — she could actually feel it, the faint deliberation that the resonance was now broadcasting whether she wanted to receive it or not — and she watched him decide to let it go. The decision cost him something. She could feel that too.

"If the channel's opening both ways," he said, "you should probably note that in the — whatever it is you're recording."

"The diagnostic ledger. Yes."

"Right." He picked up his book. Put it down again. "Should we — is it safe to continue the examinations, if the resonance is —"

"It's manageable."

"You just flinched hard enough to —"

"It's manageable, Malfoy. I was surprised. I won't be next time."

He looked at her. She looked back. The fire cracked and resettled and the sound was loud in the library's quiet.

"Okay," he said. And then, after a pause that almost wasn't there: "If this is getting worse, we can — I'll figure something else out."

"I know." She started packing the diagnostic instruments. The crystals went into their velvet-lined case, the stabilization framework collapsed into its compact form. Her hands had stopped buzzing. "I know."

She didn't say: the problem is that I want to continue, and I can't tell anymore whether the wanting is mine.

---

She spent the rest of the afternoon in the main library while Draco worked in the gated archive. They'd discovered that the archive room would hold for him alone now in short stretches — an hour, sometimes two, the walls going vague at the edges as if the room were a held breath slowly releasing. She'd been grateful for the intervals apart, though she wouldn't have used that word. The separation let the resonance dim to a low background hum — his location in the house, his wakefulness, the general texture of his attention — manageable, something she could nearly fold into the peripheral awareness where it didn't require examination.

Today the hum wouldn't settle. Three rooms away and a floor above, she could feel him in the archive with a clarity that was new. Not just the fact of his presence but the quality of his concentration — the focused steadiness when a text engaged him, a slight cooler shift when something confused or frustrated him, a thing she could only describe as brightness when he found a connection, a pattern, an answer.

She put down her quill and sat with it. She was carrying another person's cognitive weather inside her body. This was not resonance echo. This was not a known artifact of diagnostic contact with Dark objects. The literature she'd cited to herself for weeks described fading echoes, not amplification. Not this slow steady deepening where each examination left the channel wider, more detailed, more precise.

She couldn't ignore what the resonance had become. She was holding the peripheral awareness of another person inside her body, and the other person was beginning to hold hers, and neither of them had consented to this and neither of them was stopping it. The examinations continued because the research required them. The research required them because the Mark was degrading in ways that needed monitoring. The monitoring required her hands on his arm, which required proximity, which deepened the resonance, which made the next examination more intimate, which — she could see the spiral. She'd been able to see it for weeks. She kept choosing to step onto it anyway.

That was the part she really couldn't attribute to diagnostic artifact.

Work. The folios on the table in front of her were from the lower shelves of the main library — not the gated archive, which required his presence, but the general collection. Lesser texts. Malfoy family administrative records from the seventeenth and eighteenth centuries. Property surveys. Warding logs. Correspondence about ley-line routing with the Wizarding Infrastructure Authority, which had apparently existed in some form since the 1600s and was just as bureaucratically constipated then as its modern descendant.

She'd been working through these for a week, building a baseline picture of the Manor's magical engineering from the ground up. Foundation spells. Warding architecture. The structural magic that kept the house standing for centuries and maintained the ambient enchantments — heating, preservation, wards, and whatever else the house was doing — all functioning simultaneously. Genuinely useful work. Genuinely boring. The combination she found most restful because useful meant it advanced the research and boring meant it didn't engage the parts of her mind that had been circling the question she couldn't answer.

She was three folios deep in a surveyor's report on ley-line connections — dated 1743, meticulous, the magical notation standard for the period — when the text changed.

Not abruptly. The surveyor had been noting anomalies in the Manor's warding structure, places where the standard protective enchantments intersected with something older and more complex. A marginal annotation, in a different hand: *see Binding Folio, shelf 14-C, re: the resonance architecture and its maintenance.* Cross-referenced to a catalogue number that didn't match the library's current system.

She followed the reference. Shelf 14-C was behind the property records, a lower section she'd catalogued but hadn't fully examined — the folios there had been filed under Estate Administration, and she'd assumed they were more of the same. She pulled the volumes until she found one whose catalogue mark matched: a plain black folio, unadorned leather, the binding old enough that the stitching had been replaced at least twice. The kind of plain binding that usually signified either genuine modesty or deliberately hidden value.

She carried it back to the table, cleared a space between the surveyor's folios, and opened it.

The text was archaic magical notation — dense, semi-pictographic, the script that predated modern standard by several centuries. She could read it. Slowly. Word by word, building each sentence in her mind, the meaning assembling itself after the grammar like a photograph developing in solution.

The title page:

*On the Binding Architecture of the Manor and Its Proper Maintenance for the Perpetuation of the Line.*

She didn't close the folio. Her hands on the leather were steady. The quill she'd put down lay exactly where she'd left it, the ink drying on the nib. She read.

The first section described the Manor's purpose. Not as shelter. Not as fortress. As instrument. The house had been designed — the text used the word *wrought*, in a construction that implied magical engineering rather than physical construction — to sustain the Malfoy magical line across generations. The mechanism was bonding architecture: the house's magical infrastructure was built to read the emotional resonance between its inhabitants, to identify connections with potential for deep magical bonding, and to amplify those connections through environmental response.

She translated the next passage three times to be certain.

*The architecture shall respond to the resonance between inhabitants by adjusting its conditions to favor the bond's development. Warmth shall attend proximity. Cold shall attend separation. The deeper chambers shall open to those whose bond has achieved sufficient strength, that the work of the line may proceed under the most favorable influence. The resonance shall be measured through the quality and frequency of sustained contact, through the depth of emotional proximity, and through the willingness of both parties to remain within the architecture.*

The gated archive. The room that held only when both of them were present. The door that hadn't existed until the examination where his pulse was under her thumb and the Mark quieted under her touch and the diagnostic reached a layer of magic that shouldn't have been there.

She read on.

The bonding architecture worked on genuine emotional resonance. This was stated clearly, almost as a limitation — the house could not fabricate feeling from nothing. *Where no seed exists, the architecture shall find no purchase.* But where resonance existed, the house could amplify it. Temperature. Spatial configuration. Access gating. The comfort of proximity and the discomfort of distance — all of it calibrated by the house's magic, reading the emotional state of its inhabitants and adjusting conditions to encourage and deepen the bond.

A section on the amplification mechanism described the process in terms that were technical but legible: the architecture created feedback loops. Positive feelings between inhabitants were reflected back through the house's environmental response — warmth, comfort, open corridors, accessible rooms — which reinforced the positive feelings, which the house then amplified further. The feedback was designed to be invisible. The text used a phrase she translated as *seamless integration*: the amplified feelings should be indistinguishable from organic ones. The inhabitants should not be able to tell where their genuine resonance ended and the house's amplification began.

She pressed her palms flat against the table on either side of the folio and looked at the ceiling. The library ceiling was high, dark, the beams lost in shadow above the lamplight. She looked at it for a full minute before she could make herself look back down.

The section on channels of awareness was next. When inhabitants of the Manor were in the process of forming a deep bond, the architecture created mutual awareness between them — a channel through which each could sense the other's proximity, emotional state, and physical condition. The channels deepened with sustained contact. They were the house's monitoring system, its way of measuring the bond's progress, and also its way of accelerating it: the awareness itself became a bonding mechanism, intimacy imposed through involuntary knowledge of the other person's inner weather.

The Mark resonance. Every week of sensing his proximity, his pain, his wakefulness. The way she knew which floor he was on without thinking about it. The way she could feel his concentration from three rooms away, right now, as if the distance between them were tissue-thin. The examination that morning — feeling him feel her — was the channel widening, the architecture opening more bandwidth between them, and the mutual sensation she'd flinched from was the house showing her how far the bond had already progressed.

She turned another page. The text described maintenance protocols — how the bonding architecture should be renewed, what to do if the resonance stalled or reversed, the conditions under which the house might redirect its amplification from one pair of inhabitants to another. A passage near the end caught her and held:

*The architecture shall prove most efficacious when the inhabitants are unaware of its influence. Should the bonded pair come to understand the mechanism, the architecture cannot compel continuation. The bond must, in its final stages, become a matter of will. The house can cultivate. It cannot command.*

An architect's manual. Written by someone who understood the house as an instrument and the people inside it as material. Raw material for the line the house was built to perpetuate. And someone who had been honest enough, or arrogant enough, to document the system's single limitation: it worked best in the dark. Awareness was the one thing the architecture couldn't compensate for.

She closed the folio.

The library was silent except for the fire, which burned the way the fire always burned — evenly, steadily, the house maintaining the precise temperature that made staying comfortable and leaving feel like loss. Her tea sat cold at her elbow. Outside the tall windows, full dark. She'd been reading for hours.

Above her, in the archive, Draco shifted. She felt it the way she'd felt it all afternoon — the quality of his attention changing, a transition from deep focus to the vaguer register of someone surfacing from concentrated work. He'd be coming down soon. He usually did around this time, when the archive's hold started to weaken and the walls went soft at the edges.

She had known his rhythms for weeks. She'd called this familiarity — the natural product of sharing space with someone day after day. The way you learn the sounds of a housemate, their patterns, the shape of their day. She'd let herself find comfort in it.

It was the house.

It had been the house the entire time. The resonance she'd attributed to diagnostic artifact. The corridors that routed her past his door. The fire that lit itself when they were together. The comfort she'd been letting herself feel in this place where she'd once been held down and carved open — all of it the bonding architecture doing exactly what it was designed to do. Reading whatever existed between them and amplifying it. Making proximity feel natural. Making the thought of leaving feel like cost.

Every moment of genuine connection was now suspect.

The intellectual intimacy in the library — was that them, or the house creating conditions where their minds worked better together? The post-drawing-room tenderness — real grief and real gentleness, or the architecture accelerating emotional recovery to preserve the bond? The way she'd felt when she caught him watching her with something careful and unguarded in his face — was that want, or was it the house's interpretation of what want should feel like, fed back to her through a channel she hadn't known existed?

The examination that morning. Feeling him feel her. The accumulated attention, the quiet desire he carried so deep inside himself. Was that the channel widening because the house wanted it wider? Had she been reaching for the deeper layers of the Mark, or had the house been guiding her diagnostic spells toward the exact contact that would create maximum resonance?

She didn't know. That was the thing she kept arriving at, the wall at the end of every line of reasoning. She could not tell. The folio described the mechanism but did not distinguish between what the house amplified and what the inhabitants felt on their own. The architecture was designed to make the distinction impossible. *Seamless integration.* The whole point was that you couldn't tell. The architect had built the house so that its manipulation would be indistinguishable from the inhabitants' own feelings, and that design was working exactly as intended, and she was inside it, and she could not find the seam.

She thought about the morning's examination. The shame she'd felt from inside his body — the old, ground-in shame of the Mark. That had been real. She was sure of that. The house couldn't fabricate a sixteen-year-old boy's horror at what was being branded into his arm. But the relief — his association of her touch with the absence of pain — had that developed on its own, through weeks of her diagnostic contact genuinely quieting the Mark's restless magic? Or had the house been amplifying the relief, deepening the association, making her hands feel more soothing than they actually were so that his body would learn to need her specifically?

She couldn't tell. She could not tell.

She slid the folio back behind the property records where she'd found it. She stacked the surveyor's reports neatly on top, aligning the edges. She carried her cold tea to the sideboard and set it down with the other used cups.

She should tell him. The obligation was immediate and structural — a load-bearing requirement she could feel the way she felt all obligations, as a thing that, if left unmet, would compromise everything built on top of it. He was inside this architecture. His feelings were being shaped by the same mechanism. He lived here. This was his family's house, his family's magic, his inheritance manipulating his emotional life without his knowledge or consent. He had a right to the information she was holding.

She should tell him. She was going to tell him.

His footsteps in the corridor above — the archive releasing him, the house rearranging to accommodate his movement toward the library. The resonance brightened as he got closer. She could feel the specific quality of his approach — unhurried, a slight stiffness in his left shoulder from hours of leaning over texts, the particular looseness that came after a productive session when he'd found something that engaged him enough to forget his own discomfort.

He appeared in the library doorway. Pale, angular, his sleeves still rolled from archive work, the Mark visible on his forearm. He looked tired in the way he always looked tired — not dramatically, just the permanent slight depletion of someone who slept badly and had stopped expecting otherwise. But under the tiredness, something else. An openness he'd been allowing more since the drawing room, since the quiet days after it, since they'd stopped pretending that shared meals were a matter of scheduling convenience.

"The archive's fighting me again," he said. "The back wall went soft around four. I had to transcribe the last three pages from memory before the text dissolved." He crossed to the sideboard, saw her cup among the others, glanced at her. "Have you eaten?"

"Not yet."

"It's past seven."

"I know."

He looked at her. The look was — she understood now what the look was. She had a folio's worth of technical vocabulary for it. The architecture reading his emotional state and amplifying it, the house reflecting his concern back through environmental cues that encouraged him to act on it, the whole system designed so that his impulse to feed her would feel natural and uncomplicated and his own.

Or it was just him, worried about her because she forgot to eat when she was working and he'd noticed this about her because he'd been paying attention and that was what people did when they cared.

"I'll get something from the kitchen," he said. "You look like you've been staring at the same page for three hours."

"Different pages," she said. "Many different pages."

"Right." He almost smiled — not quite, the near-miss that was his default when she said something that wasn't funny enough to earn a real one but familiar enough to register. "Anything useful?"

She thought of the folio behind the property records. The bonding architecture. The feedback loops and the seamless integration and the channels of mutual awareness that she could feel right now, broadcasting his proximity and his tiredness and his genuine, uncomplicated desire to make sure she ate something.

"Property records," she said. "Administrative stuff. Foundation engineering. Nothing exciting."

He waited a beat, as if he expected her to add something. She didn't. She held his gaze and felt the resonance between them carrying the texture of his mild curiosity — not suspicion, nothing as sharp as that, just the attentiveness of someone who'd learned to read her and was registering that today's reading didn't quite parse.

"Right," he said. "I'll be back."

He went to the kitchen.

She stood in the library and listened to his footsteps down the corridor and she felt the distance between them stretch in the resonance — his presence dimming slightly, not gone, never gone now, but reduced to the low hum that was her new permanent baseline. She'd called this normal. She'd called this the inevitable result of diagnostic contact with Dark artifacts, a known phenomenon, well-documented. Every name she'd given it had been a way of not reading the folio she'd just read.

He came back with sandwiches, the bread cut at an angle because he'd apparently decided at some point that this was how bread should be cut. A fresh cup of tea for her, properly hot. He set the plate on the table between her folios and sat across from her and picked up his book and the quiet domestic fact of it — two people eating in a library, the fire going, the night dark outside — was so precisely the thing she'd been letting herself want that the wanting sat in her chest like a stone.

She ate a sandwich. It was good. He'd put too much butter on it, which was how she liked it, and he knew this because they'd been sharing meals for weeks and he'd been paying the kind of attention she now understood might be the house's attention routed through his nervous system. Or the attention of a man who lived alone and had suddenly found himself feeding someone and was quietly, carefully good at it. The two explanations sat on top of each other like the doubled perspective of the morning's examination, and she could not peel them apart.

Or his attention. His own. There was no way to tell. The folio had been very clear about that.

"You're quiet," he said, not looking up.

"I'm eating."

"You're usually quiet while eating. This is a different quiet."

She looked at him across the table. The firelight catching the angles of his face. The book propped in his hand. The Mark on his forearm, exposed because he'd stopped rolling his sleeve down around her — a trust so small and so specific that she'd been thinking about it for days, the decision to leave uncovered the thing he was most ashamed of because he'd decided she was safe.

"Tired," she said. "Long day with the property records."

"The property records that were nothing exciting."

"They're still nothing exciting. I'm just thorough."

"I know." He said it simply, without weight, and the absence of weight was the weight — the ease of knowing someone, the unremarkable fact of having learned their patterns. "Try to sleep before midnight for once."

"I'll try."

She went to her room. The corridors were lit and well-heated, the house routing her through the shared spaces, past the kitchen, past the wing where he slept. She noticed the route. She'd always noticed. She'd called it the Manor's odd sense of direction, or efficient architecture, or nothing at all, and every name she'd given it had been a way of not naming the thing the folio named.

She closed her door. The bed had been turned down — the house had been doing this for weeks, and she'd told herself it was residual enchantment and she'd told herself it was just a well-maintained old house and she'd told herself it was nothing, and none of those thoughts had been quite dishonest enough to qualify as lies but none of them had been straight either.

She sat on the bed in her clothes and felt Draco three floors above settling into his room, the resonance transmitting the slow dimming of his attention as he moved toward sleep. The house held them both. The house measured the space between them and found it, even now, insufficient for its purposes, and she could feel the subtle architectural pressure — a warmth, a pull, a corridor-length adjustment she could sense rather than see — encouraging her to be closer.

She should tell him.

She was going to tell him, after she verified the text. The notation was archaic. Her translation might be inaccurate. She needed to cross-reference the folio against other sources, confirm that the bonding architecture it described was still active, check whether centuries of modification had altered or disabled the mechanism. She was a researcher. Verification was not avoidance. Verification was the responsible course of action before raising an alarm about something that might, on closer examination, turn out to be—

She stopped.

She sat on the bed and listened to herself think and she heard what she was doing. She was building a structure — careful, logical, brick by brick — a framework of reasonable justification that would let her keep the knowledge locked inside her own head, where it wouldn't require action, where it wouldn't force a conversation she wasn't ready to have.

She wasn't going to verify. Not soon, not urgently, not the way verification required — which was telling him what she'd found and working through it together, subjecting the text to the collaborative rigor that was the only honest approach to a finding this significant. She was going to keep the knowledge because keeping it preserved the framework where she was the researcher and this was a problem and her feelings — the tenderness she'd been allowing, the want she'd been not-examining, the specific warmth she felt when he brought her tea without asking how she took it and got it right — could remain in the category of data rather than something she had to decide about.

If she told him, they would have to name what existed between them. She was not ready to name it. She was especially not ready to name it now that the naming itself was compromised. Was the pull toward naming it hers or the house's? Was her reluctance to name it a genuine scruple or the architecture prolonging the bonding process, keeping feelings unexamined because unexamined feelings were easier to amplify?

She couldn't tell. The house had been designed so she couldn't tell. And the recursion — I can't tell whether my feelings are real, and I can't tell whether my inability to tell is a product of the architecture — fit so precisely into the shape of her own specific weakness that it felt almost personal. She'd spent her entire life needing to understand before she acted. Needing to verify before she committed. Needing certainty before she let herself feel anything she couldn't take back. And the house had built a trap that was her exact shape: a doubt that could never be fully resolved because the instrument of resolution was itself compromised.

She pulled the blanket over her legs without undressing. Above her, through the resonance, Draco's awareness had gone quiet — the slow, diffuse texture of sleep. He was dreaming, or close to it. She could feel the edges of his unconscious attention the way you could feel the heat of a banked fire from across a room — faint, ambient, impossible to ignore once you knew it was there.

Tomorrow she would wake up and examine his Mark. She would eat his sandwiches. She would sit across from him in the library and feel his presence through the resonance and she would do all of it under knowledge she was not sharing. And the word for that was not verification. The word for that was not prudence or due diligence or responsible scholarship.

She knew the word. She was precise enough, and honest enough with herself if with no one else, to name it.

The house was warm around her. The house was always warm. She lay in the dark and let the lie stand and she did not sleep.


# Chapter 12

The corridor to the library was twenty-three paces on Tuesday. By Thursday it was forty-one.

Draco counted because counting was something he could do — a small, precise task for the front of his mind while the rest of it paced through darker territory. Forty-one paces. The walls unchanged, the same grey stone, the same sconces casting their steady amber glow, but the distances between things had stretched, the house breathing out, making room in the way large animals make room: slowly, without explanation, as if the new arrangement had always been there. He did not want room. He wanted the corridors tight and warm the way they had been last week, when she would come around a corner with a translation in her hands and look up and say something about a fourteenth-century suffix and he would pretend he had not been counting the minutes since her last appearance.

The gated archive would not open. He went on Wednesday morning, early, before she arrived at the library — testing, because the room had always required both of them and he knew it, but he went anyway. The alcove in the library's south wall was blank. Stone, seamless, cool under his palm when he pressed it. No door. No seam. No suggestion that a door had ever existed in this wall. He stood there with his hand flat against the stone for longer than made sense, as if his own warmth could coax the house into yielding, and the stone gave nothing back.

He tried once more on Thursday. Same wall, same nothing. The Manor held the research they needed behind a condition he could not meet alone, and the condition was her, and she had gone somewhere he could not follow.

The temperature was the other thing. Not the ordinary cold of early winter in a stone house — he had grown up with that cold, knew its textures, the way it pooled in certain corridors and climbed your legs if you stood still too long. This was different. Specific. The heating charms still worked; fires still burned. But the warmth stopped at the edges of whatever room he occupied, pooling in a tight radius around the grate and fading before it reached the walls. Walking from the library to his rooms was like crossing a series of thermal borders — warm pocket, cold passage, warm pocket — each warm space smaller than it should have been, the house rationing its heat in a way that felt deliberate. Like a body conserving warmth when it thinks it's failing.

He did not mention any of this to her.

She was still in the Manor. She was still working. She arrived at the library at nine each morning and left by five, her notes squared and stacked at the edge of her table, her quill cleaned and laid in its leather case, the chair pushed in with the precise geometry of someone who was finished for the day rather than merely stepping away. The tidiness was new. A week ago she had left books open on the table, quills bleeding ink onto the blotting paper, her cardigan thrown over the chair back. A week ago her presence in the library had been porous, expansive — she took up space, spread outward, left traces. Now she was compact. Every sign of herself gathered and removed when she left, as if she were a guest who knew she might not be invited back.

Draco had gone through every conversation. Three days of them — not very many, which was the problem. The change had happened between one evening and the next morning, no conversation to mark the boundary, no argument, no miscalculated word he could identify and retract. They had been working on a sixteenth-century text about ambient field theory, and she had been close, her shoulder near his, and she had said something about the semantics of a word that meant both *to anchor* and *to bind*, and he had suggested a connection to an older Malfoy notation, and she had looked at him with the expression that was the best thing in his days — the one that meant her mind was moving fast and he was keeping up — and then the next morning she was brisk, professional, present only when the work demanded it and gone the moment it did not.

The obvious explanation was the drawing room. Not the confrontation itself — three weeks past, the rawness scabbed over, the things they had said settling into the silt of what they now knew about each other — but what it had opened up. Maybe the aftermath had been too gentle. Maybe the domestic register of the past week, the shared meals and the books left on his reading chair and the tea he made without asking, had alarmed her in its ease. Maybe she had looked at whatever was growing between them and decided the roots were in poisoned soil. She would not have been wrong. The soil in this house was very old and had held terrible things.

Or maybe it was simpler than that. He was a Malfoy and she was Hermione Granger and the distance between those facts was always going to win.

He matched her withdrawal with his own. He had been matching things his entire life — his father's formality, his mother's composure, the social temperature of every room he entered — and he matched her distance, and hated every word of the performance because it was a performance and because he was good at it and because being good at this particular thing meant he would never be the one to break first and say *what happened, why have you gone.*

He passed her in a corridor on Wednesday afternoon. She was carrying an armful of texts from the second-floor stacks, her chin propped on the top volume to keep it from sliding, and he was going nowhere in particular — just walking, learning the Manor's changed distances, because if the house was going to rearrange itself around him the least he could do was learn the new map. She nodded. He nodded. She said, "I'll be in the library until four," which was information rather than invitation, and he said, "Right," and she passed, and the corridor behind her was warmer than the corridor ahead of him, a trail of residual heat that the house left in her wake. He stood in it for a moment, the warmth thin and fading. Then it was gone, and the stone was just stone again.

The meals were the worst part. They had been eating together — not by arrangement but by the gradual collapse of the pretense that they were merely sharing a kitchen by coincidence. It had become a thing: the small dining room, Narcissa's dining room, the one meant for family rather than display. Sitting across from each other and talking about the research or about nothing. She ate slowly, read while she ate, sometimes looked up to say something about a passage and sometimes forgot to look up at all. He had learned to read the quality of her concentration by the angle of her head and the stillness of her fork, and all of this was gone now. She ate early or alone or somewhere he wasn't, and the dining room was too large for one person, and the silence in it was ordinary silence, and ordinary silence was worse than the other kind because the other kind at least meant the house was paying attention.

On Thursday night he sat at the table and ate what the elves had prepared — warm, adequate, he did not taste it — and the fire was built high and still the warmth failed at the walls, and the chair across from him was empty, and he considered pouring himself a drink and decided against it. Alcohol and self-pity were a Malfoy tradition he was trying to break.

The Mark was worse. That was the concrete thing, the measurable thing, the thing that existed outside his interpretive spiraling. Without the sustained diagnostic contact of their regular examinations, the dark magic was restless again — not the old summoning burn, which had been sharp and purposeful and at least made a kind of sense, but the new ache, the one that felt like magic trying to move through channels that no longer connected to anything. The ache thickened each evening and kept him awake, a slow persistent pressure in the bones of his forearm that radiated to his shoulder if he let it. During the examinations, her spells quieted it — the dark magic going still under her diagnostic contact like a restless animal under a specific hand. Without the examinations, the restlessness built, and built, and he could feel it now at the dinner table, the Mark pulsing its slow idiot pain, and the pain was a reminder that the arrangement they had set — library access for Mark help — required her hands on his arm at regular intervals, and she had pulled away, and the Mark did not care about her reasons.

He lay awake that night in his rooms. The bed was cold — the sheets, the heavy duvet, the stone walls bleeding winter into the room despite the fire he kept going. The Manor's upper floors had always been inconsistent, the warming charms layered over centuries and never quite adequate, but this was worse. The house was withdrawing from him the way she had withdrawn, and he lay in the gap between the two absences and pressed his thumb into the Mark through his sleeve and the pain sharpened briefly, a hot flare, and then settled back into the ache. The Mark remembered her. The dark magic had been given something it wanted — her touch, her spells, the specific frequency of whatever her diagnostic magic did to the curse — and now the magic was being denied, and the denial was pain.

He stared at the ceiling and thought about the last look she'd given him before the mask went up, on Monday evening, the fraction of a second before the professional distance clicked into place. The expression underneath had been — he couldn't name it. It had looked, for a moment, like guilt.

He did not sleep. At some point after midnight the Mark flared — not the sharp emergency of the old summons but a long, slow burn that spread from his forearm into his shoulder and sat there, radiating, the curse reminding him of its residency. He got up and stood at the window in the dark. The grounds were silver with frost, the hedges casting moon-shadows across the lawn, and the Manor was enormous and silent around him, and the silence was the kind that could drive a person to conversation with the walls. He had done that, in the first months after the war, when his mother had moved out and the house was his alone. He had talked to the corridors, to the walls, to the empty rooms. He had asked the library to open doors. He had negotiated with the architecture like a man talking to God — uncertain whether anyone was listening, unable to stop. He had not done it in months. The fact that the impulse was back told him something about what her presence had changed and what her withdrawal was undoing.

He went back to bed and lay in the cold sheets and waited for morning, and the Mark ached, and the house was silent, and the silence was not the kind that meant peace.

Friday he was in the library before her. She stopped in the doorway, her coat still buttoned — she had taken to keeping her coat on longer, a layer of *outside* between herself and the house — and she looked at him and he looked at her and for a moment the distance between them was just distance, crossable, ordinary.

"The diagnostic series," she said. "We've let the interval run too long."

She was right. The examinations had a rhythm — every three to four days — and the rhythm was broken.

"I know," he said.

"After lunch. The usual setup."

"Fine."

She unbuttoned her coat, crossed to her table — the one she had established six feet from his, the gap new as of this week, a measured distance that hadn't existed when they worked shoulder to shoulder — and began unpacking her satchel. Quill, ink, notes. The small precise motions of someone who had already moved past the conversation.

He watched her for a moment. Then he looked down at the text he had been failing to read.

---

They set up after lunch at the worktable by the tall windows. The arrangement was familiar: table cleared, her instruments laid out in a neat row — wand, the set of diagnostic crystals she had calibrated to the Mark's magical frequency, the probe resonator she had built herself and handled with the particular care of someone who had made a thing and knew exactly what it could do, a clean sheet of parchment for notations. She was efficient about it. Professional. He had watched her prepare for examinations a dozen times now and the ritual was steady — the way she squared the parchment, checked the crystal alignment, rolled her sleeves to the elbow with two practiced motions. Right sleeve first, pushed up with her left hand. Then left sleeve — though the left always came down further, the fabric folded one extra turn, positioned with more precision. He had noticed this before without understanding it.

"Sleeve," she said.

He rolled his left sleeve to the elbow and laid his arm on the table, palm up. The Mark was vivid today, the snake and skull sharper-edged than they had been in weeks, the skin around the lines faintly inflamed. The dark magic in it was agitated, the ache visible in the way the lines seemed to pulse, and she would see that. She would register the deterioration without his having to say it.

Her fingers circled his wrist. Cold. The library was cold and her hands carried it, and the jolt of her skin against his after three days of nothing was worse than he had braced for. He kept his face still.

"Your hands are cold."

"The library's cold."

"It is."

She pressed her thumb to the inside of his wrist — the pulse point, her starting baseline. His heartbeat under her thumb before the first spell was even cast. He looked at the window. Outside, the grounds were iron-grey under low cloud, the hedges black with frost, the lawn colourless. The light was already thinning — dark by half three today. They had maybe an hour of daylight before the candles would have to carry the room.

"Standard series first," she said. "Then I want to run the deeper mapping again. The decay rate in the secondary channels — I've been reviewing last week's data and the pattern's not—" She caught herself, trimmed the sentence back. "I want to confirm something."

"All right."

The first diagnostic charm settled over the Mark like a net of pale blue light, fine-grained, the spell catching on the dark lines and illuminating the magical substrate beneath. The dark magic surged once — the habitual resistance, the curse pushing back — and then quieted. It always quieted for her. The restless ache that he'd lived with for three days eased under her spell like a cramp releasing, the pressure in his forearm dropping, and his hand unclenched on the table before he could stop it.

She noticed. She always noticed. "The pain's been worse."

"Manageable."

"Your knuckles were white, Malfoy."

He didn't answer that. She moved to the second spell, a mapping charm that traced the Mark's deeper magical architecture and projected it as a luminous overlay above his skin — a three-dimensional rendering of the curse layers Voldemort had embedded, the channels connecting the Mark to the ambient magical field. She studied this overlay with the same focused intensity she gave everything — her wand moving in slow precise arcs over his forearm, her left hand holding his arm steady at the wrist.

The mapping proceeded. She recorded readings, murmured notations to the self-scribing quill that hovered beside the parchment, adjusted the crystal alignment twice when the probe resonator's numbers drifted. He watched her work because he could. During the examinations her attention was on the Mark and not on him, and the arrangement gave him the one form of proximity the withdrawal had not taken — permission to look while being looked at. He knew her working expressions: the slight furrow between her eyes when a reading came back unexpected. The way she held her lower lip between her teeth when parsing something dense. The half-squint against the spell light when she was too absorbed to shade her eyes. He knew that she mouthed translations to herself and didn't know she was doing it, her lips forming silent words in Latin and sometimes in something older, and he knew the sound her breathing made when she was concentrating hard — slower, deliberate, as if she were rationing her focus.

He made himself look away from her face and watched her hands instead. Her right hand holding the wand, tracing the diagnostic patterns. Her left hand on his wrist, fingers wrapped around his forearm just below the Mark, holding the arm in position. Her left sleeve had been pushed back for the work, the cuff folded neatly above her wrist, and the skin of her forearm was smooth and unblemished in the candlelight — the same forearm it had been in every examination, the careful surface she showed the world.

She pushed deeper today. The diagnostic spells shifted from the Mark's visible surface into the substrate — older magic underneath Voldemort's brand, something that predated the Dark Lord and connected to the Manor's own magical infrastructure. This was the work they had been doing before her retreat: mapping the deep channels that linked the Mark's residual curse to the ambient field's decay. It required sustained output. Her wand traced analytical patterns over his forearm while she simultaneously maintained the mapping charm and fed data to the recording quill — three concurrent spell operations, each demanding its own thread of concentration. He could see the effort in the tendons of her wrist, in the slight tremor in her fingers on his arm, in the sheen building at her temples. She was channelling more magic than she usually did, mining layers they hadn't reached before, and the expenditure was visible.

He lost track of the minutes. The diagnostic work had its own rhythm, the spells cycling through their analytical patterns, her readings murmured in a low steady voice, and inside that rhythm the silence between them was bearable — structured, purposeful, the silence of two people doing something difficult and technical together. It was not the dead silence of the past three days. The work gave them a frame to sit inside, and inside the frame they could be what the arrangement had always asked them to be: examiner and subject, researcher and research, two competent people solving a hard problem. That the problem was on his arm and her hands were the instruments and his pulse was the baseline — those intimacies were incidental. Part of the frame. Nothing more.

He nearly believed it.

The light outside thinned and went. Cloud thickening over Wiltshire, the short day surrendering early. The candles she'd lit at the start of the session were doing most of the work now, their uneven glow falling over the table, over his arm, over her hands.

Her left forearm shimmered.

Not a flicker of light — a shift in the surface. The way heat haze warps the air above a road in summer, a ripple passed over the skin below her rolled sleeve, the smooth even tone wavering. Then the glamour thinned and he saw through it the way you see the bottom of a stream when the current stills — not all at once, but in a slow, irreversible clarification.

The letters were carved deep.

Not cut — carved. The difference was in the furrows, wide and deliberate, the strokes of someone who had taken her time, who had wanted the work to last. The scar tissue was raised and silvery-white, almost luminous against the skin beneath, the edges ragged where the cursed blade had torn rather than sliced cleanly. The word was legible. It had been made to be legible. It had been carved by a woman who believed the word was true and wanted it written where it could be read for the rest of the body's life.

MUDBLOOD.

Five letters and five more. Spanning the width of her forearm from the inside of the wrist to the edge of the rolled fabric. The first downstrokes were the deepest — the M, the opening cuts, the blade biting hard into skin that had been held flat against marble. The letters were not even — they tilted slightly toward the elbow, following the angle of Bellatrix's hand, the writing of a woman who had been kneeling on her subject's chest for leverage. The final D was shallower. By then the arm had been slick and Bellatrix had been laughing — the laugh he could still hear if a room went quiet enough — and the blade had found less purchase. He knew this. He knew the order and the depth and the sound because he had been standing against the wall of the drawing room twelve feet from where it was happening, and he had not moved, and his memory of that night had the merciless fidelity of a memory you have tried to destroy a thousand times and cannot.

His hand was on the scar before he made any decision about it. Not reaching — reaching would have been conscious, something he could have stopped. His hand moved from the table to her forearm and his fingers settled over the letters the way one touches something to verify that it is real, and the scar tissue under his thumb was warm and raised and real.

She flinched.

Not away. Inward. A full-body contraction, the pulling-in of a person hit by a gust of wind — not pain but exposure, the shock of air against skin that has been covered for years. Her arm went rigid under his hand. The diagnostic spell she was maintaining shattered. The mapping overlay above his Mark dissolved into scattered motes. The probe resonator's readings went to noise. The recording quill scratched a wild line across the parchment and stopped. Three concurrent spells collapsed in an instant, and the library was suddenly darker and quieter and full of the absence of magic.

His hand stayed.

He could feel her pulse. Fast — not the measured baseline she took from his wrist at the start of every examination but rapid, hunted, the heartbeat of a caught thing. And beneath his thumb, the ridged letters. The texture of cursed damage that had lived under glamour and fabric for seven years and was now under his hand in the candlelit library of the house where it had been made.

She was looking at his hand on her arm. Not at his face — at the point of contact, his fingers over the word, his pale skin against her skin and the silver-white ridges of the scar between. Her face was very still, the expression on it something he had never seen: not anger, not the sharp fierceness she wore in confrontation, not the guarded professional mask of the past three days. Something older than any of those. The face of a person who has carried a weight alone for a long time and has just had it seen by the one person she could not bear to show.

"Granger."

She didn't move.

"Granger, I—"

"Don't." Her voice was flat. Not angry — load-bearing. Holding up the rest of her. "Don't say you're sorry."

He closed his mouth. He had not been going to say sorry. Though sorry was in the room, had been in the room since a night in 1998 when a woman with wild dark hair and the Dark Lord's favour had held this arm against cold marble and taken a cursed knife to it while a boy in black school shoes stood against the wall and did nothing. Sorry was the size of the house and the weight of the name and the permanent fact of the word under his fingers, and sorry was the smallest possible coin and they both knew it would not buy a thing.

He moved his thumb. Not away — across. Slowly. Tracing the first letter, the M, the deep furrows that Bellatrix's blade had opened in the arm of an eighteen-year-old girl on his drawing room floor. The scar tissue had its own topography, raised and dense, the way cursed wounds heal — not back to what they were but into something new, something that is both healed and not, the body's best effort meeting a magic designed to make repair impossible. He traced the U. The D. Under his thumb, the letters were a braille, a record written in the only material that could not be edited or revised or lost.

Her breath changed. Not faster — deliberate. The breathing of someone deciding, with each inhale, not to bolt.

The candles had burned low. The library was lit mostly by firelight now, the grate across the room still holding a few red logs, and the thin remainder of the November afternoon through the tall windows — a blue-grey wash that would be gone in minutes. In the dimness the scar was harder to see but easier to feel. He traced the letters by touch the way a person reads in the dark.

B. L. O. O. D.

He went back to the beginning and started again, and this time she made a sound — small, closed-mouthed, somewhere between a word she swallowed and a breath she didn't intend to let out.

"I see it," he said. His voice came out wrong — scraped down to something he didn't usually let into the air. "Every time I close my eyes, the room's there. The floor and the chandelier and you on the floor and—" He stopped. The sound she'd made while Bellatrix worked. Some doors should not be opened from this side. "I see it."

"I know."

"I didn't know it was still here. On you. I thought—" He stopped again, and this time the stopping was the sudden understanding of how monstrous the assumption had been. He had assumed it was gone. For seven years he had assumed that someone — a Healer, a spell, the sheer force of Hermione Granger's competence applied to her own body — had fixed it. Erased it. Returned the arm to what it was before. He had assumed this the way people assume that other people's damage heals, because the alternative is to know that every person you pass is carrying something permanent, and that knowledge is not survivable in quantity.

"I didn't know," he said again, and the inadequacy of it lay on the table between them like the diagnostic instruments that were no longer needed.

She looked at him. Not at his hand on her arm but at his face. The expression was clear-eyed and dry and very tired.

"Where would it go?"

Three words. The simplest question. And it opened a hole in the floor of every assumption he had built over seven years. Where would it go.
