Where does a Dark magic scar go when the Dark witch who made it is dead and the war is over and the world has agreed to move on. Where does the word carved into a woman's arm by a cursed blade in a house of Dark wizards go, once the Ministry has held its trials and the Prophet has printed its retrospectives and everyone has decided that what happened was terrible and is now past tense. The answer was nowhere. The answer was the same arm, every morning, glamoured because the world had moved on and the arm had not.

"Dark magic," she said, in the flat tone of a thing she had explained too many times. "It doesn't heal clean. You know that."

"I know that." His thumb was on the L. He could feel the ridge of it, the way the tissue had knitted around the cut without closing it, the body growing over the wound like a tree growing over a fence wire — incorporating the damage, not erasing it. "I know that. I just didn't — I wasn't thinking about yours."

"No," she said. "People don't."

He heard what was underneath that. People looked at Hermione Granger and saw competence, brilliance, the war heroine, the brightest witch, the divorced researcher who worked too much and cared too deeply about dead languages and dying magic. People did not see the arm. She had made sure of it. The glamour, the long sleeves even in summer, the habitual positioning of her left forearm against her body or below a table — all of it a system, engineered and maintained with the same thoroughness she brought to everything, for the purpose of making the worst thing that had ever happened to her invisible.

"You hide it," he said. "Every day."

"Every day."

"Why."

It wasn't a real question. He knew why. But she answered anyway, and the answer was different from the one he'd expected.

"Because people see it and they do the maths." Her voice had gone tight, the composure under strain. "The scar and my name and the war and the fact that I was eighteen and held down on a floor while a woman carved it into me with a knife, and the number they come up with is pity. Every time. And I can't—" She stopped. Restarted sharper, the way she did when the first attempt wasn't precise enough. "I can't function in a room where the first thing people know about me is the worst thing that was done to me. I can't be that person. I won't."

"So you hide it."

"So I hide it."

He traced the M again. She let him.

"I watched," he said. The words came out heavy, ungraceful, with the weight of things stored too long. "I stood against the wall and I watched and I didn't move. I didn't — I was sixteen and she was Bellatrix and my father was in the room and I was terrified and none of that's enough. I know it isn't."

"No." Not cruelly. Just true. "It isn't."

The silence after that was bad. He could hear the fire, the small ticking of the logs settling, and the wind outside pressing against the windows, and neither of those sounds was loud enough to fill the space where a better man would have known what to say. He was not a better man. He was the boy who had stood in the drawing room and watched, and he was the man who sat in the library and held the record of what he had watched, and the distance between those two people was seven years and a lifetime of learning that watching was its own form of participation.

"I'm not going to apologize."

Her eyes sharpened.

"Not because I don't owe—" He stopped, reorganised. The habit of rerouting away from the honest thing was so deep it was nearly physical, the words being pulled toward safe before he could force them back. "An apology is a transaction. I say sorry, you say something, we agree the account's closer to settled. This isn't — there isn't a word that settles this. I can't give you something that makes it balance. And I won't pretend sorry covers any of it."

She was quiet for a long time. Her pulse under his fingers had slowed, the hammering easing into something steadier, though still faster than rest. His hand was on her scar. Her left hand — the one that had been holding his wrist through the examination — was still there, her fingers loosely circling his forearm below the Mark. He noticed this the way you notice something that has been true for several minutes and only now registers: they were holding each other at the point of damage. His fingers on the word in her arm. Her fingers below the mark on his.

"That's the first honest thing anyone's said to me about it," she said. Her voice was quiet. "In seven years."

He didn't know what to say to that. The correct response didn't exist in any register he'd been trained in — not the pureblood formality, not the careful precision, not the dry deflection that kept the world at its distance. So he said nothing, and the nothing sat between them, and the silence was not the Manor's silence or the withdrawal's silence but one they were making together, and it was enough.

She moved first. Not away — forward. She turned her hand on his arm, the one still resting below the Mark, and placed her palm flat over the skull and snake. Not diagnostic. Not clinical. Her hand on the place where they had branded him at sixteen, her palm warm across the dark lines, her fingers curving over the edges, and the gesture was so simple and so deliberate that something in him that had been locked since that night in the study — his father's hand on his shoulder, the burning that whited out the world — gave way, not dramatically, not with any sound, but the way a bolt gives that has been holding under strain for years and finally doesn't.

He kept his hand on her scar. She kept hers on his Mark. They sat like that in the near-dark, the fire down to embers, the candles guttering in their pools of wax, the November light entirely gone from the windows. The library was enormous around them and the two of them were at the centre of it, at a worktable covered in diagnostic instruments and abandoned parchment, and the house around them was quiet. Not the cold hostile quiet of the past three days. Not the Manor's attentive, testing silence. Something closer to stillness — the hush of a structure recognizing that the people inside it were doing something it could not engineer and, for once, holding itself back.

Outside, frost thickened on the grounds. Inside, they did not relight the candles. Did not add wood to the fire. The cold crept in from the corridors and the warmth held where they were touching, and neither of them moved to fix anything because what had happened was not a thing fixing could reach.

At some point her breathing steadied fully, the deliberate quality gone, and his matched it without his trying. The probe resonator on the table between them had gone dark, its readings abandoned, and the diagnostic crystals sat in their case catching the firelight in small prismatic flickers, and the parchment with its wild terminal line of quill-scratch lay curled at the edge. The detritus of the examination, the clinical apparatus, was all still there, but it belonged to a different evening — the one where this was an examination and nothing more.

Eventually she drew her hand away. Slowly — not reluctant, but careful, the withdrawal itself a gesture, as if she were setting something down rather than letting go. She looked at her own forearm where the scar was still visible, the glamour still gone, the letters legible in the dim orange light from the dying fire.

"I should recast it," she said. Meaning the glamour.

"Should you."

Not a question. Not really. It was the closest he could come to saying what he meant, and the indirectness was his native mode — the real thing said at an angle, offered sideways so the other person could pick it up or leave it. He watched her hear it. The small shift in her face. The recognition that he'd said more than the words contained.

She looked at the scar. She looked at him. She did not recast the glamour.

"The diagnostic readings," she said, after a pause that held its own weight. "Before the spell collapsed, some of the deep-layer data came through. I should transcribe it while it's fresh."

"All right."

She stood. Gathered her wand, her instruments, her notes. The motions were efficient — the professional frame coming back like a coat taken from a hook — but something was different in the way she moved, something less rigid, the competence still there but the armour loosened. She crossed the library to her table, the one six feet from his, and sat and began transcribing, her quill moving in quick sure strokes across the parchment, her head bent over the work.

Her left sleeve was still pushed back. The scar was visible each time her arm moved across the page, the silver-white letters catching the last of the firelight, and she had not pulled the fabric down. He did not know if this was a decision or an oversight and it did not matter. The glamour was off and the scar was present in the room with them, as real and as permanent as the Mark on his own arm, and she was letting it be there.

He sat at his table and did not pretend he wasn't watching. The Mark was quieter than it had been in days — the restless ache gone, the dark magic stilled. Not by the diagnostic spells, which had collapsed twenty minutes ago. By something else. By a hand placed on it not to study it or map its architecture but to touch it, the way you touch a thing that hurts in order to say *I know this hurts.*

He rolled his sleeve down slowly. He thought about the word under his thumb, the ten letters in cursed silver he could still feel in his skin like an afterimage. He thought about the fact that she hid this every day, from everyone, the glamour cast each morning and maintained through every interaction, and that today she had let him see and was still letting him see, and that this was a trust he had done nothing to earn and had no idea what to do with except sit here in the cooling library and be the person she had decided not to hide from.

Her quill scratched across the parchment. The fire ticked as the last log settled into ash. The frost outside made no sound at all.


# Chapter 13

The pub was a Muggle place near Charing Cross Road, a narrow room with uncomfortable chairs and middling beer and no associations. She and Ron had started using it after the divorce because neither of them had ever kissed or argued or decided anything in this building, which was exactly why it worked. No memory to navigate around. Just a room.

She'd Apparated into the alley behind the used bookshop three doors down and walked the rest, her coat pulled close against the December cold. The Floo would have been easier but the Floo was unreliable now — three failures in the last week alone, connections dropping mid-transit, and she didn't fancy rematerializing in someone's kitchen in Birmingham because the network had lost her routing. So Apparition, with its familiar squeeze and the ringing in her ears afterward, and then ordinary London pavement under her boots, and the simple solidity of it — pavement that stayed where it was, walls that didn't rearrange themselves when she wasn't looking — felt strange. Not bad. Just strange, the way any normal thing feels after weeks inside the abnormal.

Ron was already there. Always early now, the way he'd never been when they were married. He stood when he saw her, which was new too — some politeness he'd adopted after the separation that she hadn't asked for and didn't correct because correcting it would mean discussing it, and discussing it would mean admitting the shape of their interactions had changed.

"Hey." He kissed her cheek. His face was cold. "You look — you look good."

It came out slightly wrong, the compliment landing with too much weight because he'd had to decide to say it, and the decision was audible. She let it pass.

"Got you a cider. Is that still right?"

"That's perfect."

They sat. The pub was half-empty at two in the afternoon — a few regulars nursing pints, a woman reading a paperback in the corner, a football match on the mounted television with the sound off. Ron glanced at it. He'd become genuinely interested in football since the divorce, one of the small expansions his life had made without her in it. He'd explained the offside rule to her twice and she still didn't understand it, which he found funny in a way that wasn't mean, and the fact that he could find it funny instead of irritating was one of the ways she knew they'd done the right thing.

"How's work?" she asked.

"Good. Weird. George has got me managing the books for the second shop and I'm completely rubbish at it, but it turns out that when I'm rubbish at the books, George does them himself, and then they're actually done right. So it's a system."

She laughed. He grinned — pleased with himself for getting the laugh, then slightly embarrassed by the pleasure, which was so precisely Ron that the familiarity of it ached in a specific, low-grade way. Not loss. Fondness for the thing they'd been that had become the thing they were now, which was different and less and also, in some ways she couldn't fully articulate, more honest.

"You?"

"Still at the Manor. The research is — it's significant. Material in the collection that nobody's documented in centuries." She reached for the professional frame, the one that cost her nothing. "It could be important for the decay."

"Right." Ron drank his beer. He was broader than she remembered, which happened every time she saw him after a gap — her mental image of him still nineteen and lanky, the actual Ron twenty-five and solid and slightly sunburned from the running Harry had mentioned. He'd taken it up in the summer and kept going through the autumn, and now apparently he ran along the canal near his flat three mornings a week, and the idea of Ron voluntarily running — Ron, who at school would have considered a brisk walk to the Great Hall excessive exertion — was one of the small surprises of their post-marriage life that she found genuinely, uncomplexly good.

"So you're there full-time," he said. Not a question.

"Mostly. I come back for meetings."

"And Malfoy's there."

"It's his house, Ron."

"Yeah, I know. I know that's — look, I'm not." He stopped. Rubbed the back of his neck. Started again with the visible effort of someone rerouting a sentence away from where it was headed. "I mean, the house. How's the house? Like, is it... fine? As a place to be?"

She could answer this several ways. The house was extraordinary, terrifying, beautiful, sentient, responsive, and currently adjusting its internal geography to accommodate her preferences in ways she hadn't asked for. The house had shown her the room where she'd been tortured and then, weeks later, started warming her bathwater to the exact temperature she preferred. The house knew things about her that she hadn't told it.

"It's unusual," she said. "But manageable."

Ron looked at her the way he looked at her when she was being evasive and he could tell and was deciding whether to push. He'd gotten better at this since the divorce — the deciding. During the marriage he'd pushed or he hadn't, binarily, without much calibration. Now there was a middle range. A pause where he weighed it.

"Hermione."

"What?"

"You said manageable. You didn't say fine."

She drank her cider. It was warm in the pub — the ordinary warmth of a radiator working on a December afternoon, not the intelligent, adjusting warmth of a house that noticed her. She could hear the woman in the corner turn a page of her paperback, and the muted commentary of the football match coming from the regulars' end of the bar, and somewhere below all of it, like a radio signal from very far away, the low hum of the Mark resonance. Faint from London. Degraded by distance. But there. Draco's pain — the restless ache that the Mark carried now, the magic in it searching for a channel that no longer existed — transmitted through miles of December air and settled in her forearm like an echo.

She pressed her wrist against the edge of the table. The pressure helped.

"I don't have a neat answer for you," she said. "The house is complicated. The research is real and it matters. And I'm handling it."

Ron nodded slowly. He was peeling the label off his beer bottle — a nervous habit she'd forgotten about and now remembered with the sharpness of something found at the back of a drawer, the recognition almost physical. He'd done that all through their engagement, sitting in restaurants, peeling labels while they talked about the wedding or didn't talk about the things they should have been talking about instead.

"George and Angelina are getting serious," he said after a while.

"Really?"

"Yeah. She's been at Sunday dinners three weeks running. Mum's broken out the good china. You know what that means."

"Molly Weasley pulling out the good china is practically a betrothal announcement."

"Don't say that to George, he'll bolt." Ron smiled. The smile was genuine and easy and had nothing to do with Hermione, which was the kind of smile she liked best from him now — the ones that were about his life, not about their shared past. "Anyway. She's good for him. She's got this way of just not taking his shit, you know? Like, he'll do the George thing where he deflects with a joke and she just... waits. Doesn't laugh. Waits until he says the real thing."

"Good."

"Yeah."

They were quiet. The football match continued its muted drama. Someone at the bar laughed at something. The woman turned another page. Normal human sounds in a normal human room, and Hermione sat in the middle of them and felt the resonance pulse — faint, faint, but continuous, a thread that connected her to a house in Wiltshire and the man inside it, and she thought about how Ron, sitting across from her peeling his label, had no idea that she could feel another person's pain in her own arm, that she'd been touched in places that weren't physical by a house that might be engineering everything she felt, that the reason she couldn't say "fine" was so much stranger than anything he was probably imagining.

"Listen," Ron said. He put the beer bottle down. The label was half off, a ragged strip of wet paper. "I know it's not my — I know I don't get to say this. But I'm going to say it once and then shut up."

"Okay."

"You do this thing." He wasn't looking at her. He was looking at his hands on the table, the way he did when he was being honest about something that embarrassed him. "Where you find a problem and the problem becomes the whole world and you forget that there was a world before the problem. You did it with Buckbeak's appeal third year. You did it with SPEW. You did it with the Horcruxes. You did it with the marriage — our marriage — when it was ending, you researched divorce like it was a thesis project, and it was the most organized breakup in history and also somehow the saddest thing I've ever been part of."

"Ron—"

"I'm not — that's not a complaint. That's just you. It's how you work. And I used to think it was brilliant and then I thought it was the thing that ruined us and now I think it's just — it's the thing about you that's never going to change, and it's not good or bad, it's just the way your brain works when it's scared."

She didn't say anything. The cider was going warm in her glass.

"That house," he said. "That family. It's the kind of problem that could swallow you. And I don't mean Malfoy — or I don't only mean Malfoy — I mean the whole thing. The research and the library and the decay and whatever's happening with the magic. You'll make it the whole world because that's what you do with the things that scare you, and by the time you come up for air—" He stopped. "I don't know. I just. Be careful."

The phrase landed. Be careful with that house. And she didn't know if he meant the architecture or the heir or the research or the pattern she couldn't see in herself because she was inside it, and the ambiguity was its own kind of precision because Ron, who people had always underestimated, was saying something true about all of those things at once.

"You're not wrong," she said.

He blinked. "That's new. You admitting I'm not wrong."

"Don't get used to it."

He smiled. The rueful, self-knowing one she'd always liked best on him. "Wouldn't dream of it."

He finished his beer. She finished her cider. He told her about the flat — he'd finally hung the shelves in the bedroom, badly, and they'd fallen down once already, and he was going to try again this weekend with a level this time because Harry had pointed out that eyeballing it wasn't working. She told him about a translation problem she'd been stuck on, keeping it vague, keeping it professional, and he listened the way he always listened — not tracking every detail but getting the emotional shape of it, the frustration and the fascination, and nodding in the right places.

"Oh," he said, when they were putting coats on. "The other thing. Mum went to St. Mungo's for her knee — the old jinx, the Moody one — and they turned her away. Said they're rationing healing magic. Prioritizing critical cases."

"I know about the rationing."

"I figured you would." He wound his scarf around his neck — a Chudley Cannons scarf, orange and faded, that she'd given him three Christmases ago when they were still married. He still wore it. She didn't know what to do with that. "The Floo dropped again this morning. Two hours. And a kid in Diagon Alley — six, maybe — set a rubbish bin on fire. Accidental magic, but the kind that shouldn't happen that young, not that strongly."

"I know."

"Right." He looked at her. "If the research matters — the stuff you're doing at the Manor — then I suppose it matters. I just want you to come back from it. That's all."

She hugged him at the door. He was warm and solid and smelled like beer and the cold and the specific Ron-smell that had been the first thing she'd noticed about him when she was twelve and wouldn't be able to identify if asked. He hugged her back carefully, his arms settling in the specific configuration of someone who knew her body from years of holding it and was being precise about the distance between memory and the present.

"Take care of yourself," he said.

"You too."

He left. She watched him walk up Charing Cross Road toward the Leaky, hunching into his coat, and felt the particular ache of watching someone you loved walk away from you in the most ordinary way — not leaving, just going, back to his flat and his badly hung shelves and his Sunday dinners and the life he was building that didn't need her in it. The ache wasn't loss. It was the specific sadness of something that had been finished properly, all the seams closed, the wound healed clean enough that what remained wasn't pain but the memory of where pain had been.

She stood on the pavement and felt the December cold on her face and the Mark resonance in her arm and pressed her wrist against the brick wall beside her, and the wall was just a wall, cold and rough and utterly indifferent to her touch.

---

Harry's office at the Department of Magical Law Enforcement was the same corner room they'd given him two promotions ago. He'd done nothing with it except accumulate files and add a photograph of Ginny and James that sat on the desk facing outward. James was nine months old in the picture, chewing on a toy broomstick with the focused commitment of someone who'd found his purpose.

"You look tired," Harry said.

"Hello to you too."

He stood and hugged her — a real hug, both arms, the kind of contact that required no negotiation because they'd known each other since they were eleven and the friendship had survived a war and everything after and could take the weight. He smelled like cedar soap. He'd used the same brand since fourth year.

"Tea?"

"Please."

He made it badly. Too strong, not enough milk, the cup repaired so many times the handle was slightly crooked. She drank it anyway because Harry's tea was one of the constants of her life and she wasn't prepared to lose constants right now.

"Ron said you saw him," Harry said, settling behind his desk.

"An hour ago."

"How was it?"

"Fine. Good. He told me about Molly and St. Mungo's."

Harry nodded. He had the particular look of someone who'd been absorbing bad news professionally for long enough that it had reorganized the muscles around his eyes — not haggard, exactly, but set. Alert in a low-frequency way that didn't switch off.

"It's not just the rationing," he said. "The Floo's been intermittent for three weeks. We've had four accidental magic incidents in the last ten days, all kids under seven, all discharging way beyond what their age should produce. One of them blew the windows out of a flat in Camden. Muggle flat. We had to send a team — Obliviators, the glass, the structural damage, the neighbors who'd heard the explosion. And the parents, the magical parents — they were terrified, Hermione. Because their child can't control what's happening and neither can they, and no one at the Ministry can explain why a five-year-old is producing magic that should be beyond a third-year."

"The ambient field's destabilizing," she said. "The infrastructure that regulates baseline magical pressure — it's degraded to the point where young wizards can't discharge safely. They're getting surges because the field isn't absorbing the excess."

"I got the Mysteries briefing. They said roughly that, with more words."

She would have smiled but the information was too specific and too bad. The decay that had been background noise in her professional life — the thing she'd been studying, tracking, watching worsen from the clean distance of data — was now a public emergency. Families with children who couldn't trust their own magic. Hospitals turning away patients because the healing spells drew on the same failing infrastructure. Transportation collapsing. The entire substrate of wizarding life in Britain coming apart, not in a dramatic catastrophe but in a slow, grinding degradation that was somehow worse because you couldn't point to the moment it broke.

"How's the research going?" Harry asked.

"There's material in the Malfoy library that documents the original magical infrastructure. The anchoring points, the ley-line connections — the deep structure that the ambient field depends on. It was built centuries ago, partly by the Malfoy family."

"And the war damaged it."

"Yes. That's what's failing. The anchoring points were designed to be permanent, but the scale of Dark magic during the war — the curses, the protections, the sheer volume of hostile magic passing through the field — it degraded them. Not all at once. Slowly. And the degradation has been compounding for seven years."

"Can you fix it?"

"I don't know yet. The texts are in archaic notation that takes both the Malfoy family's inherited knowledge and formal theory to decode. We've made progress but there's more to do."

Harry looked at her for a moment. "We," he said.

"Malfoy and I. He reads the family notation. I handle the theory. Neither of us can do it alone."

"Right."

Through the office wall she could hear someone arguing with a filing cabinet. The cabinet was making a grinding, petulant sound, the kind of noise an enchanted object makes when its underlying magic is misbehaving — another symptom of the ambient instability, institutional magic fraying at the seams, the small enchantments that kept the Ministry running starting to stutter and fail. The wizard outside swore. Something metal fell.

"People are talking," Harry said.

"About the decay?"

"About you. Where you've been." He leaned forward, elbows on the desk, the posture of someone delivering information he'd rather not deliver. "You've spent most of the last month at the Manor. People notice, Hermione. You're not someone who disappears into a Malfoy property for weeks without generating opinions."

"I'm doing critical research in the only archive that—"

"I know. The Prophet had a paragraph last week. Skeeter's column. 'Ministry researcher spending extended time at the Malfoy estate.' Nothing direct, just the implication. She doesn't have to say it outright."

"I don't read Skeeter."

"Ginny does." He paused. His hand went to his hair, the gesture so automatic he probably didn't register it. "Ginny said to tell you — and this is Ginny, not me — that if you're going to set up camp at Malfoy Manor, you should have a cover story better than 'the library is very interesting,' because no library is that interesting."

"I'm not setting up—"

"Her words. I told her it's about the research and the decay and the infrastructure. She said she believes you about the research. She also said the research isn't the only reason you're still there, and she's not going to insult your intelligence by pretending she doesn't notice."

Ginny Potter. Who'd been possessed by a Horcrux at eleven, watched her brother die at seventeen, married the most famous wizard alive, and come out the other side with a clarity of perception that none of them could match. Ginny didn't soften things. She didn't hedge. She'd survived enough to stop performing diplomacy and the result was a woman who said what she saw and didn't apologize for the seeing.

Hermione wanted to be angry about it and couldn't quite get there, because Ginny was right in a way that bypassed argument — not right about the specifics, which Ginny didn't know, but right about the shape of it, the outline of a situation that looked, from the outside, like more than what it claimed to be.

"The research is real," she said. "The decay is getting worse. The archive is the only source."

"I know. I believe you." Harry looked at the photograph of James and the broomstick. Looked back. "I'm not asking you to justify being there. I'm telling you other people are watching and not all of them are going to be generous with their assumptions. The Malfoy name still means what it means. Your name means what yours means. The two of those in proximity gets people's attention."

"Since when do I care what people assume?"

"Since always. You just pretend you don't."

He said it without edge. The particular Harry directness that was too kind for anger and too honest for comfort — the thing about him that had made her want to strangle him since she was twelve and also the thing that made him irreplaceable.

She drank the terrible tea. Outside the office, the filing cabinet had apparently won its argument with the wizard, because something heavy slammed and footsteps retreated. The ambient hum of the Ministry felt different than it had six months ago — thinner, less stable, the way a building feels when the power is fluctuating and the lights aren't quite steady.

"How bad is it going to get?" Harry asked. Quieter now.

"Bad. If we don't understand the anchoring infrastructure well enough to repair it — or at least stabilize it — it'll keep degrading. The Floo is just the beginning. Healing magic draws on the same field. So do the wards at Hogwarts, the protections at Azkaban, the enchantments on the Ministry itself." She gestured at the room around them. "Everything magical in Britain sits on top of the same foundation, and the foundation is cracked."

Harry absorbed this. He was good at absorbing bad information — a skill learned the hard way, over years of bad information arriving at inconvenient times in escalating quantities.

"And the Malfoy texts are the only way to understand it."

"The Malfoys helped build the original infrastructure. Their records are the only documentation of how the anchoring points work. There's nothing in the Ministry archives. Nothing at Hogwarts. Nothing anywhere I've looked. The original builders kept the knowledge in the family."

"Convenient."

"It was designed to be."

He didn't say anything for a moment. Then: "Look. Do what you need to do. I'll deal with Skeeter if it escalates. But, Hermione—" He met her eyes with the particular steadiness that meant he was about to say something she wouldn't enjoy. "Be honest with yourself about why you're there. Not with me. I don't need to know. But with yourself."

"I'm being honest."

He looked at her with the expression he used on suspects who were almost but not quite convincing, and she felt a flare of irritation because he was right and she was lying and they both knew it, and the specific lie was not that the research was fake but that the research was sufficient explanation, when the truth included a sentient house and a resonance she could feel in her arm right now, sitting in an office at the Ministry of Magic, the low pulse of Draco Malfoy's pain transmitted across sixty miles of English winter.

"Okay," Harry said.

He let it go. She could see him letting it go, the same way Ron had — the deliberate decision not to push, which both of them had learned to do with her over years of pushing and watching her shut down. It was possible that the two men who knew her best in the world had independently arrived at the same strategy for handling her, which was to say the honest thing once and then retreat, and the fact that she had taught them both to do this by being difficult about honesty was not lost on her.

She stood. He stood. He hugged her again — briefer this time, an arm around her shoulders, a squeeze.

"If you need anything," he said. "You know."

"I know."

She left the Ministry through the visitors' entrance, the red telephone box that rattled and rose and deposited her on a grey London street in December. Four o'clock. The light was already failing — mid-December, the sun gone or going, the sky a flat steel colour that would be black within the hour. She stood on the pavement and London moved around her, commuters and shoppers and a man selling roasted chestnuts from a cart on the corner, the smoke rising into the cold air and dispersing.

She walked. Not toward anything. Just through the city, the way she used to walk when she needed to think and the flat was too small and the office was too full of people who wanted her to have answers. Past the bookshop where she'd bought her first non-magical novel after the war. Past the cafe where she and Ron had sat on a Tuesday afternoon and agreed, over lukewarm coffee, that the marriage was done — not in those words, not dramatically, but in the accumulation of small honest sentences that added up to the same conclusion. Past the entrance to the Tube where a busker was playing something she almost recognized, his guitar case open on the pavement, and a woman dropped a coin in and the busker nodded without breaking the melody.

Ordinary. All of it ordinary. Walls that stayed where they were. Buildings that didn't rearrange themselves to suit their inhabitants' moods. People who didn't know that the magic holding their world together — the magic they didn't even know existed — was degrading underneath them like rot in the joists of a house that looked fine from the street.

She passed a shop on Shaftesbury Avenue where the display window should have held a concealed advertisement for Madam Malkin's — one of those charmed billboards that Muggles saw as a shoe advert — and the magic was flickering. The image stuttered between dress robes and brogues, two frames overlaid, and a passing Muggle glanced at it and kept walking, registering the glitch as a faulty display. But Hermione saw what it was: the concealment charm drawing on the same failing ambient field, the same cracked infrastructure, the same damage that was making children's magic spike and Floo connections drop. The same damage she'd spent a month studying in a library in Wiltshire, getting closer to understanding, getting closer to the man whose family had built the thing that was now breaking.

She Apparated from the alley behind the telephone box, and the world compressed to a needle-point and expanded again, and she was standing on the frosted gravel of the Manor drive with the last grey light dying over Wiltshire and her ears ringing and her breath coming out in clouds.

---

The walk up the drive took four minutes. She'd timed it once, early on, when the Manor had still been a professional assignment and she'd still been pretending that measurement could make this manageable. She didn't time it anymore. She walked. The cold bit at her face and her boots crunched on frozen gravel and the bare trees along the drive stood like structural drawings of themselves — all line and no softness, black against the darkening sky.

The Mark resonance strengthened with each step. The faint signal she'd been carrying all day in London was filling in, gaining resolution, the way a radio station clarifies as you drive toward its tower. His pain. His wakefulness. A quality she couldn't name but had learned to recognize — something like agitation, something like waiting. Two floors up, the resonance said, though she was still outside and the information shouldn't have been that precise at this distance. The bond was learning her. Or she was learning it.

The front door opened before she reached it.

Not the way doors open when you turn a handle. The way a door opens when the building behind it has decided. The heavy oak swung inward, smooth and unhurried, and the entrance hall beyond was lit with that golden light the Manor made when it was — she didn't want to finish the thought. When it was pleased.

She stepped inside.

The warmth was immediate and specific. Not the blunt heat of a radiator or a fire but something that seemed to know her — the cold spots in her coat, the numbness in her fingers, the particular chill that December air leaves on the skin of someone who's been standing on a London pavement thinking about things she doesn't want to think about. The warmth found all of it and addressed it with an attention that felt less like a heating charm and more like being noticed by something enormous and careful.

The air smelled of the library. Old paper, preservation spells, the faint mineral tang of the Manor's stone. And beneath that, the other quality — not a smell but a settling, the way the house exhaled when she came back, as if the building itself had been holding some tension that her presence released.

She stood in the entrance hall with her coat still on.

Ron's voice: be careful with that house.

Harry's voice: be honest about why you're there.

Ginny's voice, through Harry, clean as glass: the research isn't the only reason.

The corridor to the library was short tonight. This morning, when she'd left, it had been longer — she'd noticed, the way she noticed everything about this house now, the way the path between her room and the entrance stretched when she was leaving, as if the house were reluctant. Coming back, the corridors offered themselves generously. A left turn that usually passed two empty rooms opened instead directly into the hall outside the library doors, the house editing its own geography to shorten her walk.

She took off her coat and hung it on the hook the Manor had made for her. Iron, plain, at exactly the right height. She'd never asked for it. It had appeared the third night, and she'd used it without comment because commenting would mean acknowledging that the house was accommodating her, which would mean acknowledging that she was being accommodated, which would mean acknowledging that she liked it.

The library door was already open. The room beyond was warm and golden, the lamps lit, the shelves steady — the archive stable tonight, not shifting, the house in its most generous configuration, as if it were showing her the best version of the life it could provide. Her desk was where she'd left it, her notes stacked, a cup of tea sitting beside them that she hadn't made and that was, she knew without touching it, exactly the temperature she preferred.

She'd spent an afternoon in the ordinary world. She'd sat in a Muggle pub with a man who'd loved her for years and worried about her in the specific, informed way of someone who knew her worst patterns. She'd sat in a Ministry office with her best friend, who'd told her the world was watching and asked her to be honest. She'd walked through London where the walls didn't move and the rooms didn't respond and the silence was the regular silence of a city full of people who didn't know her name or her scar or the weight of the research she was carrying.

And she'd come back here. To the house that warmed for her, shortened its corridors for her, made her tea and opened its doors and arranged its light to please her. The house that knew her better than was reasonable and wanted her here with a persistence that couldn't be called desire because buildings don't desire, except that this one did, and the specificity of its wanting was more unsettling than anything it had ever done that frightened her.

She sat down at her desk. The chair was warm. The tea was perfect.

She picked up her notes and the resonance settled into its familiar register — Draco, two floors above, awake, the Mark's pain a low hum underneath her own pulse. She could feel the quality of his wakefulness, though she couldn't have said how. Not thoughts. Not words. Just a texture, like knowing whether rain is heavy or light without looking out the window. He knew she was back. She was sure of this without evidence — the resonance didn't carry that kind of information, or it shouldn't — but the quality of his pain had shifted when she came through the door, a fractional easing, the Mark quieting by the smallest degree, and she'd felt it the way you feel a held breath released in the next room.

She worked. The texts were where she'd left them, the translation half-finished, the notation dense and archaic and yielding slowly to the combined pressure of her theory and his family knowledge. Tomorrow they'd work on it together. Tomorrow she'd sit across from him in this library and their hands might not touch and the clinical frame would hold or it wouldn't and the house would be warm and watchful around them, and she'd know, because she'd spent an afternoon in the world where no one watched her and no house wanted her, exactly what she was choosing by being here.

The house dimmed the lamps when her eyes got tired. She didn't ask it to. The tea stayed warm long past when it should have cooled.

She kept working.


# Chapter 14

The texts were in a script he had never seen his ancestors use.

Draco sat with the folio open across his knees — too large for the desk, leather-bound in something that was not leather, the pages thick as card stock and oddly warm to the touch. The notation ran in cramped vertical columns, a hybrid of runic shorthand and what looked like architectural drafting symbols, measurements embedded in the text itself as though the writer couldn't separate the language from the thing it described. He could pick out fragments. Family names. Compass bearings. The word *fundament* recurring in what might have been Latin or might have been an older cousin of it, something that predated the standardization of magical script by centuries.

He could not read it. Not alone.

Granger sat across from him at the long table in the gated archive, a room that had opened that morning with what he could only describe as willingness. No resistance, no testing. The door had been there when they came down to the library together after breakfast, wide and unhesitating, as if the house had been waiting for them and was glad they'd arrived.

She had noticed. He'd seen her notice — the brief pause, the way her eyes moved to the doorframe and then away, cataloguing without commenting. She did that more now. Registered things about the Manor and kept her observations internal, where before she would have spoken them aloud as data. He didn't know what had changed. He knew only that something had, during those few days she'd been gone, and that the version of her who'd returned was different in ways he couldn't fully map. Present, but guarded. Close, but holding something back.

He'd stopped trying to decode her withdrawal. The effort had nearly wrecked him the first time — three days of assuming he'd done something wrong, defaulting to his oldest conviction that he'd been found wanting in some essential way. When she'd left for London he'd been certain she wasn't coming back, and the Manor had confirmed it, going cold and sullen, the corridors stretching between rooms like the house was pulling away from itself. He'd eaten alone and the dining room had felt like a punishment.

Then she'd returned. The evening of the third day, the Floo flaring green in the entrance hall, and the house had responded before he had — warmth flooding the ground floor, the library doors swinging open, a corridor shortening between the entrance and the wing where she'd been sleeping. He'd come around the corner and found her standing in the hall with her bag over one shoulder and an expression he couldn't read. She'd said, "The Floo's getting worse. It took three tries," and he'd said, "The archive hasn't opened since you left," which was true and which was not the thing he'd meant to say.

That had been four days ago. They'd fallen back into the work with an ease that should have unsettled him but didn't — or did, but in a way he'd grown tired of fighting. The gated archive was behaving differently now. Where before it had required their proximity as a condition, testing them, closing if one stepped out, now it seemed to welcome their sustained presence. The room was stable. The light from the high windows held steady, a thick winter afternoon gold that shouldn't have been possible given how dark it was outside. The Manor's contribution to their working conditions was visible and generous, and Draco was aware of it the way you're aware of someone pouring your drink without being asked — a gesture you couldn't refuse without making it into something.

"This column here," Granger said, not looking up. She had a quill tucked behind her ear and another in her hand and a third lying across the open text she'd been cross-referencing. Her hair was piled on her head in something that had started the morning as a knot and was now a structural event, held together by what appeared to be a pencil and determination. "The notation shifts. It's not the same script as the first three pages."

He moved his chair closer. Not a decision, exactly — the table was wide and the text was small and he needed to see what she was pointing at. But he registered the proximity. The sleeve of her jumper against the sleeve of his shirt. Her hand on the page, index finger resting beneath a line of text, and her nails were short and unvarnished and there was an ink stain on the side of her middle finger that she'd had since breakfast.

"Different hand," he said, looking at the script. "The earlier pages — that's a formal record. Someone trained. This is rougher."

"Rougher how?"

He studied it. The strokes were thicker, less uniform, the vertical alignment wavering in places as if the writer had been working quickly or in poor light. The runic elements were simplified, almost shorthand, and the architectural symbols were mixed in more freely, less separated from the text.

"Field notes," he said. "Someone was out at the site. These aren't library records, they're — this was written while the work was being done."

Granger looked at him. He could feel her attention shift, the way it always did when he said something that opened a path she hadn't seen. Her pupils were slightly dilated from hours of close reading in dim light. The archive had windows but they were narrow and the Manor's golden warmth, generous as it was, did not extend to adequate reading lamps. They'd been working by wand-light and the candles she'd found in a box under the second shelf, and the effect was something closer to a medieval scriptorium than a research facility.

"Field notes from the original construction," she said. "That would be — when was this? Fifteenth century?"

"Fourteen-thirties. The first Malfoy to build on this site was Armand's great-grandson, Braccus. The Manor itself is older in pieces, but the foundational magic — the anchoring work — that was his project."

"And he wrote in this?"

"I don't think so. Braccus was — from what my father told me, he was the architect but not the builder. He designed the anchoring lattice but the actual field construction was done by specialists. Families who knew the land magic, the ley-line work. Some of them predated the Malfoys in England." He paused. He was reaching into knowledge he hadn't accessed in years, things Lucius had told him during long evenings in the study that had been lessons disguised as family history, or family history disguised as lessons. "My father called them the lineal artisans. They're in the family records somewhere but I never — I didn't pay enough attention."

The admission cost him something small. It always did, the moments when his father's teaching surfaced and he had to acknowledge both that Lucius had given him something valuable and that Lucius had given him everything else too.

"So these field notes are from the actual construction team," Granger said, her voice picking up speed the way it did when she was building a framework. "The people who laid the anchoring points. And the notation is different because it's a working language, not a formal one — a technical shorthand shared among practitioners."

"Which is why neither of us can read it alone."

She was already nodding. "Your family knowledge gives us the referents — the names, the geography, the genealogy. I can work the theoretical frame, the magical-structural principles. But this script is a trade language. It would have been passed from master to apprentice. It wouldn't be in any standard reference because it was never standardized."

"We'd have to decode it from context."

"We'd have to decode it from context." She repeated it not because she hadn't heard him but because she was already thinking past what he'd said, the repetition a placeholder while her mind ran. He'd learned this about her in the weeks they'd been working together — the way she processed through speech, using his sentences as handholds while she climbed toward something she could see and he couldn't.

He should have resented it. The old Draco would have. Instead he found himself watching the way her brow creased when she was concentrating, the slight movement of her lips as she read silently, the way she'd push her hair off her face with the back of her wrist because her fingers were always occupied — quill, page, wand, pencil. She was messy when she worked. Not disorganized — the opposite, ferociously systematic — but physically messy, surrounded by stacks and lists and cross-references, ink on her hands and a smudge on her chin she didn't know about. The controlled, precise woman who'd arrived at his door weeks ago in a professional cloak with her hair contained and her sleeves down was not the person sitting across from him. This was someone in a jumper with the sleeves shoved up, her shoes kicked off under the table, a woman who'd eaten half a biscuit and left the other half balanced on the spine of a fourteenth-century manuscript without apparent concern for the archival implications.

He realized he was staring and didn't stop. That was the difference. Not that he was watching her — he'd been watching her for weeks, in the way you watch a fire or a storm, something happening in your peripheral vision that you kept turning toward — but that he didn't make himself stop. He let himself look at the ink stain and the pencil in her hair and the line of her throat where the jumper's neckline had gone crooked, baring a collarbone and the hollow beneath it.

The Mark ached. Low, persistent, the magic in it restless in a way that had nothing to do with Voldemort and everything to do with the woman two feet from him. The ache had changed since her sustained diagnostic contact — it was responsive now, attuned to her presence, and when she was close it didn't exactly hurt less but hurt differently, a pull rather than a burn. He'd stopped trying to separate the Mark's behavior from his own.

"Here," Granger said, pulling a page toward her. "Look at this. These aren't just construction notes. These are maintenance records."

He leaned in. Her index finger traced a column of symbols — repetitive, cyclical, the same cluster recurring at what looked like regular intervals.

"Inspection schedules?" he said.

"Assessment schedules. They were checking the anchoring points. Regularly. Over — I think this is a span of decades. Look, the notation for the year changes here, and here, and the readings change with it." She flipped back two pages, cross-referencing. "The anchoring infrastructure wasn't a one-time construction. It required ongoing maintenance. Sustained attention. And the readings — if I'm interpreting this right — the readings fluctuated based on the strength of the connection between the Manor and the land."

"Connection meaning the bond."

"Connection meaning whatever magical relationship tied this house to the anchoring lattice. The Manor wasn't just built on the ley-lines. It was part of them. The house itself was a node in the network."

The implication settled between them. He could see her working through it, the rapid-fire analytical process she didn't bother to hide from him anymore, and he could feel when the pieces connected because her hand tightened on the page and she went very still.

"The decay," she said. "The magical decay we're seeing across Britain. If the Manor was a node — if the Malfoy estate was part of the original anchoring infrastructure — then the damage isn't just to distant ley-line connections. The damage is here. To this house. To the magic that sustains it."

"And the house knows it."

They looked at each other. The gated archive hummed around them, the light golden and steady, the walls close in a way that felt sheltering rather than confining. Outside, December was doing its worst — the grounds frozen, the sky dark by four, the wind audible even through stone. In here, the air was warm and still and smelled of old paper and candle wax and, faintly, of her — something clean and ordinary, soap or shampoo, the domestic smell of a person who'd been living in your house long enough to have her own scent settled into the furniture.

"That's why the house is doing what it's doing," Granger said, and he could hear the caution in her voice, the careful approach to territory she wasn't willing to name directly. "The Manor's behavior — the rooms opening, the archive, the —" She gestured between them, a small, unfinished motion. "It's not arbitrary. The house is trying to repair its own infrastructure. It needs the bond between its inhabitants to be strong because the bond is part of what sustains the anchoring magic."

"So we're load-bearing."

She laughed. Short, startled, genuine — the kind that escaped before she could decide whether she wanted to. "That's one way to put it."

"The house needs us to — what? Work well together? Be in the same room? Be —" He stopped before the word. He was doing it again, starting a sentence that would arrive somewhere honest and pulling back before it got there. The gap between what he'd almost said and what he actually said was wide enough to walk through, and he suspected she could see it.

"I think it needs the resonance," she said, steadier now. "The sustained magical interaction between people who are — who have a strong enough connection to feed the anchoring network. The gated archive, the way the house responds to our proximity — it's not a test, or not only a test. It's a mechanism. The house is channeling our combined magical output through its own infrastructure."

"Using us as a battery."

"Using our collaboration as a power source." She corrected him reflexively, the precision instinctive, and then caught herself and made a face that was almost an apology. "Sorry, I'm — it matters, the distinction. It's not about raw magical power. It's about the quality of the interaction. The sustained, intentional, collaborative engagement with the material. When we're working well together, the archive is stable. When we're not —"

"It collapses. I know. I was here when you weren't."

The sentence landed harder than he'd intended. He saw it hit — a small flinch, quickly controlled, and then the careful evenness of her expression settling over whatever she'd felt.

"I know you were," she said.

Silence. The candles flickered. The archive held around them, steady, patient, as if it had all the time in the world and was willing to wait while they figured out what they were to each other inside its walls.

Draco looked down at the folio. The field notes from six centuries ago, the handwriting of artisans who had built the magical infrastructure that sustained an entire country, and it was sitting in his lap because his family had built this house and her mind could read what his eyes could only see. They were, between them, the only two people alive who could decode this. The thought was staggering and he didn't let himself stagger at it. He sat with it instead, turning it over, and what he found underneath was not the intellectual satisfaction he'd expected but something closer to vertigo. They were indispensable to each other. The word sat wrong in his mind, too large and too precise simultaneously.

"We should keep going," he said.

"Yeah." She pulled a fresh sheet of parchment toward her, already organizing, already systematic. "If these are maintenance records, there should be a baseline — a healthy reading, before whatever damage occurred. If we can find that baseline, we can start mapping the degradation. Compare it to the current decay patterns I've been documenting."

They worked. The afternoon darkened outside the narrow windows and the candles burned lower and neither of them moved to light new ones, adjusting instead by mutual, unspoken agreement to the dimming light, leaning closer over the texts, their heads nearly touching over a passage that required both of their attention.

The script began to yield. Not quickly — the trade notation was dense, contextual, full of abbreviations that assumed knowledge neither of them had — but in fragments, in pieces that assembled when he could identify a place name and she could connect it to a theoretical principle. A Malfoy ancestor's shorthand for a geographic feature, cross-referenced against her knowledge of ley-line mapping. A rune cluster he recognized from his father's study, matched to a structural spell she'd read about in a text that no longer existed in any other archive. Each breakthrough required both of them, his knowledge and hers, and the process of decoding was slow and absorbing and — he admitted this only to himself, in the deep interior where no one was listening — more satisfying than anything he'd done in years.

"Draco Malfoy, sixteenth of his name," he murmured, tracing a genealogical marker in the margin. "Not my direct ancestor. A cousin line. They split after Braccus — one branch stayed at the original estate in Normandy, the other came here."

"And the cousin was involved in the construction?"

"He was the site supervisor, I think. The person mediating between the lineal artisans and the family. Look — here's his notation mixed with theirs. He's translating between registers."

Granger leaned across the table to see, and her shoulder pressed against his. She didn't move away. He didn't move away. The contact was warm and unremarkable and he was ferociously aware of every point where her body met his — the shoulder, the arm, the way her elbow rested against his forearm when she reached for the page.

"If the sixteenth Draco was mediating," she said, her voice thoughtful, unaware of or indifferent to their proximity, "then the full notation might be a creole. A hybrid of the family's formal script and the artisans' trade language. That would explain why we can partially read both registers but neither is complete on its own."

"A creole."

"A mixed language. It forms when two speech communities work together long enough that their vocabularies merge. It wouldn't be codified anywhere because it only existed in practice, between these specific people, during this specific project."

"So the only way to reconstruct it —"

"Is to do what they did. Put the two knowledge bases together and let the hybrid emerge."

She turned her head and their faces were very close. Her eyes were brown, lighter near the iris, and he could see the fine lines at their corners that hadn't been there when she was eighteen. The candlelight was orange on her skin and he could count the freckles on the bridge of her nose if he wanted to, and he wanted to, and he didn't count them. He held still and she held still and neither of them breathed for a beat too long and then she looked down at the text and said, "Here. This symbol — I've seen it before. Third-century anchoring theory, Ptolemaic. It means *root* or *source*. And this one next to it, can you —?"

"It's a property marker. The Malfoy crest, simplified. Braccus's branch used a variant without the serpent."

"So *root-of-Malfoy*. The anchoring point's identity was tied to the family."

"The family was the anchor."

Another silence. The house breathed around them — he could feel it, the way he'd always been able to feel it, the Manor's awareness like a pressure in the walls. But it was different now. Warmer. The house was not testing them or pushing them together or engineering proximity. It was simply present and content, the way a house feels when its inhabitants are doing the thing they are supposed to be doing, and the contentment was its own kind of seduction because it showed him what this could be. Not the fraught, careful negotiation that had defined their weeks together, but something sustained and natural and his.

He went to get more candles. The archive held while he was gone, which was new — the room had never been stable without both of them present before. He found the candles in the library's supply closet, fat ivory pillars that smelled faintly of beeswax, and when he returned she'd reorganized the desk in his absence, making space for his folio next to hers, arranging the reference texts in a shared arc between their two chairs. A working space designed for two. She'd done it without thinking, probably — the organizational instinct was as natural to her as breathing — but the effect was intimate in a way that knocked something loose in his chest. She'd made room for him.

He set the candles down and lit them with his wand and the archive brightened, the golden light expanding, and she looked up from the text with an expression that was entirely unguarded — slightly dazed from the close work, her eyes adjusting, a question she hadn't finished forming dying on her lips when she saw him looking at her.

"What?" she said.

"Nothing." He sat down. Their chairs were closer now, angled toward the shared workspace she'd created. When he settled into his, their knees were an inch apart under the table.

"You were looking at me."

"You were looking at the fifteenth-century drainage notation. I was looking at the person looking at the drainage notation. These are different activities."

She almost smiled. The almost was the thing — not the smile itself but the effort of suppressing it, the way her mouth tightened against the impulse and her eyes gave it away anyway. He liked making her almost-smile. He liked that she tried not to and failed. He liked the specific quality of the failure — not a surrender but a concession, as if she was allowing herself this one small thing and would reevaluate later whether it had been a mistake.

They worked through the afternoon and into the evening. The folio yielded more as they developed a shared vocabulary for the hybrid notation — not a complete translation but a working one, built from his fragments and hers, tested and refined by consensus. They argued about a symbol that could have been a direction marker or a temporal reference and the argument went on for twenty minutes and was, Draco realized with a clarity that startled him, the most engaged he'd been in anything since — since he couldn't remember. Since school, maybe. Since before the Mark, before the war reduced everything to survival and its aftermath.

She was right about the symbol, ultimately. It was temporal, not directional — a marker for the seasonal cycle that affected ley-line flow. He conceded the point and she accepted the concession without gloating, which was its own form of generosity, and then she said, "But your instinct about directionality wasn't wrong. Look at this passage — the artisans were mapping flow direction as a function of the season. It's both."

"It's both," he repeated, and the sentence had a weight he hadn't put in it.

Evening deepened. The windows were black now, the December dark absolute outside the Manor's walls. Inside, the archive was a pool of warm light and old paper and the specific domestic comfort of two people who'd been working together all day — the empty teacups on the floor beside his chair, the crumbs from the biscuits she'd brought down from the kitchen, the companionable disorder of shared labor. He could feel the Mark settling into something like quiet, the restless ache easing the way it always did when she was near and their interaction was good, the dark magic in his arm responding to her presence as if her proximity were a kind of medicine, which maybe it was. The resonance between them was a low hum, present and undemanding.

He watched her. He let himself watch her.

She was bent over a passage they'd cracked an hour ago, re-reading it now with the benefit of the notational key they'd been building together. She'd taken the pencil out of her hair at some point and the whole structure had collapsed, dark curls falling around her face, and she kept shoving them back with an impatient hand that achieved nothing. She was mouthing the translation as she worked it, her lips forming words in a language that no one had spoken in six hundred years, a language that existed only in the space between his knowledge and hers. Her feet were tucked under her in the chair. One sock had a hole in the toe.

The thought arrived not as a decision but as a fact, the way gravity arrives when you step off a ledge: he was in love with her. Not falling. Not beginning to. Was. The tense was wrong — he'd been in love with her for weeks, probably, the way you're in a river before you notice you're wet, the way weather changes while you're looking at something else. The realization was not a dramatic upheaval. It was more like coming home to a house you'd been living in without knowing the address, and finding that you recognized the furniture.

He was in love with Hermione Granger in his family's archive, surrounded by texts that proved his ancestors had built the magical infrastructure of Britain, while outside the windows his country's magic was dying and inside his left arm the Dark Lord's curse was quieter than it had been in years because she was close enough to touch.

The facts of this were absurd. He could hear Theo's voice in his head, the dry precision: *A Malfoy in love with a Muggle-born in the cursed family library while the world ends. Somehow you've managed to make catastrophe romantic.* And Draco would have said something cutting back, something about Theo's own talent for constructing narratives that kept the actual feeling at arm's length, but the hypothetical Theo was right. It was absurd. And he didn't care, which was the truly dangerous part — not the feeling itself but the collapse of the architecture he'd built to prevent it.

He'd spent years constructing the conditions of not-wanting. After the war, after the trial, after the slow understanding that his life would be lived in the diminished space his name allowed — he'd made himself small. Careful. He'd learned to need nothing from anyone because need was leverage, and he'd given the world enough leverage already. The Manor was too large and he let it be too large. He ate alone and he worked alone and his friendships were limited to the people who could tolerate his precision and his silences, which meant Theo and his mother and, distantly, the occasional colleague who hadn't known him before the Mark.

He'd built this. Deliberately. A life where no one could hurt him because no one was close enough to reach, and the life had worked — not well, not happily, but it had worked, which was a lower bar than happiness but a bar he could meet. The Mark hurt and the house was too quiet and the world looked at him and saw the Mark and that was survivable because he expected nothing else.

And now she was sitting two feet from him with a hole in her sock and ink on her chin and he wanted everything. Not just her — the whole of it. The work, the house alive and warm instead of cold and watchful, the shared meals and the arguments about notation and the sight of her asleep in his library with a book as a pillow. He wanted to bring her tea in the morning without the careful distance that made the gesture safe. He wanted to brush the hair out of her face and feel her lean into his hand instead of away from it. He wanted to know what she looked like when she wasn't careful, when the precision dropped and the competence stopped being armor and she was just a woman in his house who was glad to be there.

He wanted to touch her. Not the clinical contact of the Mark examinations, where her fingers on his forearm were professional and his stillness was a kind of holding his breath. He wanted the ordinary touching of someone who belonged in your space — a hand on her back as she passed, his fingers in her hair, the thoughtless contact of a shoulder or a hip that two people develop when proximity is not a negotiation but a condition.

He wanted this and the wanting was the most dangerous thing he'd done since the war.

"Malfoy."

He looked up. She was watching him. The text was forgotten in her lap, or not forgotten but released — her hands were resting on the folio's edges and she was sitting very still, the way she sat when she was paying attention to something that wasn't the work. Her expression was — he didn't have a word for it. Open, careful, warm, suspicious, all at once, all overlapping.

"You're staring," she said.

"I know."

He didn't look away. That was the thing. The old Draco — the Draco of a week ago, a month ago, the careful precise diminished version who occupied this house and this name and this Mark — would have looked away and said something deflecting, a dry remark about the quality of the light or the aesthetic superiority of archaic drainage notation. He would have taken the moment and made it safe, and she would have let him, and they would have returned to the work and the distance would have held.

He didn't look away. He sat in his chair in his family's archive with the candles burning low and the Manor warm and steady around them and he looked at her and she looked at him and the distance between them was an inch of air and six centuries of notation and the entirety of what they hadn't said.

"You've got ink on your chin," he said.

She touched her chin. Wrong side. He reached out — not quickly, not slowly, at the speed of someone doing something ordinary — and brushed the smudge with his thumb. Her skin was warm. She went still under his hand, not a flinch but an attention, the way someone goes still when they want to be sure of what's happening before they respond to it.

His thumb rested on her chin. He didn't move it. She didn't move away.

"There," he said. His voice was rougher than he'd planned, which was fitting because he hadn't planned any of this.

"Thanks," she said. Her voice was careful.

He let his hand drop. The contact had lasted maybe three seconds and his pulse was doing something he'd have to think about later.

She looked at him for another long moment — assessing, or deciding, or struggling with something he couldn't see — and then she turned back to the folio and said, "I think this passage is the inspection schedule for the northeast anchoring point. Can you check the property markers? I need the geographic referent."

"Yes," he said. He picked up his quill. His hand was steady. He was aware of the steadiness as a separate phenomenon from the state of his interior, which was not steady at all but liquid and rearranged and, for the first time in years, not braced against anything.

They worked. Side by side, closer than the work required, in the warm archive while the Manor held around them like hands cupped around a flame. The house was showing him something — not forcing, not testing, but showing. This is what it looks like. This is what it could be. The light, the warmth, the woman, the work. The life where the corridors don't stretch and the rooms don't punish and someone is here and you are not alone.

He'd spent years learning not to want. He'd been good at it. The discipline of not-wanting had organized his entire post-war existence and he'd mistaken the discipline for the thing itself, confusing the absence of desire with the absence of need. She'd undone this — not deliberately, not through seduction or strategy or anything so dramatic. She'd undone it by being in his library with ink on her hands, by knowing things he didn't know and needing things he could give, by arguing about notation with an intensity that made his blood move, by having a hole in her sock.

The archive was the warmest room in the Manor. The fire they hadn't lit — no fireplace in this room, there never had been — should have left the space cold. Instead the walls themselves seemed to radiate, a gentle, encompassing heat that was the house's contribution to the evening. The beeswax candles gave the light a quality that was almost domestic, almost intimate, the kind of light you'd read by in a bedroom you shared with someone. The manuscripts were still open between them and their work was still the center of the space they occupied, but the work had changed valence. It was no longer only research. It was the thing they did together, the activity that made the house warm and the archive stable and the Mark quiet and the distance between them impossible to maintain.

It was late. He knew it was late because the candles had burned to their last inch and the winter silence outside was the deep, pressurized quiet of a world past midnight. They should stop. They should go to their separate rooms in separate wings and sleep separately and maintain the architecture of distance that had kept them both alive in here.

Granger closed her folio. She stretched — arms overhead, spine arching, the jumper riding up to show a strip of skin at her waist that he saw and filed and would think about later in the dark of his room. She rolled her neck and her hair moved across her shoulders and the pencil that should have fallen out hours ago finally clattered to the floor.

"I think we've got enough of the notation to build a proper key," she said. "Tomorrow we can start on the southern anchoring records. If the pattern holds, the early readings should show us the baseline before the degradation."

"Tomorrow."

"Mm." She stood. Her socked feet were silent on the archive floor. She gathered her notes — a stack of parchment covered in her cramped, precise handwriting, cross-referenced and indexed because she couldn't help herself — and tucked them under her arm. She looked around the archive, at the golden light and the warm walls and the candles dying on the desk, and her expression was the one he'd been learning to read over weeks of proximity: the one where she wanted to say something and was calculating whether saying it was worth the risk.

She didn't say it. She said, "Goodnight, Malfoy," and turned toward the door.

"Granger."

She stopped. Didn't turn. Waited.

He looked at her back — the jumper, the disastrous hair, the rolled parchment under her arm, the socks on the stone floor — and he thought about what he wanted to say and what he was allowed to say and the gap between the two, and then he stopped calculating.

"This is the best work I've ever done," he said. "I wanted you to know that."

She was quiet for a moment. Then she turned her head, not fully, just enough that he could see the line of her jaw and the corner of her mouth. "Yeah," she said. "Me too."

She left. The archive held. The room stayed warm and lit and open, as if it would have waited for them all night, and the Manor around it hummed with a contentment that was not his and not hers and not the house's alone but something shared between the three of them, a resonance that was as much about the work as the want, and Draco sat in the golden light with his family's texts open on his lap and the Dark Mark quiet in his arm and did not pretend, even to himself, that what he was feeling was anything other than what it was.

He was in love with her. The house knew. He suspected she knew. The only person who hadn't known, until today, was him, and that was because knowing required a kind of surrender he'd trained himself against, and the training had finally, mercifully, broken.

He blew out the last candle. The archive dimmed but didn't close. He walked through the library and the corridors were short and warm, the Manor guiding him with the specific gentleness it reserved for the moments when he was most himself, and he went to his room and lay on his bed and stared at the ceiling and felt the Mark settle into its quietest register, the dark magic resting, and felt the resonance telling him she was one floor down, awake, in the room that the house had placed close enough to hear if she called and far enough to make the distance a choice.

He didn't sleep. He lay in the dark and wanted her and didn't do anything about it and the wanting, for the first time, was not something to be survived but something to be inhabited, and the difference between those two was the whole of what had changed.


# Chapter 15

The Levett manuscript was proving resistant.

They'd been at it since dinner — or since what had passed for dinner, which was tea and the remnants of a bread-and-cheese plate that the house-elves left in the library around seven each evening. Hermione had eaten most of the cheese without noticing. Malfoy had eaten the bread. They'd divided the meal without discussing it, the way they divided most things now: by habit, by proximity, by whatever silent negotiation had replaced the careful formality of their first weeks.

The text was a construction manual for anchoring infrastructure, written in the fourteenth century by a Malfoy who had either invented a personal notation system or been actively hostile to future readers. Possibly both. The marginal annotations were in three languages and at least two different hands, and the primary text used runic modifiers that neither of them had encountered independently but that, when she cross-referenced her theoretical framework with his family knowledge of the symbols, resolved into something almost legible.

Almost. The current passage had been refusing resolution for forty minutes.

"It's not 'binding,'" she said, frowning at the glyph cluster at the center of the page. "The radical is wrong. It's more like — threading? Weaving through?"

"Plaiting," he said. He was sitting close enough that his shoulder was near hers when they both leaned over the text, which was often. She could smell the cedar of his soap and the undertone of something else — ink, or the specific mineral tang that clung to everything in the deep archive, the preservation charms layered so thick down there the air tasted like chalk.
