He'd been writing in the margins of their working translation all evening, his handwriting getting smaller and denser as the hours passed. "The secondary radical modifies it. It's a textile metaphor. My great-great-however-many-times grandmother was apparently a weaver before she married into the name."

"How do you know that?"

"There's a tapestry. Third floor. She wove it herself, and my mother made sure I knew, because—" He stopped. The rest of the sentence would have been something about Narcissa's opinions on Malfoy women's contributions to the family legacy, and he seemed to decide it was too much to offer at nearly midnight. "It's a textile metaphor."

She looked at the glyph again. Plaiting. The anchoring magic was described as threads drawn through a substrate — the land itself, the ambient field — and plaited into a structure that held. Not bound, not hammered. Woven. The image made the decay suddenly more visceral: not a wall crumbling but a fabric unraveling, threads pulling loose one by one.

"That works," she said. "That actually works. Look — if you read it as plaiting, then this passage here—" She turned the page and found the corresponding section, her finger tracing the line of glyphs until they resolved. "The whole anchoring system is a textile structure. The ley-line connections aren't channels, they're threads. And war damage would be—"

"Cuts," he said. "Tears in the fabric."

"Which means the Ministry's been trying to mend a tapestry by building scaffolding around it. No wonder nothing's held."

"Mm." He pulled a second text toward him — a later manuscript, this one in Latin with French glosses. The page he wanted was bookmarked with a strip of parchment in his handwriting from two days ago. She watched him scan the Latin without thinking about it; his reading speed in Latin was faster than hers, one of the few areas where the pureblood education outpaced hers, and she had stopped resenting this and started relying on it sometime in the third week. "This passage references a repair method. Rethreading. Not patching — actually running new thread through the existing warp."

"You'd need access to the original structure. The loom, essentially."

"Which would be the anchoring points themselves."

"Which are—"

"Under the house. Among other places."

They looked at each other. The implication settled between them the way implications had been settling between them for weeks — with weight, with the quiet understanding that what they were finding in this library was going to matter beyond the two of them and this room.

"We should map them," she said.

"Mm." He pushed his chair back slightly, not standing but stretching his spine. His neck cracked. "Not tonight."

"No. Not tonight."

They worked on the maintenance protocol for another half hour. Malfoy found a cross-reference in the Latin manuscript — a passage describing the location of what the text called *nodi*, knot-points where the plaited threads converged — and she pulled the Levett folio back toward her to check whether the construction manual corroborated the locations. It did, approximately. The notation was different enough that the correspondence wasn't exact, and they spent fifteen minutes arguing about whether a particular glyph indicated compass direction or depth below surface. He was wrong, she was almost certain, but he made his case well enough that she wrote both interpretations in the margin and bracketed them.

"We'll need the estate maps," she said. "The old ones. Pre-war."

"Second gallery. Third shelf from the end. There's a case with the surveyor's records going back to the sixteenth century." He paused. "I think. I haven't opened it since I was twelve."

"What were you doing with surveyor's records at twelve?"

"Hiding from a dinner party."

She almost laughed. The image was specific enough to be true: a twelve-year-old Malfoy, bored and trapped, opening a case of old maps in the upper gallery while his parents entertained downstairs. She could see the boy clearly and the seeing was dangerous in the way all these moments were dangerous — the small, unintended disclosures that accumulated between them like sediment, building a picture of a person she hadn't meant to know this well.

He got up to make tea. She heard him at the side table where the kettle sat, the small sounds of the preparation — the tap of the caddy, the pour of water, the clink of the spoon — and she kept her eyes on the manuscript and listened to him the way she listened to everything he did when he wasn't watching: with an attention she did not want to name.

He set a cup beside her elbow. She didn't look up. "Thanks."

"Mm."

The tea was right. It had been right for weeks — the amount of milk, the brewing time, the specific way she took it — and he had never asked. He'd simply observed and adjusted until the cup he made her matched the cup she made herself, and the care in that, the quiet precision of it, was the kind of thing that could undo a person if she let it.

She drank. He sat back down. The work continued.

The library at this hour had a quality she'd come to think of as its real self — not the grand sunlit room she'd first catalogued in early November, but this smaller, darker version with the lamplight making islands on the desk and the shelves drawn close and the silence not empty but populated, as if the books themselves settled differently when the people reading them stopped pretending to be professional about it. The upper galleries were dark. The windows showed nothing but the reflection of the room and, behind it, the black of a winter night without a moon.

He rubbed the back of his neck with one hand and she watched the gesture — the long fingers, the wrist where the Mark sat under his rolled sleeve. The lamplight caught the edge of the Mark and she looked away. She knew it by touch better than by sight anyway. She knew, from the resonance, that the Mark was quiet tonight. She could feel it the way she felt the temperature of the room: a low-level awareness, not quite pain, just the fact of his body's condition seeping into hers like damp through stone.

She knew what she knew. That was the problem.

The bonding texts she'd found in the deeper archive — four weeks ago, or close to it; she'd lost the exact date, which bothered her in a way she didn't have time to examine — described this sensation with clinical precision. The resonance. The growing inability to distinguish between your own physical state and the other person's. The Manor's architecture doing what it was designed to do: amplifying what existed between its inhabitants, creating conditions for connection, gating access behind the strength of the bond. She had read the texts twice and understood the mechanism and she had not told him. Every evening since, they sat like this, close over a manuscript, their elbows almost touching, and the knowledge sat in her like swallowed glass — present always, cutting only when she moved wrong.

"You've gone quiet," he said.

"I'm thinking about loom mechanics."

"You're a terrible liar."

"I'm an excellent liar, actually." She said it before she thought about it, and the truth of it — the weight-bearing truth, given what she was concealing — made her hand tighten on the pen she was holding until the barrel dug into her fingers. She looked back at the manuscript. "I'm thinking about how you'd access an anchoring point that's been buried under a house for six hundred years without destabilizing the house."

He didn't press. He rarely did. It was one of the things about him that made the silence between them bearable: he asked once, accepted the deflection, and let the question exist unanswered without making it a wound. Other people — Harry, Ron in the years before — would have circled back. Malfoy let things sit, and the absence of pressure was its own kind of closeness. He trusted her to speak when she was ready. He was wrong to trust her about this specific silence, but he didn't know that.

The library had settled into its night configuration — quieter, the shelves drawn closer, the shadows deeper in the upper galleries where the lamplight didn't reach. The fire in the grate had burned low and neither of them had moved to rebuild it because the Manor itself was warm, had been warm for days now, the stone walls holding heat with the steady generosity of a house that wanted its inhabitants comfortable. Wanted them here. Outside, December was doing what December did in Wiltshire: frost on the grounds, darkness since four o'clock, the wind finding the old window joins and making a thin sound that Hermione had come to associate with these late working hours, this specific quality of time.

She turned a page in the Levett manuscript. Her handwriting in the margins had gotten worse over the weeks — faster, less careful, abbreviated in ways that only made sense if you'd been following the work from the beginning. His annotations were neater but increasingly dense, and she'd started reading his marginal notes the way she read the primary text itself: as a record of a mind working on the page, showing its reasoning, trusting her to follow.

She reached for her tea and found it cold. The cup had been sitting there for an hour at least. She drank it anyway — cold tea was still tea, and the bitterness was clarifying.

"This section," he said. He angled the text toward her with one hand, his index finger under a line of archaic notation. "I think this is a maintenance protocol. The language shifts — imperative, not descriptive. Instructions rather than theory."

She leaned in. His finger was on the page and her gaze followed it and the notation was dense, the glyphs small, and she tilted her head to read them and his face was right there. Closer than it should have been, closer than the text required. The lamplight was on his jaw and the shadow of his eyelashes on his cheek and the notation she was supposed to be reading had become entirely illegible because he had gone very still.

He was looking at her. Not at the page. His finger was still under the line of text but his eyes were on her face, and the expression in them was something she had been pretending not to see for weeks — the unguarded thing that appeared when he forgot to be guarded, the look that was neither careful nor dry nor precise but simply, nakedly, want.

She should have leaned back. She should have said something about the notation, about the hour, about anything that would have rebuilt the distance they'd been maintaining like a bandage over a wound neither of them wanted to examine. She knew what the Manor did. She knew what the resonance was. She had a month of concealed knowledge telling her that every moment of closeness was suspect, that the warmth she felt might be architecture rather than desire, that the pull between them could be the house's amplification rather than the simple, devastating fact of wanting someone.

She didn't lean back.

His mouth was slightly open. She could see the small dry place on his lower lip where he bit it when he was concentrating, a detail she had catalogued weeks ago without examining why she was cataloguing it, and his breath came shallow and she could hear it because the library was that quiet, the fire that low, the world outside that dark.

"Granger," he said, and his voice was quieter than usual, slightly rough, and the roughness was not from talking — they'd been mostly silent for the past hour. The roughness was something else.

"Yeah."

"I'm not—" He stopped. The self-correction, the retreat from honesty that was so characteristic she could have predicted the pause before it arrived. Except this time there was nowhere to retreat to because she was right there, her face inches from his, the page between them the only barrier and neither of them looking at the page. "Never mind."

She put her hand on his.

On the manuscript page, his finger still under the line of archaic text, and her hand covered his and neither of them breathed. His hand was warm — warmer than she expected, warmer than her own fingers that had been turning stiff manuscript pages for hours. His knuckles under her palm. The texture of his skin, dry from the chalk-dust of preservation charms that coated everything in this library. The slight catch of his breath, audible because the room was that quiet.

He turned his hand under hers. Not pulling away — turning it over, so his palm met hers and his fingers closed around hers and the manuscript page crumpled under their combined weight and neither of them cared about the fourteenth-century parchment they were ruining.

"I don't know what I'm doing," she said. Her voice was not the voice she used for research, for arguments, for the precise and measured delivery that had organized her entire adult life. It was smaller than that. "I don't — I should—"

He kissed her.

It was not smooth. His mouth found hers at a wrong angle and she felt the bump of his teeth against her lip and the startled inhale through his nose and his hand tightened on hers and his other hand came up and hovered near her jaw without quite landing, as though he was waiting for permission or for his own nerve to catch up with his body. She made a sound — not a word, something pulled from the center of her chest — and she leaned into it and the angle corrected itself and his mouth was on hers properly and it was clumsy, it was genuinely clumsy, his nose bumped hers and she could feel the tension in his jaw under her palm when her free hand came up without her deciding it should and the whole thing was nothing like what she'd imagined in the sleepless hours when she let herself imagine.

She'd imagined composure. She'd imagined his precision extending to this. It didn't. His hand finally landed on her jaw, too firm, his fingers pressing into the soft skin below her ear with a grip that was closer to holding on than to tenderness, and she didn't mind — she actively didn't mind, the force of it was the most honest thing she'd felt in months, his body telling her something his speech never would. Her free hand went to his shirt — not unbuttoning, just holding, the fabric bunched in her fist against his chest, and she could feel his heartbeat through the cotton and it was fast, hammering, and the resonance between them —

The resonance. The thing she could feel along the frequency of the Mark, the connection that ran between them like a wire pulled taut, and it was more now, not louder but more total, the awareness of him flooding through her like water through a split in a dam. She could feel his heartbeat in her own chest. She could feel the adrenaline in his blood as though it were hers. The Mark on his forearm was doing something — not hurting, not for either of them, but resonating, vibrating at a pitch that matched the frequency of her own pulse, and the sensation was the most intimate thing she had ever felt and the thing she trusted least in the world.

*Is this me?* The thought came like a fracture in the surface of the moment. *Is this me or is this the house?*

She kissed him harder. That was her answer — not an answer, a refusal to let the doubt have the moment when the moment was already happening. His hand slid from her jaw to the back of her neck, into her hair, which had come loose from its tie hours ago, and his fingers tangled there and she felt the tug at her scalp and her body responded before her mind could categorize the response. She opened her mouth against his and tasted tea and the faint mineral air of the library and beneath that something that was just him, particular and impossible to attribute to architecture.

He pulled her closer. The desk was between them and the motion was graceless — a stack of reference texts toppled sideways, one hit the floor with a flat heavy sound that should have broken the moment but didn't. She was half out of her chair, her hip jammed against the desk's edge, his hand at the back of her neck and her hand still fisted in his shirt and their joined hands still on the manuscript, fingers interlaced over the Levett folio's ruined page about maintenance protocols for magic that had been woven into the English earth six centuries ago.

She could feel the doubt and the want occupying the same space in her body, like two instruments playing different melodies in the same key. The want was specific: his mouth, his hands, the way his breath came short and warm against her upper lip when they broke apart for a half-second and came back together. The doubt was equally specific: the resonance was real, the amplification was real, the house around them was warm as a held breath and she could not, even now, even with his mouth on hers, separate what she felt from what the Manor was helping her feel.

She pulled back. Not far. An inch. His forehead rested against hers. His breathing was ragged and his eyes were closed and the hand in her hair had gone still.

"Is this—" he started.

"Don't," she said. She wasn't sure what she was refusing — the question, the doubt, the conversation that would follow if either of them tried to name what had just happened. "Don't. Not yet."

She kissed him again. Slower this time. His mouth was softer when he wasn't startled by it, and he kissed her back with a deliberateness that was nothing like the careful restraint he'd maintained in every other interaction since she'd arrived at the Manor. This was deliberate in the other direction — choosing to be present, choosing to be thorough. His lips against hers and then against the corner of her mouth and then her jaw, and she tilted her head and felt his breath against her neck and the sensation traveled the length of her spine and she thought *I know what the house does and I am here anyway and I don't know what that makes me*.

He said her name against her neck. "Granger." Not her first name — the surname they'd maintained like a last piece of formality, except his mouth was on her throat and the name was a vibration against her skin and it sounded nothing like distance.

She let go of his shirt. Moved her hand to his face, his jaw, the angular line of it under her palm. He was thinner than anyone she'd touched in years — Ron had been solid, broad, a comfort of scale — and the difference was not better or worse, it was simply this body, this specific person, the faint scratch where he hadn't shaved since morning. She ran her thumb along the hinge of his jaw and he turned into the touch, eyes still closed, and the gesture was so unguarded that she felt something in her chest fold in on itself — not pain, not quite tenderness, something closer to the shock of seeing an animal let itself be handled after months of flinching.

They stayed like that. His face against her neck, her hand on his jaw, the desk still between them and the library around them and the fire almost dead and the lamp guttering to its last half-inch of oil. The Manor, warm and present around them like a third body in the room. She could feel the house's response the way you feel a pressure change before a storm: not as a thought or a sound but as a shift in the quality of the air, a deepening of the warmth that was already too warm, the stone walls holding heat with an enthusiasm that had nothing to do with weather.

When they separated — fully, leaning back into their own chairs — the absence of his hands felt like the removal of something structural. The air between them was cooler by comparison. He was looking at her with an expression she'd never seen on his face: open, stunned, the dryness and the precision stripped away, and underneath it a fear that was recognizable because it matched her own.

"I should go," she said.

"Yeah." He ran his hand through his hair and it stood up wrong and he looked, for a moment, like somebody she hadn't met yet — not the boy who'd watched her scream on his drawing room floor, not the careful man who weighed every word, but someone between those, newer, still forming. "Yeah."

She gathered her notes. The motion was automatic — straightening papers, closing the manuscript, recapping the inkwell. Her hands were shaking. She didn't try to stop it. He watched her tidy the desk and said nothing about the shaking and the kindness of that silence, the deliberate not-noticing, sat in her throat.

"Tomorrow," she said. "The anchoring points. We should map them."

"Tomorrow."

She stood. He stood. The library settled around them with a sound like an exhalation — the old wood, the old stone, the old magic in the walls doing whatever it was the magic did when the people inside the walls did what it wanted them to do.

"Goodnight, Granger."

"Goodnight."

She left the library. The corridor outside was lit by wall sconces that burned low, the flames dipping as she passed and then rising again behind her, as if the house were tracking her passage through its body. The stone floor was warm under her shoes. It shouldn't have been — stone floors in December should have been cold, should have made her hurry — but the Manor had been heating its floors for weeks, for her, and she'd stopped being startled by it. That was the worst part: how quickly the abnormal had become baseline. How easily she'd adapted to living inside something that was adapting to her.

The corridor to the guest wing was shorter than she remembered. It had been this way since she'd returned after the weeks away — the house compressing the distance between the library and her room, trimming the walk from five minutes to three as if it couldn't bear for her to be in transit any longer than necessary. She hated that she noticed it. She hated more that the shortened walk felt like consideration rather than manipulation, the same way a person might walk you to a closer door because they could see you were tired.

She was tired. She was also vibrating at a frequency that was incompatible with sleep, her lips still tender from the pressure of his mouth and her hand still holding the ghost of his hand and her body carrying the imprint of the last hour like a photograph developing in reverse — the image getting sharper, not fainter, with every step she took away from him.

---

Her room was warm.

She stood in the doorway and felt it on her face. The fireplace was lit — she hadn't left it lit. The bedcovers had been turned down and the lamp beside the bed was on at its lowest setting and the room smelled faintly of lavender, which it had never smelled of before, and the whole arrangement was so pointed, so blatantly tending, that she pressed her hand against the doorframe and stood there for ten seconds fighting the urge to laugh or to scream or both.

She closed the door. Locked it with her wand, then with her hand, the deadbolt a physical reassurance that a locked door still meant something in a house that rearranged its own corridors at will.

She sat on the bed. Pressed her hands to her face and breathed.

The resonance was still there. Quieter now, attenuated by the distance between them, but steady, humming along the connection like a plucked string losing amplitude in slow degrees. She could feel him — not his thoughts, never his thoughts, but the register of his wakefulness, the pace of his heart closer to normal now but not normal, the fact that he was still in the library standing where she'd left him and he was awake and his body was running the same circuit of shock and want and fear that hers was, and she knew all of this because the house had built a wire between them and the wire carried everything.

She pulled off her shoes and dropped them on the floor. Her feet ached in the specific way feet ache after a long day of not moving — not exertion but stiffness, the body's complaint at being held still too long. She pulled her jumper over her head and sat in her vest top and jeans, her arms exposed to the warm air. She looked at her left forearm. The glamour was intact; it held better when she wasn't casting. Underneath it the scar was there, the letters she'd carried for seven years, the word carved into her by a dead woman in the room downstairs. Malfoy had traced those letters with his thumb three weeks ago and tonight she had kissed him in his library and neither of them had mentioned the scar. She didn't know if that meant they were past it or if it meant they were building something on top of it without looking down.

She lay back on the bed. The mattress held her with the particular generosity the Manor extended to her sleeping arrangements — not a generic comfort but a specific, attentive one, adjusting to her weight and her position in a way that better mattresses simply did not do. The sheets warmed under her body heat within seconds.

She should have been thinking about the research. The plaiting model changed everything — the repair methodology, the approach to the anchoring points, the entire framework the Ministry had been using was wrong. She had the shape of a solution for the first time since she'd arrived at the Manor. It was the kind of breakthrough that would normally have kept her up for hours with excitement, filling parchment, drawing diagrams, building the argument.

Instead she was lying on her back staring at the ceiling with her fingers pressed to her mouth and the taste of him still on her lips.

The bonding texts had been specific. Not metaphorical, not oblique in the way of most Dark Arts theory. They described the mechanism in the neutral, instructional language of builders, of craftsmen recording process for future generations: the Manor reads the emotional frequency between its inhabitants and amplifies it. The amplification is not invention. The house cannot fabricate feeling from nothing. But it can take a thread and thicken it, a warmth and deepen it, a pull and strengthen it, until the inhabitants cannot distinguish the organic signal from the architectural one. It can make the proximity more electric. It can make the distance more painful. It can make the quiet evenings in the library feel like the only real hours in a person's day, and the accidental brush of a hand feel like being touched for the first time.

This was the problem. Not that the Manor was lying to her — it might not be lying at all. The feelings might be entirely real. She thought they were real. In her worse moments, in the small hours when the analytical framework crumbled and she was just a woman lying in a bed in a house that knew her too well, she was certain they were real. But they had been grown in a greenhouse. She would never know what size they'd have reached in open air.

She had kissed a man tonight. The kiss had been clumsy and desperate and specific and it had tasted like cold tea and chalk-dust and his particular skin and none of those things could be faked by a house, surely, a building could not manufacture the way his teeth had bumped her lip or the way his hand had shaken when it found her jaw. But the intensity — the need — the sense that the distance between them had become physically unbearable, that not kissing him was a kind of pain — was that hers? Was the volume of the want hers, or was the house turning the dial?

She thought about the way he had looked at her in the seconds before. The unguarded expression that he couldn't hold for long, that appeared in flashes and then disappeared behind the dryness and the precision the way the sun disappears behind fast-moving clouds. He had been looking at her with an honesty that cost him something — she could see the cost, could feel it through the resonance, the vulnerability of wanting something when your entire post-war life had been organized around the practice of not wanting.

He didn't know what the house was doing. He didn't know about the texts. He had kissed her believing that whatever was between them was between them, and the difference between his belief and her knowledge was the specific, unbridgeable distance she could not bring herself to close.

She rolled onto her side and pulled the blanket over herself without getting under the covers properly, still in her jeans and her socks, too hollowed out to finish undressing. The pillow was cool against her cheek — the one surface in the room the Manor hadn't warmed — and she pressed her face into it and closed her eyes.

Behind her eyelids: his face in the lamplight, the manuscript between them, the moment before. The expression. The way he had started to say something and stopped. *I'm not—* What? Not what? She would never know what the end of that sentence was because she had put her hand on his before he could finish and now the sentence would stay incomplete, a piece of syntax permanently suspended between intention and utterance.

The resonance carried his state to her across two floors of stone. He was lying down now. Not sleeping — his heart rate was too fast for sleep, the pattern of his wakefulness too irregular. The Mark on his forearm was quiet, quieter than she'd felt it in weeks, the dark magic in it settled into something close to calm. As if the Mark, too, was satisfied. As if the curse-brand that Voldemort had burned into his skin at sixteen was responding to the fact that someone had touched him with her mouth and her hands and her willing body, and she couldn't tell if that was beautiful or horrifying and possibly it was both.

She lay still and the Manor breathed around her. She could feel it — not hear it, feel it, the way you feel the motion of water when you're in it. The house was settling into something she could only describe as satisfaction, if architecture could be satisfied. The warmth was its warmest since she'd arrived five weeks ago. The corridor outside her door was still and short. The walls gave off heat like sunned stone. The whole wing, the whole house, felt like a body curled around her in sleep, protective and encompassing and impossible to refuse without getting up and walking out the door.

She should tell him. Tomorrow. She should sit across from him in the library and open the bonding folio and say: I found these four weeks ago and I didn't tell you and I should have and here is what they say. The house has been amplifying everything between us. The resonance is part of its design. Every moment of closeness — the evenings in the library, the examinations, the way the archive only opens for us together — has been occurring under conditions I understood and you didn't. And tonight when I put my hand on yours and when you kissed me and when I kissed you back I did all of it carrying a knowledge I hadn't shared and you deserved to have.

She should tell him tomorrow.

She would not tell him tomorrow.

She knew this with the specific clarity that arrives at one in the morning after a kiss that has rearranged the architecture of your nervous system. She would not tell him because telling him would mean watching his face close — watching the openness collapse, watching him retreat behind the precision and the dryness and the careful, defended distance that had taken her five weeks to erode. She would not tell him because she had seen his face when it was unguarded and she could not make herself be the reason it closed again. She would not tell him because she was selfish in the way of a person who has been alone for a long time and has finally been handed something warm and cannot make herself put it down, even though she knows — she knows, she knows — that the warmth may not be entirely hers.

The fire crackled. The Manor held her. The lavender smell in the room was new and deliberate and almost certainly the house's idea of comfort and she breathed it in anyway because what else was she going to do, stop breathing.

She lay on her side in a warm room in a house that wanted her and she could feel, two floors up, the boy she had kissed lying awake in his own warm room, and the distance between them was the width of the house and the width of what she knew and the width of what she couldn't bring herself to say, and all three distances were exactly the same.

She did not sleep for a long time.


# Chapter 16

The archive had been letting him in for three days.

Not properly — not the way it opened when Granger was there, the shelves steady, the light warm and constant, the deeper rooms unfolding like a series of answered questions. Alone, the space tolerated him. The ceiling flickered between heights. The reading table materialized but tilted slightly, one leg shorter than the others, as if the room couldn't quite remember its own geometry. The first time he'd tried, Tuesday afternoon while she was down in the library cataloguing spell-failure reports from St. Mungo's, the archive had held for twelve minutes before the walls started contracting and he'd had to leave at a pace that was not running but was not walking either.

He'd gone back the next morning. Twenty minutes. The shelves along the far wall were accessible but the deeper alcoves stayed dark, their contents invisible behind a kind of architectural refusal — not a door, not a wall, just a persistent elsewhere that his hand couldn't reach through.

Today was better. The room was colder than it had been with her, the light a thin grey instead of the amber that pooled on the reading table when they worked together, but the space held. He'd been here for over half an hour and the walls hadn't moved.

Draco stood at the far shelving unit, the one that had surfaced texts on anchoring infrastructure when they were together two weeks ago. His fingers moved along the spines with the practiced care his mother had taught him — Malfoy texts were old, some of them skin-bound, and you didn't grab at them. He was looking for a notation reference Granger had mentioned yesterday, a cross-index between the ley-line maps and the original construction records. She'd described it precisely, as she described everything, and he'd memorized the description without meaning to because that was what happened now when she spoke. The words stayed.

He found the cross-index. It was shelved behind another text, a thick folio in a binding he didn't recognize — dark leather, older than the anchoring records, the preservation charms on it faintly different in texture from the standard Malfoy library wards. He pulled the folio out to reach what was behind it, and the spine cracked as it came free.

Old binding. He should have been more careful. He set it on the tilted reading table and the room shuddered once, the light dimming before catching itself.

The folio was in his great-great-grandfather's hand — Draco recognized the notation, the compressed marginal references, the way the man wrote his Ms with an extra flourish that looked accidental but was actually a ward-signature, a habit passed down through four generations of Malfoy archivists. The title page was in archaic magical notation layered over Latin, and Draco's Latin was good enough to parse it: *On the Residential Bonding Architecture of Malfoy Manor, Its Purposes, Constraints, and Conditions of Activation.*

His hand stopped on the page.

He read the title again. The words didn't change.

*Bonding architecture.*

The folio was organized in sections. He sat down on the chair, which creaked but held, and he read. The archaic notation was dense — he couldn't parse all of it without Granger's theoretical training — but the Latin underneath was clear enough, and his great-great-grandfather had written marginal glosses in plain English for sections he apparently wanted his descendants to understand without scholarly effort.

*The Manor sustains the magical line through forging connections between its inhabitants.* A gloss: *The house reads what is between them and makes it stronger. This is its primary function and the reason it was built.*

*Access to the deeper archives is gated by the resonance between bonded inhabitants. The stronger the bond, the deeper the access.* A gloss: *The house will not open its heart to one alone. It requires two, and it requires them in truth.*

*The resonance manifests as mutual awareness — proprioceptive, emotional, and eventually physical. The inhabitants will feel each other at distance. This is not a side effect. It is the mechanism by which the house assesses the bond's strength.*

Draco's hands were flat on the table. The page beneath his right palm was slightly damp where the leather binding had sweated — or where his hand had.

He turned to the section on conditions of activation. The notation was denser here, layered with ward-diagrams he couldn't fully read, but the marginal glosses continued.

*Activation requires genuine emotional resonance. The house cannot fabricate connection where none exists. However, once resonance is present, the house amplifies it — strengthening attraction, deepening mutual awareness, and creating conditions of proximity that reinforce the bond.*

He read the sentence twice. Three times.

*The house amplifies it.*

The gated archive. The corridors that shortened between her room and the library. The warmth that followed her through the Manor. The way the house went cold when she pulled away and warm when she came back. The Mark quieting under her touch and only her touch. The way he could feel her attention in a room before he saw her — the low hum at the edge of his awareness that he'd been attributing to the Mark's resonance, which he had, which she had said was diagnostic residue, which —

He stopped reading. He looked at the far wall of the archive, where the deeper alcoves still refused him, dark and elsewhere and waiting for a presence that wasn't here.

She'd found this.

The certainty arrived without preamble. It was not an inference. It was the same folio, the same shelving unit, the same texts she had been pulling for weeks. She'd been working in this room for longer than he had. She had read these words before he did. She read Latin and she read archaic magical notation and she was the most thorough researcher alive and she had found this and she had not told him.

He sat in the tilted chair in the cold grey light and the dates arranged themselves with the clarity of something that had always been visible and he had simply failed to see.

Five weeks ago. Her withdrawal — the sudden briskness, the professional distance she'd reinstated overnight. He had interpreted it as rejection, had spent a week quietly destroying himself trying to identify which of his many failures had finally registered as disqualifying. The Manor had gone cold. The corridors lengthened. The archive wouldn't open. He had assumed — he had thought —

She found the texts and she pulled away.

He remembered that week with a specificity that embarrassed him now. He'd spent it auditing his own behavior — replaying conversations in the library, examining meals they'd shared, trying to locate the moment where he'd said the wrong thing or pushed too close or let something show that should have stayed hidden. He'd assumed it was the resonance. That she'd sensed his want through the Mark-bond and been repelled by it, by the naked fact of Draco Malfoy wanting something he had no right to want. He'd eaten dinner alone for five nights and the house had gone cold around him and he'd thought: *This is correct. This is how it was always going to end. You allowed yourself to forget what you are, and she remembered.*

He'd been wrong. She hadn't been repelled. She'd been *informed.* She'd found the texts and pulled back not because she felt too little but because she suspected the house of making her feel too much, and the difference between those two things was the entire distance between the story he'd been living and the story she'd been living, and she hadn't told him they were in different stories.

Then the scar. The glamour flickered during an examination — her sustained casting destabilizing the concealment she'd worn since he'd known her, probably since the war — and he saw what Bellatrix had carved into her forearm. MUDBLOOD. The letters warped by scar tissue that would never be smooth, the cursed wound from his aunt's knife on his drawing room floor. He'd touched it before he could stop himself. She flinched and his hand stayed and they'd sat there in a silence that was the most honest they'd been with each other, and he had thought — he'd been certain — that this was the last wall coming down. That after the drawing room and the Mark memory and now this, the scar she'd hidden from everyone, there was nothing between them that wasn't known.

He'd been wrong about that too. She'd let him trace those letters with his thumb, let him sit with the weight of what his family's house had done to her body, and she knew — she'd known for weeks by then — that the house might be engineering his guilt and her forgiveness alike. She'd let him have that moment and it might have been the architecture's production and she'd said nothing.

After the scar, she came back. Softened. Returned to proximity, to late nights in the library, to their work. He had been grateful. He had been so fucking grateful that she'd come back that he hadn't questioned why she'd left.

And then the kiss. In the library, late, the translation they'd been arguing about, and she looked at him and the distance was gone. He could still feel the exact pressure of her mouth, the way her hand came up and gripped his sleeve, the sound she made when he pulled her closer. He'd been thinking about that sound for three days. He'd been thinking about her hands and her mouth and the way she tasted like the tea she'd been drinking and the way the resonance between them had sung, amplified, so loud in his awareness that he couldn't tell where his wanting ended and the house's began.

She'd known. When she kissed him. She knew the house was amplifying what was between them and she kissed him anyway and she didn't tell him.

The folio was still open on the table. The words hadn't changed. His great-great-grandfather's handwriting was steady and certain, the marginalia of a man who understood what his house was and wrote it down plainly for the people who would come after.

Draco closed the folio. He stood up. The archive room shuddered again and this time the walls did contract, the space narrowing, and he walked out before it could close on him. The library beyond was empty. Late morning light came through the tall windows, weak and winter-thin, and the reading chair where she usually sat had a book open on its arm, spine up, the way she left them when she was coming back.

She was always coming back. The house made sure of it.

---

He found her in the library after lunch. She was at the cataloguing table, surrounded by the St. Mungo's reports and two of the anchoring texts they'd been decoding together, and she looked up when he came in with the expression she always wore when she was deep in something — slightly startled, slightly annoyed at the interruption, already composing the sentence she'd been about to write.

"I found the section on the ward-layer interface," she said. "The third anchoring point has a completely different binding structure than the first two. I think that's why the decay is uneven — the original architects used at least two different foundational—"

"When did you find the bonding folio?"

The sentence stopped. Not trailed off — stopped, like a recording cut mid-syllable. Her hand was still holding her quill and the ink was forming a slow dark circle on the page beneath it where the nib was pressed down.

"What?" she said.

"The folio on the Manor's bonding architecture. It's in the deep archive, third unit from the east wall, behind the ley-line cross-index. I found it this morning." He was standing in the doorway. He didn't come closer. "When did you find it?"

She put the quill down. The motion was careful, deliberate — the way she did things when she was buying herself time. He'd catalogued that too. He'd catalogued everything about her and now the catalogue had a caption he hadn't written: *she knew*.

"Malfoy—"

"When."

"I don't — can we—"

"It's a simple question, Granger. I can count back from when you started acting differently if you'd rather I work it out myself."

Her face changed. He watched it happen — the micro-expressions he had learned to read over weeks of proximity, the way her features organized themselves when she was calculating options. She was deciding how to tell him. She was deciding what version of the truth would cause the least damage, which was what she did, which was what she had always done, and he had mistaken it for honesty because the calculations were so good.

"Five weeks ago," she said. "After the — after the resonance became bilateral. I was looking for an explanation and I found the folio."

Five weeks.

"So when you pulled back. When you went—" He stopped. Started again. "The week where you could barely look at me. That was because you found out the house was engineering this."

"I didn't know that's what it was doing, not for certain, the texts describe a mechanism but the activation conditions are—"

"Stop. Don't do that."

She went quiet. The quill was on the table and the ink stain was spreading on the page and neither of them moved to blot it.

"You found out the house was designed to bond us," he said. "You found out the Manor amplifies what's between its inhabitants. You found out the resonance, the gated archive, the way the corridors change — all of it is the house treating our — treating whatever this is as something to grow. You found that out five weeks ago."

"Yes."

"And you didn't tell me."

"I was trying to verify—"

"You didn't tell me. You pulled back for a week, you came back, you — the scar. The Mark. The examination after the examination. You sat across from me every day and you let me think what was happening between us was—" His voice was doing something he hadn't authorized. Getting louder. He corrected it, pulling it back to the register that cost him more — the quiet one, the controlled one, the one that sounded like his father at his most dangerous, and he hated that but it was what he had. "You kissed me."

Her chin came up. Not defiant — bracing.

"You kissed me knowing the house might be making us feel this. You kissed me knowing our — the resonance, the way I feel you in every room, the way my Mark quiets under your hands — you knew all of it might be architecture. And you kissed me anyway and you didn't tell me what you knew."

"That's not—" She stood up from the cataloguing table. The chair scraped back across the stone floor and the sound was too loud in the library. "That's not what happened."

"Then what happened."

"I found the texts and I panicked. I didn't know what they meant. I didn't know if the amplification was — I couldn't tell if what I was feeling was mine or the house's and I needed to understand before I—"

"Before you what? Before you told me that my house was manipulating us? Before you gave me the chance to decide for myself whether I wanted to — whether what I—" He stopped again. The sentence he'd almost finished would have been too honest and he was not going to be honest right now. Honesty was what she'd taken from him. The right to be honest on his own terms, with full information, making choices he understood.

"You were the researcher," he said. "I was the subject. Again."

"No. That's not fair."

"Fair." The word came out flat. "You found out the experiment was compromised and you didn't report it. You continued running it. You continued running it on me."

"I wasn't running anything on you, I was trying to — God, Malfoy, do you think I was sitting in my room cataloguing data? I was terrified. I couldn't sleep. I couldn't tell if the fact that I wanted to be near you was real or if the house was — I couldn't tell. And I couldn't tell *you* because the moment I did, everything between us would become—"

"Become what? Honest? Informed?"

"Would become about the house instead of about us."

The sentence landed in the library like something dropped from a height. He heard it. She heard herself say it. For a moment neither of them spoke and the only sound was the January wind against the library windows and the faint settling creak of shelves that had been rearranging themselves for centuries.

"There is no us that isn't about the house," he said. "That's the point. That's what the texts say. That's what you knew and I didn't."

"I know."

"You stayed. You came back after the scar, you — you let me touch it. You let me sit there and trace the letters like some — and the whole time you knew the house was doing this to us and you didn't say a word."

She flinched. The flinch was real — he could see it in her shoulders, in the way her hands came together in front of her, and he didn't care. He didn't care that she was hurting because she had made this choice, she had chosen to know and to stay and to not tell him, and every moment of closeness between them was now something he couldn't trust.

"You treated this like a research problem," he said. "You found the variable and you kept it to yourself so you could study it. You could watch me — what, fall? Respond? You could track the bond's progression with a control group of one and I didn't even know I was being observed."

"That's not what I was doing." Her voice had shifted — harder now, the professional precision that meant she was fighting back. "I was living in it. I was living in this house feeling you through the walls and not knowing if any of it was mine. Do you understand what that's like? To want someone and not know if the wanting belongs to you?"

"I'd have liked the chance to find out."

"I'm telling you what it was like."

"You're telling me now. Five weeks later. After I found it myself. That's not confession, Granger, that's getting caught."

The words hit her and he saw them land. Her mouth pressed closed and her hands flattened on the table and for a moment she looked like she was going to walk out. He almost wanted her to. It would have been easier if she'd left then — if the confrontation had ended in the familiar pattern of retreat and door and the sound of someone deciding he wasn't worth the argument.

She didn't leave. She stayed and she said: "You want to know why I didn't tell you about the scar? Before the glamour slipped? Because I wasn't ready. Because showing you what your aunt carved into me while you stood there and watched wasn't something I could do on anyone's schedule but mine. And you understood that. You touched it and you didn't ask me why I'd hidden it. You understood that some things take time."

"That's not the same thing."

"It's exactly the same thing. I had information about myself that I wasn't ready to share, and I shared it when I could. The bonding texts—"

"The bonding texts aren't about you. They're about us. They're about me. They're about my house and my Mark and whether the thing I feel when I look at you is something I chose or something this building decided I should feel, and you had that information and you kept it because *you* weren't ready. My feelings. My agency. My choice. And you made it about your readiness."

She opened her mouth and closed it again. The self-correction was visible — the sentence she'd started that she pulled back from, and whatever it was he'd never know because she swallowed it and what came out instead was smaller and rawer.

"You're right," she said. "You're right about that."

"I know I'm right."

"But you're also — you're making it sound like I was cold. Like I was sitting with this information and calmly evaluating outcomes. I wasn't calm. I was barely functional. I was examining your Mark every week and touching your arm and feeling the resonance sing and thinking *this might not be real, this might not be real, this might not be real* — and then you'd say something and I'd forget to doubt it. That's not calculation. That's someone who couldn't hold two things at once."

"Then you should have told me so we could hold them together."

She didn't answer that. Her eyes were bright. Not crying — he'd never seen her cry and he suspected she'd sooner bleed — but the brightness was there, the surface tension of something held.

"I was scared," she said. The repetition was different this time — not a defense but a fact, stripped of argument. "I was scared that if I told you, you'd look at me the way you're looking at me right now."

He didn't have an answer for that either. They stood on opposite sides of the cataloguing table with the scattered reports between them and neither of them spoke and the silence was not the kind they'd shared after the Mark memory or after the scar — it was not the silence of two people resting in shared understanding. It was the silence of two people who had run out of ways to hurt each other and found they were not done yet.

"So you decided for both of us. You decided I didn't get to know. You decided the timeline. You decided when the information would be shared and on what terms and in the meantime you kissed me and you examined my Mark and you sat in my library and you—" His voice broke. Not dramatically, not a crack that anyone would notice, but he heard it. A fracture in the controlled register where the real sound leaked through — not anger but the thing underneath anger, the thing he was trying very hard not to name because naming it would give her something she hadn't earned.

"Even this," he said. He was quieter now. The quietness was worse. "Even this was something that happened to me. The Mark was done to me. The house was done to me. My father decided I would live here and serve and carry the name and the brand and I did. And then you came and I thought — I thought this was the one thing I chose. That wanting you was mine. That being wanted was — but it wasn't. It was the house's. Or it might have been. And you knew that and you let me keep believing I was choosing."

The library was very still. The winter light had shifted while they talked and the shadows from the tall windows lay differently across the floor now, longer, the day already turning toward the early dark of January. The ink stain on the cataloguing page had dried into an irregular dark bloom.

"I wasn't calculating," she said. Her voice was different. Smaller. The competence stripped down to something raw and insufficient. "I know that's what it looks like. I know that's — but I was drowning. I've been drowning since I found those texts. Every time you touched me, every time I — I didn't know if my own feelings were mine. I didn't know if the fact that I couldn't breathe when you were close was because I wanted you or because the house had decided I should. And I couldn't tell you that because—"

"Because telling me would have meant trusting me with it."

"Because telling you would have meant watching you look at me the way you looked at me in the drawing room. Like I was something the house had done to you."

The sentence hit him somewhere he hadn't braced for. She saw it. He could tell she saw it because something in her face shifted — not triumph, not leverage, just the terrible recognition of having landed a blow she hadn't wanted to land.

"That's what you think?" he said. "That I'd have blamed you for the bond?"

"You're blaming me now."

"I'm blaming you for the secret. Not for the—" He stopped. His hand was gripping the edge of the reading chair and the wood was biting into his palm and he made himself let go. "I'm blaming you for keeping it from me."

"And if I'd told you? If I'd sat you down the day I found the texts and said, 'The house is a bonding artifact and everything we feel might be its construction' — you'd have been fine? You'd have kept working with me, kept letting me examine your Mark, kept sitting across from me at dinner knowing the house might be — you'd have been fine?"

"I'd have been informed. I'd have had the same chance you had to decide what to do with the information."

"You'd have shut down," she said. The words were quiet but steady and he heard in them the specific certainty of someone who had spent five weeks running the scenario. "You'd have gone formal and careful and you'd have stopped letting me near the Mark and the archive would have closed and the research would have ended and we'd have been two people in a house that was getting colder and colder and neither of us would have—"

"You don't know that."

"I know you, Malfoy."

The claim sat between them. She was right — she did know him, in the specific and devastating way of someone who had been paying close attention for weeks. She knew his defenses and his patterns and the way he retreated when threatened. She knew that the formality he was deploying right now was the same formality that would have walled him off if she'd told him the truth five weeks ago. She was probably right about what he'd have done. And the fact that she was right didn't make what she'd done less wrong. It made it worse. She'd used her knowledge of him to make a decision about him, and the precision of her prediction didn't change the fact that it was hers to make and not his.

The brightness in her eyes spilled over — one line, down the left side of her face, and she wiped it with the back of her hand, fast, angry. She knew she'd lost. He could see it in the way her shoulders dropped, the way the fight went out of her posture. Not because his argument was better but because they were both right and being right wasn't going to fix anything.

"I should have told you," she said.

"Yes."

"I should have told you the day I found them."

"Yes."

"I was wrong."

He didn't say anything to that. The admission sat in the air between them and it was true and it was not enough and they both knew it.

"What do you want me to do?" she said.

The question was wrong. He could feel it being wrong — the way it framed the next move as hers to make, the way it put him in the position of deciding what would fix this, as if there were a prescription and she just needed the correct one. It was how she operated. Give her the parameters and she'd solve the problem. But he was not a problem and this was not solvable and the fact that she was standing there asking him to tell her what to do meant she still didn't understand what she'd broken.

"I don't want you to do anything," he said.

"Malfoy—"

"I think you should go."

The words came out in the register he'd been trying to hold — quiet, controlled, the formality that was his oldest defense, the voice that said *I am Draco Malfoy and you cannot reach me from where you are standing.* It was a lie. Everything about the way he was standing, the way he'd folded his arms, the way he was looking at her from across the library with the reading table and the cataloguing table and six feet of cold stone floor between them — all of it was armor. And she knew it was armor because she'd spent five weeks learning what was underneath it.

"Go where?" she said.

"Home. London. Wherever you — just. Not here."

"The research—"

"I don't care about the research right now."

"The decay is getting worse. The Floo failures are escalating. Children are having accidental magic spikes because the ambient field is—"

"I know what's happening to the magic, Granger. I've lived in it my whole life. My family built the infrastructure that's failing. You don't need to brief me on the urgency while I'm—" He stopped. Breathed. The air in the library tasted like old paper and preservation charms and the faint mineral undertone of the Manor's stone walls. He'd been breathing this air his entire life. It had never occurred to him that the air itself might be part of the architecture. "I can't look at you right now. I need you to not be here."

She was waiting. He understood what she was waiting for — not forgiveness, she was too smart to expect that, but something. A word, a gesture, a crack in the formality that would tell her this was survivable. She was looking at him the way she'd looked at the drawing room when the Manor first opened it — the expression of someone standing in front of a thing that could destroy them and not being able to leave.

He didn't give her what she was waiting for. He couldn't. The anger was too large and too specific and underneath it was the thing he would not name because naming it would mean admitting that her five weeks of silence had cost him something he might not get back, and he was not ready to know the size of that loss. Not yet. Not in front of her.

"Go home, Granger."

---

She went upstairs. He heard her footsteps on the stone — not running, not slow, a measured pace that was its own kind of performance. The library corridor amplified sound the way it always had, the high ceilings and bare walls turning every step into a small announcement, and he stood at the cataloguing table and listened until the sound faded into the upper floors.

He looked down at the table. Her handwriting was everywhere — the notes she'd been making, the cross-references in her small tight script, the marginal annotations on the St. Mungo's reports that were more insightful than anything the Ministry's own analysts had produced. She wrote with a slight leftward lean and her lowercase e's were distinctive and he was noticing this because he was not going to think about the sound she'd made when she said *I was scared that if I told you, you'd look at me the way you're looking at me right now.*

He was not going to think about that.

The Manor knew. He could feel it in the walls — a contraction, a pulling-in, the way the house responded to loss. The library light dimmed. Not dramatically, not a plunge into darkness, but a greying-out, the warmth draining from the air. The tall shelves along the east wall shifted, a slow grinding realignment that he felt through the soles of his feet, and two texts fell from a high shelf and landed face-down on the floor.

He didn't pick them up.

He went to the library window instead, the one that faced the front drive. The glass was old — handblown, wavy, the kind of glass that had been in Malfoy windows for two hundred years — and through it the January grounds were flat and grey, the frost on the lawn catching the fading light. The drive curved away from the house and out through the gates and beyond the gates was the lane and beyond the lane was everything that wasn't here.

The bonding folio was still in the archive. He'd left it on the tilted table when the walls contracted. It was probably still there, open to the section on activation conditions, his great-great-grandfather's handwriting explaining calmly and precisely how the house was designed to make its inhabitants fall in love.

Not fall in love. He corrected himself with the precision she'd taught him. *Amplify existing resonance.* The house didn't create. It enhanced. The distinction mattered, or it was supposed to matter, except right now the distinction felt like the difference between being pushed off a building and being pushed off a slightly lower building. You still hit the ground.

He sat down in the reading chair. Her reading chair — the one she'd claimed without asking, the one that always had a book on its arm, the one that smelled faintly of her ink and the hand lotion she used after hours of handling old texts. The book she'd left was still there, spine up, the pages spread. He picked it up and closed it properly because that was what you did with books and he was still himself even if everything else had been a construction.

Upstairs, he could hear her moving. The guest wing was directly above the library's northwest corner and the Manor's stone carried sound in unpredictable ways — she was opening drawers, closing them, the faint thud of what might have been a trunk lid. She was packing. She was actually packing and leaving and the house was contracting around the fact of her departure like a body responding to a wound.

The temperature dropped. Not the gradual cooling of the Manor settling into evening but a sharp, measurable decrease — the stone under his feet was cold in a way it hadn't been since before she arrived. Since the weeks of empty rooms and corridors that went nowhere and the Mark throbbing without relief and the particular silence of a house that was waiting for something and the person inside it who was trying very hard not to be the thing it was waiting for.

She came down twenty minutes later. He was still in the reading chair. He'd thought about moving — about going to his rooms, about not being here when she left — but his body had made the decision for him, which was what bodies did in this house apparently, and he was sitting in the library with a closed book in his lap when she appeared in the doorway with her bag over her shoulder and her coat on.

The coat was Muggle. She'd arrived in it, weeks ago, a dark wool thing with too many buttons. It looked wrong on her now, or it looked right in a way that was wrong — it was the coat of the woman who'd walked into his house as a researcher, a professional, a person with a job to do, and the woman standing in the doorway was all of those things and also the woman who'd kissed him in this room three days ago and he couldn't make the two of them fit together.

"The notes are on the table," she said. "I duplicated everything that was shared. Your family texts are in the second stack."

"Right."

"The anchoring analysis is — there's a section I didn't finish. The third-point binding structure. If you can get the archive to hold, the reference you'll need is—"

"I'll manage."

She stood in the doorway. The light from the corridor behind her was grey and the library light was grey and between the two greys she was a particular shape — coat, bag, hair escaping from whatever she'd tied it in, her face doing the thing where it tried to be composed and failed at the edges.

"I didn't mean to — I know that doesn't matter. I know 'I didn't mean to' doesn't—" She stopped. Restarted, and the restart was worse because it was more precise. "I made a choice. Every day for five weeks I chose not to tell you and I don't have a defense for that that would mean anything to you right now. I was wrong and I was scared and those aren't different things even though I want them to be."

He looked at her. He let himself look, which was the cruelest thing he could do to both of them, because looking at her while she stood in his doorway about to leave was exactly the kind of self-destruction he'd been raised for. The Malfoy inheritance: beautiful things in beautiful rooms that you couldn't trust.

She waited another moment. He said nothing. She turned and walked down the corridor and he listened to her footsteps on the stone — two, four, six, eight, each one fainter than the last — and then the front door. The Manor's main entrance, the one that hadn't opened for anyone but him in years until she came. It closed with a sound that was heavier than wood and iron should produce, a finality that he felt in the floor, and then the house began to grieve.

That was the only word for it. Not the slow cooling of her withdrawal in November but something faster and more complete — a thermal collapse, the warmth draining from the walls like blood from a face. The library shelves groaned and shifted and the window light went from grey to the colourless nothing of deep winter at four o'clock. The corridor outside the library shortened, then lengthened, then shortened again, the house confused by the sudden absence of the person it had been built around for weeks.

The Mark woke up.

Not the low ache he'd been carrying since the resonance began — this was the old pain, the pain from before she came, before her diagnostic magic quieted the curse's restless thrashing. It came back in a wave that made him grip the arm of the chair, the dark magic in his forearm churning without direction, without the channel her sustained contact had carved through it, and the pain was not metaphorical or symbolic or architectural. It was the specific, physical, nauseating burn of a Dark Lord's brand reasserting itself in the absence of the one thing that had made it bearable.

He sat in the reading chair in the cold library in the cold house and he held his arm and the Manor contracted around him, corridor by corridor, room by room, the great house pulling in on itself.

She was gone. The research was unfinished. The magic was still dying. His arm was on fire and his house was grieving and the woman who could have told him the truth had chosen, for five weeks, to study the problem instead.

He thought about getting up. Going to the kitchen, making tea, performing the motions of a life that worked. He'd done it before she came — years of it, the careful choreography of a man living alone in a house built for a dynasty. Tea at seven. Library at nine. Lunch he sometimes forgot. Mark pain managed with a rotation of spells that didn't work well but worked enough. Theo every few weeks, a dinner where they didn't talk about the important things and the not-talking was its own kind of care. It had been livable. It had been the shape of his days and he'd made peace with it the way you made peace with anything — not by accepting it but by forgetting to notice it.

She'd made him notice. Weeks of shared meals and late-night translations and the way she pushed her hair back when she was thinking and the sound of another person's footsteps in the corridor and the specific weight of being known by someone who was paying attention. She'd made the livable thing look like what it was — a controlled diminishment, a life with the edges sanded down until it couldn't cut him — and now that she was gone the old shape didn't fit. The edges were back and everything was sharp.

The book she'd left was still on the chair beside him, closed now, its spine straight. He didn't look at it. He looked at the library windows where the January dark was settling in, early and complete, and the pain in his arm was steady and familiar and not enough to fill the quiet she'd left behind.

The corridor outside the library shortened. Then lengthened. The house couldn't decide how to hold itself without her in it, and Draco understood that because neither could he.

He sat in the chair. The dark came in. The cold did its work, settling into the stone and the shelves and the table where her handwriting was still spread across the reports, her small e's and her precise notation and the ink stain she'd left when he said the words that ended them.

He didn't get up. He didn't make tea. He sat in the cold house and held his burning arm and waited for the cold to become familiar again.

It didn't. It had been too warm for too long. The cold felt like something borrowed from a life he no longer fit into, and that was the worst part — not the anger, not the betrayal, not the Mark's pain returning. The worst part was that the cold had once been comfortable and now it wasn't, and she had done that too.


# Chapter 17

The flat hadn't changed.

Hermione had been gone for five and a half weeks — she knew the number because her brain produced it unbidden, the way it produced everything, whether she wanted it or not — and her flat had waited with the particular patience of a space that didn't care whether she came back. The mug she'd left by the sink had grown a pale film. The stack of post on the hall table had thickened into something architectural. Her bedroom smelled of closed windows and dust, the mild staleness that accrued when no one opened a door for over a month.

She'd been back for three days.

The walls did not shift. This was the first thing she'd noticed and the thing she couldn't stop noticing — the fixed, dumb permanence of plaster and paint. The corridor from her bedroom to the kitchen was exactly as long at midnight as it had been at seven in the morning. The bathroom door opened onto the bathroom. The windows showed the same stretch of wet December street, the same sodium light, the same skip across the road that someone had been filling with broken furniture since before she'd left for Wiltshire.

Ordinary architecture. Ordinary silence. The silence of a space where sound simply stopped, rather than the loaded, attentive quiet of a house that tracked your breathing and decided which rooms you deserved.

She'd washed the mug. She'd gone through the post — mostly circulars, a note from her mother she wasn't ready to answer, two Ministry memos marked URGENT that she'd responded to with the careful, thorough competence that functioned even when the rest of her didn't. She'd tidied the kitchen. She'd alphabetized a shelf of texts she'd already alphabetized before she left, which was the kind of thing she did when she needed to be doing something and the thing she actually needed to do was impossible. She'd stood in the middle of her sitting room at two in the morning on the second night with her wand in her hand and nothing to point it at, and the flatness of that — the absence of any surface that would respond to her attention — had been worse than she'd expected.

The flat was fourteen hundred square feet. She knew this because the estate agent had told her, a year and a half ago, when she'd been looking for somewhere to live that wasn't the house she'd shared with Ron. She'd chosen it for its smallness, its containability, the way every room was visible from every other room. After the divorce, she'd wanted a space she could hold in her head entire. No hidden rooms. No surprises.

The Manor's library alone had been larger than this. Her room in the guest wing had been twice the size of her bedroom here. The corridors between them had been — variable, alive, occasionally conspiratorial, the house shortening the path between the library and her bed on nights she worked late, as if it understood her exhaustion and took pity on it. Or as if it wanted her in the library and was removing obstacles. She'd never been certain which, and the uncertainty had been part of the texture of living there, the way you might live with a cat whose affection you couldn't fully distinguish from its appetite.

She stopped the comparison. It was the kind of thinking that inventoried what she'd lost instead of what she'd chosen to leave.

Except she hadn't chosen to leave. She'd been — no. She had left. He hadn't told her to go. He'd said what he'd said and she'd looked at his face and she'd left because staying after what he knew was impossible, which felt like not-choosing but was, she understood, a choice dressed in the costume of necessity.

She sat on the edge of the sofa and pressed her palms flat against her knees. The upholstery was a grey twill she'd picked because it was inoffensive. It didn't warm under her hands the way the Manor's surfaces had warmed, the leather of the library chairs holding heat like skin, the stone walls in the corridors near her room running faintly warm by the second week, as if the house were adjusting its temperature to hers. This sofa was just a sofa. It was the temperature it was.

The Mark resonance was still there.

Not the way it had been at the Manor — that close, involuntary awareness of him on the floor above, two rooms away, turning in his sleep. Distance had degraded it into something blunter. But it was there. A low ache in her left forearm that wasn't hers, that worsened at odd hours — four in the morning, midday, late evening — with no pattern she could map. The Mark was hurting him. She could feel it the way you felt a pulled tooth: the gap where the thing used to be, the tongue that kept finding the empty socket and couldn't stop searching it.

Without her sustained diagnostic contact, the quiet the Mark had maintained was gone. Whatever her magic had been doing to the curse — soothing it, stabilizing it, or something more structural that she hadn't finished understanding before everything ended — it had stopped when she'd walked out of the Manor. The Mark had resumed its thrashing. She could feel the edges of it from here: restless, aimless dark magic trying to move through a channel that no longer connected to anything. A curse designed to bind a servant to a master, the master seven years dead, the binding fraying into purposeless pain, and the sustained contact that had been the only thing quieting it now forty miles and three days removed.

There was nothing she could do about it from a flat in London with walls that didn't know her name.

She hadn't slept properly since she'd come back. Not the productive insomnia of research — the kind that had kept her up in the Manor's library with a text open on her knees and the resonance telling her he was awake too, two floors up, probably reading, and the strange comfort of that parallel wakefulness. This was the other kind. She lay in her bed in the dark and the dark was just dark and the silence was just silence and her brain ran the confrontation in the same merciless loop, catching on the same moments, wearing them smooth with repetition until the words lost meaning but not force.

_You knew the house was doing this and you stayed._

_After the examination, after the drawing room, after the scar._

_You knew and you didn't think I deserved to know._

She did think he deserved to know. She had. She'd been going to tell him. She'd worked through versions of the conversation — six, maybe seven, each one more carefully constructed than the last — and every version had collapsed under the weight of the part she couldn't frame. That she'd kissed him knowing their feelings might be architected. That she'd kissed him and hadn't wanted to stop, and the not wanting to stop was the thing she couldn't trust, because the house had been warming around them and the resonance had been singing in her arm and she didn't know — still didn't know — how much of what she'd felt was hers.

But that wasn't what he'd been angry about. Not really.

She'd thought about this in the dark, lying on a mattress that didn't adjust itself to her weight, staring at a ceiling that was just stippled plaster. She'd turned it over the way she turned problems — methodically, looking for the structural failure point — and she kept arriving at the same place.

He hadn't been angry about the bond. He'd been angry about the asymmetry.

She'd known something about them that changed the terms of every interaction, and she'd continued interacting on the old terms. She'd touched his arm during examinations knowing the Mark responded to her because the house was engineering a connection. She'd sat across from him in the library knowing the room held because the bond held. She'd kissed him knowing everything she knew and he'd kissed her back knowing nothing, and the gap between their knowledge was a gap in his agency, and she —

She pressed her fingers into her closed eyes until colours bloomed behind the lids.

She'd had reasons. She'd needed to verify. She'd needed to understand the mechanism before presenting it as fact. She'd needed time to think, to be sure, to figure out what to do with information that retroactively poisoned every good thing that had happened between them.

The reasons were real. They were also, she was beginning to suspect, not the point.

---

The Floo went down on Thursday.

Not a partial failure — the stuttering connections and dropped calls that had become background noise for months, the kind of degradation people had started accepting the way you accepted a road with potholes, adjusting your route and your expectations downward. A full system outage. At seven forty-three in the morning, Hermione stepped into her fireplace with a handful of powder and said "Ministry of Magic" clearly into the grate and nothing happened. The fire burned. The powder dissolved. The flames stayed their ordinary orange — no green rush, no spinning, no arrival.
